# 易因

**Yiyin**

**Title:** *Yiyin* (also known as *Ninefold Orthodox Yiyin* or *Master Li’s Yiyin*). Authored by Li Zhan of the Ming dynasty. Six juan in total. Base text from the *Wanli Continuation of the Daoist Canon*. Collated with the Qing-era Jiguge edition from the *Continued Compilation of the Four Treasuries* (hereafter "Jiguge edition").

---

### **Yiyin Upper Canon**

**Qian** 乾, **Kun** 坤, **Zhun** 屯, **Meng** 蒙,
**Xu** 需, **Song** 讼, **Shi** 师, **Bi** 比, **Xiaoxu** 小畜,
**Lǚ** 履, **Tai** 泰, **Pi** 否, **Tongren** 同人, **Dayou** 大有, **Qian** 谦,
**Yu** 豫, **Sui** 随, **Gu** 蠱, **Lin** 临, **Guan** 观, **Shike** 噬嗑,
**Bi** 贲, **Bo** 剥, **Fu** 复, **Wuwang** 无妄, **Daxu** 大畜, **Yi** 颐,
**Daguo** 大过, **Xikan** 习坎, **Li** 离.

---

### **Yiyin Upper Canon, Juan One**

**Qian** (Heaven below, Heaven above)

**Qian:** *Yuan* (Origin), *heng* (Prosperity), *li* (Benefit), *zhen* (Integrity).

**First Nine:** The hidden dragon—do not act.
**Nine in the Second:** The dragon appears in the field—beneficial to see the great person.
**Nine in the Third:** The noble one toils ceaselessly by day, vigilant by night; though perilous, there is no blame.
**Nine in the Fourth:** Or leaping in the abyss—no blame.
**Nine in the Fifth:** The dragon soars in the heavens—beneficial to see the great person.
**Top Nine:** The exalted dragon has regret.
**Use of Nine:** Seeing a host of dragons without a head—auspicious.

---

**The Judgment (Tuan #2):**
Vast is the *yuan* of Qian! The myriad things rely on it for their beginning, for it unites Heaven. Clouds move and rain descends; the ten thousand kinds flow forth. The great light begins and ends; the six positions complete their seasons. Thus, it rides the six dragons to govern Heaven. The Dao of Qian transforms and changes, each attaining its proper nature and destiny. Preserving and uniting the Great Harmony—this is *li* and *zhen*. Emerging first among the multitude of things, the myriad states all find peace.

**The Image (Xiang):**
The movement of Heaven is strong—thus the noble one ceaselessly strengthens himself.
"The hidden dragon—do not act": Yang is below.
"The dragon appears in the field": Virtue is universally bestowed.
"Toiling ceaselessly by day": The Dao is repeatedly traversed.
"Or leaping in the abyss": Advancement brings no blame.
"The dragon soars in the heavens": The great person achieves.
"The exalted dragon has regret": Fullness cannot endure.
"Use of Nine": The virtue of Heaven cannot be made a head.

---

**The Words of the Text (Wenyan):**
*Yuan* is the chief of goodness.
*Heng* is the gathering of excellence.
*Li* is the harmony of righteousness.
*Zhen* is the stem of affairs.
The noble one embodies benevolence, sufficient to lead others;
gathers excellence, sufficient to accord with ritual;
benefits things, sufficient to harmonize righteousness;
holds fast to integrity, sufficient to manage affairs.
The noble one practices these four virtues—thus it is said: *Qian: yuan, heng, li, zhen.*

**First Nine: "The hidden dragon—do not act." What does this mean?**
The Master said: It is the dragon’s virtue in concealment. Unchanging in the world, not seeking fame, withdrawing from the world without distress, acting when joyful, avoiding when troubled—firm and unshakable: this is the hidden dragon.

**Nine in the Second: "The dragon appears in the field—beneficial to see the great person." What does this mean?**
The Master said: It is the dragon’s virtue in central correctness. Words are trustworthy in common speech; conduct is cautious in common deeds. Guarding against deceit, preserving sincerity, benefiting the world without boasting—virtue is vast and transformative. The *Yi* says: "The dragon appears in the field—beneficial to see the great person"—this is the virtue of a ruler.

**Nine in the Third: "The noble one toils ceaselessly by day, vigilant by night; though perilous, there is no blame." What does this mean?**
The Master said: The noble one advances virtue and cultivates work. Loyalty and trust advance virtue; refining words and establishing sincerity secure work. Knowing the utmost and attaining it—one may approach the subtle. Knowing the end and completing it—one may preserve righteousness. Thus, in high position without arrogance, in low position without anxiety. Therefore, ceaseless toil, vigilant in due time—though perilous, there is no blame.

**Nine in the Fourth: "Or leaping in the abyss—no blame." What does this mean?**
The Master said: Neither fixed above nor below, not acting perversely. Neither advancing nor retreating without constancy, not straying from the Dao. The noble one advances virtue and cultivates work, desiring to seize the moment—thus there is no blame.

**Nine in the Fifth: "The dragon soars in the heavens—beneficial to see the great person." What does this mean?**
The Master said: Those of the same sound respond; those of the same qi seek one another.

Water flows toward the damp, fire inclines toward the dry; clouds follow the dragon, wind follows the tiger. When the sage arises, all things behold him. That which is rooted in heaven inclines upward, that which is rooted in earth inclines downward—thus each follows its kind.

**Nine in the Top: "The dragon soars to its limit—there is regret." What does this mean?**
The Master said: Exalted yet without position, lofty yet without the people, the worthy remain below without aid—thus in action there is regret.

The hidden dragon is not employed—this is the lower position.
The dragon appears in the field—this is the time of release.
All day vigilant—this is conducting affairs.
Or leaping in the abyss—this is self-testing.
The dragon soars in the heavens—this is governing on high.
The dragon soars to its limit—this is the calamity of exhaustion.
When Qian’s primal yang employs the nine, the world is well-governed.

The hidden dragon is not employed—yang qi lies concealed.
The dragon appears in the field—the world’s patterns become luminous.
All day vigilant—moving in step with the times.
Or leaping in the abyss—the Dao of Qian undergoes transformation.
The dragon soars in the heavens—thus it attains the virtue of heaven.
The dragon soars to its limit—moving in step with the time’s extremity.
When Qian’s primal yang employs the nine—thus the celestial norm is revealed.

Qian’s primal yang is the beginning that brings prosperity. That which is beneficial and steadfast is its nature. Qian’s beginning can with perfect beauty benefit all under heaven—yet it does not speak of what it benefits. How great! How great is Qian!

Strong and firm, centered and upright—pure and refined essence.
The six lines unfold and extend, communicating the fullness of its nature.
Riding the six dragons in due season—thus governing heaven.
Clouds gather and rain descends—all under heaven is at peace.

The noble person takes the perfection of virtue as their conduct, a daily visible practice.
"Concealment" speaks of what is hidden and not yet manifest, in motion yet unformed—thus the noble person does not employ it.
The noble person studies to accumulate it, questions to discern it, dwells in magnanimity, and acts with benevolence.

The *Yi* says: "The dragon appears in the field—it is beneficial to see the great person." This is the virtue of the ruler.
Nine in the third place: doubly firm yet not centered, neither in heaven above nor in the field below. Thus it is vigilant all day, ever watchful in its time—though perilous, it incurs no blame.
Nine in the fourth place: doubly firm yet not centered, neither in heaven above, nor in the field below, nor among people in the middle. Thus it "perhaps" acts. "Perhaps" means hesitation—yet it incurs no blame.

The great person harmonizes their virtue with heaven and earth, their brightness with the sun and moon, their order with the four seasons, their auspices and omens with spirits and gods.
Preceding heaven, heaven does not oppose; following heaven, they uphold the celestial seasons. If even heaven does not oppose, how could they be matched by mere humans? Or equaled by ghosts and spirits?

"Excess" speaks of knowing advance but not retreat, knowing existence but not demise, knowing gain but not loss. Is this not the sage alone? Who else knows advance and retreat, existence and demise, yet never loses their correctness?

Qian is heaven. How great is Qian’s primal power—all things rely on it for their beginning. Having relied on it to begin, they must rely on it to end. If not for Qian’s primal power governing heaven, what else could it be?

Heaven is but one thing among the myriad, yet without Qian’s primal power to govern it, how could it move clouds and send down rain, allowing all beings to flow and communicate, taking form and flourishing as they do? Thus it is said: How great is Qian’s primal power.

People merely fail to comprehend Qian’s Way in its beginning and end—hence they do not know the greatness of Qian’s primal power. If one could but understand this, they would see that the six positions of the hexagram are all already complete in their time—it is only a matter of riding the moment to act.

Thus, dwelling in the first place, one rides the hidden dragon; in the second, the appearing dragon; in the third, the vigilant dragon; in the fourth, the leaping dragon; in the fifth, the soaring dragon; in the top place, the excessive dragon. All are Qian’s natural transformations—the sage merely rides the time to govern heaven.

Each thing possesses its own Qian’s primal power—this is the rectification of each nature and destiny, which cannot be made identical. The myriad things share one body of Qian’s primal power—this is the preservation of the Great Harmony, which cannot be made different. Thus it is said: "Thus it is beneficial and steadfast."

Yet each person rectifies their own Qian’s primal power, each possessing this resource to rise above the common multitude. To attribute the governance of heaven to Qian, the riding of time to govern heaven to the sage, and willingly to rot among the common things—is this not lamentable?

The preservation of the myriad states in harmony stems from this virtue of Qian’s primal power—how could there be a single day without peace? Yet to take Qian as heaven, the myriad as things, the sage as able to pacify the states, and the states as necessarily pacified by the sage—is this not equally lamentable?

Thus it is said: Qian—primordial, flourishing, beneficial, steadfast. These four virtues are ascribed to Qian, yet only "how great" is used to praise the primordial—its meaning runs deep.

The *Wenyan* says: Qian’s primal power is that which begins and flourishes. People see its beginning and flourishing but do not see the beginning of its flourishing. For the beginning of flourishing contains what is beneficial and steadfast—this is the nature of disposition.

How great is Qian’s primal power—it is surely what all things rely on to begin. Who knows that this beneficial and steadfast quality is also what Qian’s primal power relies on to begin? Thus it is also called Qian’s beginning. Only because it possesses this beginning can it then with perfect beauty benefit all under heaven. Otherwise, how could it be so?

Qian’s beginning can with perfect beauty benefit all under heaven without speaking of what it benefits—all under heaven receives it. That Qian’s beginning bestows such beauty yet does not claim its benefit—is this not a great benefit indeed? And who knows that it is precisely this beneficial and steadfast quality that makes it so? Thus it is again praised: How great is Qian’s beneficial steadfastness!

Strong, firm, centered, upright, pure, refined, essence, beauty—though one might strive to describe it fully, there is no adequate comparison. For when disposition follows nature, it becomes the invisible virtue of strength, firmness, centeredness, uprightness, purity, refinement, and essence. When nature follows disposition, even the six lines of this single hexagram, unfolding and extending in all directions, fully express its inexhaustible meaning.

With six lines, there are six dragons to ride in due season to govern heaven. I know that even heaven does not oppose. Having ridden the six dragons, they naturally gather clouds and send down rain—all under heaven is at peace. How could they fail to benefit all under heaven with perfect beauty?

Generally, Qian’s virtue is an empty position—what is called "how great is Qian’s primordial power." Heaven, humans, and sages all share it, so it does not distinguish which is heaven, which is human, or which is sage—making clear that they cannot be divided.

Qian’s steadfastness is a substantive virtue—what is called nature and disposition. Heaven, humans, and sages all possess it, so it does not distinguish which is nature, which is disposition, which is benefit, or which is steadfastness—making clear that they cannot be separated.

The *Wenyan* says: The primordial is the chief of virtues. Observing the words of the *Xiang*, there are only the four virtues: primordial, flourishing, beneficial, steadfast. Yet when the Master reads them, why does he elaborate so? When he says "how great is Qian’s primordial power," he has already praised Qian’s primordial—his emphasis lies in the primordial, guiding people to embody the primordial through Qian, so that the four virtues are all subsumed into one.

When it says, "Qian’s primordial is the beginning and the flourishing," and "beneficial steadfastness is nature and disposition," it further praises Qian’s steadfastness—here the emphasis lies in steadfastness, guiding people to understand steadfastness. Yet it also arises from the primordial to encompass the four virtues, which have never ceased to revolve and become one another. This is the Qian of the sage, not something others can partake in.

When it says, "The primordial is what is called good—is this sufficient to describe Qian and Kun?" If one were rigid and rash yet still called Qian, or weak and ineffectual yet still called Kun, then Heaven would no longer uphold Heaven, and Earth would no longer fulfill Earth. What, then, would we rely upon for sustenance? This is inherently the self-possessed power of the most vigorous and the most yielding—what need is there for supplementation? Thus, the utmost yielding moves with vigor, and the utmost stillness embodies constancy. Earth’s capacity for compliance arises from this, just as Qian’s Dao transforms and each fulfills its nature and destiny. Heaven’s capacity for vigor arises from this: if vigor were merely supplemented by compliance, it would not be true vigor—its use stems from the primordial. If compliance were merely augmented by vigor, it would not be utmost compliance—its use stems from the primordial. Alas, how could this suffice to grasp the true nature of Qian and Kun?

Qian and Kun are one. Thus, *Qian* speaks of primordial flourishing, and *Kun* likewise speaks of primordial flourishing. Yet *Qian* speaks of beneficial steadfastness, while *Kun* speaks of the steadfastness of the mare. And not merely a horse, but a *mare*—how does this compare to the dragon’s transformations and soaring flight? Kun alone finds benefit in this because it is Kun’s steadfastness. Therefore, when the noble person sets forth and models themselves on Kun, they rest solely in the steadfastness of the mare. Thus it is said: "Steadfastness in tranquility brings good fortune." When engaging in affairs, they find benefit solely in the steadfastness of the mare. Thus it is said: "Benefit lies in eternal steadfastness," for it concludes with greatness.

"Great" refers to Qian, which concludes with greatness. Only the primordial dares to take the lead, only the primordial dares to stand at the forefront, only the primordial dares to form factions—this is steadfastness. "Those who take the lead go astray" and below refers to the steadfastness of the mare. "Taking the lead" means acting as Qian, as the master, while we follow—thus we attain the master and benefit the master. Whether west or south, we guard our position, residing in the northeast. Only by following the master does the will remain single-minded and unwavering; by dwelling in tranquility and heeding the master, we attain constancy and avoid confusion. Such is Kun’s beneficial steadfastness—this is primordial flourishing.

Human life is upright, yet uprightness seems not to be square. Now, to speak of uprightness and also of squareness is because people know only uprightness but not that uprightness inherently possesses squareness. Is not the first thought of a person their upright and unobstructed true nature? Yet as soon as it arises, it is immediately perceived—what was first is upright, and what perceives is also upright. Perception leads to stillness, stillness to squareness. When it emerges, though upright and square are united, their essence is one radiant light. Earth’s nature is vast and deep, Kun’s virtue boundless—it cannot stand alone, which is why the lines speak of both uprightness and squareness.

Uprightness is correctness, squareness is righteousness. Correctness is about stillness, righteousness about action. Stillness is reverence—reverence is not deliberate intent but simply inward uprightness. Squareness is righteousness—righteousness is not acquired through imitation but simply through acting with benefit. How could this depend on learning to become beneficial? It is inherently unlearned and yet not unbeneficial—what doubt is there?

**Appendix**
Su Shi’s explanation: The dragon transforms and acts on its own, while the horse is tamed and used by humans. To be used by humans and also to be a mare is the utmost compliance. Yet utmost compliance without steadfastness falls into depravity, hence the benefit of the mare’s steadfastness.

Li Tufang’s explanation: Utmost compliance is never unsteadfast. Utmost compliance is compliance with Qian. If one complies with Qian, how could one not be steadfast? If one seeks companions, joins factions, or travels southwest, one reverts to yin’s softness. Clinging to yin’s softness, how can one single-mindedly obey Qian’s command and achieve utmost compliance?

Another explanation: Kun’s Dao can be used by others but cannot act on its own; it can harmonize but cannot initiate. Thus, the noble person benefits from having somewhere to go. Taking the lead leads to confusion and loss of the Way, while following leads to compliance and attaining the master—this is why it is beneficial. West and south are Dui, Li, and Xun—all our companions. East and north are Zhen, Kan, and Gen—none are our companions. Two yin cannot use each other, so one must leave their kind. Breaking from companions and coming to the master resides in the northeast.

Li Tuweng’s explanation: Qian is Kun’s master. As the master, Qian does not need to be sought; seeking it leads to loss, fostering thoughts of gaining companions and failing to single-mindedly obey Qian’s command.

Another explanation: With six in the second place, one may be called yielding. Uprightness and squareness are what it wishes to express, and when spoken, it is called sincerity. Loyalty perfects sincerity, and sincerity arises from within. Who does not proceed from this righteousness? Thus, the noble person’s loyalty and sincerity endure to the end of their life.

If one does not yet know this as the utmost but merely knows it as their ordinary words and deeds, not daring to abandon it for life, they too can preserve righteousness and minimize errors. Why? Because their daily diligence fears only not aligning with righteousness. Thus, those who know the utmost hear "loyalty and reciprocity" and immediately grasp the One Thread. Otherwise, they may spend their lives upholding loyalty and reciprocity yet never understand what the One Thread means. Therefore, Master Zeng regarded loyalty and reciprocity as the ultimate, while his disciples upheld loyalty and reciprocity to the end of their lives.

This marks the distinction between attainment and non-attainment.

The Master said: *"Is there a single word one can practice throughout life? Surely it is reciprocity."* How true this is. For scholars, loyalty and reciprocity may indeed be upheld for a lifetime. Yet only when self-realization dawns does one grasp the utmost—this cannot be forced. Alas, must one first embody the *qi* of *qian*—firm, vigorous, centered, upright, pure, and refined—before such attainment is possible? If so, then the *qi* of *kun* surely cannot be spoken of in terms of knowing the utmost.

The Nine in the Fourth place stands at the apex of *qian*, poised between doubt and certainty. It seems to have grasped the utmost, yet dares not immediately affirm it. If one were to resolve to uphold this for life without finding inner peace, it would be a moment of tentative leaping. Thus, the Master admonishes: *"Virtue values constancy."* Yet now, moving up and down—how can this be called constant? *"Endeavor values perseverance."* Yet now, advancing and retreating—how can this be called persevering? Is it because the words of loyalty and reciprocity are flawed and unworthy of trust? Or must one sever all ties with the common and depart from the herd to become a perfected person? Yet once born among people, how can one stand apart from them?

I say: what matters for the noble person in cultivating virtue and refining conduct is seizing the moment with courage and decisive action. Lingering between doubt and certainty—when will true peace ever come? Without doubt, there is indeed a day of attaining the utmost; but mere doubt—when will there be a moment of stillness? One must decide resolutely in due time to ensure freedom from blame.

The Nine in the Fifth place is the soaring dragon. Here, virtue, position, and timing—all three are fully realized. There is none more fitting for the world to behold than the great person of Nine Five. Yet Nine Two possesses virtue without position—why is it also called a great person, equally the one whom all things seek to behold? For in the world, only virtue is most noble. When virtue resides within me, how can time or position constrain it? Thus it is said: *"From the Son of Heaven to the common people, all must take self-cultivation as their foundation."* If a commoner possesses the virtue of a ruler, they naturally become a great person worthy of emulation. If one in high position lacks virtue, though they occupy Nine Five, who would seek to behold them? Thus, where virtue resides, time and position are not the measure.

Can we not observe this in our Master? It is asked: *"If so, then all six lines are dragons—why only the second?"* Because the dragon’s virtue in the second line is already manifest—what is called *"dragon virtue in true centrality."* This is the dwelling of humanity, the inevitable refuge of the multitude. Thus, though it has not yet ascended to govern heaven, its virtue already pervades all. Though not a ruler, it possesses the virtue of a ruler. Why wait for *"facing the light to govern"* before the world becomes radiant?

As for the other lines, which must seize the moment to advance in virtue and refine their conduct, how can they compare themselves to the second? Thus, the first warns against premature action, recognizing the need for hidden cultivation. The top cautions against excess, lest it lead to the regrets of a dragon soaring too high. As for the third and fourth, if they do not *"vigilantly fear day and night,"* they may hesitate in their leaping—these are times for progress, not moments for emulation. Yet if one’s cultivated virtue and conduct are already manifest daily, whether as the second or the fifth, what prevents it? All are equally great persons, equally the ones whom all things seek to behold. What doubt remains?

The great person’s virtue aligns with heaven and earth. Alas, this is the great person—does Nine Five embody it? Yet the *Wenyan* does not speak of position or lack thereof. Is it truly the fifth, or does Nine Two fulfill it? It does not say *"soaring"* for the exalted or *"manifest"* for the lowly—how can we know if it is truly the second? My view is that such a person is one whom even heaven does not dare defy—not because they command heaven, but because they harmonize with it. Such a person is one whom even people do not dare defy—not because they stand above all things, but because they bring peace to all nations. Such a person is one whom even ghosts and spirits do not dare defy—not because they wield divine or spectral powers, or transform, expand, contract, hide, soar, summon clouds, or bring rain, but because it is so without knowing why.

Alas, this is the great person—something we all share. How dare we shirk responsibility onto others?

Nine Five is the soaring dragon. Yet the Master speaks of *"resonant voices and kindred qi, each following its kind."* Those not of the same kind may not wish to behold them. The third and fourth, the first and top—all occupy the six positions, yet remain unaware of one another. Thus, there is only one great person: Nine Two. Yan Hui was but one who approached it. Alas, the multitude is vast—one is not too few. Yet with Yan Hui’s passing, none remained. How poignant is the Master’s lament for the interval between the second and fifth!

To know when to advance and when to retreat is to be a sage, to never lose correctness, and to avoid excess. Thus, though times may reach excess, the sage never does. Hence, the repeated emphasis on the sage is meant to encourage, not like today’s mediocre and vulgar readers who cherry-pick passages to serve their own ends.

Nine is the active expression of *qian*, so all lines of *qian* use nine. All nines in *qian* are dragons, hence *qian*’s nine is called the *"dragon of the herd."* Yet the dragon of the herd can be seen, while the head of the herd’s dragon cannot. Without seeing this, one cannot become a dragon. Thus, those who use nine must truly perceive the head of the herd’s dragon, and naturally they will rise above all things, bringing peace to all nations. Naturally, they will seize the moment to ride heaven, and the cosmos will lie in their hands. Naturally, they will illuminate the beginning and end of the *qian* Dao, unifying heaven with a single origin, and all transformations will arise from within.

---

Thus it is said: "To employ the nine is to perceive the head of the herd’s dragon—auspicious." Auspiciousness is the *yuan* (origin), the *heng* (prosperity), the *li* (benefit), and the *zhen* (constancy). To employ the nine, the virtue of heaven cannot be made the head; if there were a head, it would not be called the virtue of heaven. Therefore it is said: "When the origin of *qian* employs the nine, the celestial principle is revealed." Su Zizhan said: "The origin of *qian* takes the head as the celestial principle." Alas, how perfect!

**Appendix**
Zhuangzi (#6) said: "The *Yi* (Changes) speaks of the Dao of yin and yang."
Yang Xiong said: "The trigrams of Fuxi and the hexagrams of King Wen, with the six lines appended by Confucius—who arranged their images and composed their words—revealed the hidden workings of heaven and earth and established the foundation of all things."
Chen Tunan said: "The August Sovereign Fuxi first drew the eight trigrams and doubled them into sixty-four, without setting down written words, allowing the people of the world to silently observe their images. If one can accord with the images, fortune and misfortune will respond; if one violates them, fortune and misfortune will reverse. This was the teaching without words of Fuxi."
Shao Yaofu said: "The *Yi* is the tool by which the sage grows the noble and diminishes the petty. When the noble grows, it is checked before it comes to be; when the petty diminishes, it is sealed before it comes to be. One diminishes, one grows; one opens, one closes—all is harmoniously united in the origin, near and far. If not for the utmost spirit of heaven and earth, who could partake in this?"
Zhou Maoshu said: "The essence of the sage is revealed in the drawing of the trigrams; the sage’s profundity is expressed through the trigrams. Without the trigrams, the sage’s essence could not be seen; without the subtle trigrams, the sage’s profundity could scarcely be fully heard. How could the *Yi* be merely the source of the Five Classics? It is the mystery of heaven, earth, ghosts, and spirits."
Qiu Zhongshen said: "The three lines of *qian* are the ancestors of all writing for ten thousand generations. The four words *yuan*, *heng*, *li*, and *zhen* are the origin of all principles of meaning for ten thousand generations. In heaven, they are spring, summer, autumn, and winter; in man, they are benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom; flowing between heaven and earth, they are the *yuan*, *hui*, *yun*, and *shi* (great eras)."
Cai Zimu said: "The first line, hidden, corresponds to Yanzi—do not act, and virtue is perfected. The second line, emerging, corresponds to Confucius—abiding in season, radiant in culture. The third line, cautious, corresponds to Gao, Kui, Hou Ji, and Xie—overcoming hardship without peril. The fourth line, leaping, corresponds to Tang and Wu—abiding in obscurity yet ultimately transforming. The fifth line, soaring, corresponds to Yao and Shun—sagely virtue shining far and wide. The top line, overreaching, corresponds to Yi and Zhou—abiding in excess yet not losing correctness. These six lines, when distinguished, each accord with their time; when unified, they return to the great illumination of the sage."
Su Shi commented: "Those who soar today were once hidden—who among them is not a great person? Again he said: "To abide in this, how could it be the way of endless regret?" Thus, what is spoken of is not necessarily so. Again he said: "When yin and yang harmonize, things are born." He said: "Without prosperity, the auspicious rite is partial and stagnant, not in accord with righteousness; without benefit, it is harsh and discordant, not harmonious."
Luo Yizheng said: "The third line, situated between the two *qian*, bears the image of ceaseless activity; at the end of the lower *qian*, it bears the image of day ending and evening coming. The third line, at the top of the lower trigram, is a perilous position. Yet because it is ceaselessly active by day and vigilant by night, it incurs no blame."
Shi Shoudao said: "Heaven is supremely high—how could it descend to interact with earth and thereby move the four seasons and give birth to the hundred things? The ruler is supremely honored—how could he not descend to connect with the people and thereby embrace the ten thousand states and govern the hundred officials? Thus, one must not reach excess."
Wang Ji said: "*Qian* is the virtue of heaven. The numinous earth (#7) congeals to form the heart. The origin of desire is the original substance of the heart. This is what is called *qian*—the movement of heaven’s virtue circles the heavens day and night, unceasing through all ages. The alternation of the sun and moon, the intermingling of the four seasons, do not harm or conflict because of their strength. The movement of sagely virtue penetrates day and night, unceasing throughout life, in harmony with heaven. Those below the worthy cannot embody the origin of desire; their desires do not necessarily sink into excess, but wherever intention turns, that is desire. Reduce it again and again, until there is none. Man is determined by heaven. The strength of the noble is modeled on heaven."
I have examined the celestial patterns: the movement of heaven has constant measure yet no stopping mechanism—does heaven not have a body? It takes the stars that cling to it as its body, like a support. For the North and South Poles are aligned, the North high and the South low; when observed through a tube, the pole is centered. One star rotates most closely, never leaving the tube—this is the pivot star, the hub of heaven’s axis. The body of heaven does not move; it is not that it does not move, but that its rotation does not depart from the pole, just as the opening and closing of a hinge does not depart from the socket—it has never moved. Thus it is said: "The mandate of heaven is profoundly unceasing." That by which heaven is heaven is that, without distinction of far or near, high or deep, it receives the mandate like an echo. This is the pivot of creation, the secret treasury transmitted by a thousand sages.
The Confucians avoid speaking of emptiness and stillness. Yet when the Master discussed the principle of stimulus and response in *Xian* (Hexagram 31), he spoke of it in detail—why? Because all stimuli in the world arise from stillness, and their responses all originate in emptiness. Emptiness adapts to change; stillness spiritually responds—without thought, without consideration. Thus all movements under heaven are unified: Yao’s centrality, Shun’s subtlety, Wen’s solemnity, Kong’s silence, Yan’s foolishness, Zhou’s stillness—all are this thing.
Scholars of the world, clinging to canonical essentials, think and become benighted. The so-called body of emptiness and stillness is why the learning of the sages is not understood, and creation nearly comes to a halt.

**Hexagram 2: Kun (☷☷)**
*Kun*: *Yuan heng*, beneficial for the constancy of the mare.

When the noble person sets forth, if they go first, they will lose their way; if they follow, they find their lord—this is beneficial.
To the southwest, one gains companions; to the northeast, one loses companions.
In tranquil constancy, there is good fortune.

*The Xiang* says:
Supreme indeed is the primal power of *Kun*!
All things draw sustenance from it, for it follows and bears up Heaven.
*Kun*’s thickness carries all things; its virtue joins with boundless space.
Embracing and magnifying, vast and great, it makes all things flourish.
The mare is of the earth’s kind: traveling the earth’s boundless expanse, yielding and compliant, it finds constancy in advantage.
When the noble person sets forth, if they go first, they lose the Way; if they follow, they attain constancy.
To the southwest, one gains companions and travels with their kind;
to the northeast, one loses companions, yet in the end there is blessing.
The good fortune of tranquil constancy responds to the earth’s boundless expanse.

*The Xiang* says:
The earth’s power is *Kun*—the noble person draws upon its thickness to carry all things.

**First Six:**
Treading on frost, solid ice will come.

*The Xiang* says:
Treading on frost, solid ice—yin begins to congeal.
Following its course, it reaches solid ice.

**Six in the Second:**
Straight, square, great—without practice, there is no harm.

*The Xiang* says:
The movement of Six in the Second is straight and square.
Without practice, there is no harm—such is the radiance of the earth’s Way.

**Six in the Third:**
Holding pattern within, constancy is possible.
If one follows the king’s affairs, without accomplishment there is still an end.

*The Xiang* says:
Holding pattern within, constancy is possible—it is timely expression.
Following the king’s affairs—knowledge is vast and great.

**Six in the Fourth:**
Tying up the sack—no blame, no praise.

*The Xiang* says:
Tying up the sack, no blame—caution averts harm.

**Six in the Fifth:**
Yellow skirts—supremely auspicious.

*The Xiang* says:
Yellow skirts, supremely auspicious—culture resides within.

**Top Six:**
Dragons battle in the wild; their blood is dark and yellow.

*The Xiang* says:
Dragons battle in the wild—such is the harm of their Way.

**Using Six:**
Beneficial for eternal constancy.

*The Xiang* says:
Using Six for eternal constancy—it ends in greatness.

*The Wenyan* says:
*Kun* is utterly yielding, yet in movement it is firm;
utterly still, yet its virtue is square.
Following, it finds its lord and attains constancy, embracing all things and transforming them with radiance.
Is not the Way of *Kun* compliant? It bears up Heaven and moves in due season.
A family that accumulates good will surely have abundant blessing;
a family that accumulates evil will surely have abundant misfortune.
When a minister murders his lord, or a son murders his father, it is not the fault of a single morning or evening—its origins lie in gradual accumulation, in failing to discern it early.
*The Yi* says: “Treading on frost, solid ice will come”—this speaks of compliance.

Straightness is its correctness, squareness is its righteousness. The noble person rectifies the inner with reverence and squares the outer with righteousness. When reverence and righteousness are established, virtue is not solitary. Straightness, squareness, and greatness—without practice, how could they not be beneficial? Thus, one does not doubt one’s conduct.

Though yin possesses beauty, it contains it to follow the king’s affairs, not daring to complete them. This is the Way of Earth, the Way of the Wife, the Way of the Minister. The Way of Earth does not complete but instead ensures continuity.

Heaven and Earth transform, and plants flourish; Heaven and Earth close, and the worthy withdraw. *The Yi* says: “Tying up the sack—no blame, no praise.” This speaks of caution.

The noble person, centered in yellow and penetrating principle, occupies the correct position and embodies form. Beauty resides within and flows through the four limbs, manifesting in undertakings—this is the perfection of beauty. When yin doubts yang, there must be conflict. For it suspects excess in yang, hence it is called “dragon.” Yet it has not departed from its kind, hence it is called “blood crow.” That which is dark and yellow is the blending of Heaven and Earth—Heaven is dark, Earth is yellow.

*Kun* is Earth, *Qian* and *Kun* establish their natures—one firm, one compliant. Firmness cannot become compliance, just as compliance cannot become firmness. To demand compliance of the firm or firmness of the compliant is to lose their essential natures. The two hexagrams *Qian* and *Kun* would then be contrary to the proper order, not the true principle of Heaven’s supremacy and Earth’s subordination.

*Qian* and *Kun* establish their positions—one husband, one wife. The husband cannot become the wife, just as the wife cannot become the husband. To demand the wife’s role of the husband or the husband’s role of the wife is to invert their proper places. The two hexagrams would then be out of position, not the true Way of the lord’s supremacy and the minister’s subordination.

Thus, above is Heaven, below is Earth—this is the *Qian* and *Kun* of the world. One husband, one wife—this is the *Qian* and *Kun* of every household. Their positions are fixed, so they cannot be reversed as a constant. Their natures are fixed, so they cannot be exchanged in function. When use matches ability, ability suits its use. This is why the sage first establishes *Qian* and *Kun*.

Otherwise, if a divine dragon were a mare, it would be of no use as a dragon; its soaring and bearing heavy loads over great distances would be mocked. If a mare were a divine dragon, it would be of no use as a horse; its carrying burdens and summoning clouds to bring rain would be beyond its ken. *Qian* and *Kun* alike—if not employed according to their strengths, both become useless. How much more so for humans?

Thus, the sage distinguishes *Qian* and *Kun* and expounds on them in detail. Yet世俗的儒者, drawing snakes and adding feet, claim that firmness without compliance leads to impetuousness and failure, while compliance without firmness leads to weakness and collapse. Alas! If this were true, how could they still be called *Qian* and *Kun*?

If one were impetuous and incapable of accomplishment yet still called *Qian*, or weak and ineffectual yet still called *Kun*, then Heaven would not uphold Heaven, Earth would not fulfill Earth, and we humans would have nothing to rely upon. They do not realize that this is precisely what the most firm and most compliant naturally possess—what need is there for supplementation?

Thus, utmost softness moves with firmness, utmost stillness embodies square virtue—this is how Earth achieves compliance. The Way of *Qian* transforms, each fulfilling its nature and destiny—this is how Heaven achieves firmness. If firmness were supplemented with compliance, it would not be true firmness; it would be of no use as firmness. If compliance were supplemented with firmness, it would not be true compliance; it would be of no use as compliance. Alas! How could this grasp the true nature of *Qian* and *Kun*?

*Qian* and *Kun* are one, so *Qian* says “originating and prospering,” and *Kun* also says “originating and prospering.” But *Qian* says “beneficial to constancy,” while *Kun* says “beneficial to the constancy of the mare.” And not just any mare—it specifies *the* mare, contrasting with the dragon’s transformations and soaring. *Kun* alone finds benefit in this because it is *Kun*’s constancy.

Thus, when the noble person goes forth and models *Kun*, they rest in the constancy of the mare. Hence it is said: “The woman’s constancy is auspicious.” When engaged in affairs, only the constancy of the mare is beneficial. Hence it is said: “Beneficial for eternal constancy, ending in greatness.” “Great” refers to *Qian*—ending in greatness means not daring to lead, not daring to take precedence, not daring to form cliques—this is constancy. “First lost” and below refers to the benefit of the mare’s constancy. The first is *Qian*, the lord, while we follow—thus finding the lord and benefiting the lord. Whether west or south, we guard the northeast, for we follow the lord alone. With single-minded devotion and no duality, dwelling in peace and heeding the lord, we attain constancy without confusion—this is *Kun*’s beneficial constancy, and thus it is “originating and prospering.”

Human life is straight—one might think straightness excludes squareness. Yet we speak of both straightness and squareness because people know straightness but not that straightness inherently possesses squareness. Is not the first thought of a person their straight, unobstructed true nature? Yet as soon as it arises, it is perceived—what was first is straight, and perception is also straight. Perception halts, halting is square. When it emerges, though straight and square are united, their essence shines as one.

Earth’s nature is vast and yielding, *Kun*’s virtue is boundless—how could it be solitary? Thus, the lines speak of both straightness and squareness. Straightness is correctness, squareness is righteousness. Correctness is for halting, righteousness is for acting. Halting is reverence, but reverence is not deliberate—it is simply the inner straightness. Squareness is righteousness, but righteousness is not forced—it is simply acting for what is beneficial. How could this depend on learning and practice to be beneficial?

Indeed, it is not through practice that one fails to attain primal benefit—what doubt could there be?

**Appendix: Su Shi’s Explanation**
The dragon is that which transforms and acts of its own accord. The horse is that which is tamed and put to human use. To be used by others and yet be a mare is the utmost in equine compliance. Yet such perfect compliance, if not steadfast, falls into deviance—thus the benefit lies in the mare’s steadfastness.

**Li Tuhe’s Remarks**
Utmost compliance is never unsteadfast. Utmost compliance is compliance with *Qian*. Having complied with *Qian*, how could one not be steadfast? If one joins with allies, associates with one’s kind, or travels southwest, then by yielding to yin one clings again to yin—how can one wholeheartedly heed *Qian*’s command and attain the utmost greatness?

**Further Explanation**
The Way of *Kun* may be employed by others but cannot act on its own; it may harmonize but cannot initiate. Thus, the noble person benefits from having somewhere to go. To precede is to go astray and lose the Way; to follow is to comply and fulfill one’s aspirations—this is why it is beneficial. West and south are *Dui*, *Li*, and *Xun*—all our allies. East and north are *Zhen*, *Kan*, and *Gen*—none of them our allies. Two yin forces cannot employ each other, so one must depart from one’s kind. To sever ties with allies and come forth is to be bound to restraint.

**Li Tuweng’s Remarks**
*Qian* is *Kun*’s master. *Qian* being the master, one need not seek it—seeking it only leads to loss, giving rise to thoughts of gaining allies. Then one cannot wholeheartedly heed *Qian*’s command.

**Further Explanation**
With six in the second place, one may be called yielding. But how does one attain straightness, squareness, and greatness? It is said: the second six is the utmost in compliance. The noble person’s compliance—what else could it be? It is simply following principle without self-interest.

**Tuweng’s Remarks**
Utmost softness moves with hardness; utmost stillness embodies squareness. The Master has already explained this perfectly—there is no need to rephrase it as "following principle without self-interest." It is precisely because of its utmost softness that it attains utmost hardness; precisely because of its utmost stillness that it attains utmost squareness. Softness is possible, but utmost softness is not. Alas, how perfect!

**Further Explanation**
Dwelling at the junction of above and below, neither position is secure. *Qian* is secure above, for it sees danger in not yet having reached the top—thus the third nine harbors the anxiety of evening vigilance. *Kun* is secure below, for it finds difficulty in first reaching above—thus the fourth six exercises the caution of tying one’s sack. For yin to advance to the third place may still be steadfast, but to reach the fourth is perilous. Thus, one binds and seals oneself to seek freedom from blame and primal praise. Blame and praise are what people cannot escape: emerging from blame, one enters praise; shedding praise, one is ensnared by blame. Blame leads to guilt, and praise leads to suspicion—how severe is the difficulty of attaining primal praise!

**Further Explanation**
When it transforms, even grasses and trees flourish. When it closes, even the worthy withdraw.

**Tuweng’s Remarks**
Reading this, one may weep in sorrow.

**Further Explanation**
Yellow is the color of the center, present only when principle is fully realized—it is the adornment beneath the lower garment. If it were yellow but not the lower garment, it would belong to the upper body. If it were the lower garment but not yellow, it would not be the lower body in its proper position. Now, occupying the correct position in the doubled *Kun* and dwelling at the center of the lower body, it is called the lower garment to clarify its lower position and yellow to clarify its centrality. For pattern in the *Yi* arises from intermingling. If yin and yang were each absolute, how could there be pattern?

**Tuweng’s Remarks**
Absolute unity itself gives rise to pattern. *Kun* does not value pattern—what it values is pattern within, the color of the central yellow. If pattern is within, why must it depend on intermingling to exist?

**Cheng Zhengshu’s Remarks**
The southwest is the direction of yin; the northeast, the direction of yang. Yin must follow yang, abandoning its own kind.

**Zhu Zhonghui’s Remarks**
In this hexagram, all three lines are even, hence its name *Kun* and its image of earth. Doubling it yields *Kun* again, signifying the utmost in pure yin compliance. It is further said: the fifth six, with yin occupying the honored position, embodies the virtue of central compliance, manifesting both internally and externally—thus its image is such, and its oracle is supremely auspicious.

The *Spring and Autumn Annals* records that when Nan Kuai planned rebellion, he divined and obtained this line, taking it as greatly auspicious. Zifu Huibo said: "If it is a matter of loyalty and trust, it may be so; otherwise, it will surely fail. Outward strength and inner warmth are loyalty. Harmony in leading with steadfastness is trust. Thus it is said: *Yellow lower garment, primal auspiciousness*—yellow is the color of the center, the adornment beneath the garment, the chief of all virtues. Without loyalty, one cannot attain its color; without reverence, one cannot attain its adornment; without goodness, one cannot reach its extremity. Moreover, the *Yi* cannot be used to divine peril. If any of these three are lacking, even if the divination is correct, it is not yet so." Later, Kuai indeed failed.

**Jin Rubai’s Remarks**
"First lost" is one phrase; "later attains the master and benefits" is another. The master of yin and yang is yang first, yin later—this is the constant principle. If yin precedes, it reverses the constant and goes astray; if it follows, it attains its master and benefits. Benefit is like compliance. Thus it is said: "Following, it attains the constant." It is also said: "Later attaining the master, it has constancy." Some scholars take "attains the master and benefits" as a single phrase, thus reading "later attains the master and has constancy," omitting the word "benefits." But then how could "following, it attains the constant" also omit it? Yin ought to follow yang—can it cling to its own kind? To lose allies is like *Tai*’s "allies lost"—abandoning one’s allies to follow yang, thus auspicious.

**Zheng Zhifu’s Remarks**
The image of *Qian* reveals King Wen’s virtue in founding Zhou; the image of *Kun* reveals King Wen’s heart in serving Yin.

**Zhou Yan’s Remarks**
The first nine of *Qian* says: "Do not act," guarding its subtlety. The first six of *Kun* immediately says: "Firm ice arrives," warning of its excess.

Liu Junbo remarked: The third six, with yin enveloping yang, takes the image of "containing brilliance." The fourth six, occupying the fourth position as a yin line, takes the image of "tying the sack." The sack’s emptiness at its center mirrors the heavy yin’s knotted closure. He further said: Treacherous and ambitious ministers brazenly seize power. Though the ruler may be weak and benighted, they do not yield. Stiffened corpses and flowing blood—such is the inevitable outcome. "Treading on frost, firm ice arrives"—this is precisely the warning. Hu Zhonghu said: "Its blood is dark and yellow," both sides suffer injury. Though yin may reach its extreme, how could it harm yang alone? You Dingfu said: *Qian* is pure yang; when yang reaches its extreme, it becomes overbearing. *Kun* is pure yin; when yin reaches its extreme, it wages war. If they do not transform, overbearing brings disaster, war brings injury—neither can be avoided. Yang Jingzhong said: When yin ascends to the sixth, yang is nearly extinguished. Yet though yang is faint, its name and position remain—it cannot be obliterated. At such a time, people see only the sixth; who still recognizes yang? The sage deplores its usurpation of yang, thus specially invokes the dragon to show that the dragon still dwells in the human heart, never to be forgotten or slighted. Blood is a yin substance; calling it "blood" clarifies that the sixth has not yet departed from the category of ministers. Both yin and yang are injured—not yang alone. Thus the sage uses this to temper the hearts of overbearing rulers.

**On "Using Six" and "Using Nine"**
The *Yi* combines the Three Powers and doubles them, forming hexagrams with six lines—is this not "using six"? He further said: Six is nothing other than the Dao of the Three Powers. With six and three, is this not "using nine"?—*Note appended by Wang Benhe of Xin’an.*

**Hexagram 3: Tun (屯) – Difficulty at the Beginning**
*Below Zhen (震), above Kan (坎).*
**Tun:** Primordial, pervasive, beneficial, and steadfast. Do not act; there is somewhere to go—it is beneficial to establish lords.
**The Image says:** *Tun*—hard and soft first meet and difficulty arises, movement within peril. Greatly pervasive and steadfast. The movement of thunder and rain fills all; heaven creates in the unformed. It is fitting to establish lords and not rest.
**The Image says:** Clouds and thunder—*Tun*. The noble person thereby orders and weaves.

**First Nine:** Hesitating; beneficial to dwell in steadfastness, beneficial to establish lords.
**The Image says:** Though hesitating, its aspiration is rightly conducted. Placing the noble below the lowly—greatly gaining the people.

**Second Six:** Entangled and beset, riding horses in disarray. Not bandits but marriage—if the woman is steadfast, she does not conceive; after ten years, she conceives.
**The Image says:** The second six’s difficulty comes from mounting the firm. After ten years, she conceives—returning to the norm.

**Third Six:** Pursuing deer without a guide, entering the forest’s depths. The noble person nearly does not desist—going brings regret.
**The Image says:** Pursuing deer without a guide is to follow game. The noble person desists—going brings regret and exhaustion.

**Fourth Six:** Riding horses in disarray, seeking marriage. Going is auspicious; nothing is unfavorable.
**The Image says:** Seeking and going—clarity.

**Ninth Five:** Difficulty storing its richness. Small steadfastness is auspicious; great steadfastness is inauspicious.
**The Image says:** Difficulty storing its richness—its bestowal is not yet radiant.

**Top Six:** Riding horses in disarray, weeping blood in streams.
**The Image says:** Weeping blood in streams—how can this last?

**Hexagram 3: Water over Thunder – Tun**
Fang Shihua said: Following *Kun*, this hexagram is *Tun*. What is *Tun*? *Zhen* brings one yang to dwell below *Kun*—thus hard and soft first meet. Yet *Kan* and *Li* arise before it—thus hard and soft first meet and difficulty arises. What else could this be but *Tun*? *Tun* is the re-creation of *Qian* and *Kun*, the true lord’s resurgence in the unformed time. Now, one yang stirs within *Kan*’s peril—how could it not be primordial, pervasive, beneficial, and steadfast? Thus, there is no need for further action; people naturally come to submit, and it is beneficial to establish lords. Observe the movement of thunder and rain filling all—heaven creates in the unformed, precisely the time to establish lords without rest. That *Zhen*’s one yang comes to dwell below this lower trigram is the power to place the noble below the lowly and greatly gain the people’s hearts. Such a person is precisely one for whom it is beneficial to establish lords and not rest. When heaven has this time, people must have this affair—thus the first nine acts in accord with the moment. Though one hears its sound, its form is unseen; dwelling in steadfastness as usual, guarding correctness as before, hesitating yet unwavering, not shifting a step—people naturally come to submit. Thus it is said: Though hesitating, its aspiration is rightly conducted. Its right conduct suffices to shake the age; it cannot abandon the first and go elsewhere. What further need is there for action? Thus the *Image* simply says: Do not act; there is somewhere to go. And the line reiterates: Hesitating, yet beneficial to dwell in steadfastness. The second six, soft and correct, mounts the first’s firm correctness—hence entangled, beset, riding horses in disarray, so difficult that it cannot easily be betrothed to the younger brother. Yet the first is the age’s destination; I, too, am adjacent to the first, sought day and night for marriage. Though not bandits, after ten years she conceives—reversing the norm. Matching sages with sages, exalting worthies with worthies—this too must have its Dao. The third six, in an age of deer-chasing, hopes day and night to obtain it. Yet above, there is no correspondence—no guide. Without a guide, why enter the forest? Thus desisting is the wise course. The fourth six is clear. In the time of *Tun*, how could there be another like the first to seek? Riding horses in disarray, carefully seeking this proper match in marriage—going is auspicious; what is not favorable? Thus it is said: Seeking and going—clarity. The first nine is the true lord; thus even if there is steadfast auspiciousness like the ninth five, its richness is stored with difficulty, and its bestowal is not yet radiant.

The minor may be auspicious, but the great is inauspicious—though steadfast, what does it avail? This is like the King of Yue in Song, who ultimately submitted his fate to the Founding Ancestor. The sixth yang is precisely the one who chases the deer of the third line yet remains unaware. The abandoner is one who, having reached the extremity of the third’s path, finds himself at an impasse. Thus it is said: riding horses in disarray, weeping blood in streams.

The Master lamented: How can this endure? It is the fault of failing to descend. Wang Benhe said: Though the first remains hesitant and does not proceed, its will is truly upright—others cannot abandon it; what need is there to act? Its union with the second is what is called the first meeting of firm and yielding, a match made by Heaven—what need is there to await betrothal?

**Appendix**
Wang Fusi said: Thunder and rain in fullness—all arise from the first meeting of firm and yielding. *Tun* is the time of Heaven and Earth’s inception, the beginning of creation, which arises from obscurity; thus it is called the "dawn of chaos." To quell disorder with stillness, to uphold stillness while awaiting—pacifying the people lies in rectitude, and magnifying rectitude lies in humility. In an age of *Tun*-difficulties, yin seeks yang, the weak seeks the strong—it is a time when the people yearn for their lord. The first line stands at the head, and the one below it is singular; the hexagram’s lines embody this principle, and thus it rightfully gains the people’s allegiance.

Yu Lingsheng said: The people are restless—without the noble one, there is no peace. After the realm has endured the peril of *Tun*-difficulties, the later king should govern with the policy of thunder and rain. Jin Rubai said: Eagerness to establish lords is itself a lack of peace—not that establishing lords brings further unrest. Luo Yizheng said: The unshakable boulder, the sturdy plank—both are things difficult to move, hence they serve as metaphors for the appearance of difficult progress. Hu Zhonghu said: The hexagram’s judgment sometimes refers specifically to the hexagram’s ruling line; here, the ruling line is *Zhen* (Thunder), and *Zhen* governs the first line. "Hesitating" corresponds to "do not yet have somewhere to go," "favorable to abide in steadfastness" corresponds to "favorable to be steadfast"—the hexagram speaks of the advantage of establishing lords in terms of affairs, while the lines speak of the advantage of establishing lords in terms of persons. Cai Jiefu said: A girl, once betrothed, is given a courtesy name upon ascending; a man is given a courtesy name upon his capping.

Yang Tingxiu said: The third line, possessing a firm and luminous talent yet dwelling in *Zhen* (Thunder), reaches the extreme of movement—recklessly aspiring to achieve the merit of resolving *Zhun* (Difficulty). This is what is meant by "pursuing the deer." Acting recklessly and responding to the primal above is what is meant by "primal miscalculation." Master Su [Dongpo] said: In *Zhun*, the primal and the upright are the masters, yet only those who humble themselves win the people. The third line, though endowed with primal virtue, harbors a desire to seek the people—this is like miscalculating and pursuing the deer. The deer cannot be tamed by virtue, and one only labors in vain by entering the forest. He further said: The third line has no proper correspondence, and the fifth is unworthy of allegiance. Not recognizing the fifth’s unworthiness, deluded by proximity, why not sooner attach oneself to the first nine? Thus, in exhaustion, one comes to weeping blood. Yu Zhongxiang said: The rains of *Kan* (Water) are called "nourishing." As the *Book of Songs* says: "The yin rains nourish it"—this is its meaning. Wang Ji said: Heaven and Earth generate the myriad things. *Zhun* is the beginning of things’ generation, hence it follows *Qian* (Heaven) and *Kun* (Earth). When qi first intermingles yet remains unharmonious, it is called *Zhun*; when things first sprout yet remain curled, it is called *Zhun*; when the world is beset by many difficulties and not yet at peace, it is called *Zhun*. The noble one, observing the image of *Zhun*, weaves the affairs of the world to resolve its difficulties. This is the learning of the noble one in the primordial era, at the dawn of Heaven and Earth’s unfolding, who brings things to fruition and establishes order. Clouds above thunder: yin and yang first intermingle yet have not formed rain, hence it is *Zhun*. Clouds below thunder have already formed rain, hence it is *Jie* (Release). "Weaving" refers to the work of ordering silk. "Warp" (jing) is gathering the threads and separating them, like thunder’s self-gathering and dispersal. "Woof" (lun) is ordering the threads and uniting them, like clouds’ self-dispersal and coalescence. This is what is meant by "weaving the great warp of the world."

Yang Jingzhong’s general principle of correspondence among the six lines:
The first and fourth correspond, the second and fifth correspond, the third and the top correspond. Why is this so? Because the hexagrams are doubled. Originally, there were only eight trigrams, each with three lines. When doubled, the fourth line of the upper trigram becomes the first of the lower trigram, the fifth line of the upper trigram becomes the second of the lower trigram, and the top line of the upper trigram becomes the top of the lower trigram. Only those of the same kind form the image of correspondence. Yet one yin and one yang correspond, while two yins do not correspond, nor do two yangs. Here, the third six and the top six are both yin, hence they lack the image of correspondence—and thus bear the image of "pursuing the deer without miscalculation."

*Kan* below, *Gen* above: *Meng* (Youthful Ignorance): Smooth progress. It is not I who seek the youthful ignorant—it is the youthful ignorant who seek me. The first divination is answered; repeated inquiries are an imposition, and impositions go unanswered. Favorable to be steadfast.

*The Judgment* says: *Meng*—beneath the mountain lies peril, peril that halts: this is *Meng*. *Meng* leads to smooth progress, for one acts with timely moderation. It is not I who seek the youthful ignorant—it is the youthful ignorant who seek me: this is the resonance of aspirations. The first divination is answered, for there is firmness in the center. Repeated impositions are an imposition, and impositions go unanswered: this is the defilement of ignorance. Nurturing correctness in ignorance is the work of the sage.

*The Image* says: Beneath the mountain, a spring emerges: this is *Meng*. The noble one, with resolute conduct, cultivates virtue.

First six: Awakening ignorance. Favorable to employ punishments, to remove fetters and stocks. Proceeding brings regret.
*The Image* says: Favorable to employ punishments, for this rectifies the law.

Nine two: Embracing ignorance brings good fortune. Taking a wife brings good fortune. The son is capable of managing the household.
*The Image* says: The son is capable of managing the household, for firmness and softness meet.

Six three: Do not take this woman; she sees a man of metal and loses herself, with no advantage anywhere.
*The Image* says: Do not take this woman, for her conduct is not compliant.

Six four: Beset by ignorance, regret.
*The Image* says: The regret of being beset by ignorance arises from isolation and distance from substance.

Six five: Youthful ignorance, good fortune.
*The Image* says: The good fortune of youthful ignorance lies in compliance and deference.

Top nine: Striking ignorance. Unfavorable to act as a bandit; favorable to resist bandits.
*The Image* says: Favorable to resist bandits, for above and below are compliant.

Mountain and Water: *Meng*—beneath the mountain lies peril, peril that halts.

When it first halts, all is murky and undifferentiated, with no knowing where it goes. Thus is youthful ignorance; thus too is the sage. Though one might wish not to halt, how could it be otherwise? Hence it is said: Beneath the mountain lies peril, peril that halts—*Meng*. Yet if peril brings halting, then passage must come with smooth flow. And when it flows, the steadfast person is thus, and youthful ignorance is thus. Though one might wish not to proceed, how could it be otherwise? Hence it is said: *Meng*—smooth passage. To proceed with smoothness is to act in timely accord. To halt when the time is to halt, to proceed when the time is to proceed—this is precisely what it means to act in timely accord. The sage and the ignorant are not fundamentally different. Yet ignorance is the true nature of humanity; to nurture ignorance is thus to nurture correctness and to engage in the substantive work of sage-making. Hence it is said: *Meng* nurtures correctness—this is the work of the sage, and thus it is favorable and upright.

It is not I who seek the youthfully ignorant, but the youthfully ignorant who seek me—this speaks of my resonance with the fifth line’s intent, so the fifth naturally seeks me, not that I seek to instruct. Youthful ignorance is fundamentally without excess; I merely comply and encompass it, and that is sufficient. What does it seek, and what do I have to tell it?

The first divination is answered because the first six, just stepping out the door, casts the stalks and obtains the second line, drawing near to it to seek instruction. Hence it is said: "by means of firmness and centrality." Yet if it comes a second time, or a third, one ultimately does not answer—this is not refusal to answer, but fear of spoiling the ignorant. Hence it is said: To spoil is not to answer, for it would drown the ignorant. Thus the first line’s "favorable to punish," the third’s "do not take this woman," and the fourth’s "beset by regret" all reflect this intent not to answer.

Alas! Examine the *Xiang* commentary closely, and one will know the work of nurturing ignorance. If one repeatedly spoils it, this is striking ignorance, not nurturing it—this is acting as a bandit, not halting bandits. Only the top nine, who should halt at *Gen*’s stillness yet do not know to halt, would do this. How could the nine of the second line, who encompasses ignorance, act thus? If one aids it in halting bandits, then all below comply; if one acts as a bandit, then one above is already noncompliant—how could one make above and below all comply and achieve the utmost in great compliance?

Alas! Without compliance in ignorance, how can one become a sage? Without compliant nurturing, how can one nurture correctness? Thus the sage uses this to comply with all under heaven, and through earnest reverence, all under heaven is pacified—without need for reward, the people are encouraged.

Now observe its striking: the first line’s words say, "Do not divine to seek my instruction," for I wish to dispel your ignorance—this is self-imposed constraint, urgently requiring correction. The method uses words to remove the constraint; only then can the constraint be undone. Otherwise, I fear that proceeding will bring regret, and one will cling to it all the more, making it impossible to remove. Have you not seen the fourth six? Originally an honest person, yet it deems itself unworthy and distances itself; originally ignorant, yet it deems itself unworthy and calls it heavenly constraint. If it already takes ignorance as constraint, how can its regret be undone?

The fifth six, yielding and compliant, dwelling centrally, is but a youthfully ignorant one. If taken as a wife, she is the wife of youthful ignorance; if taken as a son, he is the son who manages the household in youthful ignorance, where firmness and softness meet. There is nowhere it does not bring good fortune—only when the second nine’s encompassing of ignorance brings good fortune does the fifth six’s youthful ignorance achieve good fortune, and afterward the fifth six, with its own youthful ignorance, compliantly and deferentially enters the second nine’s encompassing of ignorance. Thus the second’s fundamental "not advantageous" is fitting, like the third six—she is not a woman one can take, but an untakable woman. This woman’s knowledge is greatly opened, and her conduct is no longer compliant with ignorance. What advantage is there for youthful ignorance?

Generally, the ignorant of the world, with their growing intelligence and broadening vision, often see gold yet fail to see themselves. Thus those who hear one and know ten are deemed greatly worthy, while those who engage in trade for a day are deemed empty fools, and those who are repeatedly empty are deemed mere beggars. They take themselves to be beset by hardship and unable to emerge. If one spoils them once, they rejoice; if one strikes them, they rejoice; if one imposes constraints, they rejoice again—where can the work of nurturing correctness be applied?

Wang Benhe says: After *Qian* and *Kun* come *Zhun* and *Meng*. *Zhun* is the initial unfolding of the times, when the world is ignorant, and the sage’s way of revolving *Qian* and turning *Kun* begins here. In *Meng*, knowledge has not yet spread, and the human heart is beset by difficulty—the sage’s work of opening heaven and cleaving earth begins here. *Zhun* is the world’s ignorance; *Meng* is humanity’s *Zhun*. Thus *Zhun* establishes lords, while *Meng* is favorable for resisting bandits.

**Appendix**
Wang Fusi says: Nothing is clearer than the sage; nothing is more obscure than ignorance. To nurture correctness through ignorance is the work of the sage. Yet to nurture correctness through clarity is to lose the Way.

Program Zhengshu says: *Gen* is the mountain, signifying stillness. *Kan* is water, signifying peril. With peril beneath the mountain, one halts after surmounting danger, yet knows not where to go—this is the image of *Meng*.

You Dingfu says: A spring issues from beneath the mountain, its flow yet undispersed, its force not yet fully manifest. Observing its force’s unmanifested reach, one acts with resolve; observing its undispersed unity, one nurtures virtue.

Wang Ji says: A spring issuing from beneath the mountain is by nature still and clear—the source of water’s purity. Unconfined by east or west, unmuddied by silt and sand, guided by its singular flow, it naturally reaches the sea. The noble person models himself on *Meng*. The image shows resolute action nurturing one’s virtue. Water flows without ceasing, hence “resolute”; the mountain stands firm without yielding, hence “nurturing.” That undivided, unaroused unity is the essence of *Meng*. *Meng* is the foundation of sageliness. Yet from the meaning of *Meng*, worldly scholars fail to understand, mistaking it for obscurity. They recklessly strive to carve and dredge, fostering cunning and wit, muddying it with the desires of silt and sand, diverting it with the currents of east and west, thereby dissipating the integrity of undivided purity and obstructing the source of clarity and stillness. This not only brings no benefit but inflicts harm. The essence of sagely work lies entirely in *Meng*. In terms of heaven’s seasons, darkness is the mechanism of dawning clarity. In human affairs, obscurity is the prelude to illumination. Yet the world fails to perceive this. Mencius said: “The great person does not lose the heart of the infant.” The heart of the infant is precisely what is meant by *Meng*.

*Kan* below, *Qian* above: *Xu*. With sincerity, radiant success; uprightness brings good fortune. Favorable for fording the great river.

**The Image** says: *Xu* means “to await”; peril lies ahead. Firm and strong, one does not sink—thus one’s principle is not beset by exhaustion. *Xu*: with sincerity, radiant success, uprightness brings good fortune. Occupying heaven’s position, centered and correct. Favorable for fording the great river—advancing will bring achievement.

**The Image** says: Clouds ascend above heaven—*Xu*. The noble person takes this as a model for feasting and rejoicing.

**First Nine**: Awaiting at the outskirts. Favorable to persist; no blame.
**The Image** says: Awaiting at the outskirts—one does not rashly advance into difficulty. Favorable to persist; no blame—one has not lost constancy.

**Nine in the Second**: Awaiting on the sands. Minor words arise, but in the end, good fortune.
**The Image** says: Awaiting on the sands—one’s breadth lies in the center. Though minor words arise, the end is good.

**Nine in the Third**: Awaiting in the mire—this brings raiders to arrive.
**The Image** says: Awaiting in the mire—disaster lies without. One brings raiders upon oneself; reverent caution averts defeat.

**Six in the Fourth**: Awaiting in blood—emerging from the cave.
**The Image** says: Awaiting in blood—submissive and heeding.

**Nine in the Fifth**: Awaiting with wine and food—uprightness brings good fortune.
**The Image** says: Wine and food, uprightness brings good fortune—through central correctness.

**Top Six**: Entering the cave—three uninvited guests arrive. Treat them with reverence; in the end, good fortune.
**The Image** says: Uninvited guests arrive—treat them with reverence; in the end, good fortune. Though not in the proper position, no great loss occurs.

*Water over Heaven: Xu*

The talent of unyielding strength finds no difficulty in overcoming peril, yet struggles not to court it. At first, having stepped beyond the gate and awaited at the outskirts, one must not rush forward—I know they will not breach the gate of peril. Yet I fear they may lack endurance, lose their steadfast heart, and though they court peril, disregard it entirely. Thus, it is said: "Through constancy in action, no blame." **Nine in the Second** awaits near danger, resembling one who waits upon the sands. Though sand is not water, it is where water must inevitably flow; thus, to await upon the sands is akin to moving through the midst of water. With the Second’s resolute and luminous talent, centered in its position, though it hears minor grumblings on all sides, it attains ultimate good fortune and preserves it to the end.

**Nine in the Third**, encountering rigidity, finds no rest upon the sands and instead awaits only to sink into the mire. The mire ensnares one far more deeply than sand—how could this not be considered? Yet the Third knows only that water cannot bring disaster, unaware that peril lies without. Once mired, it cannot advance in joy nor retreat at will—raiders arrive. Who, if not oneself, has summoned them? This stems from insufficient foresight, careless awaiting, and the fault of excessive rigidity. Yet by virtue of its strength, if it humbly redoubles its reverent caution, it will surely not fail. Though not failing is good, how much better to have never reached the mire at all. Thus, when danger lies ahead and one cannot await, even the unyielding strength of Qian cannot escape entrapment or exhaustion. How much more so for the yielding **Six in the Fourth** and the **Top Six**?

Now, **Six in the Fourth** dwells beneath the abyss, its image one of awaiting while wounded—seemingly unable to emerge. Yet it escapes the pit’s depths by submitting and heeding. **Nine in the Fifth** would rather endure injury than rush forward—this is the power of central correctness. **Top Six**, positioned above the abyss, nearly exits its confines, yet instead of awaiting, it retreats into the pit’s depths—is this not extreme? Thus, it is told: though you have entered the cave, you are fortunate that three uninvited guests arrive. For **Nine in the Third**, corresponding to the Top, is the Top’s guest. Now, the Third, through reverent caution, has escaped peril and comes forth. If you treat them with reverence, you may yet secure ultimate good fortune. Though you have entered the cave, what harm is there? Yet while the Top is fortunate in the Third’s arrival, it is also heeding the **Nine in the Fifth**’s warning.

From this, I observe: the yielding fear they cannot emerge, while the firm fear they cannot await. In this hexagram of *Xu*, the Sage speaks especially to those of firm strength. "Through constancy in action" serves as a warning to all. "Minor grumblings" serve as a warning to the Second. "Summoning raiders" serves as a warning to the Third. Who is warned to await with sincerity, radiance, prosperity, uprightness, and good fortune? It is **Nine in the Fifth**, enthroned in the celestial position, who may ford great rivers and advance to achieve merit. For the Fifth, though dwelling in inquiry, forgets its peril and awaits only with wine and food—much like one in *Kun*. **Nine in the Second**, though situated in distress, forgets its distress and knows only to be content with wine and food. This is the supreme virtue and essential path of awaiting in *Xu* and enduring in *Kun*. Thus, it is said: "Uprightness brings good fortune," and again: "Through central correctness, it is supremely so."

Alas! To remain still and await, not even venturing beyond the outskirts with food and drink—how much less should one reach the mire? Liu Yongxiang says: The three guests are **Nine in the Third**. Though the Third does not occupy the Fifth’s position, it is firm and strong; treating it with reverence incurs no great loss. In this hexagram, only **Six in the Fourth** can submit and heed, while the others—**Nine in the Fifth** and the rest—bear the image of Qian’s unyielding vigor and overbearing force, unable to await or heed. Were it not for the Fifth’s celestial position, its central correctness, and its repeated, earnest admonitions, they would not await. This is why the Fifth is radiant and prosperous.

**Appendix**
Jin Rubai says: The Fourth and Top, both yin lines within *Kan*, thus both speak of the cave—just as in the *Kan* hexagram, the initial and third lines both form *Kan*.
Wang Ji says: Qian’s unyielding strength, when advancing and encountering peril, does not rush forward—thus *Xu* holds the path to prosperity. If one awaits with a yielding and weak nature, delaying without progress, then awaiting becomes a burden of affairs.
The *Shuowen* states: *Xu* (await) derives from *ye* (page, head) and *shan* (ornament, hair); originally referring to the beard, it is borrowed for the sense of "that which is needed."
Fang Shihua says: The Master used this to explain *Xu*, meaning to await **Nine in the Fifth**.

---
**Water over Heaven: Song**
*Song*: Sincerity obstructed, caution—central good fortune, ultimate misfortune. Favorable to see the great person; unfavorable to ford great rivers.
**The Judgment** says: *Song* arises from rigidity above and peril below—peril and strength contend. *Song* with sincerity obstructed, caution, and central good fortune: strength comes and attains the center. Ultimate misfortune: contention cannot be completed. Favorable to see the great person: esteeming central correctness. Unfavorable to ford great rivers: entering the abyss.
**The Image** says: Heaven and water move in opposition—*Song*. The noble person, in undertaking affairs, plans from the beginning.

**Initial Six**: Does not prolong the affair; minor grumblings, ultimate good fortune.
**The Image** says: Does not prolong the affair—contention cannot be long. Though minor grumblings arise, its clarity prevails.

**Nine in the Second**: Does not prevail in contention; retreats and flees—his village of three hundred households remains unharmed.
**The Image** says: Does not prevail in contention—retreating and fleeing. Contending from below against above brings calamity upon oneself.
