**Six in the Third**: Feeds on old virtue; uprightness is perilous, ultimate good fortune. If following royal affairs, no achievement.
**The Image** says: Feeds on old virtue—following above brings good fortune.

**Nine in the Fourth**: Does not prevail in contention; returns to the mandate, changes, and finds peace in uprightness—good fortune.

**The Image** says: Returns to the mandate, changes, and finds peace in uprightness—nothing is lost.

**Nine in the Fifth**: Contention brings supreme good fortune.
**The Image** says: Contention brings supreme good fortune—through central correctness.

**Top Nine**: Perhaps granted a leather belt, yet by day’s end it is thrice stripped away.
**The Image** says: Receiving robes through contention is hardly worthy of respect.

**Heaven and Water in Contention**
Fang Shihua says: This hexagram combines superior firmness with inferior peril, peril yet strength. Without peril, contention cannot arise; without strength, contention cannot be sustained. Only peril paired with strength brings contention to fruition. Yet can contention truly be achieved? Can it truly endure? Can it persist long enough to follow royal affairs, prolonging the affair indefinitely? Fortunately, the central and correct Nine in the Fifth, a noble person, seeks to eliminate contention entirely, offering those who contend what they truly desire to see. And Nine in the Second, firm yet yielding to the center, ensures that contention fails of itself—it cannot endure, cannot persist, cannot drag on, and ultimately sinks into the abyss. For yang is solid while yin is hollow; firmness yielding to the center embodies trust and clarity. Yet a single yang trapped within yin, peril, obstruction, and trepidation is all too evident. Obstruction gives rise to contention; trepidation ensures it does not endure—how much more so when Nine in the Fifth appears? Thus, at the first sight of Nine in the Fifth, shame and remorse leave no room for delay: contention fails, retreat and flight follow, as is fitting.

Is not the Second the master of peril? All within its inner trigram are those it ordinarily commands. Once it contends with the Fifth, great contention erupts, and the implicated multiply. Yet if it retreats and flees, the three hundred households of its village will surely escape disaster—such is the Second’s refusal to prolong contention. As for the Initial and Fourth, neither wishes to prolong contention. The Third, too, does not seek contention, though it fears provocation may lead to following royal affairs. Thus, when circumstances align, a leather belt may be granted, and the Top, too, fails in contention. All five lines thus benefit from the presence of Nine in the Fifth, and contention ultimately ceases. How supreme is the Fifth’s supreme good fortune through central correctness! Hence it is said: “In hearing contention, I am no different from others—what I must do is eliminate contention altogether.” This describes Nine in the Fifth. Thus, the Fifth line speaks only of “contention bringing supreme good fortune,” not of “hearing contention,” for supreme good fortune is its essence. Even if one hears contention well, how can it bring supreme good fortune?

From today’s perspective: the Initial, yielding and dwelling below, rests in softness, does not prolong the affair, and though minor grumblings arise, clarity prevails and contention ceases. Its good fortune in not prolonging contention is fitting. The Fourth and Fifth share the same body, benefiting from the presence of Nine in the Fifth, returning to the mandate, changing, and finding peace in uprightness—uniquely preserving the Fifth’s earnest desire to avoid contention. Its good fortune in not prolonging contention is especially fitting. Though the Top is excessively firm and dwells above, the Third feeds on old virtue, perilous at first but ultimately fortunate. Alas! The granting of the leather belt, the thrice-stripped shame—one may say the Top, too, does not prolong contention. Why? Because it still retains a sense of shame.

Fearing it might reveal its original countenance before the Nine in the Fifth.

**Appendix**
Yang Zhongli says: The heavens rotate leftward while water flows eastward—this is contrary movement. When affairs reach the point of contrary movement before one deliberates, excess has already taken hold.

Wang Ji says: Heaven is the beginning of the Three Powers; water is the beginning of the Five Phases. The noble person models himself upon them in acting and planning at the outset. In all matters, there is a beginning, a middle, and an end—contention in the middle brings good fortune, but contention at the end brings misfortune. If one can plan at the outset to sever contention, then the proper course in the middle and end need not be spoken of.

He also says: Observing the image of heaven and water moving in contrary directions—heaven’s leftward rotation and the five planets’ rightward revolution—this is further confirmed.

**Hexagram 7: 师 (Shi / The Army)**
**Judgment:** *Uprightness. The elder brings good fortune; there is no blame.*
**The Tuan (#35) says:** The army is the multitude. Uprightness is correctness. To be able to correct the multitude is to be fit to rule. Firmness in the center with resonance, advancing through peril yet in accord—using this to administer the world, the people follow. What blame could there be in good fortune?
**The Xiang says:** Water within the earth—this is *The Army*. The noble person thereby contains the people and nurtures the multitude.

**Line Statements:**
**Initial Six:** The army marches by discipline; if not good, there is misfortune.
**The Xiang says:** The army marches by discipline—losing discipline brings misfortune.

**Nine in the Second:** In the army, centeredness brings good fortune and no blame; the king thrice bestows command.
**The Xiang says:** In the army, centeredness brings good fortune—it receives the favor of heaven. The king thrice bestows command, cherishing the myriad states.

**Six in the Third:** The army may bear corpses in its chariots—misfortune.
**The Xiang says:** The army may bear corpses in its chariots—great excess in achievement.

**Six in the Fourth:** The army retreats to the left—no blame.
**The Xiang says:** Retreating to the left incurs no blame—it does not lose constancy.

**Six in the Fifth:** There are creatures in the fields; it is beneficial to seize them. No blame. The eldest son leads the army; the younger son bears corpses—uprightness brings misfortune.
**The Xiang says:** The eldest son leads the army—he acts with centeredness. The younger son bears corpses—his appointment is improper.

**Top Six:** The great lord issues a command, enfeoffing and establishing a house. The petty man must not be employed.
**The Xiang says:** The great lord issues a command—to rightly reward merit. The petty man must not be employed, for he will surely disorder the state.

**Hexagram 8: 比 (Bi / Alliance)**
Water clings to earth—this is *Alliance*. Seeking security through attachment is impossible without the firm centeredness of the Nine in the Fifth.

Water within the earth is *The Army*. The army is the multitude, yet to correct the multitude is impossible without the firm centeredness of the Nine in the Second. Thus, the hexagram takes the Nine in the Second as the elder. Only by obtaining the elder and entrusting him exclusively can one achieve good fortune without blame. How true this is! The difficulty of employing the multitude lies in correcting it. To correct the multitude is to be fit to rule the world. Thus it says: *Uprightness is correctness.* What is uprightness? "There are creatures in the fields; it is beneficial to seize them." To employ them with justification—this is what is meant by uprightness. Using the multitude in this way is to employ them to rightly govern the state, to enfeoff and establish a house, to pacify and cherish the people—what else could this be if not uprightness? Thus, using this to administer the people, the people follow, for it is upright.

Yet if one does not know the way of exclusive entrustment, even with the elder of the Nine in the Second, achieving merit will be difficult. This is what is meant by sending the eldest son to lead the army while also dispatching the younger son to bear corpses. In such a case, even uprightness brings misfortune, and ultimate defeat and disorder are inevitable. How could one hope for good fortune and no blame? What use is uprightness then? Truly, the difficulty of the army lies in correcting the multitude. The difficulty of correcting the multitude lies in obtaining the elder. Yet obtaining the elder is difficult, and being able to employ the elder is even more difficult.

Now, the Nine in the Second, dwelling below and centered, is precisely the elder’s selection, and the Six in the Fifth responds to him—it is not that the latter cannot employ him. The concern is that there may be divided loyalties, and his employment may not be exclusive. Thus, the Initial Six, upon stepping out the door, is immediately warned against losing discipline: the army’s discipline and commands issue solely from the eldest son; no other may usurp authority. If the Initial Six dares to usurp, it is a loss of discipline. Losing discipline—even if good, there is misfortune; how much worse if not good? Goodness is uprightness.

Now, the Six in the Fifth, able to regard the Second as the eldest son and thrice bestow command upon him, and the Second, in turn, achieves good fortune in the center of the army and receives the favor of heaven’s bestowal—yet the Initial Six cannot escape the warning of discipline, the Six in the Third cannot avoid the suspicion of bearing corpses, and the Six in the Fifth cannot prevent the dispatch of the younger son and the employment of petty men. The body of Kun is yielding and compliant, and this is to be feared.

Only the Six in the Fourth, attaining correctness, serves as a deputy general stationed to the left, able to retreat and await command. Thus it says: "The army may bear corpses in its chariots—great excess in achievement." The great refers to unity. If corpses are borne in chariots, greatness achieves no merit. How could the Six in the Fifth still dispatch the younger son to inquire? Thus, in the top line, it is reiterated: The great lord commands the general to lead the army, originally for the purpose of enfeoffment and establishing a house, employing them to pacify and cherish the myriad states—petty men must not be involved. Involving petty men will surely disorder the state.

For the Nine in the Second is yang firmness, the eldest son, the elder; the Initial and Third are yin softness. The soft are younger sons; younger sons should only be made to heed the commands of the eldest son. The yin are petty men; petty men should only be made to heed the commands of the elder. How could one employ the words of petty men or let the younger sons take charge of affairs? Alas! For a thousand years after, worthy generals who could not exercise exclusive authority met with disaster and defeat—this was invariably the cause.

In the Tang dynasty, when Yu Chao’en was made *Observer of the Army*, even Li Guangbi, the hero of Linhuai for the ages, and Guo Ziyi, the greatest figure of his time, could not avoid losing armies and discipline. Emperor Daizong, though he had shared hardships with them and together they had recovered the two capitals, ultimately trusted and employed petty men, forcing the emperor to flee once more to Shaan—this was no other reason than that he was yielding and easily swayed, truly like the Six in the Fifth of this hexagram.

The Sage’s earnest admonitions are indeed fitting as warnings.

**Appendix: Master Po’s Exegesis**
The *Elder* is what the *Odes* calls “a man of seasoned virtue.” Duke Mu of Zhai said: “The former kings radiated virtue and did not parade their armies.” For armies, when sheathed, must move in due season—once in motion, they display might; once displayed, they become a spectacle; once a spectacle, they fail to strike awe. Thus, “water lies within the earth.” When armies are spoken of, they should flow like water beneath the earth, unseen by men.

Liu Yongxiang said: “The second and fifth lines correspond, so the conferral of mandate is evident. Only after the fourth line aligns with the third can the second and third lines’ conferral be discerned.” The *Disciples* are none other than *petty men*—there are not two kinds.

Xiong Guo said: “The *Army* hexagram has one yang line leading five yin lines, for in ancient times five men formed a squad, five squads a platoon, five platoons a company, and five companies a battalion—all reckoned from the number five.” *A cartload of corpses*: Master Cheng interprets this as “the masses acting as the master.” According to ancient usage, *yú lùn* (public opinion) and *yú rén zhī sòng* (the people’s songs) both refer to the multitude. The *Odes* say: “Who is the master of this?”—*shī* (master) here means *to preside over*.

Yang Jingzhong said: “Water flowing beneath the earth, if there is something to contain it, accumulates and embodies the image of a gathered army. Without containment, it does not gather—precisely because there is originally something to hold it.” *The Yi’s Upper Canon, Scroll One concludes.* #1 (This heading was absent in the original and has been added by the editor.)

should be *xiān* (先), emended according to the context. #22 The character *yǐ* (以) appears as *hé* (合) in the Jigu Ge edition. #23 The sequence *kǎn xià gèn shàng* (坎下艮上) originally appeared as *kǎn shàng gèn xià* (坎上艮下) and has been emended according to the structure of the hexagram. #24 Following the character *zhī* (之), the received *Zhouyi* includes the character *lìn* (吝). #25 The character *jí* (即) should be *shí* (时), emended according to the context. #26 The character *wèi* (未) appears as *wèi* (谓) in the Jigu Ge edition. #27 The character *shī* (施) appears as *shòu* (受) in the Jigu Ge edition. #28 Following *jiè* (戒), the character *èr* (二) appears to be missing. #29 The character *wèn* (问) appears as *xiǎn* (险) in the Jigu Ge edition. #30 The character *jí* (吉) originally appeared as *gào* (告) and has been emended following the Jigu Ge edition. #31 The character *èr* (二) originally appeared as *yī* (一) and has been emended following the Jibu Ge edition. #32 The character *shān* (彡) originally appeared as *duō* (多) and has been emended following the Jigu Ge edition. #33 The character *qí* (其) originally appeared as *bīng* (兵) and has been emended following the received *Zhouyi*. #34 The character *jí* (吉) originally appeared as *gǔ* (古) and has been emended according to the context. #35 The character *tuàn* (彖) originally appeared as *xiàng* (象) and has been emended following the received *Zhouyi*. #36 The character *gǒng* (巩) originally appeared as *xiàng* (象) and has been emended following the received *Zhouyi*. #37 The character *yī* (一) appears as *èr* (二) in the Jigu Ge edition.

*The Yi’s Upper Canon, Scroll Two*

**Hexagram 8: Bǐ (比) – Union**
*Below Kǎn (坎), above Kūn (坤)*
**Union: Auspicious.**
*Original divination: Primordial, enduring, and steadfast—no blame.*
*Disquiet approaches; the latecomer meets misfortune.*

**Tuàn (Judgment) says:**
Union is auspicious.
Union means assistance; the lower follows in compliance.
*Original divination: Primordial, enduring, and steadfast—no blame,*
because of firmness in the center.
*Disquiet approaches,*
as superiors and inferiors respond to one another.
*The latecomer meets misfortune,*
for his Dao is exhausted.

**Xiàng (Image) says:**
Water upon the earth—*Bǐ* (Union).
The former kings, by this, established the myriad states and befriended the feudal lords.

**Line Statements:**
**Initial Six:**
Union with sincerity—no blame.
Sincerity fills the vessel; in the end, there comes another auspice—good fortune.
**Xiàng says:**
The initial six in *Bǐ* (Union) brings another auspice—good fortune.

**Six in the Second:**
Union from within—steadfast and auspicious.
**Xiàng says:**
Union from within—one does not lose oneself.

**Six in the Third:**
Union with the unworthy.
**Xiàng says:**
Union with the unworthy—is this not grievous?

**Six in the Fourth:**
External union—steadfast and auspicious.
**Xiàng says:**
External union with the worthy—thus following the superior.

**Nine in the Fifth:**
Manifest union. The king employs the threefold hunt, losing the game ahead.
The people of the town are not warned—good fortune.
**Xiàng says:**
The good fortune of manifest union lies in the correctness and centrality of the position.
*Containing the rebellious and seizing the compliant,*
losing the game ahead.
*The people of the town are not warned,*
for the superior employs the mean.

**Top Six:**
Union with the primordial head—misfortune.
**Xiàng says:**
Union with the primordial head—this is where it ultimately ends.

**Hexagram Bǐ (Water over Earth):**
Fang Shihua says: Water clings to the earth—*bǐ* (union). The lower compliantly follows the upper—this too is called *bǐ* (union).
In this hexagram, the single yang of the Nine in the Fifth alone can be closely united with the many yin because it is firm in the center, possesses the primordial, endures in steadfastness, and embodies the power of consent. Naturally, it may unite without blame. Thus, the Six in the Second, not losing itself within, divines again and, upon obtaining this, proceeds to unite—hence it is steadfast, auspicious, and free from blame. This is what is meant by *finding where one unites and thus being at peace*—precisely the meaning of *not losing the one and the five*.
The Six in the Fourth shares the same body with the Fifth and externally unites with it—befriending the virtuous, serving the worthy, following the superior, seeking peace—steadfast and auspicious, as is fitting. The Initial Six, occupying the first position as yin, truly responds to the primordial and correct, and its disquiet must yield to sincerity. Only then does it attain its proper union and incur no blame. Moreover, situated at the base of the earthly *Kūn*, water must fill it, presenting the image of sincerity filling the vessel. Since sincerity is already full, the good fortune of the Six in the Fourth ultimately comes to it—how could this be merely a matter of no blame?

Thus, if disquiet can be rectified and alignment drawn near, then in the end, even severed ties may yet yield other auspices. Hence it is said: "Disquiet rectified draws near"—above and below respond to one another. The "above" refers to the Fourth; the "below" refers to the Initial. For the Nine in the Fifth, firm and central in its correctness, is the true king, now manifestly befriended and aligned by all. Should any remain unaligned, the method of the threefold drive is employed to draw them in, yet the forward quarry may be lost—this is not a matter of insistence. Such is merely the king’s intent; how could the people of the domain know it? Without command or admonition, they are left to their own alignment, nor are they driven forward—yet the center above truly enables this. The king’s impartiality, in this way, becomes manifest and universally trusted. Thus the lower positions contend to align, and good fortune is assured.

Yet is not the Six in the Upper precisely the loss of the forward quarry? If the Three, instead of aligning with the Five, aligns with the Six, is this not the harm of associating with the unworthy? Therefore, the Initial Six and the Six in the Second do not join the Three in the selection of the threefold drive. The people of the domain, without command or admonition, choose compliance and reject opposition, aligning with the primal and maintaining steadfast correctness—this is how it must be. The Six in the Upper, at the apex of alignment, still clings to the primal chief as its match—how dire the misfortune! Truly, I know not how it will end. For when it stands before the Five, it is said, "the forward quarry is lost"; when it clings to the primal chief as its match, unaware of aligning with the Five, it is said, "the latter will meet with misfortune."

**Appendix**
Wang Fusi says: The upper and lower yang positions divide the people, yet the Five alone abides in honor, and all turn toward it. Above and below respond to it, exalted and at peace. Peace draws the disquieted to shelter, hence "disquiet rectified draws near"—above and below respond. The primal seeks those who possess; it does not seek those who endanger, but those who bring peace. It does not seek those who withdraw, for fire has its flame, and the cold draw near—thus, if one is at peace, the disquieted will draw near. Peace, honor, harmony, and kinship, yet lingering behind—this is why punishment brings misfortune.

Master Su of the Eastern Slope says: Alignment brings good fortune. No alignment is without good fortune, yet aligning with the wrong person, though presently fortunate, will later incur blame. Hence it is said, "divine again"—divine whom to follow. "Divine again" means to divine twice, the height of caution.

Zhao Ruqi says: Alignment does not consider correspondence but focuses solely on aligning with the Five.

Ban Mengjian says: "Quarry" is the general term for birds and beasts, those captured and controlled by humans.

Bu Zixia says: Primal sincerity in attachment, when the Dao is exhausted and alignment follows, execution will ensue. How could it end otherwise?

**Hexagram 9: Small Taming (小畜 Xiǎo Chù): Prospering.**
*Dense clouds, no rain—from our western suburbs.*

**The Judgment (彖传 Tuànzhuàn) says:**
"Small Taming": the yielding attains its position, and above and below respond to it—thus "Small Taming." Vigorous yet yielding, firm in the center with its will enacted—thereby prospering. *Dense clouds, no rain*—still advancing. *From our western suburbs*—the bestowal has not yet taken effect.

**The Image (象传 Xiàngzhuàn) says:**
Wind moving above heaven: *Small Taming*. The noble one refines his cultural virtue.

**Line 1 (初九 Chū jiǔ):**
Returning by one’s own path—what blame could there be? Auspicious.
**The Image says:**
Returning by one’s own path—its principle is auspicious.

**Line 2 (九二 Jiǔ èr):**
Drawn back to return—auspicious.
**The Image says:**
Drawn back to return, centered—it does not lose itself.

**Line 3 (九三 Jiǔ sān):**
The cart sheds its spokes; husband and wife glare at each other.
**The Image says:**
Husband and wife glare at each other—unable to rectify the household.

**Line 4 (六四 Liù sì):**
Possessing sincerity, bloodshed departs and fear emerges—no blame.
**The Image says:**
Possessing sincerity, fear emerges—above, it aligns with the will.

**Line 5 (九五 Jiǔ wǔ):**
Possessing sincerity, bound as twins—enriching with one’s neighbors.
**The Image says:**
Possessing sincerity, bound as twins—it does not enrich alone.

**Line 6 (上九 Shàng jiǔ):**
The rain has fallen, the matter is settled—still, virtue bears the load. The wife’s steadfastness is perilous; the moon nears fullness. For the noble one, advancing brings misfortune.
**The Image says:**
The rain has fallen, the matter is settled—virtue accumulates and bears the load. For the noble one, advancing brings misfortune—there is cause for doubt.

**Fāng Shíhuà’s Commentary on *Wind Above Heaven: Small Taming*:**
The hexagram is named *Small Taming* because the single yin line at the fourth position, as the yielding ruler of Xùn, has attained its proper place. Moreover, it resides above Qián, vigorous yet yielding. Thus, the ninth line above (Nine Five) responds to the first line below (Initial Nine), while Nine Two also responds above. All below respond to the fourth line, each willing to be tamed by it—hence *Small Taming*. Though the single yin of Six Four is small, it can tame the greatness of many yang lines. This must arise from more than mere vigor—it must act with vigor while practicing yielding. Here, the firm and centered Five alone can extend sincere trust, achieving the twin-like sincerity that allows Six Four’s will to be enacted, and the Dao of taming to prosper. Furthermore, Initial Nine corresponds with Four—returning to Four is returning by one’s own path. Nine Two is adjacent to Initial Nine; Initial Nine responds to Four, and Nine Two is drawn to Initial Nine—this is *drawn back to return*. Yet without Nine Five first responding to it, how could Four fulfill its will, and how would the many yang lines willingly submit to its taming? The two lines about *dense clouds* depict the scene of rising clouds and impending rain, expressing delight in its taming. The tradition says *the bestowal has not yet taken effect*—this precisely describes the process of taming. But *not yet taken effect* does not mean it will never take effect.

Six Four, being firm yet yielding, can thus employ a person of sincere substance to manifest the vigilance of Qian, casting aside its blood and directly entering through yielding. Thereby, Nine Five obtains the aid of its neighbor to enjoy its secure wealth and honored position—this is the depth of its trustworthiness. When Nine Three ascends to pair with Six Four, it forms the image of husband and wife, yet is excessively rigid and not centered. If Four regards it as a cheek to its jawbone, Three instead sheds its spokes, turns away in hostility, and pays no heed—how then can it govern the household and establish the family? This is the fault of Nine Three, not a flaw in Four.

Upper Nine’s view of Nine Three differs. It does not recognize that Four’s Way of taming has already succeeded: formerly dense clouds, now rain has fallen. Formerly one longed for rain, now rain has come and the situation has shifted. Crops now stand in the fields, gathered into granaries, and virtue is fully borne. Four is yin, Five is yang. If Four is taken as the wife, she is steadfast and vigilant; Four is the fourth month, Five the fifth day. If Four is taken as the moon, it waxes toward fullness in mutual gaze—how is this not secure? If its taming does not share in governance but seeks separate validation, then Six Four’s capacity for taming need only join with others to fulfill its taming—no doubt is necessary.

That Upper Nine resists its taming stems merely from its suspicion, in Four’s yielding entry, that Four is not a true noble. Yet this Six Four of *Small Taming* is precisely firm yet yielding, capable of greatness, benefiting the state and greatly benefiting the ruler. Such was the great minister of old—what doubt could there be?

Wang’s edition records He’s note: “When yin doubts yang, it leads to bloody conflict, hence *Kun*’s Upper Six bears the wound of dark and yellow. When yin trusts yang, blood is dispelled, hence *Small Taming*’s Six Four is free from the burden of blood and qi.”

**Appendix**
Yang Jian said: “Yi Yin’s treatment of Tai Jia was not initially smooth, but when Tai Jia suddenly awoke and sincerely reformed, Yi Yin’s will then came to fruition—thus it became smooth. If Heaven had not thundered greatly with wind to complete the transformation, and the king had not wept while holding the decree, the Duke of Zhou’s will could never have been called fulfilled, nor could *Small Taming*’s smoothness have been achieved. Six Four, utmost in softness and embodying yielding, tames the ruler with gentle compliance, so ruler and minister trust and harmonize, free from harm, free from fear, without blame. This is the natural image of yin and yang’s mutual accord.

When ministers offer counsel to rulers, discord often arises because the minister has not eliminated self-interest—whether craving reputation, prizing personal victory, or failing to align with the ruler’s will. Thus discord emerges. The *Book of Documents* states: ‘If you have excellent plans or strategies, present them. Thereafter, attend to inner matters, then outwardly comply.’ It says: ‘These plans, these strategies belong to our lord alone.’ A virtuous minister tames the ruler in this way—how could discord arise?”

The *Image* says: “Above, the wills align”—how profound is this teaching!

Jin Rubai said: “Both Four and Five speak of having trust—this is mutual trust between the two lines. Four says ‘above, the wills align,’ meaning it aligns with Five. Four relies on Five to tame, while Qian’s Five entrusts Four and they trust one another.”

**Hexagram 10: Lü (Treading)**
*Below Dui, above Qian*
Treading on a tiger’s tail—it does not bite the person. Smoothness.

**Judgment:**
*Treading* means the yielding treads upon the firm. Joyful and responsive to Qian, thus treading on a tiger’s tail without being bitten—smoothness. Firm, centered, and correct, treading the imperial position without distress—radiant clarity.

**Image:**
Heaven above, marsh below—*Treading*. The noble person thereby distinguishes above and below, settling the people’s will.

**Line Statements:**
**Initial Nine:** Plain treading—going forth without blame.
*Image:* Going forth with plain treading—acting alone on one’s aspiration.

**Nine Two:** Treading a level path—secluded persons: steadfastness brings good fortune.
*Image:* The secluded person’s steadfast good fortune—centered, not self-disordered.

**Six Three:** Dim-sighted yet striving to see, lame yet striving to tread—treading on a tiger’s tail, it bites the person. Misfortune. A martial man acts as a great lord.
*Image:* Dim-sighted yet striving to see—insufficient for clarity. Lame yet striving to tread—insufficient for movement. The misfortune of being bitten—position improper. A martial man acting as a great lord—will is rigid.

**Nine Four:** Treading on a tiger’s tail—cautious, ultimately good fortune.
*Image:* Cautious, ultimately good fortune—will is enacted.

**Nine Five:** Decisive treading—steadfastness is perilous.
*Image:* Decisive treading—steadfastness is perilous—position is correct and proper.

**Upper Nine:** Contemplating one’s treading, examining the omens—its return is supremely auspicious.
*Image:* Supreme auspiciousness above—great celebration.

**Hexagram 10: Lü (Treading)**
Fang Shihua said: “Nine Five, with Qian’s firm virtue, occupies the supreme position. Those who tread behind it are joyful and responsive to Qian—what peril could there be? Yet the line uniquely speaks of decisiveness and perilous steadfastness, indicating a specific reference. Thus the *Image* directly names the hexagram with the three words ‘treading a tiger’s tail.’ It is as if treading a tiger’s tail and merely avoiding being bitten is already fortunate, yet the Master’s commentary focuses on the yielding treading the firm, joy responding to Qian, and its firmness, centrality, and correctness—treading the imperial position without distress, so radiantly clear. This further reveals that with Qian above and Dui below, there is absolutely no cause for concern. The intent is to display its virtue through this hexagram, differing from King Wen’s *Appended Judgments*.

Six Three is neither centered nor correct, its will rigid and vision dim—hence its image. King Wen’s meaning is that when ministers serve rulers, there is always the peril of treading on a tiger. Treading requires seeing with the eyes. Now Six Three is blind and cannot see—how could it not be bitten by Initial Nine? At the bottom of the hexagram, just stepping out the door already fears treading on a tiger, yet it can tread plainly and go forth, acting alone on its aspiration—its foresight is early indeed.”

At the bottom, in the initial position, this is its plainness—hence it is called "plain treading." Nine Two, however, sees the path of treading as vast indeed. Though a reclusive person may also be steadfast and auspicious, why must one seek resonance with the fifth to tread upon its tail, spending the whole day in anxious dread, disturbing one’s centeredness? This too is one who sees in advance, avoids perilous conduct—an ancient noble scholar.

Four occupies the position of a great minister, near Nine Five, and cannot but tread upon the tiger’s tail. Yet by recognizing it as a tiger and remaining in anxious dread, it ultimately achieves good fortune and fulfills its will without being bitten. Still, it is perilous—neither the first nor the second would willingly accept such a position.

Nine at the top, having completed its affairs at the end of treading, reviews its path and examines its omens, then returns to the beginning—supremely auspicious. Naturally, those like Three, unable to see, are likened to the blind; those unable to tread, to the lame. Only through blindness does one fail to act, and thus attempt to wrestle tigers bare-handed or act as a warrior before the great lord—its misfortune is assured. Though called resolute in will, what is there to commend?

Master Li the Bald said: When King Wen faced the decline of Yin, he once personally suffered its bite. How fitting that his reflections were so deeply felt and lingering! Why else would he name this hexagram *Treading on a Tiger’s Tail* with Heaven above and Marsh below? And why, in the line statements, speak of being bitten as misfortune, yet in the *Image* declare that not being bitten brings prosperity? The prosperity of not being bitten arises from the inner softness of Dui. The misfortune of being bitten stems from Six Three’s rigid will, unlike Four’s fulfilled aspiration.

Alas! In such times, one could not tread plainly like the first, nor retreat like the reclusive second, nor return like the final nine. In the aftermath of pain, even King Wen must have regretted his warrior’s lament. Thus, the blind gaze and lame tread were King Wen’s affairs before his imprisonment at Youli. The anxious dread that ended in good fortune were his affairs after Youli. The return to supreme auspiciousness came only after he was enfeoffed as Lord of the West and granted the power to wage war—all King Wen’s hexagram. It directly illuminates *Brightness Obscured*.

Wang Benhe said: Rather than anxiously seeking to avoid being bitten, how much better to tread the Way with ease and attain steadfast auspiciousness!

**Appendix**
Cheng Zhengshu said: Heaven above and marsh below—this is the proper order of above and below, the principle of things. The noble person observes the image of *Treading* to distinguish between high and low and settle the people’s will. Only when the distinction between high and low is clear can the people’s will be settled. With the people’s will settled, governance can then be discussed.

In ancient times, from dukes and ministers downward, each position matched its virtue, and one remained in it for life, fulfilling one’s proper role. If a position did not match one’s virtue, the ruler would promote one; scholars cultivated their learning, and when their learning was complete, the ruler would seek them. Farmers, artisans, merchants, and traders diligently performed their tasks with limited rewards, so all had settled aspirations.

In later ages, from common scholars to high ministers, all daily aspired to honor and glory. Farmers, artisans, merchants, and traders daily pursued wealth and extravagance. The hearts of the multitudes raced after profit, and the world was in chaos—how could it be unified? To expect it not to descend into disorder would be difficult indeed.

Below, Qian; above, Kun.

**Peace (泰):**
"Small departs, great arrives—auspicious, smooth."

**The *Xiang* says:**
Peace—small departs, great arrives, auspicious, smooth—means Heaven and Earth unite, and the myriad things communicate; above and below unite, and their aspirations align. Within, yang; without, yin. Within, strength; without, compliance. Within, the noble person; without, the petty person. The noble person’s Dao grows, the petty person’s Dao wanes.

**The *Xiang* says:**
Heaven and Earth unite in Peace. The ruler thereby completes the Dao of Heaven and Earth, assisting and harmonizing with their principles, to guide the people.

**First Nine:**
"Pulling cogon grass by its roots, advancing with its kind—auspicious in advancement."

**The *Xiang* says:**
"Pulling cogon grass, advancing auspiciously"—the aspiration lies in the external.

**Nine Two:**
"Embracing the vast, fording the river; no distant exclusion, no clique loyalty—attaining esteem in the central conduct."

**The *Xiang* says:**
"Embracing the vast, attaining esteem in the central conduct"—thereby illuminating and magnifying.

**Nine Three:**
"Level without incline, advancing without return—steadfast in hardship, no blame. Do not worry over its sincerity; in nourishment, there is fortune."

**The *Xiang* says:**
"Advancing without return"—the boundary of Heaven and Earth.

**Six Four:**
"Fluttering, not wealthy, with its neighbors—no warning, yet sincere."

**The *Xiang* says:**
"Fluttering, not wealthy"—all lose substance. "No warning, yet sincere"—the heart’s desire.

**Six Five:**
"Di Yi marries off his younger sister—blessing, supremely auspicious."

**The *Xiang* says:**
"Blessing, supremely auspicious"—acting from the center to fulfill aspirations.

**Top Six:**
"The city wall collapses into the moat—do not mobilize troops. From the capital, decrees are issued—steadfastness brings regret."

**The *Xiang* says:**
"The city wall collapses into the moat"—its mandate is in chaos.

**Earth above, Heaven below—Peace.**
Peace: small departs, great arrives. "Auspicious, smooth" speaks of the way to achieve Peace. Only when small and great exchange departures and arrivals can the small be auspicious, the great smooth, and Peace enduring. Thus, the First Nine, situated at the base of the three yang lines, embodies the inception of greatness yet aspires to the external. The yin of the outer seeks to unite with the small, pulling the three yang lines forward in kind—hence the four lines flutter in mutual trust, and small and great exchange.

The three yin lines, all lacking wealth and substance, should not sincerely respond to the yang. Yet now they flutteringly advance to unite with yang—how could this involve mobilization or warning? It is the heart’s desire, unwarned yet sincere. It is not for the wealth of their neighbors but to serve between the ruler and minister of the second and fifth lines. Thus, Nine Two, with the virtue of embracing the vast, esteems the host, while Six Five, with blessing and supreme auspiciousness, marries off the younger sister—not only fulfilling aspirations but also realizing them. Is this not the way to achieve Peace: a ruler who empties himself to heed his ministers, and ministers who embrace the vast to illuminate their ruler?

Yet knowing the way to Peace lies in the exchange of departures and arrivals, one also knows that such exchanges cannot endure. Peace is difficult to preserve—this too must be considered. Thus, within the inner trigram at the third line and the outer trigram at the top, it is uniquely emphasized. The "boundary of Heaven and Earth" speaks of their juncture. The union of Heaven and Earth, once united, disperses; once joined, separates—it cannot last. For level and incline, advance and return, sincerity is evident, yet this is not something human effort can control. Yet knowing this and steadfastly enduring hardship, one may avoid the blame of great adversity, the blame of incline, the blame of advance. Why? Human determination can overcome Heaven.

Thus, it warns: "Do not worry over its sincerity." If sincerity is thus, and one assumes Peace must advance, merely knowing this and enduring hardship to preserve it—without treating it as perpetual—can sustain Peace. Then one may not only avoid the blame of great adversity but also long enjoy the fortune of Peace. Hence: "In nourishment, there is fortune." Thus, if you but endure hardship, Peace will not depart. Though there be constant patterns, what can they do to you?

The third line, at the top of the lower trigram, marks the juncture of Peace’s exchange—hence special attention is given. The top line, at the extreme of Peace, sees the city wall collapse into the moat, reverting from above to below. Do not mobilize the masses—they will not heed. Why? Above and below do not unite. From the capital, decrees are issued—its mandate is already in chaos. Though one wishes to endure hardship, it is no longer possible. Alas! Should one not be warned early?

**Appendix:**
Feng Qi said: After Qian and Kun, one first enters the human Dao, traversing six Kan trigrams, experiencing all hardships and dangers, accumulating within and treading without—only then is Peace achieved. Yet after Peace, Adversity follows, revealing how difficult it is for humanity to stand and how easily chaos arises. This is the profound intent of those who arranged the *Yi*.

Wang Fusi said: Cogon grass, when pulled, has roots that connect and draw together. *Ru* describes this connected appearance. The three yang lines share the same aspiration, all directed outward. The first is of its kind; when the leader rises, the kind follows. Hence: "advancing with its kind—auspicious in advancement."

Cheng Zhengshu said: The *Xiang* cites "embracing the vast" to comprehensively explain the meaning of the four lines. It says that thus one can align with the virtue of the central conduct, and one’s Dao will be luminous and grand.

Shi Shoudao said: Beyond the second line, "level without incline"; beyond the fifth, "the city wall collapses into the moat."

Yang Tingxiu said: Level and incline alternate; advance and return shift. Those in an age of Peace must not assume the times are level—danger will soon arise. They must not assume yin has departed—its return is swift. Nine Three is the extreme of yang’s flourishing. Yin will return, Peace will turn to Adversity. Should one not fear? Why? The juncture of Heaven and Earth’s exchange, the interplay of yin and yang, lies between Nine Three and Six Four.

Jin Rubai said: "Attaining esteem in the center" is like a feudal lord marrying a princess.

The virtue of Nine Two enables it to esteem the central conduct of Six Five as its lord.

Wang Fusi further said: Dwelling at the utmost of *Tai* (Peace), each returns to its counterpart—thus the Dao of *Tai* is about to perish. Above and below do not interact; the lowly do not ascend to serve, the exalted do not descend to bestow. Hence, "the city wall collapses into the moat." "Do not mobilize troops"—no need for attack. "From the city, proclamations are issued"—this is the *Pi* (Adversity) Dao’s steadfast constraint, where commands already issued cannot be executed.

Master Po (Su Shi) explained: Taking earth from the moat to build a city, raising and fortifying it—this is not for the city’s benefit, but for the people’s. When *Kun* (Earth) is above and seeks to return below, it is like earth made into a wall longing to return to the moat. When the above loses its defense, the below schemes to usurp command. Thus it is said: "From the city, proclamations are issued." A city is not where commands should originate.

*Kun* below, *Qian* above—*Pi*’s "not the proper person." "Not advantageous." "The great departs, the small arrives."

The *Xiang* (Image) says: "*Pi*’s 'not the proper person'—not advantageous. The noble person’s steadfastness. The great departs, the small arrives"—this means Heaven and Earth do not interact, and the myriad things are not connected. Above and below do not interact, and the realm has no guiding state. Within, yin; without, yang. Within, softness; without, hardness. Within, the petty person; without, the noble person. The petty person’s Dao grows, the noble person’s Dao wanes.

The *Xiang* says: "Heaven and Earth do not interact—*Pi*." The noble person, through frugal virtue, avoids calamity; one must not seek glory through emolument.

**First Six:** "Pulling cogon grass, roots entwined—by its kind. Steadfastness brings good fortune and smoothness."
The *Xiang* says: "Pulling cogon grass—steadfastness brings good fortune," for the aspiration lies with the lord.

**Six Two:** "Enveloping and submitting—petty persons find good fortune; great persons find adversity, yet smoothness."
The *Xiang* says: "Great persons find adversity, yet smoothness," for they do not disrupt the group.

**Six Three:** "Enveloping shame."
The *Xiang* says: "Enveloping shame," for the position is not appropriate.

**Nine Four:** "Having mandate, no blame. Companions partake in blessing."
The *Xiang* says: "Having mandate, no blame," for the aspiration is fulfilled.

**Nine Five:** "Ceasing adversity—great persons find good fortune. 'It may perish! It may perish!'—as if striking the roots of the mulberry."
The *Xiang* says: "Great persons’ good fortune," for the position is correct and fitting.

**Top Nine:** "Overturning adversity—first adversity, then joy."
The *Xiang* says: "When adversity ends, it overturns—how can it last long?"

Heaven and Earth in *Pi* (Adversity). Only when the small departs and the great arrives—with a single interaction—is *Tai* (Peace) achieved. Thus, when the great departs and the small arrives, the lack of interaction forms *Pi*. Therefore, the way to bring about *Pi* lies entirely in "not the proper person."

What is meant by "not the proper person"? It is because they claim the noble person’s steadfastness, yet the great do not come, the small do not depart, and there is no interaction—hence *Pi*. Some ask: "Steadfastness means correctness. If steadfastness itself is not advantageous, why does it say 'not advantageous for the noble person’s steadfastness'? Moreover, First Six’s 'pulling cogon grass, roots entwined' is also linked to steadfastness, good fortune, and smoothness. Yet yin’s softness, entwined in kind, is the very opposite of steadfastness—why does the sage reverse this, calling it steadfast, good, and capable of smoothing adversity?"

It is said: This is yin departing to interact with yang, knowing only that the small should depart but entirely unaware that the noble person’s steadfastness should not depart. Thus, as this departs and that arrives, this does not see itself as petty, nor does that see itself as noble. Not seeing itself as petty is the absence of the petty person—thus the petty person’s Dao wanes. Not seeing itself as noble means both are noble—thus the noble person’s Dao grows, and growth brings *Tai*. Observing this Dao, all is small departing and great arriving—hence the way to bring about *Pi* is evident.

Now, if one wishes to reverse *Pi* and achieve *Tai*, how can one still claim to possess the noble person’s steadfastness alone? Precisely because one has steadfastness, one is "not the proper person." Thus it is said: "*Pi*’s 'not the proper person'—smoothness." *Pi* is something to contemplate.

Therefore, with First Six’s "pulling cogon grass, roots entwined," its aspiration lies with the lord—thus Nine Four’s companions partake in blessing, its aspiration fulfilled, and above and below interact. Nine Four dwells at the lower position of the upper trigram, holding the incipient signs of *Tai*’s return and the mandate’s imminent restoration. Moreover, it occupies the position of a great minister, near the lord’s domain. Having fulfilled its aspiration, it naturally incurs no blame, and all kinds align with its fortune. This is not merely "pulling cogon grass, roots entwined."

Four is the lord of First, hence "aspiration lies with the lord." Therefore, with Six Two’s way of smoothing adversity, it naturally envelops and submits, making petty persons all find good fortune—what steadfastness is there? With Nine Five’s great person’s method of ceasing adversity, the great person naturally finds good fortune—not merely making petty persons find good fortune—what adversity remains?

Yet having said "ceasing adversity," it seems at first not to regard adversity as a concern—how could one know that within, it is truly arduous, steadfast, and unyielding, constantly saying, "It may perish! It may perish!"—as if striking the roots of the mulberry. How could one consider this a momentary absence of self-preservation? "First adversity, then joy"—its impermanence is fitting.

Li Tuweng said: The way to preserve *Tai* lies above all in *Qian*. The way to smooth *Pi* lies above all in *Kun*. *Qian* is Heaven—Heaven’s origin does not cover, thus Nine Two alone, with its capacity to envelop the wild, presides over *Tai*. *Kun* is Earth—Earth’s origin does not bear, thus Six Two alone, with its way of enveloping, submitting, and compliance, smooths *Pi*. Yet though *Tai* governs the world with enveloping vastness, it truly conducts itself with arduous steadfastness—hence it is said: "Arduous steadfastness—no blame." This is what is meant by King Wen, from morning till the sun passed noon, having no leisure even to eat.

Though *Pi* treats others with enveloping submission, it truly rests in its ominous cessation of adversity—hence it is said: "Its ominousness—its ominousness—ominousness without end. Where will it return?" This is what is meant by Goujian tasting gall and sleeping on brushwood. Nevertheless, when *Tai* prevails, all believe it to be *Tai*, yet I alone regard it as *Pi*—thus I am not swayed by *Tai* and can make *Tai* enduringly *Tai*.

When *Pi* prevails, all believe it to be *Pi*, yet I alone regard it as *Tai*—thus I am not disturbed by *Pi* and can ensure *Pi* does not remain *Pi* to the end.

This inverted conduct, this counterflow, keeps the hilt of *Tai’e* ever in my grasp—truly the great one who brings *Pi* to rest. Yet the noble person does not undertake this—why? Because the noble person possesses steadfastness. With steadfastness, seeing others as unsteadfast, one will not go forth—how then can one avoid *Pi*? Only by "plucking the creeping grass, its kind all drawn together," when yin softness unites in mutual connection, does steadfastness arise. Only by enveloping submission, enduring shame, and ensuring that what goes forth does not return, does steadfastness arise. With steadfastness comes smoothness, rest, and good fortune—thus it becomes *Tai*.

Hence it is said: "Enveloping and submitting to the petty person—good fortune." Merely by enveloping and submitting to make the petty person prosper, one embodies the great one’s way of smoothing *Pi*—no need to wait for another day to achieve smoothness.

Some ask: The second nine envelops the vast, attains eminence through the center, and in action becomes the way of *Tai*—this is entirely enveloping the vast. Why then does the line also speak of "crossing the river" and "cliques bringing misfortune"? Beyond enveloping the vast, must one also employ the courage of "crossing the river"? Beyond not neglecting the distant, must one also sever cliquish partiality? Not so. One who truly envelops the vast without neglecting the distant naturally severs cliquish partiality and bravely "crosses the river." Otherwise, one is merely a vacillating, indecisive man.

As when Emperor Yuan of Western Han hoped for Xiang’s execution yet did not know how to restrain Dou Xian of Eastern Han, who seized the sovereign’s fields and gardens—what did he gain by "enveloping the vast"? Thus, though strong, one must weaken; though orderly, one must fall into chaos—how can there be any glory or attainment of the central path? The line encompasses this meaning, hence its detailed exposition.

**Appendix**
Lǚ Bogong said: *Pi* is the disunion of above and below, possessing the form of Heaven and Earth but lacking their efficacy.

Dèng Bogao said: The second, centered and correct, assists the great lord in bringing *Pi* to rest—hence the method of enveloping and submitting to the petty person.

The third, yin-soft in *Pi*, at times endures shame to heed the petty person’s clamor, or endures shame to join the petty person’s faction.

Li Sheng said: This is precisely the method of dwelling in *Pi*—what is called "spitting on one’s face and letting it dry," or "stripping off one’s robe and gambling freely." As for Li Gu’s execution, what did it profit the Han but to fan the flames of Liang Ji’s tyranny? Not only was it of no benefit, but it greatly harmed the state. Thus, the line does not attach good fortune, misfortune, regret, or remorse to enduring shame, and the *Xiang* merely notes its improper position. For it is neither the position of the great lord at nine-five nor that of the great minister at nine-four—what can it do but watch for opportunity?

The compliance of Chen Ping and others, who flattered and followed the will of the king and the Lü clan, serves as an example. What is called "the great one smoothing *Pi* without confusion" is truly so. Hence it is said: "To rebuke at court today, a remonstrating minister is not as effective as a decisive sovereign; to secure the altars of state and the Liu succession, a later lord is not as effective as a minister." Alas, who would say enduring shame is truly inappropriate? Reading this, one can only weep. Such words are truly difficult to speak to others. Only those with a true heart, unbetraying of person, family, or state, will silently understand.

Xióng Guo said: *Xiū* (rest) means people resting in the shade of trees; *bāo sāng* (bundled mulberry) refers to the budding of tender mulberry shoots, as in the *Odes*’ "mutual bundling of the boxthorn."

Dèng Bogao further said: *Bāo sāng* is not a metaphor for solid bonds but resembles the ancients’ "rotting rope driving six horses" or "treading on a tiger’s tail and spring ice." In Lu Xuangong’s *Memorial Requesting the Cessation of Troops After the Recovery of Hebei*, he wrote: "The state’s pillars, darkly and continuously linked, are like bundled mulberry, gracefully entwined—fortunate not to snap, though threadlike." Thus, *bāo* means clustered growth. Clustered mulberry, thin and weak—how can it bear heavy ties?

Shào Guoxián said: *Pi* and *Tai* are matters of timing. Timing is what the ruler and the noble person bring about. Thus, the *Xiang* of *Tai* says "the way of the ruler is above," while the *Xiang* of *Pi* says "the way of the noble person is below." In the time of *Tai*, wealth and assistance are complete, and the noble person joins in—what strength is needed? Now it is otherwise—this is called "frugal virtue."

**Li** (Fire below Heaven): *Tóngrén* (Fellowship) in the wild—smooth. Favorable to ford the great river; favorable for the noble person’s steadfastness.

The *Tuan* says: *Tóngrén*—the soft attains position and the center, responding to *qián*, hence "fellowship." "Fellowship in the wild—smooth; favorable to ford the great river"—*qián* is in motion. Illuminating and strong, centered and correct, responding—this is the noble person’s steadfastness. Only the noble person can unite the world’s aspirations.

The *Xiang* says: Heaven and fire—*tóngrén*. The noble person thereby classifies clans and distinguishes things.

**First Nine**: Fellowship at the gate—no blame.
The *Xiang* says: Going forth to fellowship—who would blame this?

**Six Two**: Fellowship within the clan—regret.
The *Xiang* says: Fellowship within the clan—this is the way of regret.

**Nine Three**: Hiding troops in the thicket, ascending the high mound—three years without rising.
The *Xiang* says: Hiding troops in the thicket—opposing the firm. Three years without rising—how can one act?

**Nine Four**: Mounting the ramparts, not attacking—good fortune.
The *Xiang* says: Mounting the ramparts—righteousness does not permit attack. Its good fortune lies in being constrained and returning to principle.

**Nine Five**: Fellowship—first wailing and weeping, then laughing. The great armies meet.
The *Xiang* says: The beginning of fellowship—centered and upright. The great armies meet—this speaks of mutual overcoming.

**Top Nine**: Fellowship in the outskirts—no regret.
The *Xiang* says: [Text incomplete in original]

The *Xiang* says: Fellowship in the outskirts—its aspiration is not yet achieved.

**Heaven-Fire Fellowship (Tongren)**
A single yin resides above the second line, while the five yang below all desire fellowship—thus it is called "fellowship in the wilds." By occupying the center and attaining its proper position, it naturally becomes the clan’s ancestral standard. A single yin resides above the fifth line, while the five yang below all belong to it—thus it is called "Great Possession," fundamentally prosperous. By dwelling in softness yet occupying the honored position, its power naturally enables greatness. Yet the fifth line finds it easy to possess greatness, while the second struggles to serve as the clan’s standard—this is the distinction between above and below, the difference in the power of honor and humility.

Thus, in *Great Possession*, the sixth five alone achieves supreme fortune in greatness, while in *Fellowship*, the six two cannot avoid the regret of being bound to the clan. Yet though the six two may see itself as burdened by the clan’s regret, the five yang regard it as truly in fellowship with the wilds. Therefore, the *Tuan* commentary settles on the four characters "fellowship in the wilds" to name the hexagram.

From a present perspective: the first line emerges from the gate in search of fellowship. The third conceals troops in the thicket in search of fellowship. The fourth mounts the ramparts in search of fellowship. The fifth alone wails and weeps, raising great armies to seek fellowship. And the top line ventures to the outskirts, gazing afar, hoping for even a single moment of fellowship. Thus, the five yang above and below all take the second as their standard, desiring to join in fellowship—this is evident. What they all share is the fellowship of the entire hexagram; if not the fellowship of the wilds, then what?

The four-character hexagram name appears only here and in "Adversity’s" "not of the people." As for "Treading the tiger’s tail" or "Stilling one’s back," these three-character hexagram names follow another pattern. Yet this hexagram is truly named *Fellowship* because the second and fifth share a single heart. Thus, the *Tuan* commentary first speaks of fellowship, saying: "The soft attains its position, attains the center, and responds to Qian—this is fellowship." "Responds to Qian" refers specifically to the fifth line.

If this is so, then why does the hexagram *Fellowship* say: "Fellowship in the wilds, prosperity, beneficial to ford the great river"? It means not only that the fifth responds to the second, desiring fellowship with it, but that all of Qian’s movements seek to respond to the second, to join in fellowship with it. Thus, it says "Qian moves"—this is clear. The reason lies in the six two’s civil brilliance and strength, its centered correctness and responsiveness, possessing the rectitude of the noble person. Rectitude unites the aspirations of all under heaven—how could this apply only to the fifth?

Thus, the fellowship of the wilds, the benefit of fording the river, the prosperity of fellowship—what doubt remains? Yet there are inevitably disputes from the third and fourth lines, and the overcoming of the nine five. Its nearness may seem regrettable, leading to the regret attributed to the second line. But if the six two truly possesses the steadfastness of the noble person—centered, correct, and responsive—then nothing can harm its fellowship in the wilds. If it truly has the power to sever metal like shared words, the fragrance of orchids, then the mutual trust between the second and fifth—how could the third and fourth contend against it? Though I may be seen as regrettable, I will not be concerned.

This is why the Master, in the *Wenyan*, concludes with the words of two people in fellowship: "like the severing of metal, the shared heart’s words, the fragrance of orchids." Alas! The transmission between Yao and Shun, between Confucius and Yan—flowing to the damp, following the clouds with the wind, responding to sound and seeking qi—how could there be any regret? Thus, King Wen was moved by the nine five, and the Master by the six two.

**Appendix**
Su Shi’s explanation: "Beneficial to ford the great river—this is Qian’s movement. If one does not achieve sincere fellowship, one may unite in times of peace but dissolve when fording the river."

Tu Xi says: "The six two does not merely respond to the nine five’s Qian; all those with whom it fellowships are Qian. To move with Qian—what is fording a river to this? A single Qian is already unbearable; how much more five Qian? The noble person may rejoice in this without ceasing."

Su Shi’s interpretation of "sincere fellowship" to explain "Qian’s movement" seems unclear. "Sincere fellowship" refers to the second and fifth lines. "Qian’s movement" refers to the five yang’s movement, the fellowship of the wilds.

Su Shi also says: "The first nine emerges from within to fellowship with others; the top nine enters from without to fellowship with those below. Emerging from within is called the gate; entering from without is called the outskirts."

Tu Weng says: "Emerging from the gate—this is fellowship. Emerging from the gate is immediately in fellowship with the second, with no separation. Thus it says: 'Who would blame this?'—meaning, who would cause them not to achieve fellowship? This is the fortunate phrase of the first line. The fifth is different: 'In the heart, they are in fellowship—how could I forget it?' This refers to the second and fifth. There is no room for not being in fellowship—how could there be regret? If there were regret, the nine five would certainly not raise great armies to brave the multitude and seek encounter."

Thus, Su Shi says: "The Way of the noble person may involve going forth or staying, silence or speech. When two hearts are as one, their power can sever metal. The words of shared hearts have the fragrance of orchids." From this, how could one slight the nine five for using armies? Though the third and fourth are strong and cannot seize it, beginning with wailing and weeping and ending in laughter, even to the point of armies overcoming one another—they cannot break their fellowship. This reveals the sincerity of the second and fifth’s fellowship.

The second is yin; the fifth is yang. Yin and yang differ yet become *Fellowship*—thus we know their fellowship is certain. The noble person may differ in going forth, staying, speaking, or silence, yet become *Fellowship*—thus we know their fellowship is certain. If it is certain, then even the hardest and strongest things cannot intervene. Thus it says: "Their power can sever metal."

Tu Weng says: "Li Bai followed Prince Yong of Lin and was sentenced to death. Guo Ziyi offered his rank and titles to ransom him. Later, Bai was again imprisoned in Xunyang."

Song Ruosi deployed three thousand troops to repel and reclaim him. Alas! In an age devoid of like-minded companions, where may the great worthies and noble persons entrust their lives? This is why, in the *Fellowship* of the wilds, the sage does not take it as the model.

Xiong Guo says: *Fellowship* and *Great Possession* are both hexagrams of five yang lines with one yin. In *Great Possession*, the ruling yin resides in the fifth line, hence it is said to be "supremely prosperous." In *Fellowship*, the yin resides in the second line, yet the yang lines honor it as their ancestor—thus it is called "prosperous" and "the steadfastness of the noble person." *Ancestor* means "honored." As the *Book of Songs* says: "The noble person honors it; though their aspirations are not yet fulfilled, they unite the aspirations of all under Heaven." This is the reversal and rectification.

Jiang Tingshan says: "Lying in ambush in the thickets, awaiting the fifth line’s vulnerability, ascending the high mound to observe the second line’s movements—yet after three years, they do not rise, for the fifth line ultimately cannot be disrespected."

---
**Qian below, Li above**
**Great Possession: Supreme prosperity.**

*The Judgment* says: *Great Possession*—the yielding attains the honored position, great and central, with all below responding to it, hence "Great Possession." Its virtue is firm and strong, radiant and bright, responding to Heaven and acting in accord with the seasons—thus it is "supremely prosperous."

*The Image* says: Fire above Heaven is *Great Possession*. The noble person thereby suppresses evil and extols good, following Heaven’s gracious mandate.

**First Nine:** At the outset, exchanges bring harm, yet no blame. In hardship, there is no blame.
*The Image* says: In *Great Possession’s* first nine, exchanges at the outset bring harm.

**Nine in the Second:** With a great wagon for bearing, there is somewhere to go—no blame.
*The Image* says: With a great wagon for bearing, the accumulation within does not fail.

**Nine in the Third:** The duke offers sacrifice to the Son of Heaven; the petty man cannot.
*The Image* says: The duke offers sacrifice to the Son of Heaven—this is harmful for the petty man.

**Nine in the Fourth:** Not in its fullness—no blame.
*The Image* says: Not in its fullness—no blame, for it is bright, discerning, and wise.

**Six in the Fifth:** Its sincerity is interwoven, its majesty is manifest—good fortune.
*The Image* says: Its sincerity is interwoven, for trust gives rise to aspiration. The good fortune of manifest majesty lies in ease without excess.

**Top Nine:** From Heaven comes aid—good fortune, nothing not beneficial.
*The Image* says: The good fortune of *Great Possession’s* top line comes from Heaven’s aid.

---
**Fire over Heaven: Great Possession**

Fang Shihua says: Su Shi observed that all great things are seen as possessed by the fifth line, hence "Great Possession." In the age of *Great Possession*, all below respond to the fifth, serving with interwoven sincerity and manifest majesty. The fifth lord, in turn, responds to Heaven and acts in accord with the seasons to enjoy *Great Possession*, receiving Heaven’s aid—good fortune, nothing not beneficial. For one born into such times, navigating the world is already fraught, and the web of connections ever widens—truly, it is arduous. One must not hastily assume it is easy.

The first nine, at the lowest position, is still in the initial stage of exchange. Yet exchange begins here. Initial exchange thus brings initial harm—one knows that exchange inevitably entails harm. Therefore, it is taught the way to avoid blame: avoiding blame lies solely in overcoming hardship. If one can overcome hardship, then even daily engagement with the world—what harm could there be? Thus, the second nine, with a great wagon, does not fail. Not failing is to avoid harm.

The third nine, offering sacrifice to the Son of Heaven, may be a duke. As a duke offering sacrifice, there is no harm. The fourth nine, not in its fullness, dwells in abundance yet knows fear; knowing fear, there is again no harm. Thus, harm lies in the person. Only when one indulges in *Great Possession* without knowing hardship to govern it does one gradually fall into harm—what has this to do with exchange?

Now observe the third nine: occupying the top of the lower trigram, it is the duke’s position. With firmness in the correct place, it is fit to share the Son of Heaven’s great fortune in *Great Possession*. For it deeply knows hardship and can be cautious from the outset—like the Grand Duke of Qi. But the likes of Han Xin and Peng Yue brought harm upon themselves and could not endure, for they knew not hardship.

The fourth nine is perilously close to the fifth six, at the height of abundance. Yet as yang dwelling in yin, it alone can exhaust all it possesses, making it the lord’s possession while daring not claim the slightest for itself—hence "not in its fullness," for it is bright, discerning, and wise. Only with true wisdom surpassing others can one deeply know hardship and be so cautious to the end—like Tao Zhugong and Zhang Zifang.

From this, I observe: in the age of *Great Possession*, the lord is yielding and central, the minister overcomes hardship; the lord has interwoven sincerity, the minister’s initial exchanges bring no harm—only then can they together enjoy the fortune of *Great Possession*. Otherwise, misfortune and blame will come. Thus, the lord’s increasing yielding and the minister’s increasing hardship are the sage’s subtle intent.

Wang Benhe says: The top nine, "from Heaven comes aid," is because the fifth six responds to Heaven and acts in accord with the seasons to attain supreme prosperity.

---
**Appendix**
Cheng Zhengshu says: "The duke offers sacrifice to the Son of Heaven"—if a petty man occupies this position, it becomes harmful. Since ancient times, feudal lords who could loyally and obediently serve their superiors nurtured their people as shields and bulwarks for the king, enriching their resources to await the ruler’s levies. If a petty man occupies this position, with a numerous people and abundant wealth, they will instead arrogate their prosperity and strength, becoming ever more disobedient—thus, *Great Possession* becomes harmful for the petty man. Moreover, *Great Possession* is the petty man’s harm.

Zhang Youyu says: The *Book of Songs* says: "The messengers are vigorous, the chariots are vigorous, the teams of four are vigorous, the four steeds are vigorous"—all conveying the sense of flourishing strength.

Yang Tingshu says: The fifth six is the lord of *Great Possession*.

With clarity yet veiling it, holding it with the emptiness of yin at the center, and entrusting it solely to softness and sincerity—thus can one stir the aspirations of those below. The spirits submit to the hearts of those below; when those below are stirred, the sincerity between ruler and minister becomes firm without need for covenant. When the spirits of those below submit, the majesty of the Dao and virtue harmonizes without need for severity.

Sincerity stirs aspiration: with my own sincerity, I stir their sincerity. Gentleness without excess: with my own harmony and ease, I penetrate their defenses. Wang Fusi says: Dwelling in honor with softness, abiding in greatness with centeredness, without selfishness toward things—above and below respond to this. Sincerity stirs aspiration, thus their sincerity intermingles like flowing water. Without selfishness toward things, things too become impartial; without doubt toward things, things too become sincere. Being both impartial and sincere, what difficulty or defense remains? Teaching spreads without words, and majesty arises without effort. If one were to be the lord of *Great Possession* yet not attain good fortune through this Dao, how could it be possible?

Su Shi’s explanation says: The fifth six dwells amid the abundance of firmness yet alone employs softness—this is the utmost lack of defense. Precisely because of this lack of defense, things trust it, and thus those who return to it intermingle like flowing water. How can this softness yet possess majesty? Because it lacks excess, knowing it has abundance. Defense arises from insufficiency; when the form of insufficiency appears outwardly, majesty wanes. It is also said: What Heaven assists is compliance; what people assist is trust. Treading trust and contemplating compliance, and moreover esteeming worthiness—thus is one aided by Heaven, and good fortune, with nothing not beneficial. Trust, compliance, and esteeming worthiness—these are the virtues of the fifth six. Gentleness without excess—this is the compliance of the fifth six. Its sincerity intermingling like flowing water—this is the trust of the fifth six. The masses return to it—this is the esteeming of worthiness of the fifth six. The top nine uniquely treads this: what you tread is both compliant and trustworthy, and moreover esteems worthiness—then to whom will the aid of Heaven and humanity turn? Thus it is said: The sage has no merit, the spirit has no name—and the top nine of *Great Possession* does not reveal the source of its blessings.

Liu Yongxiang says: The fifth six alone fully expounds the virtue by which the fifth six attains *Great Possession*. The top nine alone fully expounds the good fortune by which the fifth six attains *Great Possession*. With both fortune and virtue complete, this is *Great Possession*. The fifth six and the top nine unite as one. Thus, the top nine—it is Heaven.

**Below and Earth above: Humility.** *Humility*: Flourishing. The noble person brings things to completion.

*The Judgment* says: *Humility*—flourishing. The Dao of Heaven descends and brings illumination; the Dao of Earth is lowly yet ascends. The Dao of Heaven diminishes the full and augments the humble; the Dao of Earth alters the full and flows to the humble. Ghosts and spirits harm the full and bless the humble; the Dao of humanity detests the full and loves the humble. Humility is honored and radiant, lowly yet unblemished—this is the noble person’s completion.

*The Image* says: Within Earth there is a mountain—*Humility*. The noble person reduces the many to benefit the few, weighing things to bestow evenly.

**First six:** The humbly humble noble person—crossing the great river brings good fortune.
*The Image* says: The humbly humble noble person—using lowliness to cultivate the self.

**Second six:** Resonant humility—constancy brings good fortune.
*The Image* says: Resonant humility—constancy brings good fortune, for the center is attained.

**Third nine:** The toiling and humble noble person—bringing things to completion brings good fortune.
*The Image* says: The toiling and humble noble person—all the people submit.

**Fourth six:** Without excess, vigorously practicing humility.
*The Image* says: Without excess, vigorously practicing humility—this is not transgressing the norm.

**Fifth six:** Not wealthy, yet with one’s neighbors—beneficial to employ in punitive expeditions, without excess.
*The Image* says: Beneficial to employ in punitive expeditions—punishing the disobedient.

**Top six:** Resonant humility—beneficial to employ in military campaigns, punishing city-states.
*The Image* says: Resonant humility—the aspiration is not yet attained. It is permissible to employ in military campaigns, punishing city-states.

**Earth above Mountain: Humility.** *Humility*—flourishing. The noble person brings things to completion.

It has already spoken of flourishing—why then speak of the noble person’s completion? Completion contrasts with beginning. That *humility* must flourish is beyond doubt; *The Judgment* has already expounded this in detail. Yet generally, for the noble person who is modest and yielding, there may be times at the beginning when flourishing is not assured, but in the end, flourishing is beyond doubt. The sage fears that seeing the lack of flourishing at the beginning, one might become uneasy with humility, and thus emphasizes completion.

King Wen’s humility, surpassing even that of Taibo and King Wen—when Taibo thrice yielded the world to Jili, cutting his hair, tattooing his body, and fleeing to the barbarians of Jing, this was distress without flourishing, reaching the limits of what human sentiment could endure. Yet in the end, he gloriously possessed the state of Wu—this was Taibo’s noble completion. King Wen, yielding two-thirds of the world to Shang, could also be called diminishing the self to give to others, like King Tai’s affair with the Di. What flourishing was there? Yet in the end, he established the eight-hundred-year Zhou—this was King Wen’s noble completion. Thus the Master judged these two sages as possessing the highest virtue. This hexagram further clarifies that *humility* must flourish, and the intent is evident.

Thus it is said: If you do not boast, none in the world will contend with you in ability. If you do not vaunt, none in the world will contend with you in merit. Was it only after the four hundred years of the Xia that Yu’s humility, truly without contenders in merit and ability, became apparent? Kongzi and Yan Hui, humbly humble and untried, yet in the end became the teachers of the world for ten thousand generations—this is the greatest and most manifest of the noble person’s completion.

The hexagram speaks of *humility* flourishing and again speaks of completion—how profound! The hexagram speaks of the noble person’s completion, yet the line alone speaks of the noble person’s completion bringing good fortune—how could there be no intent? For the third nine is the lord of the hexagram—the toiling and humble one.

The rest merely echo humility or respond in humility. Alas! To toil without boasting, to achieve merit without claiming virtue—this is the utmost of generosity. One whose virtue is profound casts a deep radiance; the giver will surely reap its recompense. That the noble person’s completion brings good fortune—why should there be any doubt regarding the third nine? **Appendix:**

Xiong Guo says: The lower position is called "humble-humble."
Fang Shihua says: Lowliness is not humility; it is through lowliness that humility is revealed. Hence it is said: "Use lowliness to cultivate the self." The character *mu* (牧, "to cultivate") in *Shuowen* is composed of *zhi* (支), which is interchangeable with *pu* (扑, "to strike"), and *niu* (牛, "ox"), meaning "one who tends oxen." The ox is a massive creature, symbolizing human strength and yang; when it is tamed and submissive, there is the Way of cultivation. Su Shi explains: When the male crows, the female responds, so the *Yi* uses the interplay of yin and yang to convey the resonance of humility. The essence of humility lies in the third line. *Gen*’s control rests in the third, and the third humbly places *Gen* below *Kun*—its humility is extreme, hence it is called "toiling humility." The second six is its neighbor, the upper six its mate, so both harmonize with it and echo humility. Moreover, the second six, being yin and positioned in the center of the inner trigram, is thus called "steadfast and auspicious." *Wu* (呜, "to echo") describes their harmony; *zhen* (贞, "steadfast") in the third shows that this arises from nature, not compulsion.

Jiao Ruohou says: *Dao* (捣, "to pound") means to break apart. It is composed of *shou* (手, "hand") and *gao* (高, "high") as phonetic, and is interchangeable with *hui* (麾, "to wave"). In the *Book of Songs*, it appears as "waving with the arm," and in the *Book of Documents* as "holding a white banner to wave"—it is also written as *dao*. Yang Jian says: Six yin lines, four yin lines, the body of *Kun* being yin, and not centered—this presents the image of excessive humility. Thus the sage teaches to "pound away" this humility, yet fearing doubt, adds "nothing is not advantageous" to *pounding humility*. It says: "If it does not transgress principle," meaning that even when pounding away humility, it does not reach the point of transgression.

Cheng Zhengshu says: To subdue through civil virtue—this is what humility and compliance cannot achieve. If civil virtue cannot subdue, yet one does not employ might and martial power, how can the world be governed? Here humility has gone too far. The Bald Elder says: *Kun*’s excess in humility may stem from fear of harm or disadvantage. Now, with *Kun*’s compliant hexagram and the fourth six’s line, yielding and compliance reach their utmost. Upholding this compliance, not daring to transgress principle—though it brings no disadvantage—if one adds humility, is this not too excessive? Excess invites contempt; this is not humility. Thus the upper three lines all instruct to "pound away" humility, making use of punitive expeditions. In sum, it is not about waging war over a neighbor’s wealth; if there are those who refuse to submit, how can one not strike? This is like Tang’s campaign against Ge. The upper six reaches the extreme of humility and compliance. If it still echoes in harmony, does the third nine’s humility not become excessive?
