Even within one’s own domain, can one truly have one’s way? This is like the Duke of Zhou’s eastern campaign. Thus do I recognize the sage’s skill in discoursing on humility. The inner three lines emphasize restraint, so their virtue is displayed; the outer three lines emphasize compliance, so their excess is guarded against. The sage’s way of gathering the abundant to supplement the deficient is like this.

Wang Ji says: Concealing the high within the low—this is the image of humility. The noble person takes from the surplus to benefit the deficient, so that the worthy and unworthy are equalized. By lowering one’s position before others, the noble and base are equalized. None of humility’s six lines are inauspicious. Humility means inner restraint and outer compliance. If one lacks inner restraint but maintains outer compliance, this becomes feigned reverence and affected dignity—something the noble person does not value.

**Hexagram 16: Yu (豫, "Delight")**
*Below Kun (坤, "The Receptive"), above Zhen (震, "The Arousing")*
**Yu:** Favorable for establishing lords and deploying armies.

**The Judgment:**
*Yu*: The firm finds resonance, and its will is carried out. Compliant yet active—this is *Yu*. *Yu* is compliant yet active, so heaven and earth follow its pattern—how could it not be favorable for establishing lords and deploying armies? Heaven and earth move in compliance, so the sun and moon do not err, and the four seasons do not falter. The sage moves in compliance, so punishments are clear and the people submit. The timeliness of *Yu*—how great it is!

**The Image:**
Thunder emerges from the earth, arousing—this is *Yu*. The former kings, in accordance with this, composed music to exalt virtue, offering it reverently to the Supreme Emperor and pairing it with ancestral spirits.

**Line Statements:**
**Initial Six:** Echoing delight—misfortune.
**The Image:** The initial six’s echoing delight—its will is exhausted, hence misfortune.

**Six in the Second:** Firm as stone, not a full day—steadfast and auspicious.
**The Image:** "Not a full day, steadfast and auspicious"—this is due to central correctness.

**Six in the Third:** Sighing in delight—regret. Delay brings regret.
**The Image:** "Sighing in delight brings regret"—its position is not appropriate.

**Nine in the Fourth:** Deriving delight—great attainment. Do not doubt. Friends gather like hairpins.
**The Image:** "Deriving delight, great attainment"—its will is greatly fulfilled.

**Six in the Fifth:** Steadfast in illness—constantly on the verge of death.
**The Image:** The fifth six’s steadfast illness—it rides the firm. "Constantly on the verge of death"—its center has not yet perished.

**Upper Six:** Dark delight—if it reaches completion, there will be change; no blame.
**The Image:** "Dark delight in the upper position"—how can this last long?

**Hexagram 16: Thunder over Earth (Yu)**
Fang Shihua says: Of *Yu*’s six lines, except for the fourth nine, which delights in the world and attains great success, the rest all err through delight. Only the second six, centered and correct, guards itself and refuses to follow others. Though it partakes in delight, it is "not a full day"—this is the nature of its steadfast integrity. Is there anything inauspicious here? The *Great Commentary*’s discussion of "knowing the incipient" precisely warns of *Yu*’s inevitable misfortune. Only the second six’s firm-as-stone resolve can foresee the incipient and thus avoid being submerged in delight. The third line’s position is inappropriate, the opposite of the second, so in its delight with the fourth, it looks up and desires to go. Having regretted its delay and hesitation in not going, it then regrets going—its fear is of the second six, its envy of the fourth nine. This is what is meant by "emerging to see splendor and magnificence, unable to suppress the conflict in one’s breast."

Alas! How few in the world possess the unyielding resolve of stone? Without such steadfast firmness as stone, one cannot consort with the nine in the fourth place—this much is clear from the *Yu* of *Great Possession*. For the nine in the fourth place is precisely that which is "firm in response yet its will enacted," moving in accord and thus the very source of the hexagram’s *Yu*. Both *Yu* and *Return* encounter five yin lines, hence both take the image of companions. Xiong Guo said: "Great Possession" means one yang gains five yin. "Companions gathered like hairpins" means five yin unite with one yang. The six in the fifth place, yin and soft yet not upright, moreover rides atop the firmness of the nine in the fourth—it knows only ease and delight as joy, unaware that power has shifted downward and authority has entirely departed. Is this not the true and incurable malady of rulers, like the Later Lord of Liu? Death—it has long since come. That it has not yet perished is merely because the name and title barely remain, and the center has not yet turned inauspicious—yet this is no better than death. "Center" refers to the central position. But how many ministers like the Loyal Martial Marquis are there? Without the Loyal Martial Marquis arising from *Yu*, those who ride firmness ride the firmness of Wang Mang, ride the firmness of Sima Yi and his like—can they long avoid death? As for the six in the top place, it is submerged in *Yu* and never alters its course—why? *Yu* has already taken form and cannot be changed. If what has formed could still be altered, what blame would there be? But to change early and only then alter what has already formed—this is too late. For one should alter early, hence it is said: "Submerged in *Yu* above—how can this last long?" Tu Fang said: The *Tuan* statement and *Tuan* commentary of *Yu* extol the utmost beauty of attaining *Yu*, yet the six lines of *Yu* and their *Xiang* commentaries all speak of the calamities of indulging in *Yu*. Does this mean *Yu* can be attained but not enjoyed? Not so. The ruin of states and the downfall of families, ceaseless in their succession, all stem from *Yu*. Thus, if not submerged in *Yu*, one dies by *Yu*. Alas, *Yu*—to sigh in *Yu* until one’s will is exhausted is already inauspicious. Without the six in the second place’s unyielding resolve, "not a full day," how could one preserve *Yu* to the end? This is precisely what it means to enjoy *Yu*—it is also the movement of compliance, the very source of attaining *Yu*. Is there any other way? Now, instead of reflecting on the source of attaining *Yu*, one merely enjoys the blessings of ease and delight—no wonder one is utterly lost in *Yu* and fortune turns to misfortune.

Let us set aside these strange cases for now. Today’s scholars, clinging to the sage’s words that "joy lies within," claim to know joy and forget sorrow, indulging their desires and wills, seeking only utmost pleasure. Is this truly the joy of the sage? How does this differ from sighing in *Yu* and ultimately perishing by *Yu*? The joy of the sage never departs from ardent effort. To speak of joy apart from ardent effort is to know nothing of joy. The sage’s forgetting of sorrow originates from the same place as forgetting hunger—if one is unwilling to forget hunger yet seeks only to forget sorrow, how can one not fail to forget both? Unaware of food, unaware of sorrow, unaware of old age, only lifelong ardent effort is known as joy—thus, one’s view of the world’s ease and delight is truly "not a full day." Therefore, those who study the Dao must be unyielding as stone—not only in *Yu*. This is called the divine insight of "knowing the incipient," the Way that penetrates above and below.

**Appendix**
Qiu Xingke said: *Tun* has *Zhen* below and *Kun* above, hence it speaks of establishing lords but not of deploying armies. *Modesty* has *Kun* below and *Zhen* above, hence it speaks of deploying armies but not of establishing lords. *Yu* combines *Zhen* and *Kun* to form its body, hence it speaks of both. Su Zizhan said: The source of *Yu* lies in the fourth line, and the first harmonizes with it, hence it is called "sighing." To rely on others for joy, trusting in one’s match for delight—loyalty is far indeed. Those who depend on others’ joy rejoice when others rejoice and grieve when others grieve, their will bound to dependence. When what they depend on is exhausted, they cannot but meet misfortune. Wang Ji said: Thunder rouses within the earth—this is the image of *Yu*, where the myriad things flourish and stretch. Of old, the former kings, following the harmony of heaven and earth, spread the qi of the eight winds, composed music to harmonize, and thereby aligned with the spirits and the people. From the heart it arose, through sound it moved, through posture it harmonized, through pitch it regulated, through tone it followed, and through instruments it was embodied. Thus, music is the flower of virtue, and composing music is to honor virtue. Hence, when music is complete, virtue is exalted. It is offered in the suburban and ancestral temples, sacrifices are made to heaven on the Circular Mound with the ancestor as counterpart—this is revering the revered. Sacrifices are made to the Thearch in the Bright Hall with the father as counterpart—this is honoring the honored. The Supreme Thearch is feasted, ancestors arrive, the myriad things are nurtured, and the world attains great harmony.

**Hexagram 17: Following**
*Following*: Supreme prosperity, beneficial to be upright, no blame.
**Tuan**: *Following*: The firm comes and does not yield to the soft, moves and delights—this is *Following*. Supreme prosperity, uprightness without blame, and the world follows the times. The meaning of "following the times" is great indeed!
**Xiang**: Thunder within the marsh—*Following*. The noble person, in response to dusk, enters to rest.
**First Nine**: Office changes—uprightness is auspicious. Going out to associate brings success.
**Xiang**: "Office changes"—following the upright is auspicious. "Going out to associate brings success"—nothing is lost.
**Six in the Second**: Binding to the young one, losing the great man.
**Xiang**: "Binding to the young one"—not both can be held.
**Six in the Third**: Binding to the great man, losing the young one. In *Following*, seeking brings gain—beneficial to dwell in uprightness.
**Xiang**: "Binding to the great man"—the will abandons the lower.
**Nine in the Fourth**: In *Following*, there is capture—uprightness is inauspicious. Sincerity on the path, with clarity—what blame is there?
**Xiang**: "In *Following*, there is capture"—its meaning is inauspicious. "Sincerity on the path"—clarity brings success.
**Nine in the Fifth**: Sincerity in excellence—auspicious.

**Xiang**: "Sincerity in excellence—auspicious," for the position is upright and central.

**Six at the Top**: Binding and restraining them, then further securing them. The king offers sacrifice on the Western Mountain.
**Xiang**: "Binding and restraining them"—the top is exhausted.

**Lake Thunder Following (Sui)**
With firm yang descending to dwell beneath two yin, it is firm coming down to the yielding. When firm descends to the yielding, how could the yielding not follow? Following is upright; not following is not upright. Who would not wish to follow uprightness? Moreover, the hexagram has *Zhen* (Thunder) moving and *Dui* (Lake) joyful—thus, movement with joy. Joy is following; lack of joy is not following. Is there a fixed way to follow? Can one be forced to follow? Hence it is said: "The meaning of following the times is great indeed!" Without a fixed time, there is no fixed following.

Here, the *First Nine* takes the single yang as the master of *Zhen* (Thunder), serving as the officer of *Following*. It alters the *Fourth* following the *Second*, refusing to cling to one fixed following—this is why it is "upright and auspicious." How much more so when abandoning the distant to draw near? "Going out to associate brings success"—why then abandon this to seek afar? The *Second* surpasses the *Third* to follow the *Fourth*, yet though following involves change and movement, the auspiciousness of "following the upright" is already attained from the outset. The *Second* also knows the *Third* to be a "young one," unfit to bind to, and the *First* to be a "great man," unfit to lose. Though no harm lies in holding both, how can the circumstances allow it? Thus, the *Six in the Second* would rather not lose this than bind its will to that. As for the *Third*, it now regards the *Nine in the Fourth* as a "great man," and so would rather bind to the great man and lose the young one.

At the time of *Following*, the will is set on capture, striving to gain. If one does not follow with the four virtues of *yuan* (origination), *heng* (prosperity), *li* (benefit), and *zhen* (uprightness), not only will there be no gain, but there will also be blame—how can one follow then? The *Fifth* is in the central and upright position, of the same body as the *Fourth*. It naturally possesses the sincere joy of *Dui* (Lake). The *Fourth*, as a minister following the ruler, even if following results in capture, need not be spoken of. Though it may seem improper for a minister to "capture" while the ruler speaks, who knows that the *Fourth* is truly sincere with the *Fifth*? That it truly shares the same Dao? Moreover, the *Fourth* clearly knows that following the *Fifth* can bring success. Thus, the *Fifth* surely wishes it to follow. Even if there is capture, what inauspiciousness could there be? Observe the times, examine oneself, and simply follow—that is all.

Only the *Six at the Top* acts improperly, unable to sincerely delight the world and make others joyfully follow. Instead, it binds and restrains them, further securing them to compel compliance—how could this succeed? The king, using excellent rites to draw near the state, moves and brings joy to the people. Even the spirits of the Western Mountain may be employed in sacrifice—how could they be like ministers? Why bind and restrain them? King Wen’s lament runs deep. This hexagram is entirely set forth for this line.

The *First Nine*, "going out to associate," immediately gains a companion of success, not waiting to lose one before gaining another—hence it is said, "nothing is lost." As for the *Second* and *Third*, binding to the young one must lose the great man, and binding to the great man must lose the young one—how can both be held at once? Only joy suffices. Only the four virtues of *yuan*, *heng*, *li*, and *zhen* suffice. To follow without these is to invite blame—better not to follow.

Fang Shihua said: *Following* takes the meaning of "following the times." Since the times have no fixed origin, the six lines have no fixed following—sometimes binding is possible, sometimes abandoning is possible—all follows the times.

**Appendix**
Jiao Gan said: The Han Emperor Gaozu and Xiang Yu are its clear proof.
Zhou Yan said: The lines take "following the times in movement," while the Great Image takes "following the times in rest." Movement and rest do not violate the times—this is why the meaning of "following the times" is great.

---
**Mountain Wind Decay (Gu)**
*Gu*: *Yuan heng*, beneficial to cross the great river. Three days before *jia*, three days after *jia*.
**Xiang**: *Gu*—firm above and yielding below, *Xun* (Wind) yielding and *Gen* (Mountain) stopping. *Gu*: *yuan heng*, and the world is ordered. "Beneficial to cross the great river"—proceeding has purpose. "Three days before *jia*, three days after *jia*"—ending then beginning, the way of heaven.
**Image**: Wind beneath the mountain—*Gu*. The noble one rouses the people and nurtures virtue.

**First Six**: Correcting the father’s decay—having a son, the father incurs no blame. Dangerous in the end, auspicious.
**Xiang**: "Correcting the father’s decay"—the intent is to succeed the father.

**Nine in the Second**: Correcting the mother’s decay—cannot be upright.
**Xiang**: "Correcting the mother’s decay"—attaining the central way.

**Nine in the Third**: Correcting the father’s decay—slight regret, no great blame.
**Xiang**: "Correcting the father’s decay"—in the end, no blame.

**Six in the Fourth**: Indulging the father’s decay—proceeding brings remorse.
**Xiang**: "Indulging the father’s decay"—proceeding gains nothing.

**Six in the Fifth**: Correcting the father’s decay—using praise.
**Xiang**: "Correcting the father’s decay using praise"—succeeding with virtue.

**Top Nine**: Not serving kings and lords, holding one’s affairs in high esteem.
**Xiang**: "Not serving kings and lords"—the will is worthy of emulation.

**Mountain Wind Decay (Gu)**
*Gu*: firm above, yielding below. Above and below do not communicate; below yields, above stops—neither above nor below acts. Alas, this is why decay is complete. Only when there is no communication does decay form—thus, one knows that its initial order must have been great communication. Hence, "ending then beginning"—is *yuan heng* not fitting? Only when there is no action does decay form—thus, one knows that its initial order must have involved action. Hence, "ending then beginning"—is crossing the river not beneficial? Therefore it is said: *Gu*: *yuan heng*, beneficial to cross the great river. Three days before *jia*, three days after *jia*. Three days before *jia*: *zi*, *xu*, *shen*. Three days after *jia*: *wu*, *chen*, *yin*. Since there is a "before *jia*," there must naturally be an "after *jia*."

Having "after *jia*," there naturally follows "before *jia*" once more. After reverting to before, ending reverts to beginning—such is the movement of Heaven. Thus, how could *yuan* ever decay? Should those who govern *gu* not act likewise?

If one does not rest complacent in *gu*, one will naturally understand its origins, and *gu* may be governed. There is no other technique, no other *jia*. Yet if there is no other Dao, then this is how the sage governs *gu*.

Now, governing *gu* in the world is the same as governing *gu* in a single household. Thus, the six lines focus solely on the work of rectifying *gu*. Though what they rectify differs, none take "being able to rectify" as their task, nor "willingness to rectify" as virtue. One cannot be leisurely or indulgent in accomplishing the rectification of one’s parents’ *gu*.

Thus, to the point of endangering oneself in rectifying one’s father—though perilous, one ultimately attains good fortune. Even if rectifying one’s father leads to regret, the heart remains ultimately blameless. Thus, one who can rectify *gu* must be a filial son, without doubt.

Alas! Ruler and parent are as one household; there is no division between state and family. One who can rectify the household can rectify the state; one who can rectify one’s father can rectify one’s ruler. Yet世人 often hasten to mend their household’s *gu* while treating their state as a mere lodging. This is because there are ministers who do not serve kings and lords, who may claim lofty ideals and refuse to engage with the world. But there are no sons who do not serve their parents—thus, they cannot but inherit their fathers’ legacy and rectify *gu*.

That one does not serve kings and lords may still be spoken of. Yet how many in the world truly do not serve kings and lords? Now that all are ministers to a single king and lord, can one still claim lofty ideals and refuse to take rectifying *gu* as one’s duty?

Thus, the sage speaks solely of the *gu* of parents to make it deeply personal and inescapable for all. The task is the work of rectifying *gu*—if one claims lofty ideals, one naturally does not act. Hence, it is said: "loftily esteeming one’s task." The top line, *nine at the top*, is at the summit of the hexagram, hence "lofty." It also draws from the height of *Gen* Mountain, hence "loftily esteeming."

The duty of a child to a parent is something the heart cannot shirk; the duty of a minister to a ruler is something that cannot be evaded between Heaven and Earth. Yet to be named for "loftily esteeming" and not serving kings and lords is to be one who rebels against righteousness, disrupts constancy, and inverts the proper order. The sage merely takes what can be emulated.

Those who covet such a reputation say: "The aspiration may be imitated, but the words—can they be imitated?" If the aspiration is truly imitable, then who will attend to the affairs of ruler and parent? Alas! A minister who does not serve his ruler has no ruler; a child who does not serve his parent has no parent—this is the path to great chaos. What does the sage take from this?

Fang Shihua said: The first line receives the yang below the father; the second line holds yin, representing the later mother. The third line again rides yang, representing the later father; the fourth line again rides yang, while the fifth line once more receives the yang above—thus, all share the image of the father.

Rectifying the mother’s *gu* cannot be done through steadfastness. To say it cannot be done through correctness is to grasp the mean in rectifying *gu*. The *nine in the second place* cannot be steadfast in its rectification of *gu*, for its approach differs from the first and third lines. As for *six in the fifth place*, it not only does not rectify its father through correctness but also employs praise to gently persuade him—this is the very Dao that leads to *gu*.

This is what is meant by "receiving with virtue," what is called "following the highest." The subtlety of imperceptible transformation—even the father may not realize it. This is what it means to be able to rectify *gu*, not to indulge it.

**Appendix**
Su Zizhan said: A vessel long unused breeds insects—this is called *gu*. A person long indulgent breeds illness—this is called *gu*. To take the world as having no affairs and refuse to act is to later be overwhelmed by affairs. This is why *gu* becomes a matter of concern. Vessels should be used constantly, the body should be labored constantly, and the world should always be engaged in affairs. The disaster of *gu* does not arise in a single day.

Human nature has no greater fear than hardship. Once the world is governed, to still make "crossing the river" one’s task is to dread its decay! Shi Baihuo said: The days follow the six *jia*, ending and beginning again like a ring without end—such is the movement of Heaven, the reason the workings of creation never decay.

Shao Guoxian said: To govern *gu* is called *gu*, just as to govern disorder is called disorder, to govern desolation is called desolation.

Zixia said: Facing affairs with dedication sharpens the will, thus avoiding blame—this is the meaning of "having affairs."

Zheng Kangcheng said: A son altering his father’s way may begin perilously but end auspiciously, for though the act may not be smooth, the intent is.

"Indulging the father’s *gu*"—proceeding brings no gain.

Cai Jiefu said: Why say "there is no *gu* like *gu*"?

Liu Yongxiang said: "Indulging the father’s *gu*" is the inability to rectify *gu*. The lines take "being able to rectify" as their task, though what they rectify differs. Yet the emphasis on the mother’s "cannot be steadfast" and the father’s "employing praise" also has its Dao. Why? The affairs of parents must not be ignored. The minister’s duty to the ruler is likewise.

Jiao Ruohou said: The fifth line of *Gu* all speak of "rectifying the father’s *gu*." As for the top line, it states "not serving kings and lords." At the end of the hexagram, speaking of one family, this refers to the days when parents are in their dotage, and one does not go out to take office. When Master Zeng resided in Lu, Qi sought to appoint him as a minister. Master Zeng said: "Living with aged parents, I cannot bear to be far from my kin. To be made another’s servant—can this be my will?" Wang Ji said: *Gen*’s firmness resides above, *Xun*’s softness below; the upper is haughty and cannot descend, while the lower is base and cannot ascend to meet it. Stopping yet yielding—this is how *gu* is formed. The noble person has a Dao for governing *gu*. The *gu* of the people’s hearts arises from idleness, decay, and neglect, leaving them unaware of how to rouse themselves. "Rousing" means to stir and inspire. Thus it is said: "Renew the people." This is not merely about setting rules or issuing commands; it is about addressing the root. It is like a skilled physician treating illness: "rousing" is to expel external pathogens, while "nurturing" is to cultivate the original *qi*. Wind speaks of "rousing," mountain speaks of "nurturing." Zhou Yan said: "Rousing the people" is to awaken their base and yielding ailments. "Nurturing virtue" is to transform their careless stagnation. *The Yi’s* Upper Canon, Scroll Two, concludes here.

---

**Hexagram 19: Lin (Approach)**

*Lin*: Originating, prosperous, beneficial, and correct. As for the eighth month, there will be misfortune.

**The Judgment** says: *Lin*—firmness gradually grows. Joyful and compliant, firmness centered and responsive—great prosperity through correctness is the Dao of Heaven. As for the eighth month and misfortune, decline will not be long in coming.

**The Image** says: Above the marsh is earth—*Lin*. The noble person, in teaching, thinks without limit and in nurturing, protects the people without bounds.

**Line 1 (Nine in the first place):** Mutual approach—correct and auspicious.
**The Image** says: Mutual approach—correct and auspicious, for the will is rightly enacted.

**Line 2 (Nine in the second place):** Mutual approach—auspicious, with nothing unbeneficial.
**The Image** says: Mutual approach—auspicious with nothing unbeneficial, for it does not yet follow commands.

**Line 3 (Six in the third place):** Sweet approach—nothing beneficial. If one worries over it, there will be no blame.
**The Image** says: Sweet approach—its position is not proper. If one worries over it, blame will not last.

**Line 4 (Six in the fourth place):** Utmost approach—no blame.
**The Image** says: Utmost approach—no blame, for its position is proper.

**Line 5 (Six in the fifth place):** Knowing approach—fitting for the great lord, auspicious.
**The Image** says: Fitting for the great lord—this means acting from the center.

**Line 6 (Six at the top):** Sincere approach—auspicious, no blame.
**The Image** says: The auspiciousness of sincere approach lies in the will’s inward focus.

---

The hexagram *Earth over Marsh: Lin* depicts two yang lines approaching four yin lines. The four words—originating, prosperous, beneficial, and correct—solely praise the growth of the two yang. The six words "as for the eighth month, there will be misfortune" then consider the decline of the two yang. To praise their growth yet immediately fear their decline, to extol them without end yet follow with dread of misfortune—how earnest is the sage’s heart in upholding yang! Thus the hexagram’s words and the *Judgment* speak of *Lin* in this way.

**Line 1 (Nine in the first place):** "Mutual approach" refers to approaching the fourth line. The first delights in the fourth’s compliance, approaching it through resonance. The fourth, being utmost in its approach to the marsh, and the first, as its minister, with one yang line approaching, does so only through the Dao of resonance and response. Thus it is called "mutual approach." Though the first is yang, it approaches with correctness; though the fourth is yin, it responds with rectitude. Is it not fitting that "mutual approach" is correct and auspicious?

**Line 2 (Nine in the second place):** Not only is it joyful and compliant, but it is also firm, centered, and responsive. Thus it is also called "mutual approach," also "auspicious," and further "nothing unbeneficial." The one it responds to is the fifth line, the knowing approach of the great lord—how could it compare to the fourth line? Yet though it is said to be "nothing unbeneficial," the *Judgment* reverses this and says it "does not yet follow commands." Why? The fifth line, dwelling in the center of *Kun*’s compliance, only knows to listen and obey the second. Thus, for the second, it is "nothing unbeneficial." The second, approaching with yang virtue, does not necessarily follow every command of the fifth. Thus, for the fifth, it is fitting for the great lord.

Yet if the fifth merely receives in compliance while the second responds from the center, were it only a matter of following commands, they would not be two distinct matters. How could this bring great illumination to the fifth? The fifth also knows that the second’s centeredness cannot be momentarily abandoned, and to listen and obey exclusively with wisdom while occupying the honored position—this is no empty deference. Only thus can it enable the noble one to grow daily, which mere compliance cannot. This is what is meant by “Shun was of great wisdom.” Shun applied his centeredness to the people, and his wisdom thereby became great. If the fifth line enacts its centeredness toward the second, how could it not be fitting for the great lord?

The sixth line in the third place is neither centered nor correct, positioned at the mouth of *Dui*. It knows only a single flavor of sweet compliance. If it seeks compliance from above yet above does not comply, what use is its sweetness? Sweetness turns to worry, sweetness reverts to bitterness—this is the inevitable course of events. This is why its worry incurs no blame. Though it was originally said that sweetness has “nothing for which it is beneficial,” it is in fact because the two yang lines approach so closely to the sixth in the third place that it unknowingly generates anxiety. Above, it knows only *dun* approaching. From its position above the earth, seeing the fifth as the one who knows approach, the second also has this intention. Within, the second sees the fourth’s arrival as approach. The first also has this intention. Within the first, the two yang lines’ approach to this place becomes even more earnest—hence it is also called *dun lin*.

The *lin* hexagram’s “originating, penetrating, beneficial, and correct” do not derive from this; rather, this is called “great penetration through correctness, the Way of Heaven.” **Appendix:** Yang Jian says: “As for ‘in the eighth month there is misfortune,’ this refers to the month when the two yin lines grow.” *Lin* is the growth of two yang lines; *gu* is the growth of two yin lines. Every hexagram’s change spans seven, hence *Fu* says, “After seven days, it returns.” Now *Lin* speaks of the eighth month because, counting from the beginning of the first yang, *Fu*, *Lin*, *Tai*, *Dazhuang*, *Guai*, *Qian*, *Gou*, and *Dun* make eight. Yin is called “month,” yang “day.” Xiong Guo says: “‘Arriving at *lin*’ refers to the interchange of *Kun* and *Dui*, earth and marsh approaching one another.” *Dun*, according to the *Erya*, means “like an overturned *dun* vessel”; in the Jiangdong region, a high mound of earth is called *dun*. The upper line resides at the end of two earth lines, resembling a high place. *Dun* has seven meanings.

Wang Ji says: “Nothing approaches as earth approaches water, hence marsh above earth is *lin*.” Earth restrains water, which seems effortless. Yet if pressed by peril and constriction, it bursts forth in all directions, breaking only when exhausted. If treated with generosity, it gathers into a marsh. The noble one’s relation to the people is likewise. The people of the Three Dynasties never forgot the inexhaustible beneficence of the former kings’ teachings. Marsh is the image of storing things without limit; earth is the image of containing things.

**Hexagram 20: Guan (Observation)**
*Xun* below *Kun* above
*Guan*: Washing hands but not yet presenting the offering—with sincerity, it is manifestly thus.

**Judgment:**
Great observation is above. Compliant and yielding, centered and correct, it observes the world. *Guan*: washing hands but not yet presenting the offering—with sincerity, it is manifestly thus. Below, they observe and are transformed. Observing the divine Way of Heaven, the four seasons do not deviate. The sage establishes teaching through the divine Way, and the world submits.

**Image:**
Wind moves over the earth—*Guan*. The former kings toured the provinces, observed the people, and established teaching.

**Line Statements:**
**Line 1 (Initial Six):** Childish observation. For the petty person, no blame; for the noble one, regret.
**Image:** The initial six’s childish observation is the way of the petty person.

**Line 2 (Six in the second place):** Peeping observation. Beneficial for a woman’s correctness.
**Image:** Peeping observation for a woman’s correctness—this is also shameful.

**Line 3 (Six in the third place):** Observing my life, advancing and retreating.
**Image:** Observing my life, advancing and retreating—this does not lose the Way.

**Line 4 (Six in the fourth place):** Observing the state’s splendor. Beneficial to be a guest of the king.
**Image:** Observing the state’s splendor—esteeming the guest.

**Line 5 (Nine in the fifth place):** Observing my life. The noble one incurs no blame.
**Image:** Observing my life—observing the people.

**Line 6 (Top Nine):** Observing their life. The noble one incurs no blame.
**Image:** Observing their life—the aspiration is not yet at peace.

**Hexagram 20: Wind over Earth, Observation**
This hexagram has four yin lines below, symbolizing ministers and the people—those who observe from below. Two yang lines above symbolize the noble one—those who observe the world. Only by having the means to observe the world can one become the world’s observation. Yet though the two yang lines above both constitute “great observation above” and both rest centered and correct with *Kun* bearing *Xun*, only the nine in the fifth place can observe with centered correctness. This is why it is named *Guan*.

The Master says: In observation, sound and scent are both absent—it is just like Heaven. What does Heaven say? Yet the four seasons proceed. The observer, considering himself divine, what teaching can be established? What transformation can be achieved? No teaching can be established, hence “establishing teaching through the divine Way”; no transformation can be achieved, hence “through the divine Way, the world submits.”

Now observe the observer: just as he washes his hands before the guest arrives, before the offering is presented, those below observe and are transformed by the manifest sincerity, as if already solemnly facing a great guest. Thus the nine in the fifth place is truly the divine Way. To observe the nine in the fifth is truly to observe Heaven. Yet those below who observe the fifth do so as Heaven. What the fifth observes is the self. Those below who observe the fifth do so as the divine; what the fifth observes in the self is the people. My life, the people’s life—there is no distinction. This is called the world’s peace. The people, unknowing and unperceiving, and the fifth, without sound or scent, naturally communicate without words, and those below observe and are transformed.

Yet those who observe and those who are observed do not transform uniformly. Without uniformity, each follows their own depth and cannot be the same. Thus the six in the fourth place, as the ruler of *Xun*, draws near to the nine in the fifth and advances to observe the state’s splendor, becoming a beneficial guest of the king.

Herein lies the observation that effects transformation—is this not what is meant by "those below observe and are transformed"? The nine in the third place, too, can observe my life, yet it does not rashly advance to behold the state’s splendor, for the third has not lost the Dao. Those below it, however, know only to observe the fifth, gazing upon mere appearances without perceiving the truth, seeing only the small without grasping the great—how then could they comprehend the noble one’s freedom from blame? Though they may not yet speak of transformation, one cannot say their hearts do not submit to the fifth. Thus, only by combining the observations of the four yin lines can one recognize the nine in the fifth as the true spirit of the Dao, not easily fathomed or known.

As for the nine in the top place, its aspirations remain unsettled, and "my life" persists as a distinction—this is not the great observation of the Dao. Though it, too, is a noble one free from blame, it is far from the centered rectitude of the nine in the fifth. Therefore, only the six in the fourth place can serve as the master of transformation, and only the nine in the fifth can serve as the master of observation.

**Appendix**
Su Shi’s explanation: When the great vessel moves, the people follow; without words, things are understood—this is the Dao of observation. When the sage governs through the spirit of the Dao, rewards, ranks, punishments, and penalties exist yet are unused.

Xiong Guo’s note: Zheng Xuan said: The feudal lords present scholars to the Son of Heaven, and ministers present scholars to their lord, honoring them as guests with ritual. Only the host washes and offers drink to the guest, and the guest washes and toasts the host, while disciples arrange the offerings. Now, in the six lines of *Guan*, all lack the meaning of sacrifice, revealing only the text of observing the state and serving as the king’s guest.

The *Shuowen* states: The six in the second place, yin and yielding within, though corresponding with the fifth, is separated by the third and fourth—like a woman within the gate peering outward.

Wu Youqing said: The first line, yang in position, resembles a young boy. The second, yin in position, resembles a girl. The third refers to itself as "I." The great virtue of Heaven and Earth is the daily generation of life. The six in the third place stands at the top of the lower trigram, near *Xun*. *Xun* signifies advance and retreat, so the six in the third observes "my life" to examine its learning, like the saying, "I have not yet proven this."

Yang Jian said: Zixia excelled in discussing subtleties, and after Confucius’ death, believing a lord resembled the sage, he sought to serve him as he had served Confucius, pressuring Zengzi. Only Zengzi could not be swayed—only he could avoid the shame of reckless observation. It is also said: The six in the third place stands at the limit of the lower trigram, poised to ascend to the upper trigram—this is the moment of advance and retreat. With the six in the third’s symbol of advance and retreat, the six in the fourth advances to observe the state’s splendor.

Wang Jibai: Wind moves over the earth, touching all things—this is the image of comprehensive observation. The former kings, wishing to influence the world, established the institution of imperial tours. Whether conducting annual inspections or quinquennial assemblies, they harmonized seasons and months, standardized pitch pipes, measures, weights, and balances, commanded grand masters to present poetry to observe the people’s customs, and examined market prices to discern their likes and dislikes. Investigating local practices, they established teachings to instruct where differences arose: using ritual to distinguish extravagance (as in Cao) and exemplifying frugality (as in Wei), or demonstrating propriety to unify morality and harmonize customs.

☰ below ☲ above
*Shihe*: Smooth and prosperous.

**Favorable for administering legal judgments.**

*The Image* says: Within the jaws lies an object—this is *Shihe* (Biting Through). Biting through brings prosperity. Hard and soft are divided; movement brings clarity. Thunder and lightning unite to manifest brilliance. The yielding attains the center and ascends; though not in its proper position, it is favorable for legal judgments.

*The Image* says: Thunder and lightning in *Shihe*—the former kings thereby clarified punishments and enforced laws.

**First Nine:** Shackles cover the toes, yet no blame.
*The Image* says: Shackles cover the toes—movement is restrained.

**Six in the Second:** Biting through soft flesh, the nose is lost, yet no blame.
*The Image* says: Biting through soft flesh, the nose is lost—this is riding upon the firm.

**Six in the Third:** Biting through dried meat, one encounters poison; a minor regret, yet no blame.
*The Image* says: Encountering poison—this is due to an improper position.

**Nine in the Fourth:** Biting through dried lungs, one obtains a metal arrow. Favorable to persist through hardship—auspicious.
*The Image* says: Favorable to persist through hardship—auspicious, yet its brilliance is not yet fully realized.

**Six in the Fifth:** Biting through dried meat, one obtains yellow gold. Perseverance brings danger, yet no blame.
*The Image* says: Perseverance brings danger yet no blame—this is due to proper attainment.

**Top Nine:** Carrying shackles that cover the ears—misfortune.
*The Image* says: Carrying shackles that cover the ears—hearing is no longer clear.

**Fire below, Thunder above: *Shihe***

Cheng Zhengshu says: *Shi* means to bite; *he* means to close. With an object in the mouth, one must bite before closing. The hexagram’s upper and lower lines are both firm, while the center is yielding—this is the image of jaws. Moreover, a single firm line obstructs within, forming the image of an object in the jaws that cannot be closed without biting. Hence the name *Shihe*.

Yang Zhongli says: *Shihe* is the hexagram of removing obstructions. Legal judgments serve to investigate and seek the truth; when governance attains the truth, the world submits.

Li Zisi says: Heaven and earth produce things, and whatever obstructs them must be struck and shattered by thunder and lightning. The sage governs the world, and whatever obstructs the people must be severed by legal judgments. Thus, *Shihe* removes the obstruction in the jaws, thunder and lightning remove obstructions among living things, and punishments remove obstructions in the world.

Zhu Zhonghui says: Biting through dried meat is harder than flesh yet easier than preserved meat. The lungs are yellow, the color of metal, and decisive in judgment.

Liu Junbo says: *He* means to carry; *xiao* refers to wooden restraints. "Covering the ears" means the shackles rest upon the neck, obscuring the ears.

Xiong Guo says: In the *Rites of Zhou*, the *Laren* (preserved meat official), Zheng’s commentary states: Small creatures fully dried are called *teng*. *Teng* means "evening"—exposed in the morning and dried by evening. The *Marriage Rites* require fresh meat, hence the distinction.

Liu Yongxiang says: The Fourth’s dried lungs are harder than preserved meat, yet its possession of the metal arrow’s talent aids in resolving the difficulty. Even in the initial stage of hardship, one must persist with caution to attain auspiciousness. Though auspiciousness is achieved, its brilliance remains incomplete. As Master Zeng said: "When the truth is obtained, one should mourn and sympathize rather than rejoice." If there is no time even for mourning, how can there be brilliance? Thus, the Third’s encounter with poison leads to regret, and the Fifth must persevere through danger to avoid blame—what doubt remains?

Fang Shihua says: Every legal case arises from some obstruction causing dispute. If judgment fails to uncover the obstruction, the case cannot be easily resolved—like carving meat without first arranging the bones. *Shi* (biting) means to gnaw through the obstruction to resolve it. The bottom line symbolizes shackles covering the toes; the top line symbolizes shackles covering the ears. The Second, riding upon the firm, faces a difficult case—biting through flesh deeply enough to lose the nose means severing the bone, thus attaining the truth without blame. The Third, in an improper position, bites through preserved meat and encounters poison, incurring minor regret. Yet having personally bitten through it, there is still no blame. The Fourth, firm in virtue, bites through dried lungs and obtains a metal arrow (a tool for severing sinews). The Fifth, yielding yet central, bites through dried meat and obtains yellow gold—hence "proper attainment." The Second, though riding upon the firm, is at the beginning, thus "flesh." The Third corresponds to the Top Nine’s firmness, representing preserved meat of the whole bone. The Fourth is "dried lungs," the Fifth "dried meat"—this is the distinction between the Nine and Six.

**☲ below ☶ above**
*Bi* (Adornment): Prosperous. Slightly favorable for advancing.

*The Judgment* says: *Bi* is prosperous, for the yielding comes to adorn the firm—hence prosperity. The firm is divided and ascends to adorn the yielding, hence slightly favorable for advancing. This is the pattern of heaven. Cultivated brilliance at rest—this is the pattern of humanity. Observing the patterns of heaven, one examines the changes of the seasons; observing the patterns of humanity, one transforms and completes the world.

*The Image* says: Fire beneath the mountain—*Bi*. The noble person thereby clarifies all affairs but does not dare to adjudicate legal cases.

**First Nine:** Adorning the toes, one abandons the carriage and walks.
*The Image* says: Abandoning the carriage and walking—righteously, one does not ride.

**Six in the Second:** Adorning the beard.
*The Image* says: Adorning the beard—this rises with the above.

**Nine in the Third:** Adorned yet moistened, perseverance brings auspiciousness.
*The Image* says: The auspiciousness of perseverance—none will ultimately overstep.

**Six in the Fourth:** Adorned yet radiant, a white horse soars. Not a bandit but a marriage alliance.
*The Image* says: The Fourth, in its proper position yet suspicious. Not a bandit but a marriage alliance—ultimately without blame.

**Six in the Fifth:** Adorning the hills and gardens, a bundle of silk—meager, yet ultimately auspicious.
*The Image* says: The Fifth’s auspiciousness—there is joy.

**Top Nine:** White adornment—no blame.
*The Image* says: White adornment—no blame, for the above attains its aim.

**Mountain below, Fire above: *Bi***

Originally, the lower was *Qian* (☰) and the upper *Kun* (☷). Now, one line from *Kun* descends to the center of *Qian*, transforming the firm into the brilliant *Li* (☲), while the yielding comes to adorn the firm.

Yet *Qian* is the firm thing—at its origin, it does not flow. Adorned with the brilliance of *Li*, it indeed flows; unadorned by the brilliance of *Li*, it also flows. For it follows the yielding in adornment, yet its flow does not depend on adornment—only because adornment also flows is it said: *Bi* flows.

When the middle line of *Qian* is further divided and ascends to the top of *Kun*, the yielding transforms into the firmness of *Gen* Mountain, and the firm rises to adorn the yielding. Yet *Kun* is the yielding thing—it awaits *Qian* to advance, for it cannot go forth alone. If one were to adorn it solely with the firmness of *Gen* Mountain, then wherever it went would ultimately prove unprofitable. Thus it is also said: "Slight profit in having somewhere to go."

The Master said: "The intermingling of yin and yang, the blending of firm and yielding—these are the natural transformations of the *Qian* Dao, which is called the patterns of Heaven. Without the patterns of Heaven, how could the four seasons alternate and display their brilliance in the sky? Yet the inner trigram is *Li*, brilliant, while the outer is *Gen*, still—this is the image of brilliance coming to a halt, of stillness without adornment, which is called the patterns of humanity. Without the patterns of humanity, how could the world be transformed and all return to the primal adornment of white? The responsibility of primal color lies in white adornment. Thus, without observing the patterns of Heaven, one cannot know the spontaneity of transformation, which is not something human effort can achieve. Without observing the patterns of humanity, one cannot know that humans can surpass Heaven, that the sage can assist where Heaven falls short—and the achievement of *Gen*’s stillness is great indeed.

Therefore, the first line is firm and occupies the lower second position. It would adorn its toes with brilliance, yet initially, it is contrary to principle—abandoning the carriage to go on foot. Though its edges may seem adorned, in truth it halts itself and cannot be adorned. Thus, the second line alone initiates adornment for the third. How could it know that the third, being firm in the upper position, would, like a beard moistened by proximity, remain steadfast in its firmness and eternal correctness? The second also halts itself and ultimately cannot add adornment.

The flow of *Bi* is to be observed in the first and third lines. As for the topmost line, it is truly white adornment. *Gen* is the mountain, also resembling the recluse of hills and gardens. Though the fifth line, the yielding ruler at the center, willingly receives adornment from the hills and gardens, and though its bundle of silk is meager—making it seem stingy and unadorned—there is still adornment present, so it does not yet qualify as white adornment. Why? The elder of the hills and gardens, the old man bearing his staff, what use has he for a bundle of silk? Yet if he does not exalt the topmost line, he too will ultimately find joy. Since the fifth has joy, the top attains its aim—what doubt remains?

The fourth, however, cannot escape doubt, for it occupies a position of pure yin. Yet it too is swiftly turning white. *Qian* is the horse, and it soars white. Not taking the above as a bandit but as a marriage alliance, it daily draws nearer to white adornment. Ultimately without blame—what doubt remains? Its profit in having somewhere to go is like this—is this not the firm rising to adorn the yielding?

Thus do I know that wherever yang firmness resides, the yielding truly profits from it and receives its adornment. To say that the yielding can adorn the firm—is there such a principle? Therefore, though the whole world follows the latecomers, the Master alone follows the rustic. Who could adorn him? Even after a thousand or a hundred years, adornment cannot be added—how much less in his own time? Alas.

Lord Wang Ji states: Beneath the mountain there is fire, its brightness illuminating all things. Grasses and trees glow in mutual radiance—this is the image of *Bi* (Adornment). The noble person, observing this image of luminous illumination, brings clarity to all governance yet dares not rely on it to adjudicate legal cases. Cultivating governance is how civil patterns (*wen*) are perfected, but in the administration of justice, what matters is the truth of human feelings. If adornment is applied, the truth of those feelings is obscured; thus, one dares not use civil patterns to decide cases.

Su Shi’s *General Explanations of the Hexagrams*:
*Qian* transforms into *Li*, *Kun* into *Gen*—this is the configuration of yin and yang, the workings of number. "Cultural brilliance restrained" (*wen ming yi zhi*) is the virtue of *Li* and *Gen*. The dynamics of configuration and number unfold as Heaven bestows its virtue upon humanity.

The *Yijing* speaks of the mutual exchange of firm and yielding, of ascent and descent, but its most prominent expression is in the *Tuan* commentary on *Bi*. Hence, scholars have long debated the transformations that give rise to it, claiming that *Tai* transforms into *Bi*—a great confusion. A single hexagram may transform into sixty-three others; why should *Bi* alone be singled out? Scholars know only that *Tai* becomes *Bi*, but how do they know that *Bi* does not become *Tai*?

All the *Yijing*’s references to the mutual exchange of firm and yielding derive from *Qian* and *Kun*. *Qian* bestows one yang upon *Kun*, transforming one of its yin lines to produce the three sons, each with one yang and two yin. In all hexagrams of the three sons that speak of "firmness coming," it is clear that these originally belonged to *Kun*, and *Qian* came to transform them. *Kun* bestows one yin upon *Qian*, transforming one of its yang lines to produce the three daughters, each with one yin and two yang. In all hexagrams of the three daughters that speak of "yielding coming," it is clear that these originally belonged to *Qian*, and *Kun* came to transform them. Thus, all such references pertain to hexagrams where the three sons and three daughters meet. In hexagrams where this is not the case, such language does not appear.

The *Tuan* commentary on *Gu* states: "The firm ascends while the yielding descends."
The *Tuan* on *Bi* states: "The yielding comes to adorn the firm, dividing the firm above to adorn the yielding below."
The *Tuan* on *Xian* states: "The yielding ascends while the firm descends."
The *Tuan* on *Heng* states: "The firm ascends while the yielding descends."
The *Tuan* on *Sun* states: "Diminishing below to augment above."
The *Tuan* on *Yi* states: "Diminishing above to augment below."
These six are chosen as they arise in their particular contexts. Of the eighteen hexagrams where the three sons and three daughters meet, why are only these six singled out? The answer lies in the Sage’s multifaceted approach to selecting hexagrams: sometimes by image, sometimes by line, sometimes by transformation, and sometimes by the mutual exchange of firm and yielding.

When selecting by image: *Heaven and Water moving in opposition* exemplifies *Song* (Conflict).
When selecting by line: *The sixth three stepping on a tiger’s tail* exemplifies caution.
When selecting by transformation: *Something in the jaws* exemplifies *Shi He* (Biting Through).
When selecting by the mutual exchange of firm and yielding: *Bi* (Adornment) is the example.
The mutual exchange of firm and yielding is but one method among many for selecting hexagrams. When it is chosen, the text speaks of it; when it is not, the text remains silent. One should not be perplexed by this.

---
*Kun* below, *Gen* above: *Bo* (Splitting Apart). "Unfavorable to have somewhere to go."
The *Tuan* states: *Bo* means splitting apart, the yielding transforming the firm. "Unfavorable to have somewhere to go"—the petty person grows strong. "Following and halting"—observing the image. The noble person values the waxing and waning of the full and the empty, the movement of Heaven.

The *Xiang* states: "The mountain clings to the earth—*Bo*. The ruler above thickens the foundation below to secure the dwelling."
**First Six:** "Splitting apart the bed at the legs—disdaining constancy, misfortune."
The *Xiang* states: "Splitting apart the bed at the legs—diminishing what is below."
**Six in the Second:** "Splitting apart the bed at the frame—disdaining constancy, misfortune."
The *Xiang* states: "Splitting apart the bed at the frame—no one supports it."
**Six in the Third:** "Splitting apart—no blame."
The *Xiang* states: "Splitting apart with no blame—losing connection to above and below."
**Six in the Fourth:** "Splitting apart the bed at the skin—misfortune."
The *Xiang* states: "Splitting apart the bed at the skin—disaster is near."
**Six in the Fifth:** "A string of fish, favored by the palace women—ultimately without harm."
The *Xiang* states: "Favored by the palace women—ultimately without blame."
**Top Nine:** "A great fruit uneaten. The noble person gains a carriage; the petty person strips the hut."
The *Xiang* states: "The noble person gains a carriage—the people bear him. The petty person strips the hut—ultimately unusable."

*Mountain and Earth Splitting Apart*:
Fang Shihua states: "The yielding transforming the firm" refers to the first through fifth lines. Formerly five yang, now five yin—this is *Bo* (splitting apart, decay). Yet one yang remains, destined to become the fruit of renewal. Thus, the Top Nine bears the image of "a great fruit uneaten." Moreover, it is carried by the five yin, bearing the image of "the noble person gains a carriage." As for "the petty person strips the hut," it means that if the five yin reach this point without the Top Nine’s solitary yang above, they would be stripping their own shelter—what would cover them? Thus, the petty are deeply fortunate.

Yet when the petty grow strong, it is "unfavorable to have somewhere to go." One should remain still and await the time, as night awaits day and winter awaits spring. It is not that winter and night are unnavigable, only to be fled. Where would one flee? Thus, from *Fu* (Return) to *Lin* (Approach), to *Tai* (Peace), to the brightness of *Da Zhuang* (Great Strength)—facing the light, one may proceed. From *Shi* (Beginning) to *Gu* (Decay), to *Pi* (Stagnation), to the darkness of *Bo* (Splitting Apart)—facing the dusk, one rests and does not proceed. This is the movement of Heaven. Thus, "following and halting" is how one observes the image of waxing and waning, the full and the empty—this is Heaven’s movement, in which the noble person does not interfere.

The First Six begins the transformation below, bearing the image of "splitting apart the bed at the legs." Though it occupies the lowest position and remains humble, even as a yielding line, it may yet hold to constancy—how could misfortune be spoken of? Only when it disdains humility and scorns constancy, rushing toward misfortune, does this become evident. The Six in the Second is the same.

When the second line transforms into a six, its image is "stripping the forest to distinguish boundaries." Boundaries mark the division between above and below. Yet, abiding in the center and upholding correctness, it responds in harmony with the yang above. Though yielding, it remains steadfast—how could misfortune be spoken of? Only when it abandons centrality and correctness, scorning constancy to follow deviance, does misfortune first appear. Thus, these two lines—if they do not scorn constancy—will avoid great misfortune. Even in the time of *Bo* (Splitting Apart), occupying the second position after the first, the sage does not discard them.

Consider the Six in the Third: though it loses the four yin lines above and below, it never dares lose the Nine in the Fifth, rightly responding to its steadfast correctness. In this way, though situated amid *Bo*, it naturally avoids blame—how could misfortune arise? From this, we know that the many yin await yang to find stability. If yang does not stabilize yin, it too is gravely imperiled. Yet if one clings to yang, though in *Bo*, what blame could there be?

Observe further the Six in the Fifth: it leads the many yin to support the Nine in the Top, its image "threading fish to court the palace’s favor." The sage rejoices in its initial lack of advantage yet trusts its ultimate outcome—no fault remains. Even if it transforms into yang, it still follows yang’s lead. Now, to emulate the Third and Fifth but instead follow the Six and Fourth is mistaken. Occupying a position close to the ruler, it courts imminent disaster—"stripping the forest to the skin," willingly embracing harm—how foolish!

**Appendix**
Cheng Zhengshu said: All yang lines have vanished; only the Nine in the Top remains. Like a great fruit untouched by men, it will become the seed of renewal. If the Nine in the Top were to transform, it would become pure *Kun*. Yet yang is at its utmost limit.

Cai Jiefu said: "The noble one gains a chariot; the petty man strips a hut"—these two principles do not operate together, nor do their omens coincide. If the noble one gains a chariot, the petty man too will keep his hut.

Wang Benhe said: The Nine in the Top stands above, borne by five yin—this is "the noble one gaining a chariot." If the Nine in the Top were to transform into pure *Kun*, heaven would collapse and earth overturn—what shelter would the five yin have? This would be "the petty man stripping a hut."

Zhu Zhonghui said: The mountain clings to the earth. Only because the earth is thick can it secure its place.

*Zhen* below *Kun* above: *Fu* (Return): Smooth and free.

Entrance and exit are supremely swift, and friends arrive without blame. Following the Way in reversal, the return comes in seven days. Propitious for setting forth.

*The Tuàn* says: *Fu* (Return) is smooth and free. The firm returns, moving in accordance with compliance—thus entrance and exit are supremely swift, and friends arrive without blame. Following the Way in reversal, the return comes in seven days—this is the course of heaven. Propitious for setting forth, for the firm is growing. In *Fu* (Return), do we not see the heart of heaven and earth?

*The Xiàng* says: Thunder within the earth—*Fu* (Return). The former kings, on the solstice, closed the passes; merchants and travelers did not journey, and the lord did not inspect the regions.

**First Nine:** Returning from not far, there is no great regret—supremely auspicious.
*The Xiàng* says: Returning from not far is for the cultivation of the self.

**Six in the Second:** A joyful return—auspicious.
*The Xiàng* says: The auspiciousness of a joyful return is in following the humane.

**Six in the Third:** Frequent return—danger, but no blame.
*The Xiàng* says: The danger of frequent return is such that, in principle, there is no blame.

**Six in the Fourth:** Returning alone in the midst of the way.
*The Xiàng* says: Returning alone in the midst of the way is to follow the Dao.

**Six in the Fifth:** Sincere return—no regret.
*The Xiàng* says: Sincere return with no regret is to examine oneself from the center.

**Top Six:** Deluded return—misfortune. There is calamity in mobilizing troops; in the end, great defeat. For the lord of the state, misfortune—even after ten years, he will not be able to campaign.
*The Xiàng* says: The misfortune of deluded return is to reverse the lord’s Way.

**Earth above Thunder—Fu (Return):** The firm returns, and in returning, there is smoothness. How is *Fu* able to be smooth and free? Before return, there is restless coming and going, with illness and obstruction. Once returned, entrance and exit are supremely swift. Before return, even if friends follow your thoughts, blame cannot be avoided. After return, though the five yin’s friends arrive, what blame is there? Naturally, all take my cultivation of the self as their model, follow the humane as their standard, blame themselves for danger, set their sights on solitary return, examine themselves in sincere return—unceasing until the *Qian* does not turn back. How could one willingly accept the misfortune of deluded return? Moreover, deluded return is utterly inadmissible. Thus, the extreme misfortune of deluded return is emphasized to warn the First Nine. Only by returning from not far, with no great regret, is supreme auspiciousness attained. "No great regret" means that, having turned back and returned home, there is no regret for having gone east or west—thus it is supremely auspicious. In return, there is supreme auspiciousness; in delusion, there is calamity and misfortune—what doubt is there? Therefore, the second line, being near the first, rests and follows the humane, and the second line’s return is thus achieved. In return, there is auspiciousness. The third line, not centered, frequently returns and is in danger, yet ultimately returns. In return, there is no blame. The fourth line, dwelling in the midst of the five yin yet responding alone to the first, is solitary return. In solitary return, there is auspiciousness—this goes without saying. The fifth line, dwelling in the center of the upper trigram and able to examine itself, is sincere return. Sincere return is not only the fourth line’s return. The first yang’s return is like this; the second, third, fourth, and fifth yin’s return is like that. This is the momentum of the world turning toward the humane—what place is there for the five yin’s friends to arrive? If, in this way, one does not return, then there will be no return at all. Either the yin has reached its extreme and does not revert, or delusion is too deep to awaken. Thus, the top line twice speaks of misfortune, and also of calamity and great defeat. For if one does not return and is deluded, then good fortune turns to misfortune—there is no regard. Even with the First Nine’s humane influence, what can be done? Fang Shihua said: When the yin is heavy, it strips the yang. Suddenly, in a single day, the original heart is seen again, and the brightness of yang returns. Thus it is said: *Fu* (Return) is smooth and free—the firm returns. The yang’s Way is not externally imposed; returning is how one recovers the Way. From *Zhun* to *Meng*, to *Pi*, to *Guan*, to *Bo*, to *Kun*, to *Fu*—seven days. Once the first yang returns, the five yang naturally grow—propitious for setting forth. *Bo* has just exhausted the yang, and *Fu* immediately follows, thus the First Nine is "returning from not far." The Six in the Second follows the First Nine’s humane influence. Humane is to center and lower oneself, desiring to return with it—is this not a joyful return?

**Appendix**
Qiu Xingke said: "The firm returns" speaks of the single firm line of *Bo* reaching the top, turning back below, and becoming *Fu*. "The firm grows" speaks of the single firm line of *Fu* advancing from below upward, becoming *Lin*, becoming *Tai*, and ultimately becoming *Qian*. Because it has departed and then comes back, there is smoothness. Because it has returned and then gradually grows, it is propitious for setting forth. "The firm returns" speaks of the very beginning of return; "the firm grows" speaks of after return.

Jin Rubai said: Observing *Fu* allows one to know the humane. The humane is the heart of heaven and earth in generating things—profound and ceaseless. The *Tuàn* speaks of *Fu* revealing the heart of heaven and earth, and the six lines all concern the affairs of the humane. The First Nine, "returning from not far," corresponds to Yanzi—this is the Way of *Qian*. The nine obtains the first line of *Qian*. The second, "joyful return with friends," corresponds to Fuzi, Min Sun, and their followers. The third, "frequent return," is like the sun and moon. The fourth, "returning alone," refers to Mencius, born a hundred years later, who alone privately admired the Master to follow the Dao. The fifth, "sincere return," is the Way of *Kun*. The Six in the Fifth is the central line of *Kun*—perhaps Zengzi corresponds to this. The top, "deluded return," is self-abandonment and self-neglect—this is the unhumane.

Wu Ziyi said: *Fu* reveals the heart of heaven and earth—not that *Fu* itself is the heart of heaven and earth.

Yang Tingxiu said: Frequent return is not dangerous; frequent transgression is dangerous. "Danger, but no blame" means the sage warns of frequent transgression yet opens the way for frequent return.

Wang Benhe said: Frequent return is frequent loss. If there were no loss, how could there be return? Thus, the sage both worries over frequent return and rejoices in it.

Wang Fusi said: The fourth line, with two yin above and two yin below, occupies its proper position and responds to the first—alone attaining return, complying with the Dao in reversion. Thus it is said: "Returning alone in the midst of the way."

Cai Jiefu said: The fourth line, moving alone in the center to return, is like advancing amid the multitude of yin, turning back alone to follow yang—yielding yet attaining correctness.

Xue Juncai said: The auspiciousness of restful return lies in honoring humanity. Later generations, neglecting self-cultivation, thus fail to recognize the duty of drawing near to the worthy.

Xiong Guo said: Yu Fan called *Kun* the state and nation, hence the ruler’s ten-year misfortune.

Li Dingzuo said: The number of *Kun* governs ten; the primal *qi* of *Qian* is ten, the primal *qi* of *Kun* is one—thus whenever ten is mentioned, it is the image of *Kun* reaching its end.

**Hexagram 25: Wuwang (Innocence)**
*Qian* below, *Zhen* above
**Wuwang:** Primordial, penetrating, beneficial, and correct. If not correct, there will be misfortune; no advantage in having somewhere to go.

**The Judgment** says: *Wuwang*—the firm comes from without and becomes master within. Movement and strength, firmness in the center with response—great penetration through correctness is the mandate of heaven. "If not correct, there will be misfortune; no advantage in having somewhere to go." Where can one go in *Wuwang*? Heaven’s mandate does not protect—how can one act?

**The Image** says: Thunder moves beneath heaven; all things partake of *Wuwang*. The former kings, in abundant response to the seasons, nurtured the myriad things.

**First Nine:** *Wuwang*—going is auspicious.
**The Image** says: Going in *Wuwang* attains the will.

**Six in the Second:** Not plowing yet reaping, not weeding yet clearing—then advantageous to have somewhere to go.
**The Image** says: "Not plowing yet reaping"—not yet enriched.

**Six in the Third:** Disaster in *Wuwang*—someone ties an ox; a traveler gains it, the villagers suffer misfortune.
**The Image** says: The traveler gains the ox; the villagers suffer misfortune.

**Nine in the Fourth:** Correctness is possible; no blame.
**The Image** says: "Correctness is possible; no blame"—firmly possessing it.

**Nine in the Fifth:** Illness in *Wuwang*—do not take medicine, and there will be joy.
**The Image** says: Medicine in *Wuwang* must not be tried.

**Top Nine:** *Wuwang*—acting brings misfortune; no advantage in having somewhere to go.
**The Image** says: Acting in *Wuwang* brings the disaster of exhaustion.

**Heaven and Thunder: Wuwang**
*Wuwang* is primordial delusion. When truth is real and without delusion, then naturally there is primordial penetration, benefit, and correctness. Great penetration through correctness is the timing of heaven’s mandate. How could heaven’s mandate have delusion? Since heaven’s mandate is itself primordial delusion, the *Judgment* speaks of *Wuwang*, and the *Commentary* again speaks of heaven’s mandate. Heaven’s mandate is greatly penetrating and supremely correct. Those who call heaven’s mandate incorrect are deluded. Seeing specks in the eye, one perceives flowers in empty space. Without specks in the eye, how could there be flowers in empty space? How could heaven’s mandate ever be incorrect? Only because of specks does one see flowers in empty space; only because of delusion does one see heaven’s mandate as incorrect and disadvantageous to act. Not knowing to blame oneself but falsely claiming heaven’s mandate does not protect me—this is primordial delusion turning into outright delusion. How could any place one goes not be primordial delusion? Where could one go that is not the hidden arrangement of heaven’s mandate? If not for heaven’s mandate as primordial delusion, silently protecting and subtly assisting, one could not take a single step. Thus it is said: "Heaven’s mandate does not protect—how can one act?" Heaven’s protection is precisely the absence of deluded action. Alas, people are all deluded—thus they are protected yet do not know it.

The *Judgment* speaks of *Wuwang* as primordial, penetrating, beneficial, and correct—this is heaven, earth, and the myriad things sharing one primordial delusion. The *Image* speaks of thunder moving beneath heaven, all things partaking of *Wuwang*—this is heaven, earth, and the myriad things each having their own primordial delusion. Scholars may take this as proof that *Wuwang* is not delusion.

The first line, a single yang below, is thunder stirring at its inception—delusion is entirely dispelled. This is the moment when the solitary yang attains its will—where could one go that would not be auspicious? As for the second line, though thunder stirs below, delusion seems suddenly stilled. The sage still suspects its stinginess, thus reverses the phrasing to encourage it: "If the second line can refrain from plowing yet reaping, not weeding yet clearing, then it will be advantageous to have somewhere to go." Yet its position is yielding, its nature yin—perhaps this is not yet certain. The Master, reading it, borrows the phrasing to rebuke it: "The yin nature is stingy, constantly desiring wealth. Now the second line is not yet enriched—how could it refrain from 'not plowing yet reaping'?" Alas! Considering the words of these two sages together, what then is expected of the second line?

The third line is three. One person ties an ox, one gains the ox, one loses the ox—there is gain and loss. Disaster is self-inflicted; though called *Wuwang*, what can be done? The fourth line, a *Qian* body dwelling below, is vast emptiness where nothing yet exists—thus it has not entered hesitation, has not been deluded by incorrectness, and can trust in the firm possession of heaven’s mandate, attaining correctness without blame. The fifth line, firm *Qian* centered and correct, should know what illness *Wuwang* could have. Trusting that medicine should not be tried, yet doubting due to the second line’s influence, it falsely suspects illness—this is delusion. The top line is precisely what is meant by "not correct, there will be misfortune; no advantage in having somewhere to go." The body is within *Wuwang*, the heart transcends *Wuwang*—though it knows wealth cannot be attained, it does not know that exhaustion also brings disaster. Alas! How could one find someone like the first or fourth lines to discuss the meaning of *Wuwang* with?

Fang Shihua said: The firm comes from without and becomes master within—movement and strength, firmness in the center with response—this is great penetration through the correctness of heaven’s mandate. Great penetration through correctness—who commands this? The commander is heaven. How could heaven’s mandate have delusion? The first line, thunder’s master emerging from the earth with a single resounding crack, is the key to why the hexagram is called *Wuwang*. Its going is auspicious—how could it not attain its will? The second line, *Kun* yin governing wealth, *Kun* earth governing plowing, reaping, weeding, and clearing.

The third line, yin and soft without centeredness, thus suffers disaster from *wuwang* and delusion in gain and loss. Now, why is an ox suddenly tied, suddenly lost, and suddenly made for a traveler to obtain? This disaster arises from human action—how could it be called *wuwang*? The fourth line, a *Qian* body dwelling below, thus may hold to correctness without blame. Yet correctness is what all people inherently possess—it is not the fourth line’s correctness alone. The fifth line, centered and rightly positioned, is inherently *wuwang* and inherently aligned with correctness; yet by having a responsive relationship with the second line, it doubts itself as deluded—this is the illness of *wuwang*. If left untreated, it will heal on its own; if medicine is administered, the illness will worsen. Thus it is said: the remedy for *wuwang* must not be tested. To test it is to invite delusion, and the affliction of delusion will turn to disaster. The top line, excessively firm without centeredness, fails to recognize the *Qian* body’s inherent *wuwang*; driving it forward and forcing it in, it reaches the point of exhaustion—where is the benefit in going? In truth, it brings ruin upon itself and invites its own calamity. Appendix.

Bear Guo said: "In farming, a field returning to grass after one year is called *miao* (sprouts); after three years of thorough weeding, it is called *hui* (gathering)."

Wu Youqing said: "The fifth line, firm and centered, suffers illness from *wuwang* when it has a responsive relationship. The sage’s response to things is like this: when they come, he illuminates them; when they depart, he does not retain them. This is no different from the illness of *wuwang*. Without rejecting external things, external things cannot burden him—why then resort to medicine?"

**Below *Qian*, above *Gen*: *Da Xu* (Great Accumulation).** **Auspicious to be correct.**
**Not eating at home—good fortune.**
**Auspicious to ford the great river.**

**The *Tuan* says:** *Da Xu*: firmness, strength, sincerity, and brilliance—daily renewing its virtue. Firmness rises and esteems worthiness. It can restrain strength—this is great correctness. "Not eating at home—good fortune" means nurturing the worthy. "Auspicious to ford the great river" means responding to Heaven.

**The *Xiang* says:** Heaven within the mountain—*Da Xu*. The noble person, by remembering the words and deeds of the past, accumulates virtue.

**First Nine:** There is danger—beneficial to desist.
**The *Xiang* says:** "There is danger—beneficial to desist" means not courting disaster.

**Nine in the Second:** The carriage sheds its shafts.
**The *Xiang* says:** "The carriage sheds its shafts"—centered, it has no fault.

**Nine in the Third:** A fine horse gallops—beneficial to be correct amid hardship. Daily practice in charioteering and defense—beneficial to have somewhere to go.
**The *Xiang* says:** "Beneficial to have somewhere to go"—it aligns with the will above.

**Six in the Fourth:** The young bull’s yoke—supremely auspicious.
**The *Xiang* says:** "Six in the Fourth—supremely auspicious"—there is joy.

**Six in the Fifth:** The boar’s tusk—good fortune.
**The *Xiang* says:** "Six in the Fifth—good fortune"—there is celebration.

**Top Nine:** What thoroughfare of Heaven! Smoothness.
**The *Xiang* says:** "What thoroughfare of Heaven!"—the Dao greatly advances.

**Mountain above Heaven: *Da Xu*.** *Gen* is firm and great. Nurturing the worthy is accumulation. What is meant by "worthy"? The three yang lines of *Qian*. *Qian* is firm, strong, sincere, and solid. The fourth and fifth lines are *Gen*. *Gen* shines brilliantly and daily renews its worthiness in this way. Thus, all are seen to be accumulated by the Top Nine, completing the greatness of what is accumulated. Yet the virtue of the Top Nine is truly sufficient to accumulate them. Its virtue is firmness rising and esteeming worthiness. Esteeming worthiness, the worthy are all willing to be accumulated by it. Occupying the superior position, it naturally has the power to accumulate them. With firmness, it can restrain strength, so it must nurture and perfect their worthiness before accumulating them—not hastily accumulating. Such is the great correctness of this line. Thus, all worthy of the time partake in the great feast. If even one person ate at home, it would be clear that the wilds had no unrecognized worthy. Together, they bear the thoroughfare of Heaven, respond to Heaven, and ford the great river—what doubt could there be?

Now observe the First Nine, dwelling below, which could advance and be accumulated by the Top Nine. Yet it sees advancement as perilous and halts, avoiding the disaster of rash progress. Thus, the worthiness of the First is to be esteemed. The Nine in the Second has already harnessed its carriage, yet it sheds its shafts—this worthiness of the Second is to be esteemed, for its centeredness has no fault. The Nine in the Third originally aligns with the Top Nine’s will. Now, its carriage is harnessed, its fine horse gallops, and the three yang lines surge forward. Yet it still daily practices charioteering and defense, not forgetting the mind of hardship and correctness—this worthiness of the Third is to be esteemed. Naturally, it is "beneficial to have somewhere to go." As for the Fourth and Fifth, below they ride the three yang lines, above they accumulate *Gen*’s firmness. This is called the "young bull" and the "boar," while the single yang above resembles its castrated form and the restraint of its tusks. Thus, it preemptively restrains their strength and accumulates them to completion—supremely auspicious with joy, good fortune with celebration, which goes without saying.
