The Third aligns in will. The Fourth has joy, and the Fifth has celebration—thus, the Top Nine’s path of accumulation reaches its great advancement. The way of all correctness opens the path for the many yang lines, clearing Heaven’s thoroughfare to smoothness. At this point, all flourish and aspire to be employed—who could surpass *Da Xu*? Yet observe the three yang lines: first, there is danger; then, the shedding of shafts; and the upholding of hardship and correctness. Observe the Fourth and Fifth: they are restrained by the castrated form and guarded by the tusks, preventing their strength from being hastily displayed or tested. Its accumulation is not taken lightly—such is the case. Truly, this is the time of *Da Xu*, akin to the era of Yao and Shun.

Someone asked: "How do you know that 'brilliance daily renewing' refers specifically to *Gen*’s restraint?" I replied: "If it halted without advancing, how could it be 'daily renewing'? Thus, observing the Fourth and Fifth, one must restrain before advancing—this can be known."

**Appendix.**

Su Po’s explanation states: The *Xiao Xu*’s discourse on the wheel-spokes arises from necessity, hence husband and wife turn against each other. The *Da Xu*’s discourse on the shaft is willingly embraced, thus there is no blame within.

Cai Jiefu says: The shaft and the spokes differ. The spokes are the converging wheel-rims, thirty in total. The shaft is the crossbar beneath the carriage, bearing its weight. The *Xiao Xu* speaks of the spokes—where yin restrains them, the discourse concerns plans for prolonged stability. The *Da Xu* speaks of the shaft—where it halts of its own accord without advancing, the discourse is subtle: a temporary pause from which one may swiftly rise again.

Xiong Guo says: According to Zheng Xuan and Guanxia’s *Riyue Jueju*, Guan Shi states: "At dawn it is brilliance; at midday it is light." As for the *tusks*, the *Kunya* says: "That which substitutes for tethering a pig is called *ya*." The upper firm line lies ahead—like a substitute tusk. Lu Shinong says: The *ya* is what accumulates in place of a pig’s tether. Now, in the regions of Hai and Dai, striking a pig with a rack is called *ya*.

Jiao Ruohou says: "Daily training in chariot and guard" and "daily" in seal script are quite similar. What is the thoroughfare of Heaven? What is the cangue?

What does *he* mean? Homophonous with *he* (賀), it signifies "to bear" or "to carry." The *Fu on the Hall of Magnificent Light* states: "Bearing Heaven’s thoroughfare, it attains primal flourishing." This demonstrates the interchangeability of ancient graphs. The *Zhou Book of Documents* commentary cites *Zhuangzi*’s "bearing the azure sky without hindrance" as further evidence. Xiang Ziqī said: "Nothing halts like a mountain—nothing is greater than Heaven. Heaven resides within the mountain: this is the image of *Great Accumulation*."

Below *Zhen* (震), above *Gen* (艮): *Yi* (頤). "Persistence brings good fortune. Observing nourishment, one seeks sustenance for oneself."

The *Tuan* says: "*Yi*: persistence brings good fortune, for nourishing the correct brings good fortune. Observing nourishment is observing what one nurtures; seeking sustenance for oneself is observing how one nourishes oneself. Heaven and Earth nourish the myriad things; the sage nourishes the worthy and thereby the myriad people. How great is the timing of *Yi*!"

The *Xiang* says: "Thunder beneath the mountain: *Yi*. The noble person is thus cautious in speech and moderates eating and drinking."

**First Nine:** "Abandoning your numinous tortoise, you observe my chewing—misfortune."
The *Xiang* says: "Observing my chewing is likewise unworthy."

**Six in the Second:** "Overturning nourishment, defying the norm; seeking nourishment from the hill—advancing brings misfortune."
The *Xiang* says: "Advancing brings misfortune for Six in the Second, for one loses one’s kind."

**Six in the Third:** "Defying nourishment, persistence brings misfortune. For ten years, do not act—nothing is favorable."
The *Xiang* says: "For ten years, do not act—such a path is greatly perverse."

**Six in the Fourth:** "Overturning nourishment—good fortune. The tiger’s gaze is fierce, its desires relentless—no blame."
The *Xiang* says: "Good fortune in overturning nourishment: bestowing radiance above."

**Six in the Fifth:** "Defying the norm, dwelling in persistence brings good fortune. One cannot ford the great river."
The *Xiang* says: "Good fortune in dwelling in persistence: compliance follows from above."

**Top Nine:** "Deriving nourishment—danger brings good fortune. Favorable to ford the great river."
The *Xiang* says: "Deriving nourishment—danger brings good fortune, for great felicity ensues."

*Mountain-Thunder: Yi*
Why, in *Yi*, is it said that "persistence brings good fortune"? The so-called *yi* amounts to nothing more than seeking a single mouthful for oneself—what persistence or good fortune lies therein? Alas, *yi* is what nourishes others. To "observe nourishment" is to observe what one nurtures, not how one nourishes oneself. If one merely seeks sustenance for oneself, knowing only self-nourishment, what is there to observe? Thus, observing Heaven and Earth, one sees that what they nourish are the myriad things—Heaven and Earth do not nourish themselves. Observing the sage, one sees that what he nourishes are the worthy and thereby the myriad people—the sage does not nourish himself. How correct is this nourishing! Persistence therein brings good fortune. Hence it is said: "How great is the timing of *Yi*!"

The Top Nine nourishes the worthy. The Fifth, yielding and centered; the Fourth, yielding and correct—both rightly align with the worthy whom the Top Nine nourishes. Yet Six in the Fifth is the ruler. When the ruler ought to nourish others but is instead nourished by them, is this not contrary to the norm and defiant of principle? Though one may recognize the ruler’s inability to nourish and thus turn to the Top Nine for sustenance, one still cannot ford the great river. Yet dwelling in persistence brings good fortune, which must return to him—this is compliance following from above.

The Top Nine is the firmness of *Gen*; the First Nine is the firmness of *Zhen*. Both are yang firmness and thus both should nourish the worthy. Yet the firmness of *Zhen* lies below, desiring to nourish the worthy but unable to do so. If one remains envious of the Fourth’s nourishment above, then though one may daily embody yang firmness, how can one be deemed worthy? Beholding the Fourth’s nourishment from above, one is moved by observation—this is what is meant by "abandoning the numinous tortoise to observe chewing," a base act indeed. *Duoyi* (朵颐) is the Fourth’s *yi*, an image of hanging downward.

The Second and the Fifth are proper correlates. If the Second seeks to respond to the Fifth’s hill, then the Fifth, awaiting nourishment from above, cannot but lose its kind and advance to misfortune. If it seeks to respond to the mountain’s summit above, this is not its proper correlate, defying the norm—how can one predict the outcome? As for Six in the Third, its proper correlate is above, and it should rightly receive nourishment from above. Yet it fails to recognize that the above nourishes the worthy and thereby the myriad people, instead harboring thoughts of defying nourishment and lofty aspirations—this is not impersistence. Yet why does it incur misfortune from losing its timing? Though it may remain unused for ten years, it merely withers in isolation, greatly perverse to the Way.

What profit could there be in this? Only Six in the Fourth, dwelling within the same *Gen* body, merits the straightforward declaration of "fortune in inverting the nourishment" as a stroke of luck. For when Nine at the Top radiates its light, who would not submit? As King Wen excelled in nurturing, even Boyi and Taigong came to join him—how much more so for those of the same body? Or those in proper correlation? When nourishment is thus, even a tiger’s devouring brings satisfaction; what fault could others find, that one would turn to defying nourishment to assert one’s own loftiness? Does one not know that the above is the very source of the hexagram, the root of nourishment wherein the nurturing of Heaven and Earth and the sages resides? To nourish all under Heaven is to bear its cares—how could one not take this as a warning? Yet those who would nourish all under Heaven must first nourish the worthy. How, then, could this not be called the profit of "crossing the river by means of nourishment"? The celebration of *Dayou* is surely what the sage desires to behold—yet if the time is not ripe, what can be done? Even if one possesses a spirit-turtle, if others fail to value it, one cannot but value oneself. Alas, on what day, at what hour, will people cease their restless pursuit and instead regard one as a mighty tiger?

**Appendix**
Liu Junbo says: Above *Gen*, below *Zhen*—outer firmness, inner softness—forms the image of a turtle. The turtle can subsist without food, nourishing itself through *qi*.

Deng Bogao says: *Duo* (朵), according to the *Benyi*, means "to hang down." The *Shuowen* states: *Duo* refers to the hanging blossoms of a tree. The movement of the jaws causes them to hang down, revealing their activity.

Su Shi explains: Above, stillness; below, movement—this is the image of nourishment. What all know yet find difficult to practice is to be cautious in speech and moderate in eating. Once words are spoken, they cannot be retracted; once food is consumed, it cannot be expelled.

Hexagram 59: *Daguo* (Great Exceeding), with *Xun* below and *Dui* above: The ridgepole sags. It is beneficial to have somewhere to go; there will be smooth progress.

**The Image** says: *Daguo* means greatness in excess. The ridgepole sags—this is because the root and branches are weak. Though firmness exceeds, it remains centered; advancing with compliance and joy brings benefit to one’s journey, leading to smooth progress. How momentous is the time of *Daguo*!

**The Image** says: The marsh submerges the wood—this is *Daguo*. The noble person stands alone without fear, encountering a world of deep gloom.

- **First Six:** Using white couch grass as a mat incurs no blame.
  **The Image** says: Using white couch grass as a mat—this is because softness lies below.

- **Nine in the Second:** A withered poplar sprouts new shoots! An old man takes a young wife—nothing is advantageous.
  **The Image** says: An old man with a young wife—excess in their union.

- **Nine in the Third:** The ridgepole sags—misfortune.
  **The Image** says: The misfortune of a sagging ridgepole—it cannot be supported.

- **Nine in the Fourth:** The ridgepole is raised—good fortune. If there is another, there will be regret.
  **The Image** says: The good fortune of a raised ridgepole—it does not sag beneath.

- **Nine in the Fifth:** A withered poplar blooms; an old woman takes a young husband—no blame, no praise.
  **The Image** says: A withered poplar blooms—how can this last? An old woman with a young husband—this is also shameful.

- **Top Six:** Crossing the water, one is submerged past the crown—misfortune, yet no blame.
  **The Image** says: The misfortune of crossing the water—it cannot be blamed.

The time of *Daguo* demands a foundation and branches both firm and strong, a body purely *Qian*-like in vigor, only then can there be great smooth progress without blame. Yet if it were purely *Qian*, it could not be called "greatness in excess." It is only because the top and bottom lines are yin—soft and weak—that excess arises.

What constitutes excess? A lord exceeding his ministers, a father exceeding his son, a husband exceeding his wife. If a lord exceeds his ministers, who will serve as his limbs? If a father exceeds his son, who will carry on his legacy? If a husband exceeds his wife, who will manage the inner domain? If yang exceeds yin, who will complete the cycle? This is precisely the time of *Daguo*—it cannot be delayed. One must swiftly go forth to seek firmness and uprightness, only then can there be smooth progress and the sagging ridgepole be avoided. Thus it is said: How momentous is the time of *Daguo*! This means that in such a time, one cannot follow the usual path; one must act with greatness beyond others—doing what others dare not. Like setting aside the Four Mountains to promote the obscure, or passing over the eminent to summon the humble from the cliffs of Fu Rock, the sages of old were largely like this. In this hexagram, only the Nine in the Fourth is sufficient to meet this moment. As Nine occupies the fourth place, firmness exceeds yet remains centered; with *Xun* containing *Dui*, it advances with compliance and joy. Thus it can benefit from having somewhere to go and achieve smooth progress, bringing the good fortune of a raised ridgepole.

Suppose it were said: "It responds to the First Six"—this would be mistaken. The Nine in the Fourth, embodying compliance and joy, firmness and centered vigor, is not something the lowly First Six can sway. Now that it has achieved the good fortune of a raised ridgepole, there may be times when it relies on the First Six, using couch grass as a mat—what blame could there be? But if, having attained the smooth progress of a raised ridgepole, it merely waits for another’s response, it would be like trying to support the peak of a great hall with a single blade of grass—impossible. How could the Fourth incur such regret?

The Nine in the Third also possesses the timber of a ridgepole, but as Nine occupies the third place, firmness exceeds without centering. At the top of *Xun*, it knows only compliance and cannot advance with joy. Unable to go forth and achieve smooth progress, what can be said of the misfortune of a sagging ridgepole? When the ridgepole is raised in good fortune, even the white couch grass of the First Six—though not *Xun* wood—can be used without blame. But when the ridgepole sags in misfortune, even wood submerged past its crown, the toil of crossing the water, cannot support it to success. This is why the time of *Daguo* demands talent that is itself *Daguo*!

The Top Six is precisely the image of the marsh submerging the wood—submerged so deeply that the crown is not seen. At the extreme of *Dui*, the marsh waters run deep; crossing the water, one is submerged past the crown, and the wood is equally submerged—what misfortune! Yet the marsh can submerge the wood but cannot make the wood sink; when the waters recede, the wood emerges—what blame does the wood truly bear? Thus, the First Six’s weakness lies in its position below *Xun*, so its regret is with the wood, not the marsh. The Top Six’s weakness lies in its position above *Dui*, so its regret is with the marsh, not the wood.

The Second and Fifth also possess the timber of ridgepoles. Now, west of the Taihang Mountains, every household plants poplars—not only for their rapid growth but also for their straightness and strength, suitable for beams and ridgepoles. Yet if they wither past their time, they become useless. What is fortunate is that the withered may sprout new shoots. The reason they sprout new shoots is their connection below to the First Six, gaining a young wife. Though the First Six sees the Second as an old man, though past their time, their union allows the Second to sprout new shoots, becoming beams and ridgepoles—nothing is disadvantageous. Unlike the Fifth’s connection to the Top Six, where a young husband gains an old wife. Fleeting blossoms cannot last, nor do they sprout new shoots—what use are they? The Fifth’s end as a withered poplar is truly deluded.

Fang Shihua says: The ridgepole is likened to *Xun*, to wood. The four yang lines stand in parallel; the third and fourth, occupying the center, are like the ridgepole of a house. The withered poplar is wood past its time; the Second and Fourth share the same function, so the withered poplar sprouts new shoots, aiding the Fourth. The Third and Fifth differ in position, so the withered poplar only blooms, offering no support to the Third. The First Six originally symbolizes white couch grass, and with yin below yang, it also symbolizes the pairing of an old man with a young wife. The Top Six originally symbolizes submersion past the crown, and with yin above yang, it also symbolizes the pairing of an old woman with a young husband.

**Appendix**

Yang Yuansu says: "Not bound by conventional righteousness" means one must not be constrained by ordinary morality. "Not for everyday use" signifies that the extraordinary can be employed. At the moment of exercising discretion, the pivot of success or failure—can this not be called great?

Xiong Guo says: As stated in *Essential Techniques for the Common People*, the white poplar is suitable for house construction. Lü Zhongmu says: A lord in distress who obtains a minister capable of restoring order—is this not like fish and water? A father who establishes a household who meets a son capable of sustaining it—does not their bond reach the spirits? This is the withered poplar sprouting new shoots.

Wang Ji says: Marshes can drown wood yet cannot fell it—it stands fearless. They can submerge wood yet cannot destroy it—it endures through the ages without distress. This is what is meant by the conduct of one who surpasses others.

**Hexagram 29: Kan (The Abysmal)**
*Repeating peril. Possessing sincerity, only the heart is free; in action, there is esteem.*

**The Judgment** says: *Repeating peril*—layered danger. Water flows yet does not overflow; traversing peril yet not losing its trustworthiness. Only the heart is free, for it is firm and centered. In action, there is esteem, for going forth brings achievement. Heaven’s peril cannot be scaled. Earth’s peril consists of mountains, rivers, hills, and mounds. Kings and lords establish peril to guard their states. The timely use of peril is truly great!

**The Image** says: Water flows ceaselessly—*Repeating peril*. The noble one thereby constantly cultivates virtue and practices the affairs of teaching.

**Line 1 (Initial Six):** Repeating peril, entering the pit’s depths—misfortune.
**The Image** says: Repeating peril, entering the pit—losing the Way brings misfortune.

**Line 2 (Nine in the Second):** Peril lies within; seek small gains.
**The Image** says: Seeking small gains—yet to emerge from the center.

**Line 3 (Six in the Third):** Coming and going, peril upon peril—danger and rest, entering the pit’s depths. Do not act.
**The Image** says: Coming and going, peril upon peril—ultimately no achievement.

**Line 4 (Six in the Fourth):** A jar of wine, a basket of food, earthen vessels—offerings modestly presented through the window. In the end, no blame.
**The Image** says: A jar of wine, a basket of food—where firm and yielding meet.

**Line 5 (Nine in the Fifth):** The peril is not full; it has just leveled. No blame.
**The Image** says: The peril is not full—the center is not yet great.

**Line 6 (Top Six):** Bound with ropes and fetters, placed among thorns—three years without release. Misfortune.
**The Image** says: The Top Six loses the Way—misfortune for three years.

Kan is water. The sage establishes peril to secure the foundation, loving the state so that the Way naturally opens and the self is at peace. Thus, the sage constantly fears that peril is not layered enough, yet through *Repeating peril*, freedom is attained. The worthy enter peril for the sake of the state, yet their intent is not to love peril—yet in the end, they bring calamity to the state and misfortune to their families. Thus, peril overflows like a flood, beyond salvation, and *Repeating peril* begins in danger. One relies on peril for security; another emerges from peril yet cannot—here the distinction between sage and worthy, the divergence of their teachings, can be observed.

**The Judgment** speaks of *Repeating peril* possessing sincerity, only the heart being free, and action being esteemed. The sage takes the world’s perils as his own defense. Viewing peril thus, he only fears that peril is not layered enough—what peril could he not emerge from? What peril could he not enter? But let this not be discussed further.

What is most regrettable is that all great worthies, in times of peace, do not treat the cultivation of learning as urgent, and when matters arise, they cling to the norms of reputation to sustain themselves. Once they have entered peril, only then do they seek a way out—by then, it is too late.

Thus, it is shown: The noble person does not detest peril but detests entering peril of his own accord. If one does not enter peril, what peril can assail him? Now, if one enters peril due to one’s own missteps, then one is already within peril—how can one hastily emerge? Therefore, the noble person does not rejoice in his ability to emerge from peril but rather rejoices in his ability to cautiously enter peril. Why? Because when faced with such unavoidable circumstances, there must first be a situation that can be managed, and only then can one calmly plan. This is like King Wen’s imprisonment in Youli.

Thus, once one has entered peril, one must settle into it without haste, without rushing to escape. One should treat peril as a pillow and delve into the depths of its abyss—this is the foremost principle in handling peril. It is not that one does not desire to emerge, but at such a time, to seek escape would ultimately be futile. If one pursues minor gains without realizing that the layered perils cannot be swiftly overcome, then one may fear that the abyss will become perilous, and in the end, one may never emerge again.

Only the sixth line of the third position truly grasps the Dao of dwelling in peril, and King Wen again raises it as a warning—its meaning is profound. The initial line represents one who enters the abyss of his own accord. Once one has entered the abyss, one is already enriched—who can one blame? This is like Chen Shu and Ouyang Xun. The top line represents one who has fortunately emerged from peril, yet is still bound by restraints, placed among thorns, and remains subject to three years of misfortune—can this be allowed to continue? This is like Li Gu and Chen Fan. These are all figures the world calls great worthies, yet in the end, they could not protect themselves, brought calamity to the state, and still prided themselves on their reputations for integrity—how tragic!

As for the *zun* wine and the *fu* vessel, this is like Shuzi of Wei. Before the abyss is filled, it is like Duke Cheng of Wei. The second line of the nine seeks only minor gains, so it has not yet emerged from the center. Now, the fifth line of the nine faces peril ahead, meaning the abyss is not yet full. If one points to the day of emerging from the abyss, it is as if the abyss has already been leveled. Though still within the abyss, the center is not greatly flawed. The second line of the nine seeks the companionship of minor gains, which is why it can emerge together with the fourth, ultimately without blame.

The *Commentary* states: Water flows without filling, traverses peril without losing its sincerity—this is the image of possessing sincerity. The second and fifth lines are centered and firm, with only the heart being free—this is the image. One abyss follows another, two abysses layered together, advancing without end—this is the image of esteemed action and meritorious achievement. Thus, the abyss truly bestows merit and power upon people, and its great utility in due time is evident. Therefore, the *Judgment* and *Commentary* uniquely emphasize the peril of the abyss, inverting it to esteem the abyss—this is fitting.

*"Pillow"* refers to resting upon the second line.

**Appendix**
Jin Rubai said: Among the eight trigrams, only *Kan* is the hexagram of hardship and adversity. Confucius once remarked: *"Was the author of the Changes not one who knew hardship?"* Thus, he arranged the nine hexagrams to clarify the Dao of handling them. Both yang lines are firm and centered, yet the second is not equal to the fifth. The fifth is centered, upright, and about to emerge from peril.

Xiong Nansha said: *"Fu"* (enriched), according to Wang Su, refers to the bottom of the abyss, beneath the *Repeating Peril*. The *Shuowen* states: *"Within the abyss, there is yet another abyss."* *Repeating Peril* refers to the combination of the inner and outer trigrams. *"Entering the abyss"* refers to the inner trigram’s interior, meaning arrival. *"Coming and going"* refers to the abyss within the abyss. The lower abyss has just been escaped, while the upper abyss lies ahead.

*"Wine"* is the dark water of the abyss. *"Fu"* (vessel), according to the *Erya*, is a type of jar. Sun Yan said: The earthenware vessel is also an image of *Li* (fire), like a drum or jar, which can accompany singing, hence the *Book of Songs* says: *"Kan! They strike the jars."*

The fourth line is even and empty, allowing light to pass through—this is the image of a window. The fourth and fifth lines intertwine, not through the door but through the window, as they are not in proper correspondence. Among the six children, the lines of the doubled hexagrams do not correspond; their meaning lies in close proximity alone. The fourth line of *Kan* is yielding, closely aligned with the fifth’s firmness—hence it is said: *"Firm and yielding meet."*

*"Zhi"* (only), according to Zheng, is written as *"zhi"* (paper). According to the *Book of Songs*: *"Wan is in the water like paper."* The *Shuowen* states: *"A small islet, not yet fully formed."* This explains *"the abyss is not full"* in three instances.

*"Hui"* refers to the thighs; *"jin"* refers to both thighs. *Kan* represents thorns. *"Placed among thorns"* means deliberating under the nine thorns. The *Nine Schools of the Changes* states: *"Those who harm others are subjected to severe punishment."* For those who can reform, the gravest offense is punished with three years of confinement, followed by three years of probation. Those who cannot reform and leave the prison grounds are executed—hence the misfortune.

Su Shi explained: *"Zun* wine and *fu* vessel"* are meager offerings. *"Entering through the window"* is the height of simplicity. Those who share interests become close without formal bonds; those who share hardships trust without prior agreement. The fourth line is not the fifth’s master, nor is the fifth the fourth’s shield. Though they exchange meager gifts and act with simplicity, they ultimately do not blame each other—this is the relationship between the fourth and fifth lines.

Li Zisi said: The top line rides below five firm lines, foreshadowing the calamity of being bound and placed among thorns—like a man trapped in prison, hands entangled in ropes, feet treading upon thorns.

Sima Junshi said: Water flows continuously, accumulating to form great rivers. A person’s learning, when practiced without cease, leads to great worthiness. Thus, the noble person constantly cultivates virtue and practices teaching.

---
**Hexagram 30: Li (Clinging)**
*Li*: Favorable for constancy, offering. Nurturing a cow brings good fortune.

**The Image** states: *Li* means clinging. The sun and moon cling to the heavens; the hundred grains, grasses, and trees cling to the earth. Layered brightness clinging to uprightness transforms and completes the world. Yielding clings to the center and uprightness, hence offering. Therefore, nurturing a cow brings good fortune.

**The Image** states: Brightness doubled forms *Li*. The great person, with unceasing brightness, illuminates the four directions.

**Line 1 (Nine at the Bottom):**
Treading with caution, reverence brings no blame.
*The Image* states: Reverence in treading with caution averts blame.

**Line 2 (Six in the Second Place):**
Yellow clinging—supreme good fortune.
*The Image* states: Yellow clinging, supreme good fortune, attains the central Dao.

**Line 3 (Nine in the Third Place):**
The sun’s clinging in decline—if one does not drum the jar and sing, there will be the lament of great old age. Ominous.
*The Image* states: The sun’s clinging in decline—how can this last long?

**Line 4 (Nine in the Fourth Place):**
Bursting forth as if ablaze, perishing as if dead, discarded as if cast away.
*The Image* states: Bursting forth as if ablaze—there is no place to contain it.

**Line 5 (Six in the Fifth Place):**
Tears flowing in streams, sorrowful sighs—good fortune.
*The Image* states: The good fortune of the six in the fifth place clings to the king and duke.

**Line 6 (Nine at the Top):**
The king employs military force, achieving merit by beheading leaders, capturing those not of his kind—no blame.
*The Image* states: The king employs military force to rectify the state.

*Li* is fire.
At the time of transformation, *Fang* says: *Li* means clinging. Clinging and then brightness—sun and moon cling to the heavens; the hundred grains, grasses, and trees cling to the earth. This illustrates layered brightness clinging to uprightness. How does *Li* benefit from constancy? The hexagram’s refined and luminous yielding clings to the central and upright positions of the second and fifth lines. At this time, the hidden is fully revealed, the myriad beings are each rectified, and all find peace in the constant rhythms of labor and rest, plowing and eating, digging and drinking. What more is there to do? Only nurturing a cow brings good fortune.

The inner trigram’s three lines form the lower *Li*. The first is the dawn of the sun; the second, the sun at midday.

Three marks the reversal into the years of decrepitude, when the funeral procession draws near.

For a person, the plan for a single day lies in the morning. Opening the door and stepping forth, each attends to their duties—this is the image of treading with caution. The second line attains the central Way, its brilliance becoming yellow. "Yellow brilliance, supreme fortune"—this is the great illumination at its celestial hour. Beyond this lies the reversal of the third, which cannot endure long. If one does not strike the earthen jar and sing, then the lament of extreme old age arrives, and ominous signs follow. Rejoice in the moment while still able, and do not forget that death approaches. Not that death itself is evil, but rather that one should cherish these twilight years and delight in one’s own joy.

The outer trigram’s three lines form the upper *Li*. The fourth, fifth, and top lines combine to extend the illumination. The fourth line, *yang* at its peak, is like the sun atop the ridge—its paired shadows dissolve, speaking of swift dissolution, of sudden eruption, of burning and perishing, of abandonment and forsaking. Yet it was never a matter of rejection from the origin; thus the fifth line says "as if." Should misfortune befall the people—like the lords of Youmiao or Gebo—who suffer harm, their grievances flow as tears to the great state. How could a benevolent ruler not sigh in sorrow? And if one eagerly seeks to rescue them, then the top line’s army marches forth, achieving meritorious triumph: annihilating the ringleaders and breaking their heads, capturing the living and presenting prisoners—all because they were not of our kind, and there was no other choice.

Such are the ministers and sovereign of layered brilliance; such is the work of great illumination. "Nurturing a cow brings good fortune"—truly no empty saying.

*Appendix: Conclusion of the Third Scroll of the Upper Canon of the Yi Yin.*

*Annotations in the original text are preserved as editorial markers in the translation.*

---

#1 Originally "夫曰," but according to the context, it should read "夫子曰." #2 "强" was originally written as "张," emended according to the Jigu Ge edition. #3 The hexagram image was originally missing; supplied according to the context. #4 The hexagram image was originally missing; supplied according to the context. #5 "白" was originally written as "日," emended according to the Jigu Ge edition. #6 The hexagram image was originally missing; supplied according to the context. #7 "自" was originally written as "白," emended according to the *Zhouyi Benyi*. #8 "才" appears as "未" in the Jigu Ge edition. #8 According to the Jigu Ge edition, a "危" character is missing after "人"; now corrected. #10 The hexagram image was originally blank; emended according to the context. #11 "于" was originally written as "干," emended according to the standard *Zhouyi*. #12 According to the Jigu Ge edition, a "涕" character is missing after "出"; now corrected.

*Appendix to the Yi Yin*
*Lower Canon*
*Hexagrams: Xian (咸), Heng (恒), Dun (遁), and Dazhuang (大壮)*

**Hexagrams of the Lower Canon (Continued):**
*Mingyi* (明夷), *Jiaren* (家人), *Kui* (睽), *Jian* (蹇), *Jie* (解), *Sun* (损), *Yi* (益), *Guai* (夬), *Gou* (姤), *Cui* (萃), *Sheng* (升), *Kun* (困), *Jing* (井), *Ge* (革), *Ding* (鼎), *Zhen* (震), *Gen* (艮), *Jian* (渐), *Guimei* (归妹), *Feng* (丰), *Lü* (旅), *Xun* (巽), *Dui* (兑), *Huan* (涣), *Jie* (节), *Zhongfu* (中孚), *Xiaoguo* (小过), *Jiji* (既济), *Weiji* (未济).

*Yiyin Lower Canon, Volume One*
**Hexagram Xian (咸):**
*Xian*: Smooth progress. Beneficial for constancy. Taking a wife brings good fortune.

**The Judgment (彖):**
*Xian* means "responsive." The yielding is above and the firm below; the two *qi* respond to one another and interact. Stillness and joy, the man submits to the woman—thus there is smooth progress, benefit in constancy, and good fortune in taking a wife.
Heaven and Earth respond, and the myriad things transform and arise; the sage responds to the human heart, and the world attains peace. Observe their responsiveness, and the nature of Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things becomes visible.

**The Image (象):**
Above the mountain is the marsh: the image of *Xian*. The noble person empties himself to receive others.

**Line Statements:**
**Initial Six:** Responds with the big toe.
**The Image:** Responds with the big toe—his aspiration lies outward.

**Six in the Second:** Responds with the calf. Misfortune. Dwelling brings good fortune.
**The Image:** Though misfortune, dwelling brings good fortune—submission avoids harm.

**Nine in the Third:** Responds with the thigh, clinging to what follows. Going leads to regret.
**The Image:** Responds with the thigh—he does not remain still. His aspiration is to follow others; what he clings to is lowly.

**Nine in the Fourth:** Constancy brings good fortune; regret vanishes. Coming and going in agitation, friends follow your thoughts.
**The Image:** Constancy brings good fortune; regret vanishes—no harm from responsiveness. Coming and going in agitation—its brilliance is not yet great.

**Nine in the Fifth:** Responds with the flesh of the upper arm. Supreme regret.
**The Image:** Responds with the flesh of the upper arm—his aspiration is not yet realized.

**Top Six:** Responds with the jaws, cheeks, and tongue.
**The Image:** Responds with the jaws, cheeks, and tongue—flattery flows from the mouth.

**Marsh above Mountain: Xian**
The Way of the world lies in responsiveness alone. The initial line, at the bottom of the inner trigram, aspires to the fourth line of the outer trigram—when there is response, the big toe moves. Above the toe is the calf, and above the calf is the thigh; when the toe moves, the calf and thigh follow. Thus, the initial line responds and moves, the second line follows its movement, and the third line follows as well. The second line corresponds to the position of the calf, and the third to the position of the thigh. Above the thigh lies the heart, and the fourth line occupies the heart’s position—though the heart is unseen, it appears unmoved. Yet constancy may bring good fortune and dispel regret. Responsiveness is the constant principle of the world, and regret or harm often lies within it. The noble person, moved by response, considers regret but never abandons responsiveness because of it.

Now, if the initial line responds to the fourth, yet the fourth seems unresponsive, why is this? Before response, there is no movement; without movement, there is the good fortune of steadfast constancy. Without response, there is no harm; without harm, supreme regret may vanish—is this not excellent? Yet the line anxiously probes your thoughts, and *The Image* further judges it as "not yet great"—this is because unresponsiveness is unnatural. The heart must be disturbed by coming and going; how can it not be bright and upright? Such is the mockery it invites.

Above the heart lies the diaphragm, and the fifth line occupies the position of the upper arm’s flesh. When there is response, the upper arm moves, though its movement is not readily visible. Above the upper arm lies the jaw, and the top line occupies the jaw’s position. Thus, observing the jaw’s movement reveals the fifth line’s aspiration toward the top. Otherwise, why would the top line expend such flattery? Alas! Responsiveness is the true principle—what need is there for words? Responsiveness is the true heart—how can it remain unmoved? Heaven and Earth are thus, and the myriad things are thus; otherwise, all movement in the world would nearly cease.

The initial line’s movement of the toe is responsiveness. The second line’s movement of the calf is not responsiveness but following—it moves in tandem with the initial line, which King Wen found blameworthy. Confucius said: Though the second line seems to follow the initial line’s movement, how can one be certain it does not respond to a response? Following the initial line leads to misfortune, so dwelling brings good fortune. If there is true responsiveness, then submission avoids harm.

Only the third line truly follows the initial line’s movement, hence it is said, "he does not remain still." When others move, he moves—not because of a prior response. To respond and not move is wrong; this is the principle. Movement from response is free of calculation, while movement from following is mere servitude. Thus it is said, "what he clings to is lowly"—like a servant obeying commands, not the conduct of a true person. Alas! If following is not responsiveness, then the world’s responsiveness is abandoned. Movement not arising from oneself—how can this be the true nature of responsive movement? Thus the sage values responsiveness over following, for response arises from oneself, while following is initiated by others. The distinction between self and other—can the scholar fail to examine this?

If there is response without reciprocation, then the world’s responsiveness is hollow. Spiritual response and spiritual reciprocation are the utmost in natural swiftness. Thus the sage speaks of response but not reciprocation, for response here is reciprocation there—they are one mechanism. Can the scholar fail to examine this? Only when response and reciprocation are one mechanism do they occur simultaneously—what need is there for anxious thoughts to deceive others?

The Old Bald One says: Above is *Dui* (☱), below is *Gen* (☶)—the yielding is above, the firm below. Mountain and marsh share *qi*, and the two *qi* respond and interact. Stillness and joy—this is the constancy of virtue. The man submits to the woman—this is the correctness of ritual. The Way of responsiveness is complete.

He also says: Response lies in the heart, and reciprocation also lies in the heart. When the heart responds, below it moves the feet, and above it moves the mouth.

When first there is a stirring in the heart, the toes move—yet if the center of the four (the heart) does not respond, this is no true emotion. Thus the sage cuts off all anxious thoughts. The fifth line resides above the heart, its symbol being *hui* (悔, regret); when the fifth is stirred, it moves the spleen, yet the spleen, like the heart, cannot be seen. If the spleen moves, the mouth must respond—thus the primal *hui* (元悔, primal regret) becomes visible only when the cheeks assist. For when the spleen is stirred, the cheeks must respond; when the spleen moves, the mouth’s speech must follow the knees. Though *hui* and *yuanhui* may not yet be known, how can the truth of response and reciprocation be concealed?

The essential teaching of the *Xian* hexagram (☶☱) is drawn from the human body. Not only does response reside in the heart, but the upper line corresponds to the mouth of *Dui* (☱), while the lower line corresponds to the fingers of *Gen* (☶). When the heart is stirred, the toes move at once—even though this occurs in the line of *Gen* (☶), which signifies stillness, one cannot force them to stop. To insist on their immobility is nothing but deceit.

Alas! Who knows that the transformation and generation of the myriad things, the peace of all under heaven, are all brought about by this response and reciprocation? Heaven and earth, even the sages, cannot stand apart from it—yet man seeks to adorn his emotions to deceive others. Thus I return once more to the *Xian* hexagram’s principle of response, and so I reiterate it.

Alas! This is the foremost principle of inquiry and learning. Why so? To deceive oneself fundamentally is to render one’s intent sincere. When intent is sincere, the heart naturally attains correctness without effort, and the body naturally attains cultivation without contrivance—how then could family, state, and all under heaven fail to be ordered, governed, and pacified?

**Appendix**
Su Changgong says: The male submits to the female, submitting only to obtain—this is necessarily the upright. To take and obtain uprightness is the advantage of the taker. Passion is its truthfulness. The thundercloud follows the dragon, the wind follows the tiger—without cause, how could they follow one another? How could there be falsity?

Zhu Zhonghui says: *Xian* (咸) means response. When *Gen* (艮) halts, the response is concentrated in *Dui* (兑); when *Dui* delights, the response is complete. Moreover, *Gen* as the youngest son submits to *Dui* as the youngest daughter, the male preceding the female, thus attaining the correctness of male and female.

Yang Jian says: Having responded to one another in delight, why then speak of halting? Is halting not also delight? Delight divided becomes two; halting and delight united become one. Like the myriad images in still water, the water remains constant while the images move of themselves. Like heaven and earth responding to one another, they are never not serene. Great indeed is the Dao of halting and delight!

Mu Bolai says: *Xian* means response. Without response, how could there be *Xian*? The Dao of response is not only so for male and female. The Dao of heaven and earth acts above and moves below, and the myriad things flourish, transform, and come to life. The heart of the sage is sincere in this and moves in that, and all under heaven responds grandly, harmonious and at peace. Thus, observing their response, the nature of heaven, earth, and the myriad things becomes visible. Passion is the true mechanism of heaven, earth, and the myriad things. Without response, how could it be perceived?

**Excerpts from *Xiangzhi* (象指), by Hu Shenyu**
The *Upper Canon* begins with *Qian* (乾) and *Kun* (坤) because heaven and earth are established in their positions. The *Lower Canon* begins with *Xian* (咸) and *Heng* (恒) because mountain and marsh communicate in qi. Position requires distinction, so heaven and earth are divided into two hexagrams. Qi requires union, so mountain and marsh are combined into one hexagram. In the eight pure hexagrams, none of the lines respond, but in *Tai* (泰) and *Pi* (否), heaven and earth respond; in *Jian* (成) and *Sun* (损), the youngest son and daughter respond; in *Heng* (恒) and *Yi* (益), the eldest son and daughter respond. In *Xian*, the youngest son submits to the youngest daughter, making it the most complete response—thus it heads the *Lower Canon*. It is also said: The *Shuowen* states, “The spleen is the backbone; the heart is connected to the backbone to stabilize spirit and govern the myriad affairs.” Hence the *Book of Songs* says, “The backbone’s strength manages affairs.” Wang Bi took it as the heart above and the mouth below, while Minister Lu identified it as the throat’s uvula. Feng He said it is the throat’s passage for intake and release.

Wang Ji says: Mountain and marsh communicate in qi, the mountain’s emptiness paired with the marsh’s moisture. Only emptiness enables communication; fullness obstructs it. Thus it is said: “They are hollowed in mountains and rivers.” The noble person, observing the image of mountain and marsh communicating in qi, empties the center to receive others. Emptiness is the source of the Dao. The eye is only empty, thus it can receive all colors under heaven. The ear is only empty, thus it can receive all sounds under heaven. The heart is only empty, thus it can receive all goodness under heaven. If the eye retains blue and yellow, its clarity is dazzled and cannot distinguish colors. If the ear retains purity and turbidity, its hearing is impaired and cannot discern sounds. If the heart retains essential principles, it becomes obstructed and cannot adapt to change. The noble person’s learning establishes the root through attaining emptiness. *Xian* is the response of the original heart. “Emptiness within, without self” is called uprightness; uprightness brings good fortune and eliminates regret. The response of the original heart—what is meant by “no thought, no worry”? “No thought, no worry” is not the absence of thought and worry. The heart moves directly, arising naturally. Though one thinks and worries all day, there is never any actual thought or worry. Thus it is said: “All under heaven converge in one destination by different paths, unite in one principle through a hundred considerations.” Scholars of the world cling to paths without knowing their destination, drown in considerations without knowing their principle—this is the response of restless agitation, not the natural Dao.

☴☳ *Heng*: Persistence. Smooth progress, no blame, beneficial for uprightness, beneficial for having somewhere to go.
**The *Tuan* says:** *Heng*? Endurance. The firm ascends and the yielding descends; thunder and wind unite, yielding and moving, firm and yielding all responding—this is *Heng*. “Smooth progress, no blame, beneficial for uprightness”—enduring in one’s Dao. The Dao of heaven and earth endures without ceasing. “Beneficial for having somewhere to go”—ending then beginning anew. The sun and moon attain heaven and thus can shine long; the four seasons transform and thus can endure in their completion. The sage endures in the Dao, and all under heaven is transformed and completed. Observing their endurance, the nature of heaven, earth, and the myriad things becomes visible.
**The *Xiang* says:** Thunder and wind: *Heng*. The noble person stands firm and does not change his principles.
**First Six:** Dredging persistence—uprightness brings misfortune; no benefit in anything.
**The *Xiang* says:** The misfortune of dredging persistence lies in seeking depth from the outset.
**Nine Two:** Regret vanishes.
**The *Xiang* says:** Nine Two’s regret vanishes because it can endure in the center.
**Nine Three:** Not persistent in virtue—someone may bring shame upon it; uprightness brings remorse.
**The *Xiang* says:** Not persistent in virtue—there is no place for it.
**Nine Four:** Hunting in the field yields no game.
**The *Xiang* says:** Enduring where it is not one’s position—how can one obtain game?
**Six Five:** Persistent in virtue—uprightness. For a woman, good fortune; for a man, misfortune.
**The *Xiang* says:** A woman’s uprightness brings good fortune because she follows one to the end. A man’s judgment of righteousness—following a woman brings misfortune.
**Top Six:** Shaking persistence—misfortune.
**The *Xiang* says:** Shaking persistence in the highest position achieves nothing great.

**Thunder and Wind: *Heng***
Fang Shihua says: *Zhi* (值) means duration. Since it is called duration, persistence lies within me—naturally, there is no need for forced seeking. The first line corresponds to the beginning of *Xun*’s (☴) entry—now is when one begins to seek. Without seeking, seeking itself is unbeneficial.

**On *Heng*:**
*Heng* means persistence. Since it is called *heng*, persistence resides within—naturally, its depth is not perceived. The first line corresponds to the moment of *Xun*’s (☴) entry, when one begins to seek deeper penetration. Without depth, depth itself invites misfortune. Only the second line, *Nine Two*, avoids this. Dwelling in the center, it is fundamentally deep; firm yet without forced seeking, thus its regret vanishes. *Nine Three* is overly firm and not centered; moreover, *Xun* inclines toward advancement, leading ultimately to restlessness. The third line of the hexagram resides in *Xun*—its lack of constancy in virtue is evident. If one still feels shame at this, it may yet be remedied; but the restless, self-satisfied person, should others defer to their disgrace, would find no place among companions or peers.

*Nine Four* dwells at the base of the upper trigram, neither centered nor correct, its *Zhen* (☳) movement already excessive. This stems from delusional expectations of gain, unaware that hunting yields no game—ignorant that no gain is itself a gain. Thus, the Master reverses this, saying: Even in pursuing game, there is a proper way. If one, lacking central position, seeks recklessly, what use is persistence? *Zhen* symbolizes the great path and the field; *Xun* symbolizes the fowl. *Six Five*, a yin line in a yang position, merely embodies constancy in virtue—yet this still invites misfortune for the man. For it knows *heng* but not the source of *heng*.

*

The *Xiang* says: The peril of being bound in retreat stems from exhaustion and weariness. That keeping servants and concubines brings good fortune means no great undertakings are possible.

*Nine Four*: A fondness for retreat—good fortune for the noble person, misfortune for the petty person.
The *Xiang* says: The noble person’s fondness for retreat means the petty person fares ill.

*Nine Five*: An admirable retreat—constancy brings good fortune.
The *Xiang* says: An admirable retreat with constancy brings good fortune, for the will is rightly aligned.

*Top Nine*: A rich retreat—nothing is not favorable.
The *Xiang* says: A rich retreat—nothing is not favorable, for there is no cause for doubt.

The hexagram *Tian Shan Dun* (Retreat) represents a time when the two yin lines gradually lengthen while the four yang lines withdraw their *qi*. Because this is a time of withdrawing *qi*, it is called "return"; because one can act in accord with the times, it is said that "retreat is *heng* (smooth/flourishing)." In this hexagram, *Nine Five* holds its proper position, while *Six Two*, a yin line in gradual ascent, responds to it. If one fails to recognize this as a time of withdrawing *qi* and that as a time of gradual yin growth, knowing only to maintain the old correspondence and move in step with the times, can this not still be called "retreat that is *heng*"? Not pursuing is precisely what makes for a good retreat. Otherwise, how could it be *heng*, how could it be great, how could it benefit the significance of the times and the act of retreat?

Thus, the noble person retreats from the world all day long, never for a single moment failing to retreat. Originally, this is not because one waits for petty persons to grow gradually before considering a return to seek *heng*. Would one die only when the time of gradual growth arrives? Hence it is said: "The significance of retreat in its time is great indeed!"

*Initial Six* steps out the door and immediately associates with noble persons. If the noble person does not recognize this, he too will readily engage with them—this is failing to retreat at the very beginning. Only when, in the end, he sees their hearts are not of the same kind does he grow averse and retreat. Is this not too late? Can he avoid peril? Thus it is said: "If one does not proceed, what disaster is there?" This means that if one retreats at the outset and does not proceed, what peril could there be? As with Kou Zhun’s recommendation of Ding Wei and Zhang Jun, or Qin Hui and his two sons—once they seized power and gained the emperor’s favor, only then did others grow averse and retreat, only to be ensnared by them in the end. When Zhang Jun said to Emperor Gaozong, "Only after dealing with him closely did I realize his darkness," the peril of retreating too late was most evident. In the hexagram, the top is the head, and the bottom is the tail, hence "retreat at the tail."

*Six Two* arrives precisely at the time of gradual yin growth, like Li Linfu of the Tang. When Linfu first saw Emperor Xuanzong trust Li Juezhi, he privately told Juezhi: "Mount Hua is most auspicious, fitting for imperial tombs." Juezhi then reported this to Xuanzong. Delighted, Xuanzong immediately told Linfu, who feigned shock: "Your servant did not know this, but Mount Hua is Your Majesty’s natal sign—how could it be lightly disturbed?" Xuanzong then demoted Juezhi and revered Linfu, allowing Linfu to firmly secure the emperor’s favor. Qin Hui of the Song likewise used this tactic to strip the three great generals of their power and win Emperor Gaozong’s favor. This is what it means to personally seize someone in court, binding their hands and feet with the hide of a yellow ox so they cannot escape—yet those bound still do not understand. How pitiful! Why have they not studied the art of retreating from the world?

*Three* is the master of *Gen* (☶, Keeping Still), sharing the same body. If one is bound and halted, the shared body falls ill, and peril must reach *Three*. However, *Two* and *Initial* are both long-standing servants and concubines: at home, *Three* is the master; in the state, *Three* is the superior. Though *Three* is bound, *Gen*’s firm yang resides above, naturally able to restrain the growing influence of its servants and concubines, allowing only minor matters and not great ones. What misfortune could there be? Since it is auspicious, what peril remains?

*Nine Four* shares a fondness with *Initial*, turning back while still inclined toward it. The noble person’s good fortune needs no explanation. Since the noble person fares well, the petty person naturally fares ill and cannot burden the noble person. If one waits until the tail to retreat, how can one avoid sharing in their ill fate?

*Five* recognizes that pursuit stems from fondness and thus alone admires retreat. This further reveals *Nine Five*’s will, which *Six Two* cannot firmly grasp. Hence, constancy brings good fortune. When the yang of right will honors what is admirable, the yin of firm will naturally has no means to sell its gradual growth. Thus, even if two yin lines daily encroach at *Nine Five*’s side, what harm is there? Yet only with a *Nine Five*-like ruler can one deeply admire a fond retreat. Such a ruler is rarely encountered in a thousand years, and the yang firmness of a thousand years will likewise be oppressed by the petty yin of small-minded persons.

Di Renjie’s service under Empress Wu, or Feng Changle’s conduct during the Five Dynasties—hiding in the marketplace, acting amid peril and chaos, laboring with the turbid and emerging with the clear—each had their own way of wandering. Tao Hongjing refused to serve the Liang, Chen Tuan refused to serve the Song—this was resolute. Yet Li Bi, in commoner’s garb, met with Emperor Suzong at Lingwu; Liu Bingzhong, in monk’s robes, met with the emperor at Shangdu; and in our dynasty, Master Yao, from beyond the world, aided the Jingnan campaign. Even his elder sister, a former acquaintance, refused to see him, saying, "This is the monk who kills people"—yet Master Yao had no doubt in his actions. Is this not also a sorrowful wandering? Alas! Only thus can one cross safely, with nothing not favorable.

Who but the "Nine at the Top" of Qian could bear this? Liu Yongxiang said: "The mountain stands beneath heaven, still and unmoved—what flight could there be? To halt yet wander is to halt in excess. With feeling yet without feeling—how distant it is! Following the high, descending to the low, adapting to the world’s ways—how could one not detest it? Unshaken by disturbance, unshaken by quaking—how stern it is! Thus do petty men regard the noble: as if the noble pursued them, only to halt. Yet it is they who halt."

**Appendix**
Su Zizhan said: "When yin flourishes in Pi and reaches Bo, the noble has never failed to dwell within its midst. Then, with two yin lying beneath four yang, yin still cannot overcome yang—yet the noble meets with misfortune. Why? The noble’s error is not one of abandonment or irretrievable loss; it is believed to hold the path of prosperity."

Yun said: "Among the sixty-four hexagrams, the fifth line is always the ruler’s position. Yet sometimes one in this position lacks the ruler’s virtue, while another in a different position possesses it."

Jiao Ruohou said: "The character *fei* (肥) was anciently written as *pi* (琶), resembling *bei* (輩). Later generations mistook *ling* (鸰) for *fei*." The *Nine Masters’ Daoist Teachings* states: "To retreat yet take flight—what greater fortune could there be?" Zhang Heng’s *Rhapsody on Contemplating the Mysterious* says: "Longing to take flight and retreat to preserve one’s name." Cao Zhi’s *Seven Stimuli* declares: "Taking flight to depart from the vulgar." The *Jinling She Mountain Stele* recalls: "Cherishing thoughts of flight and retreat." All serve as evidence.

Wang Ji said: "Petty men’s deep resentment arises from the noble’s provocation. Without malice yet stern—how could one incite hatred with harsh words or fierce demeanor? Speech is humble, conduct upright; appearance gentle, heart resolute. Seeking no flaws to slander, no blemishes to mar—stern as heaven, which none dare violate." In this hexagram, the first, fourth, second, and fifth lines correspond to four yang, governing with firmness—this is the image of "without malice yet stern."

**Hexagram 34: Great Vigour (Dazhuang) – Radiance in Rectitude**
*The Judgment*: "Great Vigour. Radiance in rectitude. The great is vigorous. Firmness in motion—thus vigour. Great Vigour. Radiance in rectitude. The great is rectitude. In rectitude and greatness, the sentiments of heaven and earth are revealed."
*The Image*: "Thunder above heaven—Great Vigour. The noble refrains from treading where propriety does not permit."
- **First Nine**: "Vigour in the toes. Advancing brings misfortune. Yet there is sincerity."
  *The Image*: "Vigour in the toes—its sincerity is exhausted."
- **Nine in the Second**: "Rectitude brings good fortune."
  *The Image*: "Nine in the Second’s rectitude brings good fortune—through centrality."
- **Nine in the Third**: "The petty man employs vigour; the noble employs restraint. Rectitude is perilous. The ram butts the fence, entangling its horns."
  *The Image*: "The petty man employs vigour; the noble employs restraint."
- **Nine in the Fourth**: "Rectitude brings good fortune; regret vanishes. The fence is breached, no entanglement. Vigour in the great carriage’s shafts."
  *The Image*: "The fence breached, no entanglement—advancing is esteemed."
- **Six in the Fifth**: "Losing the ram at Yi. No regret."
  *The Image*: "Losing the ram at Yi—position is inappropriate."
- **Top Six**: "The ram butts the fence, unable to retreat or advance. No advantage. Hardship brings good fortune."
  *The Image*: "Unable to retreat or advance—this is inauspicious. Hardship brings good fortune—misfortune does not endure."

**Hexagram 34: Thunder over Heaven – Great Vigour**
Fang Shihua said: "Only the Nine in the Fourth embodies the firm and active line, thus movement in rectitude brings good fortune. Movement dispels regret; the fence’s path opens wide, the carriage’s shafts are sturdy—how can one hesitate to advance? This is the great one’s vigour, hence *Great Vigour*; this is the great one’s rectitude, hence *Great Vigour*. Radiance in rectitude, greatness and rectitude—thus are heaven and earth. Without rectitude, how could one act? Thus the Nine in the Second, abiding centrally and unmoving, attains rectitude and good fortune. The First Nine, at the base and far from the thunder’s body—how could it hastily act? Thus its toes seem vigorous, yet advance brings misfortune. Even with yang’s solid sincerity, it will surely meet exhaustion. The Nine in the Third, though adjacent to thunder, still faces a fence—how can it recklessly butt? Overly firm and uncentered, it employs vigour directly—thus the sage says: this is the petty man’s affair; the noble must not act thus. When the noble encounters vigour, it knows only rectitude. Thus vigour may be possessed, but not employed. To employ vigour is perilous even in rectitude—like the ram butting the fence, its horns entangled. The horns are merely entangled, the fence remains unbreached—what is this if not restraint? Thus the image is borrowed to depict restraint’s form, a profound warning to the noble. Should one rely on the Six in the Fifth’s ease and overlook the error of employing vigour? Though the Fifth is easygoing and overlooks one’s error, does it not also overlook one’s merit? Emperor Wen of Han said: 'Alas! You did not meet your era. Had you lived in Gaozu’s time, a marquisate of ten thousand households would be nothing.' Thus he lost the ram at Yi. Emperor Wen, a ruler of gentle centrality, governed with ease, simplicity, and leniency. Though the many yang were vigorous, none employed their vigour. The ram, though sturdy, cannot be vigorous on level ground. Though the Fifth lost the ram, it remained unaware of regret—this is the art of governing, the method of halting movement. To restrain vigour’s force, leaving no fence to invite butting—is this not the excellence of the yielding overcoming the firm? Yet in the time of Great Vigour, one must not be complacent. The Top Six corresponds to the Third. The sage says: 'The ram butts the fence'—in its own line, this incurs the error of employing vigour, but in its proper correspondence, one must carefully examine the entangled horns’ hardship and the difficulty of union. It is not fitting to force it—unable to retreat or advance, advancing brings no advantage, and one remains forever burdened by the error of employing vigour."

After traversing the Fourth and Fifth lines, one finally encounters such hardship—like Yi Yin with Tang. Yet while the world may suspect him of self-seeking and fault him for his culinary guise, Tang discerned in him a minister of singular virtue. Though the beginning was arduous, the end proved auspicious, and one may indeed be assured that no lasting fault would ensue.

The difficulty of encountering *Great Vigour* lies not merely in heeding its warning against employing force, nor in resolutely adhering to what is beneficial and upright. Rather, those in high position must broadly open the gates of rightful conduct, lest they allow entangled horns to persist. For in the time of *Great Vigour*, when the yang virtue flourishes, the donning of caps and the tying of sashes are inevitable even for the greatest worthies. The sage, in setting forth this image, establishes a stern admonition, seeking only to guide them toward rectitude.

Yet those in authority who fail to "spit out their food" or "loosen their hair" to gather such men—like the lord of Han Wen, whose virtue was not without merit—ultimately leave Li Guang unennobled and Jia Yi to die in resentment. How could they be so reckless with talent? Did they not regard others’ skills as their own, cherishing the wise and sagely as if they sprang from their own lips?

Thus, the Fifth Line speaks solely of its misplaced position, while the Top Line further judges its failure to deliberate and act accordingly.

**Appendix**
Su Zizhan said: "In its upright greatness, the true sentiment of Heaven and Earth is revealed. To take the greater as the upright—this is the utmost sincerity of Heaven and Earth."

Yang Jian said: "*Great Vigour* in Heaven and Earth manifests as the ascendancy of the four yang lines, where yang qi is most robust. In the state, it signifies the advancement of the noble multitude, whose momentum is mighty. In virtue, it represents the attainment of the Great Dao. When Confucius reached thirty, he stood firm—unshakable in his vigour. The yielding of the world cannot be vigorous; only the firm can be so. Yet even the firm, if unmoving, cannot display its vigour. Thus it is said: 'Through firmness in motion, vigour arises.'"

The Nine in the Fourth, with its odd line obstructing its path, strikes the fence with entangled horns—an image of the Nine occupying the Third’s place. Moreover, at the culmination of the *Qian* hexagram, the petty man employs force. The Six in the Fifth, positioned ahead, is paired yet hollow, like a fence’s shadow.

Liu Junbo said: "*Qian* is the wheel and chariot; vigour resides in the wheel, and the wheel’s vigour lies in the shaft."

Yang Tingxiu said: "The Six in the Fifth, amid the flourishing strength of the many yang below, is able to pacify them. Yet it loses itself—precisely through the virtue of yielding compliance and harmonious ease that subdues them. Thus, it incurs no regret. The ram represents the four yang lines."

**Hexagram 35: Advance (Jin)**
*Kang Hou* is granted horses in abundance, thrice received in a single day.

**The Judgment** says: *Advance* means progress. Light emerges above the earth, compliant yet clinging to great brightness. The yielding advances and ascends—thus *Kang Hou* is granted horses in abundance and thrice received in a single day.

**The Image** says: Light emerges above the earth—*Advance*. The noble one thereby illuminates his own bright virtue.

**Line Statements**
**Initial Six:** Advancing, yet crushed—persistent, auspicious. Without trust, yet at ease—no fault.
**The Image** says: Advancing, yet crushed—walking alone in rectitude. At ease without fault—no mandate has been received.

**Six in the Second:** Advancing, yet sorrowful—persistent, auspicious. Receiving this great blessing from the Queen Mother.
**The Image** says: Receiving this great blessing—through central correctness.

**Six in the Third:** The multitude assents—regret vanishes.
**The Image** says: The multitude’s assent—its aspiration ascends.

**Nine in the Fourth:** Advancing like a hamster—persistent, perilous.
**The Image** says: A hamster’s persistence in peril—its position is misplaced.

**Six in the Fifth:** Regret vanishes. Loss and gain need not be fretted—going forth is auspicious, with no misfortune.
**The Image** says: Loss and gain need not be fretted—going forth brings celebration.

**Top Nine:** Advancing with its horns—only to be used in punishing its own domain. Perilous, yet auspicious—no fault. Persistent, regrettable.
**The Image** says: Only to be used in punishing its own domain—the Dao is not yet radiant.

**Fire over Earth: Advance**
Light emerges above the earth—its Dao is greatly bright. *Advance* is the time of brightness. Here, it is not called "bright advance" but "advancing advance" because the single yielding line ascends and moves upward, dwelling at the center of *Li* and occupying the Fifth’s position, thereby illuminating all under Heaven. Thus, it is called "advance." Moreover, the three yin lines of the *Kun* body are all lords who secure the state, compliant and advancing in unison to cling to the lord of great brightness—hence, it is also called "advance." Advancing thus, *Kang Hou* is granted horses in abundance and thrice received in a single day. The "three receptions" refer to the three yin lines.

Now observe the Six in the Fifth: as a lord of cultured brightness and yielding centrality, it broadly extends the earth’s measure, universally illuminating all. Toward the feudal lords of the time, whatever their losses or gains, it refrains from reckoning or contesting. Thus, those below are wholly seen in their sincerity, and going forth is auspicious, with no misfortune.

Though a yin occupying a yang position should incur regret, dwelling at the center of *Li* with its virtue greatly bright, all below comply—thus, its regret vanishes.

The Initial Six, though advancing, is immediately crushed—this is not advancing with the gaze of advance. To advance yet be crushed is to regard advance as crushing. The Six in the Second, though advancing, appears sorrowful—this is regarding advance with sorrow, not advancing with the gaze of advance. Dwelling in a yielding, compliant position amid the bright time of *Advance*, why should there be crushing or sorrow? Yet the Initial Six, at the lowest position, suddenly ascends—if there is something above that crushes it, it dare not advance further, displaying the utmost humility and deference. Thus, its persistence is auspicious. It may be at ease, yet without trust—though it does not trust the Fourth, what fault is there? Walking alone in rectitude suffices.

The Six in the Second, centrally positioned, advances in *Advance*. Though advancement may be joyful, its center is not joyful—revealing its outward compliance and inner emptiness. Thus, its persistence is auspicious. Receiving this great blessing from the Queen Mother—is this not fitting?

The Six in the Third, having reached the utmost of compliance, ascends in shared trust, its aspiration directed upward. Yet it ought not advance precipitously—only when the multitude consents may it proceed, for advancement harbors the seeds of regret, lest regret remain unavoidable.

The First, the Second, and the Third—all are compliant, all are marquises who pacify the state. From the Fifth’s perspective, all are deemed “obtained,” all are those of whom it is said: “Obtain and do not worry; to proceed is auspicious, while primacy brings no advantage.” Only the Fourth, with its Nine in residence, occupies a position not its own. With hardness lodged in advancement, it is not compliant. Active by night and hidden by day, it cannot advance in daylight—it is but a rat in the shadows. Dwelling beneath the radiance of *Clarity*, it dare not face the illumination of *Clarity*, thus forfeiting its own brightness. Yet from the Fifth’s perspective, it is precisely “loss without worry; to proceed is auspicious, while primacy brings no advantage.” If it cannot advance by day, how could it not be tolerated beneath the midday sky?

The Nine at the Top, excessively rigid, stands at the extremity of *Advance*. Taking the Fourth as its fellow townsman, it deploys force to subdue the town, unaware of the peril. Though it loves gain and detests loss, this is its good fortune; this is its freedom from blame; this is the way of rectitude. Yet regret and humiliation remain inescapable.

At this time, above stands a ruler who “loses without worry,” below stand ministers—crushed yet sorrowful, awaiting the multitude’s consent—above and below at peace, *Advance* unceasing, no leisure even to wield horns in victory. From this, it is clear: the defense of “three standards and five baits” would never be employed in an age of rotting grain and decaying coin. If only the world’s primal grievances are resolved, a single廷尉 suffices—why fret over rats usurping positions? Thus, the aged need not attend court with staff in hand—why bind the neck of the *Chanyu*? A Lu Jia is envoy enough—why fetter the head of the Southern Yue? Even if there were a young scion of Luoyang, a thousand gold pledged in a single vow, he would still die young in Daliang or grow old in Hebei—what profit lies in those who deploy force to subdue towns? Therefore it is said: “Deploying force to subdue towns—this Way is not yet luminous.”

Only when great clarity advances in broad daylight, its radiance resplendent, does compliance become effortless. This is why the *Kang* Marquis was granted steeds in abundance, received audience thrice in a single day. King Wen longed for such a time but could not attain it, hence his singular admiration.

When brightness emerges above the earth, it is *Advance*; when it sinks beneath the earth, it is *Obscured Brilliance*—how grievous! Liu Yongxiang said: How do we know the three yin lines of *Advance* are all marquises who pacify the state? It is King Wen, the Nine Marquis, and the E Marquis—who in all the world for ten thousand generations would say otherwise? Rightly so, then, that he had no leisure for daytime audiences.

**Appendix**

Yang Jian said: "The *Jie* hexagram signifies great transformation. The Grandmother is also called the Queen Mother, the tranquility of the king—likewise termed the Queen Mother. The *duan-shu* is the five-skilled rat, as Lu Mingde notes. The *Bencao* states the mole cricket is also called the *duan-shu*. Xu Shen’s *Shuowen* says: The *duan* is the five-skilled rat, able to fly yet unable to pass over a house, able to climb yet unable to scale a tree’s heights, able to swim yet unable to cross a valley, able to burrow yet unable to conceal itself, able to run yet unable to outpace others. *Xunzi* says: The *duan-shu*, though possessing five skills, remains trapped. Yang Lü’s annotated edition of the *Shuowen*—my fellow townsman Lou’s *Shangshu* also glosses the *Shuowen*—differs from the *Ode*’s *shuo-shu*.

Yang Tingxiu said: The second six, through its yielding virtue, encounters a lord of civil brilliance, occupying the position of advancement and flourishing. Abiding in the center and upholding correctness, it regards advancement with concern rather than delight. Thus it may attain good fortune and receive the great blessing of abundant steeds and thrice-daily audiences from its lord. The Queen Mother embodies the lord’s yielding center—this is the sixth five. Cheng Zhengshu said: The will ascends, desiring to rise and align with great clarity. To follow the lord of great brilliance—this is the shared aspiration of all. Wang Ji said: When the sun first emerges from the earth, advancing and ascending, it forms the image of *Advance*. When the sun rises above the earth, it is bright; when it sinks beneath, it is dark. The sun’s brilliance is fundamentally without increase or decrease—only a matter of being obscured or unobscured. The noble one, observing the image of brightness emerging above the earth, awakens to the inherent luminosity of the nature and substance, thus illuminating his own bright virtue. The noble one’s learning—whether called self-deception, self-entrapment, self-return, self-attainment, self-clarification, self-illumination, or self-knowledge—is never dependent on anything external.

**Hexagram 36: *Mingyi* (Obscured Brilliance) – Fire below, Earth above**
*Mingyi*: Beneficial to persist in adversity with steadfastness.

**The Judgment** says: Brilliance enters the earth—*Mingyi*. Within, civil brilliance; without, yielding compliance, enduring great hardship—King Wen embodied this. Beneficial to persist in adversity with steadfastness: this is to obscure one’s brilliance. Though beset by internal strife, one can rectify one’s will—Jizi exemplified this.

**The Image** says: Brilliance enters the earth—*Mingyi*. The noble one, in governing the multitude, employs obscurity to achieve clarity.

**Line 1 (Nine at the bottom):** In *Mingyi*, it flies with drooping wings. The noble one, in travel, goes three days without eating. Though there is somewhere to go, the host has words [of reproach].

**The Image** says: The noble one, in travel, righteously refuses to eat.

**Line 2 (Six in the second place):** In *Mingyi*, wounded in the left thigh, employing a strong horse for rescue—good fortune.

**The Image** says: The good fortune of the second six arises from compliance with principle.

**Line 3 (Nine in the third place):** In *Mingyi*, hunting in the south, seizing the great chief—yet one must not hasten to steadfastness.

**The Image** says: The will in southern hunting is to achieve great success.

**Line 4 (Six in the fourth place):** Entering the left abdomen, grasping the heart of *Mingyi*, then exiting the gate and courtyard.

**The Image** says: Entering the left abdomen, one seizes the heart’s intent.

**Line 5 (Six in the fifth place):** Jizi’s *Mingyi*—beneficial to be steadfast.

**The Image** says: Jizi’s steadfastness—brilliance cannot be extinguished.

**Line 6 (Six at the top):** Not bright but dark—first ascending to heaven, later entering the earth.

**The Image** says: First ascending to heaven, it illuminates the four lands. Later entering the earth—this is to lose principle.

**Earth over Fire: *Mingyi***
Both the *Judgment* and *Image* speak of brilliance entering the earth. *Mingyi* originally only signifies the image of not seeing the sun and sky, the world’s darkness. The topmost line alone directly describes what is not bright but dark: first ascending to heaven, later entering the earth—this is the image of not bright but dark. Like King Zhou, though a ruler of unyielding strength, what could he do but lose principle? To lose principle is to abandon the Dao—thus he became dark. In *Mingyi*, none is more illustrious than King Wen. From the first to the fourth line, who but he could have embodied this? Though possessing inner civil brilliance and outer yielding compliance, he was not exempt from enduring great hardship. Thus, in the time of *Mingyi*, how could one not persist in adversity with steadfastness, fully obscuring one’s brilliance?

The *Mingyi* of the first nine begins at Youli. Its image: in flight, it droops its wings; in travel, it goes three days without eating; in going forth, the host has words. What could the sage do in such circumstances? Only submit to heaven. The *Mingyi* of the second six is also afflicted at Youli. Its image: wounded in the left thigh, one cannot but swiftly rescue it. Having rescued it, one cannot but employ a strong horse. This is to comply with the people’s hearts and align with heaven’s principles—good fortune indeed. The third nine is the conduct of all worthy ministers. What could the sage dare to know of this? Only to comply with the multitude. In the rites of the hunt, great beasts are offered to the lord, small beasts kept for oneself. As the *Ode* says: "Privately I keep the fawn, but present the fattened boar to the lord"—this is the way. The third nine hunts in the south of *Li*, seizing the great chief to present to the lord. The unknowing deem this hasty, but the wise regard it as steadfast—this is the sage’s great fulfillment of will. How could it be easily discussed with ordinary people? Thus, by the fourth six, one emerges from confinement. Entering the lord’s left abdomen, grasping the lord’s heart and intent, one at last exits the gate and courtyard to achieve survival. This is what is meant by: only after being wounded here does one emerge. The image: the lower three lines represent the sun falling to earth, possessing the image of entry. The fourth line, positioned above the setting sun, possesses the image of exit. Thus, from the fourth six downward, all represent King Wen’s *Mingyi*. The *Judgment*’s "beneficial to persist in adversity with steadfastness" is fully manifest here.

When brilliance is not bright but instead becomes dark, even the sage cannot avoid entering the earth and being wounded. Yet when one persists in adversity with steadfastness and employs obscurity, even the most violent can grasp the heart’s intent and emerge above the earth.

Who would claim that the phrase "beneficial to persist in adversity with steadfastness" is not the subtle wisdom by which King Wen exited the gate and courtyard?
