The six in the fifth place, obscuring himself alongside the lord, truly embodies the *Mingyi* of Jizi. Feigning madness to avoid the deepest obscurity—yet people see only his obscurity, who knows how his brilliance never ceased? What is called "the more obscure, the more brilliant" was not merely unceasing in that moment, but persists through ten thousand ages without end. It is not that it does not cease, but that it *cannot* cease.

Xiong Nansha said: "Great peril is the peril of the world; inner peril is the peril of a single household." Moreover, *Li* (Fire) possesses the image of a flying bird. *Kun* (Earth) represents the belly, and *Li* also represents the belly. Lü Zhongmu and Cai Jiefu noted: "The heart lies in the left belly," as seen in medical texts. Scholar Li #12 said: "Great peril is external peril. Only external peril can be strategized against, seeking exit is possible, for obscurity and brilliance do not enter. If it is inner peril, one cannot exit, nor can one enter—truly perilous indeed." Thus, Jizi’s *Mingyi* never ceases in obscurity, nor does his brilliance cease. The Master specifically praised this, without needing to reiterate the warning of "persistence in adversity with steadfastness."

Heaven and earth have their times of obscurity and brilliance; the sage moves in accord with the times, and thus cannot avoid turning toward obscurity to rest. If one were to say, "I inwardly possess civil brilliance while outwardly appearing gentle and compliant, so I should not suddenly suffer great peril," this would inevitably lead to resentment toward heaven and blame of others. How could one know that inner civil brilliance must outwardly radiate light—how could it be concealed? Only when brilliance enters the earth, utterly obscuring its light, can primordial brilliance become visible, as with Jizi. This is deeply embodying the image of the *Yi*, aligning with the time of *Mingyi*. Thus, King Wen decisively recognized this as Jizi’s *Mingyi*, while viewing himself as deficient. How profound and admirable is one who knows not "persistence in adversity with steadfastness"! Were King Wen not a great sage, who could be moved to such self-reflection?

**Appendix**
Wang Ji said: "The sun rises majestically and sets in Yu Yuan—this is the image of *Mingyi*. The noble one observes the image of brilliance entering the earth and employs obscurity to become brilliant. For if one employs brilliance, one scrutinizes human sentiments, which leads to alienation and unease. Only by employing obscurity does brilliance attain the way of moving among the multitude. This is the meaning of 'hanging tassels and expanding light'—scrutinizing the fish in the abyss is inauspicious."

*Li* below, *Xun* above: *Jiaren* (The Family). Beneficial for the woman to persist in steadfastness.
**Judgment:** *Jiaren*: The woman holds her proper position within, the man holds his proper position without. When man and woman are correct, this is the great principle of heaven and earth. In the family, there is a stern lord—this refers to the parents. When father acts as father, son as son, elder brother as elder brother, younger brother as younger brother, husband as husband, and wife as wife, the family way is correct. When the family is correct, the world is settled.
**Image:** Wind emerges from fire—*Jiaren*. The noble one, in speech, has substance, and in conduct, has constancy.

**First Nine:** Guarding the household, regret vanishes.
**Image:** Guarding the household—his aspiration has not changed.

**Six in the Second:** Without self-seeking, engaging in central tasks, steadfastness brings good fortune.
**Image:** The good fortune of the six in the second—submissive and yielding.

**Nine in the Third:** The family sighs in distress—regret and danger, yet good fortune. The wife and children laugh recklessly—ultimately, humiliation.
**Image:** The family sighs in distress—yet nothing is lost. The wife and children laugh recklessly—losing the family’s discipline.

**Six in the Fourth:** Enriching the household—great good fortune.
**Image:** Enriching the household, great good fortune—submissive in one’s position.

**Nine in the Fifth:** The king approaches the household—do not worry, good fortune.
**Image:** The king approaches the household—mutual love.

**Top Nine:** Possessing sincerity and dignity—ultimately, good fortune.
**Image:** The good fortune of dignity—this means returning to oneself.

**Feng Huo Jiaren**
Fang Shihua said: "From the two women, the image of 'having a family' is taken, hence *Jiaren*. And it further emphasizes: 'beneficial for the woman to persist in steadfastness.'" Reading the *Guan Ju* poem, moved by the music of zither, se, bells, and drums, I know the sage personally experienced its fullness. The Master joyfully praised it, saying: "If the woman holds her proper position within, then the man must hold his proper position without. There has never been a man who could not hold his proper position without yet enable the woman alone to hold hers within." King Wen attributed virtue to the queen consort, hence he alone said: "beneficial for the woman to persist in steadfastness." The Master attributed virtue to King Wen, hence he added: "the man holds his proper position without"—how profound!

Today’s husband and wife are tomorrow’s parents. Today’s man and woman are tomorrow’s stern lords. If the positions of today’s man and woman are correct, then the positions of tomorrow’s father and son, elder and younger brothers, husband and wife—none in the entire family will fail to be correct. Thus, it is said: "When one family is correct, the world is settled." Alas! The settling of the world is seen in the family. The correctness of the family begins with man and woman. How then could man and woman be a trivial matter? How could the family be a trivial affair?

When Yao bestowed his two daughters upon Yu, this was how he harmonized the myriad states. When Shun observed his conduct toward his two wives, this was how he yielded the throne and brought order to the world. The matter is not trivial, nor are the people insignificant—how clear this is! For all who have families, how can they not consider the benefit of the woman’s steadfastness in holding her proper position within, and model themselves after these two women to hold their proper position without?

Holding one’s proper position without is to be a stern lord. Sternness does not mean sighing in distress or laughing recklessly. When one’s dignity is not forced yet appears dignified, when sincerity’s dignity arises without anger—this is good fortune. Returning to oneself to be correct, each resting in their proper place—this is dignity. Not esteeming sternness, yet the family all perceives a stern lord—this is what it means to hold one’s proper position without. Alas! Holding one’s proper position without—this is perfection.

Holding one’s proper position without, then the woman’s position naturally aligns, presiding over inner accomplishments without daring to overstep her duties. She but follows with compliance and yields to the five relations—this alone. To enrich the household is thus most auspicious.

She but follows with compliance and heeds the one who holds position. For compliance is the woman’s steadfastness, and thus the woman takes compliance alone as right—no other excellence. Would she dare, even in death, to employ her own strengths? This is indeed moved by the virtue and power of the one, and naturally, silently follows Heaven’s pattern without words—it is not in vain.

The primal concern of the five relations is not to add what is fitting with laughter. The *Shuowen* states: "The sound of excess, 'enriching the household is most auspicious.'" Yang Jingzhong and Jin Rubai have spoken well of this.

To take enriching the household as most auspicious, and to take enrichment as the power of compliance—when one holds position, enrichment is the urgent task of all who have families. Who would deem enrichment unworthy of the learning of the Dao? But one must obtain it by the Way.

Thus it is said:

The noble person cherishes wealth but obtains it by the Way.

**Appendix**
Xue Rengui said: "When clarity resides within, the household naturally speaks with one accord. Internally, there is separation from clarity, while externally, compliance and harmony prevail."

Wang Fusi said: "All teaching lies in the beginning, and all governance in the inception of the household. If one waits until records are kept before enforcing strict discipline, or until changes occur before implementing order, regret will follow. At the outset of managing a household, at the very beginning of its formation, one must first establish boundaries—only then can regret vanish."

Yang Jingzhong said: "The Way of governing a household lies in preventing transgressions at the outset, so that deviant and perverse intentions never arise. Only then can regret be averted. It is not called 'the absence of initial regret' but 'the vanishing of regret' because governing a household is difficult—difficult indeed to avoid initial regret. Emperor Yao was a great sage yet could not prevent Dan Zhu from indulging in licentious company. The Duke of Zhou was a great sage yet could not stop Guan and Cai from spreading slander. Thus, it is indeed difficult to speak of avoiding initial regret."

Wei Xuanzheng said: "The *Xiang Zhuan* states 'compliant and yielding,' referring to the second line rightfully submitting to the fifth. In all matters, one follows father and husband, never attaining one’s own will."

Xiong Nansha said: "The sound *dīdī* was written as *gǎo* by Liu Xiang. The *Book of Songs* says, 'In harmony like *gǎo*,' while the *Zhou Odes* express *xī* most emphatically. *Yī* and *xī* are both exclamations, with *xīxī* being more intense than *gǎogǎo*. It is a sigh of unbearable frustration, not an instruction to laugh. Since the Han dynasty, all have pronounced it as the reverse of *dīdī*. Wang Hui of the Song doubted that the *Chonggang* hexagram originally contained laughter, yet he alone grasped the hexagram’s true sentiment. The third line, being strong, makes its wife and children unable to endure, sighing in distress. How could this not bring misfortune? It differs entirely from the awe-inspiring presence of the topmost line."

Yu Zhongxiang said: "Holding position and responding to the initial line, submitting to the fifth while riding over the third, and aligning with the three yang lines—thus 'enriching the household is most auspicious.'"

Jingzhong added: "Both the second and fourth lines are yin and yielding, embodying utmost compliance. Compliance brings harmony, and harmony brings wealth. The proverb says: 'Ten people, ten minds—no wealth, only sealed markets. Ten people, one mind—wealth, golden markets.' This is true indeed."

Jin Rubai said: "The second line holds the central position, hence it is called 'compliant.' Responding to the fifth and embodying yielding, it is called 'yielding'—meaning it can enter into harmony with the fifth through the Way of compliance." He further said: "The fourth line is yin and empty, like the fourth line of *Tai*. Yet that one speaks of 'not enriching,' while this one speaks of 'enriching'—why? Because enrichment lies in the fourth, and thus enrichment lies in the fifth. The fifth, central and correct, occupies the honored position, while the fourth, by submitting to it, preserves its household. Hence it is said, 'compliant in position.'"

Yang Tingxiu said: "The fifth line, with the firm clarity of *Qian*’s virtue, resides in the central position of *Xun*, rightfully occupying the supreme place of the state and household, thereby influencing its family. King Wen as ruler, Tai Si as consort, Wang Ji as father, Tai Ren as mother, King Wu as son, and Yi Jiang as daughter-in-law—did they not love one another reciprocally? Thus the poet sang: 'He set an example for his humble wife, extending to his brothers, and so governed his state and household'—this is mutual love. Confucius praised him, saying, 'Without anxiety—this was King Wen.' This is 'no worry, auspicious.'"

Wang Ji said: "*Xun* is wind, *Li* is fire. When fire blazes, wind arises. Fire is the mother of wind. The noble person, observing the image of wind emerging from fire, understands that the root of moral transformation arises from the household. The root of the household, in turn, arises from the self. What emerges from the self? Only words and deeds. When words and deeds align, the self is cultivated. With self-cultivation, the Way of ordering the household, governing the state, and bringing peace to all under Heaven emerges from this. This is knowing the origin of wind."

---
**Fire above Marsh (38) *Kui*: Auspicious for minor matters.**
**The *Tuan* says:** *Kui*: Fire moves upward, the marsh moves downward. Two women dwell together, yet their aspirations do not align. Joy clings to clarity; the yielding advances and ascends, attaining the center and responding to the firm—thus minor matters are auspicious. Heaven and Earth are apart, yet their affairs are the same; man and woman are apart, yet their aspirations commune; the myriad things are apart, yet their affairs are alike. The utility of *Kui* in its time is great indeed!

**The *Xiang* says:** Fire above, marsh below—*Kui*. The noble person harmonizes while remaining distinct.

**Line 1 (Yang):** Regret vanishes. A lost horse—do not pursue it; it will return of its own accord. Encountering an evil person—no blame.
**The *Xiang* says:** Encountering an evil person is to avoid blame.

**Line 2 (Yang):** Meeting one’s lord in the alley—no blame.
**The *Xiang* says:** Meeting one’s lord in the alley is not losing the Way.

**Line 3 (Yin):** Seeing a cart dragged back, its oxen restrained, its driver branded and mutilated—yet initially there was a good end.
**The *Xiang* says:** Seeing a cart dragged back is due to an improper position. Initially there was a good end—this is encountering firmness.

**Line 4 (Yang):** *Kui* in solitude, meeting the primal man, mutual trust—danger, no blame.
**The *Xiang* says:** Mutual trust, no blame—this is the fulfillment of aspiration.

**Line 5 (Yin):** Regret vanishes. One’s clan bites through flesh—where is the blame in advancing?
**The *Xiang* says:** One’s clan bites through flesh—advancing brings good fortune.

**Line 6 (Yang):** *Kui* in solitude, seeing a pig laden with mud, a cart full of ghosts. First drawing the bow, then lowering it—it is not a bandit but a marriage match. Advancing to meet rain—auspicious.
**The *Xiang* says:** Meeting rain—auspicious, as all doubts vanish.

**Fire and Marsh *Kui***
Fang Shihua said: "Horses pair before moving. The first and fourth lines correspond—is this not a natural loss? Yet in the end, they unite—is this not 'do not pursue, it returns of its own accord'? However, the fourth is in *Kui* with the first, and the first resembles an evil person to the fourth. Now, if the first does not align with the fourth, this is *Kui*. Then the first is truly the 'primal man' to the fourth—what blame could there be? The lower follows the upper, hence the lower speaks of avoiding blame. The upper responds to the lower, hence the upper speaks of fulfilling aspiration."

Xiong Nansha said: "The fourth is called '*Kui* in solitude'—below, it can still stand alone; above, it cannot lack support—this is so."

**The second and fifth lines form a proper correspondence.** Thus, the second attains its "encounter in the alley," while the fifth personally relishes its concerns within its clan. For the second takes the fifth as its master, and the fifth takes the second as its origin—going forth brings felicity, so what blame could there be? *Li*’s center is empty, resembling an alley.

**The top yang and third yin lines are excessively solitary in their estrangement,** yet as they encounter firmness, they must ultimately unite. At first, estrangement breeds doubt. If the third were to see the second dragging its carriage, the fourth pulling its ox upward, and the top descending from the heavens to slit its nose—would this not be so? In truth, how could such things exist? If the top were to see the third’s mount as no ox but a mud-smeared boar, its load as no person but a cartful of ghosts, it would first draw its bow, taking them for bandits to shoot—only later to recognize them as its marriage kin, lowering the bow without loosing the arrow. Doubts multiply, and thus the third and top become estranged. Yet when they go forth, they meet the rain, and all doubts dissolve—hence their final union.

**Tuweng said:** Estrangement is the natural state of heaven and earth; estrangement resolving into union is their inevitable course. The *Xiang* commentary is exhaustive. Yet people in this world often create estrangement where none exists. Thus, some lines are fundamentally in correspondence yet become estranged—such is the case with the fourth and first. The first does not perceive estrangement; it is the topmost line that does. This is estrangement unseen, the estrangement of the highest. Some lines, though in mutual response, are not estranged—such are the second and fifth. When one goes forth, the other meets it; meeting, they unite; uniting, they flourish. Others are solitary yang without correspondence, inherently estranged—yet even they may come to see themselves as estranged, like the fourth line. Had it not encountered the primal man of the first yang, how could it have escaped the peril of solitude? When the fourth man meets the primal man, are they not two true men? Their mutual trust is fitting. Moreover, the fourth, upon meeting the primal man, immediately trusts him—this could not be so if it did not share the first’s aspiration. Thus it is said: "Mutual trust brings no blame; the aspiration is fulfilled." Once the aspiration is realized, mutual trust follows naturally.

As for the third yin and top yang, they are a proper union of yin and yang, never exhibiting estrangement or solitude—yet the top perceives them as estranged, as bandits, as boars, as ghosts, in endless distortions. The sweetness of *Dui*’s marsh turns to mud—how absurd! The third, meanwhile, sees the second dragging its carriage, the fourth pulling its ox, and the top descending from the heavens to slit its nose—how bizarre! They are not estranged, yet they see estrangement—such is the nature of most estrangement in the world. Hence it is said: "There is a beginning, there is an end." And again: "Meeting the rain brings good fortune." Was the rain not always the rain of *Dui*’s marsh? Did it ever lose its beginning or fail to correspond? Seeing estrangement, one calls it a beginning without end, inauspicious; seeing union, one calls it an end, auspicious. How grievous is the harm of perception!

In this hexagram, only the fifth yin line, centered and responsive, corresponds with the second yang’s firmness. Thus, the fifth goes forth to meet the second, and the second bears the fifth’s going. Is this not like Emperor Taizu of Song visiting Prime Minister Pu on a snowy night, where Pu’s wife served wine and roasted meat over glowing coals—the very "encounter with the master in the alley" and "the clan relishing the flesh"?

As for the first line, the marsh stirs below, delighting in the marsh’s sweetness and willingly dwelling in filth—it harbors no intent of estrangement. Thus, the horse may be lost, yet it may also return. If lost, let it be lost of its own accord—who could pursue it? If it returns, let it return of its own will—who could compel it? Such is its indifference to the world’s concerns. And this is not all.

The wicked are those no one wishes to see—yet are they not also human? Why then set oneself apart in estrangement? Fault is what all seek to avoid—yet am I not human as well? Why then insist on separation? Thus, the first line alone is called the "primordial man," and truly so. As for the primordial man, how fortunate is the fourth yang to have such an encounter! Below the primordial man lies the second yang’s firmness and centeredness—its firmness and centeredness that bring about the "encounter in the alley." And below that, does not the topmost yang’s firmness also meet? Without the top’s firmness, how could the third achieve its good end? Therefore, the noble person values firmness.

**Appendix**
Su Shi’s explanation: If one knows only conformity, then estrangement is inevitable. If one knows that estrangement can lead to achievement, then conformity is assured.
The Bald Elder says: If one knows only conformity, then estrangement is inevitable. If one knows that one cannot but be estranged, then conformity is assured. Thus it is said: In their shared dwelling, the two women’s aspirations differ, and in the end, each returns to her own place. How could they not be estranged? Yet when they are estranged, heaven and earth are estranged, yet their work is the same—they share the task of generating life. Male and female are estranged, yet their aspirations commune—they share the intent of mutual response. The myriad things are estranged, yet their tasks are of a kind—they share the work of nurturing life. How then could they not be in accord? Alike yet different, estranged yet united—from heaven and earth downward, none is not thus. Therefore it is said: The utility of the time of estrangement is great indeed! When it speaks of "small matters being auspicious," it is because yang is great and yin is small, and the image is taken from the two women’s differing paths. Yet only after their estrangement do they each find union, and thus it is specifically stated: "small matters are auspicious." This does not mean that estrangement is only auspicious for small matters.

Yang Jian says: The fourth and first lines are both yang, an image of two yangs fundamentally unresponsive, hence estrangement and solitude. Yet the first yang is bright, bearing the image of the primordial man—its estrangement arises from mutual unawareness. Once aware, their hearts would be mutually trusting. The second and fifth lines are properly responsive; if the fifth yin can employ the second yang, then the marsh’s blessings will cover all under heaven, and the people will all rely on its grace. The ruler should seek the worthy, and only then will the worthy follow—thus the fifth yin must take the initiative. Li Taifa says: Affection turns to estrangement through doubt; only firmness can dispel doubt and bring union. Thus the fourth ultimately meets the primordial man, and the top ultimately meets the rain.

Cai Jiefu says: The Dao is the correct Dao. This explains the meaning of the word "alley."
☶ below ☵ above: *Jian*.

Favorable in the southwest, unfavorable in the northeast; favorable to see the great person, steadfastness brings good fortune.

*The Judgment* says: *Jian* means difficulty, with danger ahead. Seeing danger and being able to stop—how wise! *Jian* is favorable in the southwest because going there attains the center; unfavorable in the northeast because its path is exhausted. Favorable to see the great person because going there brings success. Being in the proper position and steadfast brings good fortune, for it rightly orders the state. The timely application of *Jian* is truly great!

*The Image* says: Water above the mountain—*Jian*. The noble person turns inward to cultivate virtue.

**First Six:** Going brings difficulty; coming brings praise.
*The Image* says: Going brings difficulty; coming brings praise—it is fitting to wait.

**Six in the Second:** The king’s minister faces repeated difficulties, not for the sake of self.
*The Image* says: The king’s minister faces repeated difficulties—ultimately without blame.

**Nine in the Third:** Going brings difficulty; coming brings return.
*The Image* says: Going brings difficulty; coming brings return—inner joy.

**Six in the Fourth:** Going brings difficulty; coming brings connection.
*The Image* says: Going brings difficulty; coming brings connection—being in the proper position is substantial.

**Nine in the Fifth:** Great difficulty, friends come.
*The Image* says: Great difficulty, friends come—through central moderation.

**Top Six:** Going brings difficulty; coming brings greatness, good fortune; favorable to see the great person.
*The Image* says: Going brings difficulty; coming brings greatness—its aspiration is inward. Favorable to see the great person—following the noble.

*Water above Mountain: Jian*
*Jian* is the image of feet struggling to advance, hence it is called difficulty. The cause of difficulty is the peril of *Kan* ahead. This hexagram combines outer peril with inner stillness, so facing difficulty, one stops—thus it is called *Jian*. Yet seeing peril and being able to stop is called wisdom. However, stopping without going is the power of *Gen*’s stillness. The third line, as the master of *Gen*’s stillness, is especially relied upon. Thus all six lines speak of "going," showing that while coming is possible, going is not. Yet the hexagram repeatedly says "favorable in the southwest" and "favorable to see the great person"—why does it seem to insist on going? Precisely because seeing peril and being able to stop can be called wisdom in not going, but if one does not also speak of going to see the great person in the fifth *yang* line, how can one attain the good fortune of "going to attain the center" and "going to bring success"? Thus, while going into peril is certainly inadvisable, is going to attain the center or bring success also inadvisable? The position of the fifth *yang* is in the southwest, the place of central attainment, a peril-free zone where one should stop. Moreover, with *yang* in the fifth, properly positioned and centrally correct, it rightly orders all states and brings peace to all nations—truly one whom the four directions find it favorable to see. Only those lacking wisdom fail to recognize this, mistakenly believing the fifth line remains in peril, unable to escape danger themselves, let alone benefit others. Little do they know that while the world sees peril, the worthy of the second *yang* alone recognize it as a peril-free center. Thus it is called wisdom. Therefore, all six lines speak of "going brings difficulty; coming brings good fortune," with the auspicious phrase reserved for the top line to represent all five, using the outer to reveal the inner—a rhetorical economy. Thus, all going is difficult and inadvisable, while all coming is auspicious—bringing praise, freedom from blame, inner joy, substantial connection, or greatness with good fortune. Only coming is possible; going is not. Yet who knows there is an even greater difficulty, like the fifth line? While ordinary difficulty forbids going, the great difficulty of the fifth line paradoxically invites it. While ordinary difficulty is called wisdom for not going, the great difficulty of the fifth line brings good fortune and harmony precisely by going to join it. Here, going is stopping, and this stopping is true wisdom. The reason lies in the fifth *yang*’s firm centrality and ability to moderate. *Jie* means restraint, steadfast and unmoving. Amid peril, the great person of *Jian* is one whom all lines find it favorable to see—hence "central moderation." Is not the timely application of *Jian* truly great?

The first line, going nowhere and coming instead, gains praise. Praise comes from knowing when to stop and thus attaining. The second line, bound as the king’s minister, should not go—yet does not go not for self-love, but because minister and ruler share the same difficulty. The second line has pondered this deeply, hence "ultimately without blame." Only the third line, master of *Gen*’s stillness, can come, returning to the fifth, so the two inner lines rejoice in their shared aspiration. The third *yang*, firm and properly positioned, is substantial and great, so the fourth line connects with it, and friends come. The top line’s aspiration is for the third, the great person. With thoughts aligned, seeing the fifth line’s great person becomes effortless.

**Appendix**
Su Shi’s explanation: Being in the proper position and correct is the fifth line. The fifth line is the great person, thus standing amid peril yet able to rightly order the state. What others avoid—peril—he alone finds secure. This must be one capable of bearing the world’s greatest difficulties.

The disciples of Xiang said: If it is not peril, it is not *Jian*. Seeing peril and stopping—this is *Jian*.

Wang Fusi said: At the beginning of difficulty, in the initial moment of stillness, with unique foresight, seeing peril and stopping—how wise!

Xiong Nansha said: "Going" refers to *Kan*; "coming" refers to *Gen*.

Shen Cunzhong said: *Jianjian* means the fifth is difficult, and the second is also difficult. *Shuo* means great, distinguished from "great difficulty" by not using "great." Hence "aspiration is inward," clarifying its response to the third line. *The Original Meaning* says coming to the fifth would be redundant with "seeing the great person."

Wang Ji said: Ahead lies perilous terrain, behind stand towering barriers—this is the image of *Jian*. The noble person observes the image of *Jian* and turns inward to cultivate virtue. Mencius said: "When one’s actions yield no success, one should turn inward and seek within oneself." Turning inward takes the back of *Gen*; cultivating virtue takes the heart of *Kan*.

Zhou Yan said: Turning inward takes *Kan*—the image of water’s difficulty in movement. Cultivating virtue takes *Gen*—the image of the mountain’s constant stillness.

*Kan* below, *Zhen* above.

**Explanation:** Favorable in the southwest. No place to go—its return is auspicious. Where there is somewhere to go, early action brings good fortune.

**The *Tuan* says:** *Xie* is peril set in motion; moving, one escapes peril—this is *Xie*. *Xie* is favorable in the southwest—going forth gains the multitude. Its return is auspicious, for it attains the center. Where there is somewhere to go, early action brings good fortune, for going forth yields merit. Heaven and earth release, and thunder and rain arise; thunder and rain arise, and the hundred fruits, herbs, and trees all split their husks—how great is the timing of *Xie*!

**The *Xiang* says:** Thunder and rain arise—this is *Xie*. The noble person thereby pardons transgressions and forgives sins.

**First Six:** No blame.
**The *Xiang* says:** At the juncture of firm and yielding, there is no blame in principle.

**Nine in the Second:** Hunting, one captures three foxes and obtains a yellow arrow—steadfastness brings good fortune.
**The *Xiang* says:** Nine in the Second’s steadfast good fortune lies in attaining the central path.

**Six in the Third:** Bearing a burden while riding in a carriage invites bandits to arrive—steadfastness brings regret.
**The *Xiang* says:** Bearing a burden while riding in a carriage—how shameful! Bringing armed conflict upon oneself—who else is to blame?

**Nine in the Fourth:** Releasing the toe, friends arrive and trust is established.
**The *Xiang* says:** Releasing the toe—this is not the proper position.

**Six in the Fifth:** The noble person is bound yet released—auspicious. There is trust in the petty person.
**The *Xiang* says:** The noble person is released—the petty person retreats.

**Top Six:** The duke shoots a wild goose on the high bank, capturing it—nothing is unfavorable.
**The *Xiang* says:** The duke shoots the wild goose to resolve perversity.

*Thunder over Water: Xie*

Fang Shihua said: When peril remains unresolved, all going forth is *Jian*, and all returning is auspicious—one may return but may not go forth. Once resolved, not only is return auspicious, but one also attains the central position of Nine in the Second and finds stability. Though going forth, early action still brings good fortune, for one thereby achieves the image of resolving peril and merits success. Thus it is said: Favorable in the southwest—going forth gains the multitude. Where there is somewhere to go, early action brings good fortune, for going forth yields merit. Hence both *Jian* and *Xie* speak of being favorable in the southwest. Yet *Jian*’s southwest refers to Nine in the Fifth—the central position amid peril—while *Xie*’s southwest refers to the second, fourth, and top lines—the multitude of positions resolving peril. Thus *Jian* speaks of going forth as if speaking of coming, hence: "Great *Jian*—friends come to resolve it." Speaking of going forth, it focuses solely on going forth, hence: "No place to go." Speaking of going forth, it immediately brings early good fortune. Why is there no good fortune in "no place to go"? Above the second, one shoots the fourth—having resolved, one acts. Three make a multitude. Why does the hexagram uniquely place Six in the Third at the extreme of peril, with no correspondence? Precisely because it invites peril. Top Six corresponds with Three yet does not respond—if not shooting and capturing, it must share in the peril. How could it escape the hands of one who remains centered and unmoving? Observing First Six’s lack of blame and Nine in the Fourth’s trust among friends, it is clear that capturing it brings no disadvantage. Thus when Nine in the Fourth moves and emerges from peril, First Six responds, and no blame ensues. When Four moves to release the toe, First Six responds, and trust among friends is established. If one man stirs trouble, those who align with him all suffer its consequences. Once peril is resolved, those who align and those who respond all establish trust—this is both the natural course and the proper principle. Thus it is said: At the juncture of firm and yielding, there is no blame in principle.

Yet this hexagram is called *Xie* due to the power of Nine in the Second, who remains centered and unmoving, and whose success depends on employing the right person—namely, the duke who shoots the wild goose, a man who stores his talents and awaits the right moment to act. As for Four, it marks the beginning of movement. When in peril, one cannot act lightly; the moment one moves, one escapes peril. Thus Four releases its toe and acts. Once the toe moves, it does not stop again. Friends arrive, and trust is established—their kind all move in response. Yet Four merely enjoys the outcome and attains resolution; it is not the one who resolves peril in this hexagram. Thus it is said: This is not the proper position.

Six in the Fifth, yielding and centered, sees resolution and acts, then delights in saying: "The noble person is bound yet released—auspicious," implying that once released, good fortune follows without further action. This single thought not only establishes trust among friends but also among petty persons. Thus it is said: There is trust in the petty person. When the noble person is released, petty persons naturally submit and retreat. Once released and having retreated, what more is there to do? Otherwise, if trust is not established at heart, though resolved, it is as if unresolved, and the world’s affairs arise anew. Can one not take warning from the events of the Song? Petty persons initially wish to share caution with the noble person to preserve their wealth and status, rather than oppose them and lose their position. Only when the noble person’s resentment toward petty persons remains unresolved do petty persons constantly fear losing their position and turn to bite back, their poison deepening and becoming irresolvable. Thus the calamity of petty persons ultimately becoming irresolvable is always preceded by the noble person’s failure to resolve. This is the great mechanism of order and chaos throughout the ages, and thus the sage repeatedly emphasizes it here. Six in the Fifth, yielding and centered, occupies the honored position—one who can resolve entanglements. It no longer engages in perilous undertakings. Emperor Wen of Han is an example.

**Appendix**
Li Dingzuo said: Nine in the Fourth embodies *Zhen*, which is the foot’s toe.

Xiong Nansha said: In the *Nine Schools* interpretation, *Kan* represents the fox, and residing in the second position signifies the center. Yellow is the color of the center. Liu Yongxiang said: The second line, with one yang line horizontally interlinked within it, forms the image of the yellow arrow. The Master said: Attaining the central Dao is precisely what "obtaining the yellow arrow" means. The *Shangliu* (Six in the Sixth) occupies the top of the hexagram, forming the image of a high embankment.

*Yiyin Xiajing*, Juan One, concludes.

**Textual Notes:**
1. Originally written as *Gan* (感), emended to *Xian* (咸) according to the standard *Book of Changes*.
2. Originally written as *Ren Ge* (任各), emended to *Wang Lin* (往吝) according to the standard *Book of Changes*.
3. According to the Jigu Pavilion edition, a *zhe* (者) was missing after *xiong* (凶); now corrected.
4. Originally written as *de* (德), emended to *da* (怛) according to the Jigu Pavilion edition.
5. According to the Jigu Pavilion edition, a *zai* (载) was missing after *qian* (千); now corrected.
6. According to the standard *Book of Changes*, this should read *Xiang yue* (象曰) instead of *Chujiu* (初九). The preceding line, *"Chujiu: Strength in the toes; advancing brings misfortune; there is sincerity,"* was missing and has now been restored.
7. A *yue* (曰) was missing after *xiang* (象), emended according to the standard *Book of Changes*.
8. Originally written as *Jin yong qi jiao* (晋用其角), emended to *Jin qi jiao* (晋其角) according to the standard *Book of Changes*.
9. In the Jigu Pavilion edition, *ji* (季) was written as *nian* (年).
10. According to the standard *Book of Changes*, a *meng* (蒙) was missing after *yi* (以); now corrected.
11. In the Jigu Pavilion edition, *shi* (事) was written as *shi* (时).
12. In the Jigu Pavilion edition, *Li sheng* (李生) was written as *yu* (予).
13. Based on context, this *nü* (女) should be *zhen* (贞).
14. In the Jigu Pavilion edition, this reads: *"With the sound of great abundance, a wealthy family brings great fortune."*
15. In the Jigu Pavilion edition, this reads: *"Wealth while in office is the urgent task of a household in reality."*
16. The hexagram symbol was originally omitted, emended according to the standard *Book of Changes*.
17. Originally missing, *ge* (各) was restored according to the Jigu Pavilion edition.
18. Originally written as *yi cong jian* (以从蹇), emended to *yi cong gui* (以从贵) according to the standard *Book of Changes*.
19. According to the Jigu Pavilion edition, *neng* (能) was written as *er* (而).
20. Originally written as *zhong* (众), emended to *xiang* (象) according to the Jigu Pavilion edition.
21. In the Jigu Pavilion edition, *yuan* (元) was written as *zai* (在).
22. In the Jigu Pavilion edition, *shi* (事) was written as *nan* (难).

*Yiyin Xiajing*, Juan Two: *Dui* below, *Gen* above.

**Hexagram 41: Sun (Decrease)**

*Sun*: Possessing sincerity, supreme fortune, no blame, proper and steadfast; beneficial to have somewhere to go. What is it used for? Two sacrificial vessels may be used for offering.

**The Judgment** says: *Sun* means decreasing below to increase above, its Dao moving upward. Decreasing with sincerity brings supreme fortune, no blame, proper steadfastness, and benefit in having somewhere to go. What is it used for? Two sacrificial vessels may be used for offering. The use of two vessels has its time; decreasing the firm to increase the yielding has its time. Decrease and increase, fullness and emptiness—these move in accord with the seasons.

**The Image** says: Below the mountain is the marsh—this is *Sun*. The noble person thereby restrains anger and suppresses desires.

**Line 1 (Initial Nine):** Completing affairs and letting go of the past—no blame. Considerately decrease it.
**The Image** says: Completing affairs and letting go of the past—this accords with the will.

**Line 2 (Nine Two):** Beneficial to be steadfast; advancing brings misfortune. Do not decrease it to increase it.
**The Image** says: Nine Two’s benefit in steadfastness—its will is centered.

**Line 3 (Six Three):** When three people walk, one is decreased; when one person walks, they gain a companion.
**The Image** says: When one person walks, three cause doubt.

**Line 4 (Six Four):** Decreasing one’s illness brings joy in the aftermath—no blame.
**The Image** says: Decreasing one’s illness—this too is cause for joy.

**Line 5 (Six Five):** Someone increases it with a tortoise worth ten *peng*—it cannot be refused; supreme fortune.
**The Image** says: Six Five’s supreme fortune—it is aided from above.

**Line 6 (Top Nine):** Do not decrease it to increase it—no blame; steadfast and fortunate; beneficial to have somewhere to go; gaining ministers and a great household.
**The Image** says: Do not decrease it to increase it—this greatly fulfills the will.

**Mountain and Marsh: Decrease**
Decrease and increase are matters of the same moment. In this hexagram, decrease is spoken of first and increase afterward to show that all decrease is for the sake of benefit. If it were not for great benefit, why would one hastily decrease? Only when decrease brings sincerity, supreme fortune, no blame, proper steadfastness, and benefit in having somewhere to go—only when it brings such great benefit—is it truly decrease. Otherwise, why would one decrease? Thus, the question *"What is it used for?"* makes its meaning clear. Therefore, without increasing the yielding, decreasing the firm cannot be accomplished; without benefit in offering, decreasing the two vessels cannot be accomplished. Thus, decreasing the firm is for increasing the yielding—if the yielding does not increase the firm, one cannot hastily decrease. Decreasing the two vessels is because they may be used for offering at the proper time. If they are not used for offering, how can the vessels be hastily decreased? Therefore, when the time calls for increase, even heaven and earth, sages and worthies cannot but decrease to achieve their benefit. Only when it comes to increase is decrease no longer spoken of. Thus, in the *Judgment* of *Increase*, decrease is not mentioned; in its lines, decrease is not mentioned; in the *Judgment* and line texts of *Increase*, decrease is not mentioned. Only in the hexagram of *Decrease* is it spoken of—from this, one may know that decrease must bring benefit. And once there is benefit, it is as if no decrease ever occurred. Thus, *Increase* alone does not speak of decrease.

In the hexagram of *Decrease*, the first line speaks of considerate decrease, the fourth of decreasing illness, the second and top lines both speak of not decreasing to increase, and the third actually decreases the firm to increase the yielding of the top nine. Yet the third regains a companion—though it decreases, it is not truly decreased, but rather benefits. Though it decreases, it brings sincerity, supreme fortune, no blame, proper steadfastness, and benefit in having somewhere to go. Thus, one may say it decreases, or one may say it does not decrease, or one may say it decreases yet benefits. From this, the Dao of decrease can be understood. Therefore, the hexagram of *Decrease* is placed first.

Now observe the three yang lines of *Qian*: when one decreases them to benefit *Kun*, and *Kun*’s lines increase the top nine, it becomes *Gen*; when *Qian*’s lines decrease the three yang, it becomes *Dui*. Six Four, residing below the stillness of *Gen*, newly becomes a neighbor to the joy of *Dui*—its illness is clearly from *Dui*. What brings joy is that Six Four’s affairs are completed by the initial nine, which has long shared a sincere bond. If the initial nine can let go of the past to uphold this shared will, then Four’s illness will naturally decrease. For Four’s illness originally arose from losing harmony, so now, upon regaining harmony, its illness decreases. Thus, the initial nine incurs no blame, and Four also incurs no blame. Therefore, what the initial nine should considerately address is only letting go of the past—there is nothing to decrease. If Six Five were suddenly to increase the yang of the top nine, as if someone increased it with a tortoise worth ten *peng*, which cannot be refused and whose origin is unknown, this would bring supreme fortune. What need is there for the aid of two like-minded ones letting go to benefit it? If Nine Two were to let go, it would bring misfortune in advancing. Thus, it is said: *"Nine Two’s benefit in steadfastness—its will is centered."* This means the sincerity of shared will resides within; it is beneficial to be steadfast and requires no decrease. Then who is it that decreases? Is it not Six Three? Thus, when the three yang decrease one, Six Three becomes the walk of one person—it seems to decrease yet doubts its original companion. However, the upper trigram gains one yang, becoming Six Three’s proper correlate. Thus, though Six Three doubts the decrease, it is in fact sitting and gaining a companion—it too has not truly decreased.

From this perspective, the initial line does not decrease, the second does not decrease, and though the third seems to decrease, it has not truly decreased. The top nine, though it greatly receives benefit, is also increased without decrease. Its words—no blame, steadfast and fortunate, beneficial to have somewhere to go, gaining ministers and a great household—all pertain to the top nine receiving benefit and greatly fulfilling its will. The hexagram statement and *Judgment* are entirely consistent. If it regains a companion, how has it truly decreased? The three become the walk of one person—thus, it is also said to gain ministers and a great household. Or one might ask: Why does the initial nine speak of Six Four’s completed affairs? At the beginning of *Tai*’s exchange, *Kun*’s Six Four, though harmonious and aligned, though a neighbor to Nine Three, does not guard its center. Yet it sincerely responds to its proper correlate, the initial nine—thus, it is clear that the initial nine is the one who completed Six Four’s affairs.

Now, when Qian’s Nine Three suddenly diminishes itself to augment the top, reducing nine to form Dui, a single yin arrives to take its place—thus, Six Four loses its position and its harmonious alignment, its former grace now vanished. Is this not the malady of reckless excess? What is to be celebrated, however, is that the initial Nine does not forget its shared aspiration to ascend, and with serene resolve, it advances to console Six Four. Once Six Four again beholds the one who had completed its affairs, its affliction naturally lessens, and the one who was sent forth brings joy—this is only fitting.

Thus, in *Sun* (Diminishing), the initial Nine is indeed the one who completed Six Four’s affairs, while in *Yi* (Augmenting), the initial Nine is no longer the same, for it dares not overburden those below. As the timely transformation states: “Diminishing below to augment above—its Dao moves upward.” This refers to the lower trigram’s single yang now ascending, so that the Dao advances above, diminishing yet holding true. How could this not be supremely auspicious, blameless, correct, and beneficial for progress?

Therefore, the *Commentary* retains these words precisely to show that diminishing brings benefit. **Appendix:** Lu Bolai says: “Offerings require the full array of vessels; the use of two sacrificial stands is fitting only in the time of *Sun*, not as a constant practice.” For hardness and softness are the great divisions of the world. Diminishing the hard to augment the soft is fitting only at certain times. Diminishing and augmenting, fullness and emptiness—these move in accord with the times. The transformations of Heaven and Earth: when excess is diminished, insufficiency is augmented; when loss occurs, gain follows. If Heaven and Earth had intent, it would be only to move in step with the times.

The various masters say: In the rites of offering, displaying achievements with eight sacrificial stands is abundant, four is moderate, and two is meager. Xiong Nansha says: The formation of the *Sun* hexagram lies in the third line, where the top two lines form Dui with two yang. Cai Qing says: Two cowries make a *peng*. In antiquity, cowries served as currency, and a *peng* was worth two hundred sixteen. Wang Ji says: “Curb anger as if toppling a mountain; stifle desire as if sealing a hole.”

☴☳ *Yi* (Augmenting): Beneficial to have somewhere to go; beneficial to ford the great river.
**Judgment:** *Yi* means diminishing above to augment below, so the people rejoice without limit. When the superior descends to those below, its Dao shines greatly. Beneficial to have somewhere to go—central correctness brings good fortune. Beneficial to ford the great river—the Dao of wood proceeds. *Yi* moves and yields, advancing without limit. Heaven bestows, Earth produces—its augmentation is boundless. The Dao of all augmentation moves in accord with the times.
**Image:** Wind and thunder—*Yi*. The noble person, seeing goodness, emulates it; having faults, corrects them.

**Line Statements:**
- **Initial Nine:** Beneficial to undertake great works; supremely auspicious, no blame.
  **Image:** Supremely auspicious, no blame—those below do not overburden themselves.
- **Six Two:** Someone augments it with a tortoise worth ten *peng*, which cannot be refused. Eternally correct, auspicious. The king uses it to offer to the Supreme, auspicious.
  **Image:** Someone augments it—this comes from without.
- **Six Three:** Augmenting is used for inauspicious affairs, no blame. Holding true to the central path, it announces to the duke with a jade tablet.
  **Image:** Augmenting is used for inauspicious affairs—this is inherently so.
- **Six Four:** On the central path, it announces to the duke, who follows. Beneficial to rely upon for relocating the state.
  **Image:** The duke follows its announcement—to augment its aspiration.
- **Nine Five:** Holding true with a generous heart—do not ask, supremely auspicious. Holding true, it graciously bestows virtue upon me.
  **Image:** Holding true with a generous heart—do not ask. Graciously bestowing virtue upon me—greatly fulfilling its will.
- **Top Nine:** No one augments it; someone strikes it. Establishing the heart without constancy—misfortune.
  **Image:** No one augments it—this is partial speech. Someone strikes it—this comes from without.

**Wind and Thunder: Yi**
Diminishing Qian’s Nine Four’s single yang to augment the lower trigram’s initial Nine is augmenting below—this is the affair of the lord alone. Only Nine Five’s generous heart can truly hold fast. Thus, there is the good fortune of eternal correctness. It truly receives the treasure yet does not know whence the augmentation comes. With the good fortune of offering to the Supreme, it shares in the blessing and together with Five fulfills Heaven’s will—this is how the second line receives augmentation. But is it only the second? The third also holds true to Five’s ability to augment below. The second occupies the center, and the third likewise participates, thus both are called “central path.” The second offers to the Supreme, and the third likewise participates, thus it may announce to the duke with a jade tablet.

Now, when Nine Five alone augments below, all below trust it and truly receive its benefit. How could such sincerity and generosity not be evident? Moreover, this initial Nine is precisely the one beneficial for great undertakings, when thunder moves and wind spreads. With yang virtue so manifest, with such stirring power—how could the lower trigram now resemble the former *Kun*, thick and reluctant to act on its own? Its supreme auspiciousness and blamelessness are beyond doubt.

Yet when the lower trigram’s three yin lines suddenly gain a yang to reside in the second, the initial line naturally takes on the image of “someone augmenting it with a tortoise worth ten *peng*.” Only the second, gaining yang, is worth ten *peng*, so the third, losing yang, faces inauspicious affairs. For the third and fourth lines are neighbors, and Six Three’s possession of Nine Four was long established. Though now augmented by the initial Nine’s auspiciousness, it cannot avoid losing Nine Four’s inauspiciousness. Is this not “augmenting used for inauspicious affairs”? Yet while the third suffers loss in the outer trigram, it ultimately gains in the inner trigram. Thunder shares the same body. With yang arriving to reside in the initial line, yang Nine is ultimately Six Three’s inherent possession. Thus it is said: “Augmenting used for inauspicious affairs is inherently so.” This means it has not truly suffered loss, so it is not inauspicious.

Six Four may also be called “central path,” for Five occupies the center to augment below, and Four likewise aspires to augment below, aligning with the center in its conduct. Thus, the duke follows its announcement and may come to rely upon the state relocated by the initial Nine. Therefore, it is said: “Beneficial to rely upon for relocating the state.” Though Six Four is yin and soft, it truly augments its aspiration by following Five’s augmentation of those below—this is what it means to hold true with a generous heart.

The initial yang, being the yang that benefits those below, is already supremely auspicious—how much more so when the noble Five alone diminishes above to augment it? Its supreme auspiciousness need not even be questioned. Thus, those who truly receive its benefit ought to hold faith in its virtuous power. The aspiration to benefit those below reaches its utmost fulfillment here.

As for the upper Nine, though it possesses the sincerity of a generous heart, it seeks a response to its virtuous power. Thus, with a mind not merely passive but actively seeking the resonance of trust, its words are not one-sided. Hence it is said: “None align with it, and thus harm befalls it.” Without virtuous power, there is only the threat of attack. The retribution of cause and effect is manifest and inescapable. Those who govern must ever reflect on the generous heart that takes benefiting those below as its aspiration.

“Diminishing above to augment below brings boundless joy to the people”—this refers to the two lines Six Two and Six Three. Regarding Six Two, it may receive the augmentation of a ten-stringed tortoise-shell oracle; regarding Six Three, it employs augmentation for inauspicious affairs. Thus, from above to below, the Dao shines brightly—this speaks solely of the initial Nine’s supreme auspiciousness and absence of blame. The phrases “beneficial to have somewhere to go” refer jointly to the initial Nine and Six Four. The former is beneficial for great undertakings, while the latter is beneficial for reliance. The relocation of the state is how Nine Five’s aspiration to benefit those below attains its full realization. Hence it is said: “Central and correct, there is cause for celebration.” The phrases “beneficial to ford the great river” refer solely to Six Four as the master of compliance. As the *Xiang* says, “Announcing to the duke and gaining his compliance fulfills its aspiration.” Thus it is said: “The Dao of wood proceeds.”

The statement “Augmentation moves in compliance; heaven bestows, earth produces” encompasses the entire hexagram, clarifying that the Dao of augmentation invariably advances in accord with the times.

Wang’s *He* says: “Three and Two both receive augmentation from above and are able to announce to the duke and employ the jade tablet, so Three is also called ‘central path,’ meaning it aligns its conduct with Two’s centrality. Four and Five both take benefiting those below as their aspiration and are able to announce to the duke and gain his compliance in relocation, so Four is also called ‘central path,’ meaning it aligns its conduct with Five’s centrality. Thus, those below who truly receive its benefit—Nine One’s great achievements—who would know that what the above bestows is not merely its resources but its capacity to benefit those below? Six Four’s merit in relocating the state—who would know that the above’s generous heart values not merely the person but the aspiration? Only when the aspiration is great does the person become great. Only when there are great persons does the aspiration attain great fulfillment. The noble person may thus discern where augmentation lies.”

**Appendix**
Cheng Zhengshu says: “Announcing to the duke and gaining his compliance is achieved by announcing the aspiration to benefit those below. If the aspiration truly lies in benefiting those below, the above will surely trust and comply.”

---

**Guai (Decisiveness)**
*Below Qian (Heaven), above Dui (Lake)*
**Guai:** “Raising the matter in the royal court, trust in outcry, there is danger. Announcing from one’s domain, it is not beneficial to mobilize troops; it is beneficial to have somewhere to go.”

**The *Tuan* says:** *Guai* means to decide, to have the firm decide the yielding. Vigorous yet joyful, resolute yet harmonious, raising the matter in the royal court—this is the yielding riding atop five firm lines. “Trust in outcry, there is danger”—its peril becomes its radiance. “Announcing from one’s domain, it is not beneficial to mobilize troops”—what it esteems reaches its limit. “Beneficial to have somewhere to go”—the growth of the firm will ultimately prevail.

**The *Xiang* says:** The lake rises above heaven—this is *Guai*. The noble person thereby bestows emoluments upon those below and avoids complacency in virtue.

**Nine One:** “Strength in the forward toes; going forth without victory brings blame.”
**The *Xiang* says:** Going forth without victory—this is blame.

**Nine Two:** “Alarmed outcry; in the night there are troops—do not worry.”
**The *Xiang* says:** “In the night there are troops—do not worry”—this is attaining the central Dao.

**Nine Three:** “Strength in the cheeks—there is misfortune. The noble person is resolute and forthright. Walking alone, he encounters rain; as if drenched, he is displeased—yet there is no blame.”
**The *Xiang* says:** “The noble person is resolute and forthright”—in the end, there is no blame.

**Nine Four:** “The buttocks lack flesh; its movement is halting. Leading a ram, regret vanishes—but hearing words, it does not trust.”
**The *Xiang* says:** “Its movement is halting”—its position is not appropriate. “Hearing words, it does not trust”—its hearing is not enlightened.

**Nine Five:** “The *xianlu* plant is resolute and forthright; the central path brings no blame.”
**The *Xiang* says:** “The central path brings no blame”—the center has not yet radiated.

**Upper Six:** “Excessive outcry—ultimately there is misfortune.”
**The *Xiang* says:** “Excessive outcry brings misfortune”—it cannot endure for long.

**Lake over Heaven: *Guai***
Five yang lines decisively act below, while one yin line compliantly listens above—this is *Guai*. It speaks of the yielding and soft being unable to decide, relying on the five yang to resolve it, hence it is called *guai* (decisiveness). But how do we know it can resolve? Because this hexagram is vigorous yet able to be joyful. Thus, resolute yet able to harmonize: joyfulness gives rise to the aspiration for resonance, and its vigor can be enacted; harmony gives rise to the sincerity of the lake’s joy, and its resolution can penetrate.

The single yin above desires to resolve but cannot, while the five yang below cannot but resolve. For the five yang, this is truly difficult. With one yielding riding atop five firm lines, the five yang advance together and raise the matter in the royal court—this is the height of flourishing. “Trust in outcry, there is danger”—this refers to the correspondence between Upper Six and Nine Three. Dui is the mouth; Upper Six occupies the mouth’s position, having a mouth yet crying excessively—its yielding weakness is evident. If it were to cry out, it would surely do so first, prompting Nine Three to walk resolutely and forthrightly alone to meet it. How could Three fear the displeasure of the many yang? Yet it would bring peril and danger upon Upper Six. Why? If Upper Six could cry out to Three, Three would surely respond. Precisely its danger is what makes it radiant—this is the Dao of the ruler. Hence it is said: “Trust in outcry, there is danger; its peril becomes its radiance.” Alas, though it has a mouth and cries excessively, even if it sought peril, it could not attain it. Excessive outcry ultimately brings misfortune—how could it be merely peril and danger?

Its end cannot be prolonged—how could it be merely blame? From Nine Three’s case, one sees clearly that the five yang lines reveal Upper Six’s inadequacy for action. Thus, the Second, situated amidst the many yang, deems itself unfit for responsibility—why should it fear the warfare of dusk and night? The warfare of dusk and night is the theft of twilight. Yet since there is a master, the Second rests content in the center below. Though dusk and night bring warfare, it remains untroubled, vigilant only lest Upper Six’s outcry bring peril upon itself. Therefore, the sage praises its attainment of the central Dao. How could it resemble the reckless advance of the initial yang, unaware that its going cannot prevail and will only incur blame? That Nine Five is precisely the foremost figure extolled in the royal court—one who acts with centrality and incurs no blame, resolutely attending to affairs above, like Zhuge Liang or Jiang Wan. Even Zhuge Liang was hard to come by. Yet now, observing Nine Five’s centrality, which lacks radiance, one sees it falls far short of Zhuge Liang’s stature. Thus, Upper Six persists until the rigid and prolonged ultimately end—like Emperor Xian of Han meeting Cao Pi. One then understands that weak yin is difficult to support: even with the abilities of Yi Yin or the Duke of Zhou, *Guai* still requires the abilities of Tai Jia or King Cheng. The trustful outcry is the constant Dao of the noble person. I have indeed been moved by this resolute forthrightness.

It is said: “Announce within your own domain; it is not advantageous to resort to arms.” This speaks of the time when the five yang have gained ground and the earthly domain of *Kun* is nearly exhausted. One can only issue commands within one’s domain—who would raise arms? What is esteemed has reached its limit; even with five yang, what can be done? Yet it remains advantageous to have somewhere to go, for the five yang are all noble persons, not yet plotting usurpation. They merely await its natural end. Thus, the sage again clarifies the Dao of *Guai*’s decisive action in the six lines.

The First Nine, “vigorous in the forward toe,” incurs blame for going without prevailing—this is an excess of decisiveness, which must not be. The Second Nine, “untroubled,” knows the time and dares not act decisively—this is one who attains the center. The Third Nine, doubly rigid and vigorous, its decisiveness evident in its countenance, bears the image of vigor, yet in compliance. If vigor in the toe still brings blame, how much more perilous is vigor in compliance? Yet this Third, treading *Qian* while embracing *Dui*, stands precisely at the time of robust strength. Thus, the many yang respond to it, resenting or approving, and when it alone meets them, the two seem moistened—its yin and yang harmonious and moist, its vigor and joy following one another. Though resolute, it is not excessive—this is the noble person’s decisive action. Alas, Upper Six does not know to seek its alliance! Hence it is said: “The noble person decisively decisive.”

As for the Fourth, it is not that it does not wish to act decisively, but being yang in a yin position, it bears the image of excess and hesitation, unable to advance. Following the sheep from behind, it may perhaps avoid the regret of being led. Yet since it is not central or correct, it lacks clarity. Its following the sheep from behind, hindered by hesitation, is not due to hearing and believing, nor does it thereby avoid the regret of being led. Only Nine Five, closely approaching Upper Six with solitary yin above, can last but a short while. This is like tender amaranth treading upon hard ground—how could it not be resolute? Yet at this time, resolute though it may be, I know Nine Five cannot achieve greatness in central radiance. How difficult is Nine Three’s position! Nine Three, in correspondence, still shares the state’s concerns. Nine Five, bearing Upper Six, harbors the intent to await its inauspicious end. Alas, few ministers at such a juncture would not salivate. I hold that Yi Yin, the Duke of Zhou, and Lord Zhuge—these three great sages—are worthy of reverence.

**Appendix**
Xiong Nansha says: *Kun* is the domain.

When the five yin lines all transform, leaving only Upper Six, the domain of the land is diminished.

Fan Daxing says: Warfare requires multitudes. When *Kun* transforms to the fifth line, it nears the extremity of the multitude, hence "not advantageous to engage in warfare."

Ding Yidong says: Four yang lines constitute "strength," five yang lines constitute "resoluteness." The initial line of *Great Strength* is called "strength of the toe," thus it is distinguished by adding "resolute" to the preceding "strength."

Zhai Xuan says: Before the head of *Qian* lies compliance.

"Solitary action" refers to all lines being without correspondence, while only the third line corresponds with the uppermost, hence "solitary."

Upper Six is the master of *Completion of Joy*. Moreover, it ascends to the marsh of heaven, thus "encountering rain."

Li Tuweng says: The sage’s profound care for the Dao of yang is most earnest, hence the utmost warnings to those of yang firmness. From the first stirring of a single yang in *Return*, it is said: "The day closes the gates, merchants and travelers do not proceed, the lord does not inspect the regions"—all for protection and nurturance, lest it be harmed. The sage’s heart is thus revealed. When two yang lines show signs of gradual growth, the sage rejoices in their approach to the four yin, yet immediately says: "As for the eighth month, there will be misfortune." While it is growing, he already considers its decline—this is not true haste in its dissipation. Only because he rejoices in its growth does he immediately contemplate its decline. Here again, the sage’s desire for its growth is most evident. When three yang lines form *Peace*, the flourishing of *Peace*’s exchange has been esteemed since antiquity—this goes without saying. At four yang lines, it becomes *Great Strength*. Yet while yang delights in strength, it utterly disdains the *use* of strength. For to use strength is the affair of petty men; the noble man dares not act thus. Hence it is said: "The petty man uses strength; the noble man does not." If a noble man were to use strength, he would be but a ram—entangling his horns in the hedge, what gain is there in strength? If the strength of four yang lines is cautioned against so earnestly, how could the resoluteness of five yang lines be taken lightly? In all these five hexagrams, the sage’s deep love for yang is manifest. Those who possess the virtue of yang firmness should take care not to lightly employ their firmness, but rather strive to deeply embody the sage’s intent.

Below *Xun*, above *Qian*: *Gou* (*Encounter*). "The woman is strong; do not take this woman."

The *Tuan* says: *Gou* means encounter—softness encounters firmness. "Do not take this woman," for she cannot be partnered for long. When heaven and earth encounter, the beings of all things become manifest. When firmness encounters central correctness, great undertakings proceed throughout the world. The significance of *Gou*’s time is vast indeed!

The *Image* says: Wind beneath heaven—*Gou*. The lord thereby issues commands and proclaims to the four directions.

**First Six:** Bound to a metal *ni*, steadfastness brings good fortune. To have somewhere to go—seeing misfortune, the emaciated pig struggles and hesitates.
The *Image* says: "Bound to a metal *ni*," for the soft way is restrained.

**Nine in the Second:** Enveloping the fish—no blame. Not advantageous for guests.
The *Image* says: "Enveloping the fish"—rightness does not extend to guests.

**Nine in the Third:** The buttocks lack skin; its movement is halting and difficult. Danger—no great blame.
The *Image* says: "Its movement is halting and difficult"—its progress is not yet restrained.

**Nine in the Fourth:** Enveloping no fish—arising misfortune.
The *Image* says: "Misfortune of no fish"—distancing the people.

**Nine in the Fifth:** With *qi* to envelop the melon, containing radiance—there is a falling from heaven.
The *Image* says: "Nine in the Fifth containing radiance"—central correctness. "There is a falling from heaven"—the will does not abandon the mandate.

**Upper Nine:** Encountering its horns—regret, no blame.
The *Image* says: "Encountering its horns"—exhaustion above brings regret.

**Heaven and Wind: Gou**—*Gou* means encounter. This hexagram, originally all yang, has a single yin beginning below, thus it is called "encounter"—softness encounters firmness, earth encounters heaven, the first six, a single yin, encounters five yang. When one soft and one firm encounter, they naturally form a pair; when above is heaven and below is earth, they naturally manifest all things. But here, a single yin encounters five yang—this woman is moreover strong—what is to be done? The sage says: *Xun* is the eldest daughter. The woman is already strong—do not take this woman. For if one does not take this woman, she cannot be partnered for long. Hence it is said: "Do not take this woman, for she cannot be partnered for long."

Jin Shuxiang’s mother said: "In deep mountains and great marshes, dragons and serpents are truly born. That beauty—I fear she will bear dragons and serpents to bring calamity to you." If so, is this woman not one who truly gives birth to dragons and serpents, bringing disaster to your state and misfortune to your house? That she cannot be taken is abundantly clear. Fortunately, there is the firmness of Nine in the Second—this woman encounters it like encountering a metal *ni*. Yet once encountered, she is bound and stopped, resolutely prevented from going further. The pig, the fish, and the melon are all yin things, all referring to the first line. Thus, at the first line, it is called the "emaciated pig." "Emaciated" is like the "emaciated horns" entangled in the hedge—struggling and emaciated, desiring to go somewhere but not accepting restraint. How could it know that to have somewhere to go is to see misfortune, and that it must not be allowed to go anywhere? This is why being bound to the metal *ni* is steadfast and auspicious. Thus, at the second line, it again speaks of "enveloping the fish." A fish must have water to live—how could enveloping it allow it to live? Yet Nine in the Second must envelop it to avoid blame. The fish is not advantageous for guests, hence rightness does not allow this fish to reach guests. Thus, it is better to envelop and confine it than to pass on misfortune. But how to account for Nine in the Fourth’s desire to "arise misfortune"? "No fish" means no misfortune. Yet Nine in the Fourth, taking itself as the guest of the first line, desires to "arise misfortune"—what can Nine in the Second do? Moreover, Nine in the Fourth occupies a position near the ruler, holding to the doctrine of proper correspondence. "No fish" gives rise to misfortune, distancing the people and provoking disputes. Though Nine in the Second is a metal *ni*, I fear it will ultimately be unable to restrain it. Thus, the reason Nine in the Fourth ultimately does not "arise misfortune"—is it not entirely due to the central correctness of Nine in the Fifth?

Thus, at the Fifth Line, it speaks of "enveloping the melon." Now, the melon is inherently a perishable thing; when enveloped, it perishes all the more readily. Yet Nine in the Fifth, being central and correct, uniquely embraces the splendid virtue of Nine in the Second, trusting in its central constancy. It then employs Nine in the Second’s *qi* to envelop the melon of Initial Six. Since the melon has already fallen from heaven, Nine in the Fourth is thus able to petition heaven and avoid arising misfortune—how profoundly auspicious! Heaven is *Qian*, the Fifth. Nine in the Second, by this means, preserves its firm righteousness; Nine in the Third, by this means, avoids being entangled in action; and the Top Line, by this means, though exhausted, encounters no misfortune and so remains free of blame. From this, we know that Nine in the Fifth’s resolve truly cannot contain the mandate of its kindred line to such an extent. Alas! When a ruler of central correctness and a minister of firm centrality meet and form a splendid union, it reaches this degree. Then is there anything under heaven that cannot be accomplished? Thus it is said: "When the firm encounters the central and correct, great undertakings prevail throughout the world." The timeliness of *Sheng* is indeed momentous!

Nine in the Third, situated below *Qian* in the symbol of the buttocks, with *Xun* representing hesitation and indecision, and dwelling above *Xun* in the position of "next and nearly," its symbol being "next and nearly," thus its actions are not yet entangled with Initial Six and so it avoids great blame. Otherwise, how could danger be averted?

Herein lies a difficulty: when the first *yin* arises, it is *Gou*; when the second *yin* gradually grows, it becomes *Dun*. *Dun* does not mean "to flee" but "to restrain." Since the world cannot tolerate the primal *yin*, the firm and yang must not be idle. In *Dun*, though one spends the whole day amid filth, it is of no consequence. For the firm and yang are often intolerant, thus at the time when *yin* gradually grows, they are immediately restrained by *Dun*. Though *Dun* does not preclude encounter, even when encounter occurs, it is in truth *Dun*.

When three *yin* lines form *Pi*, if three *yin* reside below, it is still *Pi*; yet when four *yin* reside below, it becomes *Guan*—why is this? For when two yang reside above and four *yin* below, the superior remains unmoved in countenance and naturally becomes the world’s spectacle—what then of two *yin* or three *yin*? Thus, when the sage is above and *yin* and *yang* harmonize, the world attains peace through this.

Yet the world’s particular concern is the absence of those who embody great observation. If there is truly great observation, one yang suffices—why wait for two? Those who advocate delay do so precisely because of the lack of great observation.

When five *yin* lines erode and form *Bo*, one brings ruin upon oneself, losing all shelter. Thus, the sage deeply considers the multitude of *yin*, saying: one must repair one’s hut to take refuge and bear the ruler upon a carriage. For is not the great fruit here? Who would dare consume it? The sage’s guidance to humanity regarding the interplay of *yin* and *yang* is profound, and his strategies for *yang* are exhaustive. Readers should ponder this carefully.

**Appendix**

Xiong Nansha said: *Dizhu* (踯躅) follows Li Maoqin’s interpretation as "moving with halting steps," referring to the act of walking. Walking involves two feet—one step, one pause. *Xun* (巽) represents the thighs, signifying advance; to retreat the thigh is to advance is to *dizhu*. Zhen Yu believed it denoted something lofty, great, firm, and solid. Yu Bao said: the first and second lines form *Xun*, representing grasses and trees.

**Below Kun, above Dui: Cui (萃): Smoothness.**
The king approaches the ancestral temple. It is beneficial to meet the great person; smoothness and correctness are advantageous. Offering great sacrifices is auspicious; it is beneficial to have somewhere to go.

**The *Tuan* says:** *Cui* means

Firmly knowing that at Nine in the Fifth, one cannot avoid the vulgarity of *cui* in a position of power, nor the failure to demonstrate enduring constancy, nor the dimming of unfulfilled radiance. This is because one fears that the people of Initial Six may yet stir disorder to the end, hence the sigh of lament and the doubt of untrustworthiness. Such are the considerations by which the sage plans for the Fifth. It also reveals that one who would be ruler cannot but be upright, cannot but be great. *Yuan* (元) is greatness; *zhen* (贞) is uprightness. Thus enduring without cease. If, like Nine in the Fifth of *Bi* (比), one divines *yuan*, enduring constancy, and naturally becomes the one whom the five yin lines cherish and assist, then one may dispel regret and cause those who lack trust to trust, those who *cui* in positions of power to *cui* in virtue. This hexagram resembles *Bi*, so the sage uniquely considers *cui* at Nine in the Fifth, reiterating that only with the virtue of *yuan* and enduring constancy can regret be dispelled. Otherwise, *cui* will ultimately fall into chaos—how could regret be dispelled? As for Initial, Third, and Top, merely by advancing to *cui*, all avoid misfortune. As for Second and Fourth, not only do they avoid misfortune, but they also attain great fortune. How magnanimous are the sage’s plans for the people! There is no other reason. Constancy governs encounters with difficulty; the aspiration for radiance and greatness is hard to attain. To comprehend the sentiments of heaven, earth, and the myriad things is not something one meets in a single day or night. Second and Fourth understand this, so they can only draw each other to *cui*, not yet daring to demand perfection. If one must wait for a ruler like Yao or Shun before *cui*, then the scholar who would act upon his aspirations would never find the time. How could one not be magnanimous? Fourth and Fifth are both yang, but Nine in the Fifth holds position and is the one whom the multitude *cui*. Fourth does not occupy the position of Nine in the Fifth, yet turns to *cui* with Five—this is their difference. Alas! Fourth *cui* with Fifth and attains great fortune, yet what more is needed for Fifth, who is *cui* itself? This is the essence of *cui* in this hexagram.

Fang Shihua said: All *cui* gathers in one person. Nine in the Fifth occupies this position, and the first to *cui* in close alignment are the four yang lines of shared virtue. Thus, Nine in the Fourth alone is called "great fortune," "no misfortune," for Fourth possesses the substance to gather all under heaven, though it does not occupy the position. Nine in the Fifth rightfully holds the position, hence it is called "*cui* in a position of power." Yet to speak only of position is to lack radiance. If not for the position, it would not suffice to *cui*. Wang Benhe said: Water clings to earth in *Bi*, establishing the myriad states and befriending the feudal lords—this is how one befriends. Through friendship, one compares; through comparison, friendship deepens. Who would say the sage’s comparison is not all-encompassing? Marsh gathers upon earth in *Cui*. Removing weapons of war and guarding against the unforeseen—this is how one secures *cui*. Through security, one gathers; through gathering, one reflects on security. Who would say the sage dares to forget peril? Alas, how perfect! Those who possess the world may reflect upon the origins of *bi* and *cui*.

**Appendix**
Liu Junbo said: *Zhai* (斋) and *zi* (咨) are sounds of *Dui* (兑), resembling tears flowing. *Dui* is the image of a marsh.
