Wang Ji said: Water gathers without restraint and bursts its banks; the multitude gathers without discipline and falls into chaos. *Chu* (除) means to cultivate and govern, removing evil and decay. Heaven generates the five materials, and the people discard none of them—who can abolish weapons? To abolish weapons is to invite chaos. The noble one removes weapons of war only to guard against the unforeseen—this is the meaning of "securing without forgetting peril."

**Below Xun, above Kun: Sheng (升): Great prosperity.**
Useful for meeting the great person; do not worry. A southern campaign is auspicious.

**The *Tuan* says:** The yielding ascends in due time; it is compliant and obedient. The firm is central and responsive—thus great prosperity. Useful for meeting the great person; do not worry, for there will be celebration. A southern campaign is auspicious, for the aspiration is fulfilled.

**The *Xiang* says:** Within the earth, wood grows—this is *Sheng*. The noble one, in accordance with virtue, accumulates the small to become lofty and great.

**Initial Six:** Trustworthy ascent; great fortune.
**The *Xiang* says:** Trustworthy ascent; great fortune, for it aligns with the will above.

**Nine in the Second:** Sincerity makes the offering of *yu* (擒) beneficial; no misfortune.
**The *Xiang* says:** The sincerity of Nine in the Second brings joy.

**Nine in the Third:** Ascending an empty city.
**The *Xiang* says:** Ascending an empty city—there is no cause for doubt.

**Six in the Fourth:** The king offers sacrifice at Mount Qi; auspicious, no misfortune.
**The *Xiang* says:** The king offers sacrifice at Mount Qi—this is obedience to duty.

**Six in the Fifth:** Constancy is auspicious; ascending the steps.
**The *Xiang* says:** Constancy is auspicious; ascending the steps—greatly fulfilling the aspiration.

**Top Six:** Dark ascent; beneficial for unceasing constancy.
**The *Xiang* says:** Dark ascent at the top—diminishing, not enriching.

**Earth-Wind Sheng**
Fang Shihua said: When wood ascends, its initial substance is most yielding and fragile, yet it can rise from the earth to pierce the heavens. Even the hardest cannot do so in its obscurity, for it does not know that it is ascending. If it knew it was ascending, it could not ascend, and even if it did, it would not endure. Thus it is said: "The yielding ascends in due time." How could this not be greatly prosperous? The hexagram’s image is obedient and compliant; the line images are firm, central, and responsive—this is the substance of great prosperity. Six in the Fifth is the great person of the hexagram. Second, with the virtue of firm centrality, responds to Five, so Second uses this to ascend and meet the great person. Having met the great person, what aspiration cannot be fulfilled? Why worry that others will not share in the celebration? Wood flourishes in summer, the season of the southern direction. To advance southward and meet the great person is to fulfill one’s aspiration among the people, bringing great celebration and prosperity—is this not fitting? The celebration is the joy of Second; the fulfillment of aspiration is the great satisfaction of Fifth. Each line ascends in due time, and none is inauspicious. Thus, in other hexagrams, extremes inevitably reverse, but in *Sheng*, there is no such reversal.

Alone in this, it is precisely so.

At the outset, yielding below, it aligns its aspiration with the Fourth, thus justly ascending with great fortune—just as when firm centrality responds, it gains the favor of filial offering. This is why it is trustworthy, advantageous for selection and offering.

Wood emerges from the earth, fundamentally free of obstruction. This is why it ascends: the empty city and the virtue of compliance reside within, and when affairs are undertaken in due time, it ascends to the midst of heaven.

This is why it is favored: announcing enthronement at Mount Qi, with the great compliance of Six in the Fifth dwelling centrally—this is the time of the level steps of peace. Thus it is said: "Correct and auspicious." Ascending the steps, one assumes the imperial throne and becomes the Son of Heaven.

At the topmost Six, ascent is obscured; being above, its ascent is unseen, ceaseless yet without visible constancy. Alas, it has reached the utmost! In its ascent, it pierces the clouds and parts the mist—how could the wood know this? For yin is insubstantial and not abundant, ever accustomed to deeming insufficiency as regret. Yet today’s ascent, though it reaches the heavens, remains self-unaware—this may be called dispelling the mind of insufficiency.

Thus, those who excel in ascent are like the wood’s suppleness: emerging from the earth, ascending in due time to become beams and ridgepoles, so that others rely on its blessing alone. Utterly unaware of rising from earth to heaven, its aspiration is fully realized, and all benefit from its presence. Compliant and yielding, it neither displays itself to things nor rejoices, nor contends with the times. Though one might wish otherwise, how could it fail to be greatly auspicious and free of blame?

---

**Appendix**
Zheng Kangcheng said: "The earth is *kun*, the wood is *xun*; wood grows within the earth, daily lengthening and rising upward."

Master Xiang said: "*Cui* has firm centrality above, so its multitude must gather and ascend. Firm centrality below ensures its ascent."

Xu of Jinzhai said: "In *Jin*, the three yielding lines below share aspiration with the Fifth, so Six in the Third speaks of 'the multitude’s trust' and explains it as 'aspiration ascending.' The single yielding line below aligns with the Fourth, so the Initial Six speaks of 'just ascent' and explains it as 'aligning aspiration above.'"

Wang Ji said: "Wood grows within the earth, lengthening and ascending—this is the image of *Sheng*. Following its natural principle without imposing private intent is called *shun*. Complying with the wood’s growth, from the tiniest sprout to towering height, none see the limit of its ascent, for it achieves this through gradual accumulation. This is the meaning of 'journeying far from near' and 'ascending high from low.' The virtue of compliance is the image of the earth; accumulating small to reach great is the image of yielding wood."

---

**Kan below, Dui above**
*Kun*: Prospering, correct; the great person is auspicious, without blame. Words are not trusted.

**The Judgment** says: *Kun* is the capture of the firm. Perilous yet joyful, in distress yet not losing its prosperity—is this not the noble one? Correct, the great person is auspicious, for it is firm and central. Words are not trusted, for valuing speech leads to exhaustion.

**The Image** says: The marsh lacks water—*Kun*. The noble one devotes his life to fulfilling his aspiration.

**Initial Six**: The buttocks are distressed by a withered tree, entering a dark valley, three years without hunger.
**The Image** says: Entering a dark valley—darkness obscures clarity.

**Nine in the Second**: Distressed by wine and food, the crimson knee-cover arrives; advantageous for offering and sacrifice. Advancing is ominous, without blame.
**The Image** says: Distressed by wine and food—there is blessing within.

**Six in the Third**: Distressed by stone, clinging to thorny brambles; entering his chamber, he does not see his wife—ominous.
**The Image** says: Clinging to thorny brambles—riding the firm. Entering his chamber, not seeing his wife—this is inauspicious.

**Nine in the Fourth**: Coming slowly, distressed by the metal chariot—regret, but with an end.
**The Image** says: Coming slowly—his aspiration lies below. Though not in its proper place, he has support.

**Nine in the Fifth**: The nose and feet are cut off, distressed by the crimson knee-cover; then slowly there is release. Advantageous for offering and sacrifice.
**The Image** says: The nose and feet cut off—his aspiration is unfulfilled. Then slowly there is release, for he is central and upright. Advantageous for offering and sacrifice—receiving blessing.

**Topmost Six**: Distressed by creeping vines and brittle twigs; saying, "Move and repent," there is repentance—advancing is auspicious.
**The Image** says: Distressed by creeping vines—not in its proper place. "Move and repent," there is repentance—advancing is auspicious.

---

The marsh’s water *Kun*: The firm *kan* is overlaid by the yielding *dui*, so the noble one is obscured by flattering, deceptive petty men, and the yang firmness is subdued by the craft of the perverse. Thus *Kun* is evident.

Yet the Fifth dwells in the body of joy, slowly finding release and preserving its central uprightness—this is peril below and joy above. Though distressed, it does not lose its prosperity. Why? Because of its correctness. Thus it is said: *Kun*: prospering, correct; the great person is auspicious, without blame.

Nine in the Second, firm, comes to dwell in the midst of *kan* peril—this is the great person. The nature of steadfast correctness does not lose itself in distress, so it does not fail to prosper. Auspicious and without blame—what doubt remains?

*Kan* is wine, *dui* is food. Others see only the Second’s distress within confinement, unaware that it truly suffers within wine and food. Feasting and revelry continue as usual, oblivious to grievance. Thus the Fifth, though swayed by slander, repeatedly seeks to cut off its nose and feet, to distress it with the crimson knee-cover. Yet upon reflection, it finds release in the Second’s central uprightness, not believing the falsehoods of others. Thus the Second’s line says: "The crimson knee-cover arrives; advantageous for offering and sacrifice." The Fifth’s line says: "Advantageous for offering and sacrifice, receiving its blessing." The great person’s prosperity is all the more evident.

The Initial Six below, the image of buttocks, has the firm Nine in the Second as a withered tree before it. Dwelling at the bottom of *kan*, it enters the image of a dark valley, separated by three lines from encountering the Fourth—three years without hope. The First gazes toward the Fourth, three years like a single day, while the Fourth comes slowly, distressed by the Fifth’s metal chariot, unable to do otherwise.

Though near at hand, it seems but regret—yet in the end, there is resolution. This is because the Fourth’s aspiration lies below, and the Initial is truly one who would join with it. Thus, though three years pass without hope, they must ultimately meet, ensuring no misfortune. The "golden chariot" is the chariot of the king. The "crimson knee-cover" and "scarlet knee-cover" are both used to adorn the chariot. The line *dui* represents metal, hence it is also called "gold," "crimson," and "scarlet."

Now, nothing is as hard and unyielding as stone, nor as sharp and piercing as the *xianhua* thorn. Here, the Six in the Third mounts the firm, its image like one trapped beneath solid rock, pressed atop thorns—truly confined, with no escape. Not only will the self be lost, but the household too shall perish. How can one hope to see one’s wife, or enter one’s palace? Such is the inauspicious calamity that befell the Nine Lords and the Earl of E.

Yet the Nine in the Second finds prosperity in adversity not merely by its own steadfastness, but by the gradual favor of the sagely and enlightened Heavenly King in the Fifth. When favor comes, slander is disbelieved, and those who wield words as weapons are brought to ruin. Looking back upon oneself, one finds the self entangled in creeping vines, unable to shield its roots; or trapped in brittle reeds, unable to find stability. Thus, facing the mouth of *dui* above, one speaks: "In past actions, I truly repent." If one can truly repent, then misfortune turns to fortune, perversity to uprightness—how easily this may be! Would the great person, now embracing renewal, deign to contend with such a one?

Ah! This is what makes the great person great—this is how, even in adversity, they never fail to prosper, never fail to find good fortune, and remain without blame. If one truly possesses the steadfastness of the great person, what harm can adversity bring? This is no mere luck. Scholars must carefully ponder the phrase: "confined by wine and food."

Master Fang Shihua asked: How can adversity lead to prosperity? Through peril tempered by joy. Thus, to be in adversity yet not lose one’s place is prosperity. Yet this is not possible for the ordinary person. The great person, dwelling in firmness and centeredness, does not covet what lies beyond. Though adversity may surround, how can it confine the center? With firmness, one remains unassailable, never sinking into adversity. How then can it become one’s own adversity? If it could, where would firmness reside?

Thus the sage said: Adversity is common to all. Yet the great person endures it in silence, as if untouched. Though in adversity, they never fail to prosper. If one laments, "Why have I been brought to such extremity?" or boasts, "How could I fear this?"—such is the path to misfortune, not the way of the great person’s good fortune and blamelessness.

**Appendix**
Wang Zhaosou said: "Three years without hope" refers to passing through three lines from the Initial to the Fourth.
Wang Ji said: When water leaks downward, the marsh above dries—this is the image of exhaustion and adversity. *Dui*, with yin above, and *kan*, with yang below, represent the noble person oppressed by the petty, a time of hardship. "Giving one’s life" means surrendering it to others, no longer one’s own to command. "Fulfilling one’s aspiration" means realizing one’s good intentions without being swayed by misfortune.

**Hexagram 48: The Well**
*Xun* below *kan* above: The Well. The city may be changed, but the well cannot. There is no loss, no gain—people come and go, yet the well remains. Drawing near, one has not yet drawn from the well, but the jar is broken—misfortune.

**The Judgment:**
*Xun* enters water and raises it—this is the Well. The Well nourishes without end. The city may change, but the well does not—this is due to firmness and centeredness. Drawing near yet not drawing from the well means no merit has been achieved. The jar is broken—hence misfortune.

**The Image:**
Water above wood—this is the Well. The noble person encourages the people to labor and aid one another.

**The Lines:**
**Initial Six:** The well is muddy and undrinkable; an old well where no birds alight.
**The Image:** The well is muddy and undrinkable—it lies too low. An old well where no birds alight—it is abandoned by the times.

**Nine in the Second:** At the well’s hollow, one shoots at minnows; the jar is cracked and leaking.
**The Image:** Shooting at minnows in the well’s hollow—there is no one to share with.

**Nine in the Third:** The well is deep and undrinkable, causing sorrow in my heart. It may be drawn from; the king is enlightened, and all receive its blessing.
**The Image:** The well is deep and undrinkable—one’s conduct is pitiful. Seeking the king’s enlightenment brings blessing.

**Six in the Fourth:** The well is tiled—no blame.
**The Image:** Tiling the well brings no blame—this is repairing the well.

**Nine in the Fifth:** The well’s waters are clear and cold, fit to drink.
**The Image:** Drinking the cold spring’s waters—this is centeredness and correctness.

**Top Six:** The well’s cover is not closed; with sincerity, there is great good fortune.
**The Image:** Great good fortune at the top—this is great accomplishment.

The Well is the natural and inexhaustible source of nourishment. It has the power to sustain all things, but this depends on people drawing from it. If not drawn from, the well does not lose; if drawn from, the well does not gain. Those who come to draw—this well remains. Those who go without drawing—this well still remains. What remains is the well; those who come and go are people. Gain and loss belong to those who draw from the well—what does this have to do with the well itself?

Thus, the well may be drawn from but cannot compel people to draw—this is the virtue of *sheng*. Have you not seen how cities may change? Can the well be changed as well? The Nine in the Fifth dwells in the midst of *kan*, its firmness unchanging—truly like the well. Yet now, as one draws near the water, it has not yet been gathered, and the well’s pulley is already broken, the jar already cracked. What can be done? Though one draws daily, in truth, nothing is drawn.

Here the omen of misfortune is already apparent—how can there be merit?
The First Six, dwelling below, resembles the bottom of a well.
A newly dug well, still muddy, cannot be drunk from; an old well, its earthen walls crumbled and its village long abandoned, may have chickens and dogs all gone—though the well itself remains unchanged, who would draw from it?
The Nine in the Second, having lost its proper resonance, bears the image of a well-valley.
This well-valley is like the archer Yi shooting at Zhou. A leaking well with a broken jar—this is what is meant by "worn and leaky."
The broken jar, like the muddy new well, cannot be drunk from; if left undrawn, it may still be abandoned.
Yet the Nine in the Third—here the well has been dredged, the spring made pure, yet it is not drunk from. Truly, this grieves my heart.
Why? It could be drawn from, yet one refuses to draw. The king’s lack of clarity is truly pitiable.
If the king were enlightened, he would surely draw and ascend, and those below would receive its blessing.
Thus the Master said: If a well is pure yet not drunk from, it does not merely grieve my heart—all who travel the Way see it and grieve.
Therefore, to receive blessing, one must seek the king’s clarity.
Otherwise, why would the king’s clarity be sought?

The Six in the Fourth is one who repairs the well—yet it is still not drunk from.
The Nine in the Fifth: the well is clear, and people drink from it.
Truly, the cold spring is drinkable, sparing travelers from sorrow—without the king’s clarity above, how could this be achieved?
Thus, by the time we reach the Top Six, the well nourishes, and its bounty is inexhaustible.
Morning and evening, one draws from it—do not cover it, do not seal it. Gather its jars, its pitchers; take as needed, and it will always suffice. This is because the Nine in the Fifth, centered and upright, possesses sincerity, and thus the blessing of the Top Six is supremely auspicious, bringing great completion above.
Is this not what is meant by "the enlightened king, and all receive its blessing"? What need is there for the Five to seek it further?

Alas! A single well—if left undrawn, it becomes a temporal abode, a broken jar, a cracked pitcher, causing all who see it to sigh in sorrow, yet none know how a single drawing could bring blessing.
Such is the unlabored kindness, the unasked-for grace. Let the king reflect and draw from it to attain clarity.

---

**Appendix**
Su Zizhan said: *Chi* means dryness. To reach the well but not yet the water is called *chi*. To reach the water but not yet draw it from the well is called "not yet having drawn the well."
The well itself never gains or loses; the merit of the well-pulley and the misfortune of the cracked pitcher lie with the one who draws.

Liu Junbo said: *Shou* means to gather the pulley and pitcher—this is the completion of drawing. *Wu mu* means the mouth of the *kan* is not covered, making its benefit public. *You fu* means the reality within *kan* is sincere, deep and inexhaustible.

Xiong Guo said: The First Six, dwelling below without resonance, resembles a muddy well. The Nine in the Second, without resonance and below its neighbor, resembles a valley’s slope. The Nine in the Third, having completed the work of *xun* entering, thus speaks of "dredging our well."

---

**Hexagram 49: Ge (Revolution)**
*After the day of ji, there is sincerity. Supreme prosperity, advantage in constancy—regret vanishes.*

**The Judgment:**
Revolution: water and fire extinguish each other; two women dwell together, their wills incompatible—this is called revolution.
*After the day of ji, there is sincerity*—revolution is thus trusted.
Radiant and joyful, greatly prosperous and upright—when revolution is fitting, regret vanishes.
Heaven and earth revolutionize, and the four seasons are completed. Tang and Wu revolted against the mandate, following Heaven and responding to the people—great indeed is the timing of revolution!

**The Image:**
Fire within the marsh: Revolution.
The noble one, in accordance with this, regulates the calendar and clarifies the seasons.

**The Lines:**
**First Nine:** Strengthen with the hide of a yellow ox.
**The Image:** *Strengthen with the hide of a yellow ox*—one cannot act.

**Six in the Second:** After the day of *ji*, revolutionize. To advance brings good fortune, no blame.
**The Image:** *After the day of ji, revolutionize*—action brings praise.

**Nine in the Third:** To advance brings misfortune; constancy is dangerous. When the words of revolution are thrice established, there is sincerity.
**The Image:** *When the words of revolution are thrice established*—where else can one go?

**Nine in the Fourth:** Regret vanishes. With sincerity, change the mandate—good fortune.
**The Image:** *Good fortune in changing the mandate*—sincerity in purpose.

**Nine in the Fifth:** The great person transforms like a tiger. Without divination, there is sincerity.
**The Image:** *The great person transforms like a tiger*—their pattern is radiant.

**Top Six:** The noble one transforms like a leopard; the petty person reforms their countenance. To advance brings misfortune; to dwell in constancy brings good fortune.
**The Image:** *The noble one transforms like a leopard*—their pattern is lush. *The petty person reforms their countenance*—they follow the ruler in compliance.

---

**Marsh-Fire Revolution**
The revolutions of the times cannot be taken lightly—how much more so the great matter of transformation?
Shang Yang’s reforms were not without change, nor did they fail to swiftly strengthen Qin’s borders.
Yet after a year of implementation, thousands of Qin’s people spoke of its inconvenience, leading to punishments, the execution of its proponents, and the tearing apart of its architect—yet the people did not mourn. This was because the people’s hearts were not yet sincere, and he hastily sought revolution, startling them.
If the people are startled, who will trust him?
Thus it is said: *After the day of ji, there is sincerity*—only after revolution is there trust.
To say "after the day of *ji*, there is sincerity" means only after revolution is there trust. If one seeks revolution without sincerity, will the people not be startled?
For the people may be made to follow, but not to understand; they may be joined in celebrating success, but not in contemplating beginnings. Even the sage cannot but comply with this.
Only after compliance brings sincerity can revolution proceed. Once revolution is complete, sincerity naturally follows.
Sincerity lies not in the sage’s love of revolution, but in their recognition that revolution is unavoidable and fitting.
Having deliberated thoroughly and planned meticulously, there is supreme prosperity and advantage in constancy. When revolution is fitting, regret vanishes.
People see only that the sage’s revolution is trusted by the people—how could they know the sage’s careful consideration of regret?
Revolution truly begins and ends with this.
Thus all six lines speak of revolution, all speak of sincerity.
The First Nine, not yet sincere, does not dare to revolutionize. From the Six in the Second onward, revolution is complete, and the people are sincere.

The First Nine, dwelling below, though in a time requiring revolution, does not dare speak of revolution, thus employing the firmness of an oxhide’s leather. Its revolution is steadfast, like the unyielding hide of a yellow ox, inherently resistant to action. If as immovable as oxhide, even in a time calling for revolution, who would perceive one’s capacity for change or recognize one’s desire to revolutionize?

The Six in the Second resides at the center of *Li*, embodying the image of the *ji* day. The *ji* day is the day when revolution becomes fitting. Revolutionizing on the *ji* day brings auspicious征 and no blame. To act with excellence is indeed what is meant by "like a startled crow" or "swift as thunder that leaves no time to cover one’s ears." As the Second aligns with the Fifth, it speaks of "excellence." Since the Second is already ripe for revolution, by the Third, the day of revolution arrives. With such sincerity, if not revolutionized, what else remains? Though it is said to be overly rigid, bearing warnings of inauspicious征 and perilous constancy, this too considers potential future regret, ensuring the revolution is fitting—thus, it is not that revolution is impossible.

As for the Fourth, precisely at the moment of transformation, with people’s trust and resolve firmly established, how could it not be auspicious? Since the Fourth speaks of "having sincerity," the Fifth, even before divination, is already known to possess it. What else could this be but the great stabilization of a new order, the renewal of creation, when the great person transforms like a tiger and the world’s culture shines forth? For this is an act that aligns with Heaven and responds to the people, carrying out the revolution of the four seasons. Its time is the *ji* day, the moment when sincerity in revolution is trusted. Its revolution is one of cultural brilliance and joy, of great prosperity and rectitude. Thus, by the time of the Top Six, the Dao of revolution is fully accomplished. The noble person leads with ritual and music, adorned with the rich patterns of a leopard. The petty person turns their face in submission, bowing in obedience to their lord. If one were to seek further征, would it not be inauspicious? Abiding in constancy brings good fortune, for the primal force has acted and transformation is complete.

The hexagram’s image shows *Li* fire below and *Dui* marsh above. Fire can overcome water, extinguishing it; water can overcome fire, likewise extinguishing it. This is the mutual suppression of water and fire, a revolution ordained by the Dao of Heaven. Moreover, *Dui* as the youngest daughter resides above, while *Li* as the middle daughter resides below—young and elder in reversed positions, dwelling together yet at odds in will. This is a revolution required by human affairs. Thus, only the sage can align above with the Dao of Heaven and respond below to the hearts of the people, uniquely capable of revolutionizing fittingly. Hence it is said: *primordial prosperity, advantage in constancy, regret vanishes.*

From this, it follows that the *ji* day is when sincerity is achieved. Once revolution is complete and the people trust it, what doubt remains?

---

**Appendix**
Zheng Kangcheng said: *Ge* (革) means governance. Water and fire suppress each other and alternate in dominance, hence it is called *ge* (revolution).

Wang Fusi said: The people may be accustomed to the familiar but struggle to adapt to change; they may delight in completion but falter at the outset. Thus, the Dao of revolution is not trusted at first but gains trust on the *ji* day.

Cai Zimu said: *Primordial prosperity, advantage, constancy*—these are the virtues of *Qian*. *Sui* (随) means following things, while *ge* (革) means transforming them. Things cannot be blindly followed, so only with *primordial prosperity, advantage, and constancy* can one avoid blame. Nor can they be recklessly transformed, so only with *primordial prosperity, advantage, and constancy* can regret vanish.

Yu Lingsheng said: *Gong* (巩) means firmness. *Li* represents the cow. At the beginning of *Ge*, one cannot act rashly, hence it is said: *firm, using the leather of a yellow ox.*

Liu Changmin said: The Fifth is the master of revolution, where martial might is proclaimed and cultural virtue shines. "Three revolutions" refers to the threefold issuance of commands, their decrees already enacted. "Changing the mandate" in the Fourth means trust and resolve are complete, all things follow transformation. By the Fifth, there is great prosperity and rectitude without pretense; divination confirms its sincerity.

Chang Jichang said: The tiger’s transformation embodies awe-inspiring virtue, subduing enemies a thousand miles away. Its influence is such that all trust at a glance—like Shun dancing with feathered banners and the Miao submitting, or the Duke of Zhou cultivating cultural virtue and the people of Yuechang presenting pheasants. Thus it is said: *even before divination, there is sincerity.*

Wang Deqing said: The tiger’s patterns are refined and radiant, while the leopard’s are dense and lush.

Su Shi said: The *Yi* speaks of wind following the tiger. The tiger possesses patterns and spiritual power, while the leopard has patterns but lacks spiritual power.

Yang Xiong said: The wildcat transforms into a leopard, the leopard into a tiger.

Wang Ji said: Water and fire suppress each other as *ge* (革). *Ge* means change. The greatest of changes are the four seasons. In the governance of emperors and kings, nothing precedes the granting of time. The noble person observes the image of change and revolution to regulate the calendar, clarifying the sequence of the four seasons, the paths of the sun, moon, and five planets, the alternation of spring, summer, autumn, and winter. By tracking the sun and moon and examining the central stars, one can determine the four seasons—thus, regulating the calendar clarifies time. The image of *yin* below and *li* above yields the method of calendar-making. The calendar must be reformed yearly; without reform, celestial measurements gradually drift. This is the method of annual precession. Establishing the calendar’s epoch is merely its overarching framework.

---
*Xun* below, *Li* above
**Ding (鼎): Primordial Auspiciousness. Offering.**
The *Tuan* says: *Ding* is an image. Wood submitting to fire represents cooking. The sage cooks to offer to the Supreme Divinity and grandly cooks to nourish the sagely and worthy. Submissive yet perceptive in hearing and sight, advancing gently and ascending, attaining the center and responding to the firm—thus *primordial prosperity.*

The *Xiang* says: Fire above wood is *Ding.* The noble person rectifies their position and solidifies their mandate.

**First Six:** The *ding* overturns its legs. Favorable to expel the impure. Gaining a concubine for her son brings no blame.
The *Xiang* says: The *ding* overturns its legs—this is not perverse. Favorable to expel the impure, to follow the noble.

**Nine in the Second:** The *ding* has substance. My rival has illness; they cannot approach me. Auspicious.
The *Xiang* says: The *ding* has substance—prudence in where one goes.

My rival has illness—ultimately no fault.
**Nine in the Third:** The *ding*’s ears are transformed; its movement is blocked. The pheasant’s rich broth goes uneaten. As the rain just begins, regret diminishes—ultimately auspicious.
The *Xiang* says: The *ding*’s ears are transformed—this loses its proper function.

**Nine in the Fourth:** The *ding*’s legs break; the duke’s stew is spilled. Its form is befouled—ominous.
The *Xiang* says: The duke’s stew is spilled—how can trust endure?

**Six in the Fifth:** The *ding* has yellow ears and a golden beam—favorable to be steadfast.
The *Xiang* says: The *ding*’s yellow ears—centered, it serves as substance.

**Top Nine:** The *ding* has a jade beam—greatly auspicious, supremely without harm.
The *Xiang* says: The jade beam above—hard and soft are moderated.

**Fire over Wind: The Ding**
Fang Shihua says: The *Ding* is a hexagram image. The First Six represents the *ding*’s legs; the second, third, and fourth lines, all yang, represent its belly. The fifth, a single yin, represents its ears. The top, another yang, represents its beam. When wood enters fire, lifting the *ding* to cook—every household does this; what is there to speak of? Yet the sage, with a single cooking, offers to the Supreme Divinity, and with grand cooking nourishes the sagely and worthy. What is sagely? Submissive yet perceptive in hearing and sight. The Six in the Fifth, advancing gently and ascending, attaining the center and responding to the firmness of Nine in the Second—are these not the great sages and worthies? This is the cooking that nourishes the sagely and worthy. This, too, is the early offering to the Supreme Divinity. How is it supremely auspicious? How is it thoroughly prosperous? Thus this hexagram alone combines *supreme auspiciousness*, *great auspiciousness*, and *prosperity*.

The sage points to the Top Nine’s jade beam. The sagely and worthy refer to the Six in the Fifth, centered to serve as substance with yellow ears, threaded by the golden beam to be lifted. Nine in the Second, “the *ding* has substance,” is the vessel for nourishing the sagely and worthy. Though the third and fourth are also the *ding*’s substance, the third’s ears are transformed and cannot be lifted, while the fourth spills the stew and breaks its legs—thus only the second truly has the *ding*’s substance and can be used to nourish the sagely and worthy. The First Six, below as the *ding*’s legs, overturns its legs to expel the impure. Overturning the legs is contrary to the Way, yet it is said to be “not perverse” because it is favorable to expel the impure and follow the noble. “Gaining a concubine” is the image of expelling the impure; “for her son” is the image of following the noble, hence “no blame.”

Nine in the Second, firm and centered, has substance in the *ding*’s belly. Looking up at the third and fourth, they are truly my rivals. Yet the third’s ears are transformed, and the fourth spills the stew—they have illness and cannot approach me. Thus I cannot but offer my substance to the Fifth. If others shared the same substance, there would be reliance. Now only the second remains—how can one not be prudent? Without prudence, how could the supreme sage offer to the Supreme Divinity and nourish the sagely and worthy? The third stands at the time of transformation; its ears are altered and it cannot move, so the pheasant’s rich broth from the clinging fire goes uneaten. As the Top Nine’s rain just begins, regret from deficiency in response diminishes—yet what is lost is the *ding*’s nourishing function, while what remains is the vessel for nourishment. With the jade beam above, the ears will ultimately be lifted—hence “ultimately auspicious.” As for the fourth, the *ding*’s belly is already full, its legs break, and the stew is spilled, its form befouled and unusable. How can it be otherwise? The “centered to serve as substance” is the emptiness within *li*. What the *ding*’s substance relies on to be lifted lies entirely in this clarity of the center. This is called “centered to serve as substance,” hence “the *ding* has yellow ears.” The “golden beam” speaks of its ability to lift this substance: the Six in the Fifth, centered and yellow, serves as the ears, and uses yellow gold as the beam. Yellow is the color of the center, like gold. The image of lifting the ears must include the beam—there is no lifting the ears without the beam. Thus the Fifth combines “yellow ears” and “golden beam.” This is the steadfastness of the sagely and worthy, hence “favorable to be steadfast.”

As for the Top Nine, the *ding*’s substance is already lifted, supremely using the beam at the *ding*’s proper place. Thus it is named by its virtue of the jade beam, saying that the second and fifth, hard and soft, respond in moderation: the golden beam lifts, and the *ding*’s substance is consumed. The Top Nine’s jade beam is truly the supreme accomplishment of transformation. It is fitting that “greatly auspicious, supremely without harm” belong to it. “Greatly auspicious” is supreme auspiciousness; “without harm” is supreme auspiciousness and prosperity. The *Ding*’s supreme auspiciousness and prosperity—if this were merely about the mouth, as in *Yi*, what would there be to mention? Yet to the sage, nourishing the worthy extends to all the people—this is the *ding*’s cooking. In essence, there is no great matter, yet to the sage, nourishing the sagely and worthy is to offer to the Supreme Divinity.

Liu Yongxiang says: If *Yi* were only about the mouth and *Ding* only about the belly, what would there be to observe in what is cooked and nourished? Ma Fengyang says: The sage cooks to offer to the Supreme Divinity, and grandly cooks to nourish the sagely and worthy. If one cannot grandly cook to nourish the sagely and worthy, then even if one wishes to offer to the Supreme Divinity, the Supreme Divinity will not accept it. Thus offering to the Supreme is called “cooking,” while nourishing the sagely and worthy is called “grand cooking.” How insignificant are the sagely and worthy in the world! Today one dies unused; tomorrow another dies unseen in their lifetime—how lamentable the *ding*! I know not what the cooking and nourishing are for.

**Appendix**

Bear Guo says: According to Zixia’s *Tradition*, “The center is hollow to form the ears.” “My mate” is the noble person’s fine mate. The *Han Records of the Five Phases* and Yu Fan both say: “The *ding* moves by its ears.” If the ears are damaged, its movement is obstructed. The *ding*’s capacity reaches its limit at four; beyond that are the ears. To receive contents, there must be surplus capacity to allow for overflow; at four, it overflows and topples. Shao Guoxian says: “Five in the *ding* are the ears.” The ears are what admit the crossbar to lift it. “Above in the *ding* is the crossbar.” The crossbar is what threads through the ears to lift the *ding*. Yang Jian says: “Nothing transforms things like the *ding*. Tang and Wu’s revolution was truly mandated by Heaven. When Heaven commands the noble person to rectify their position, how can the noble person fail to rectify their position? Yet Heaven’s mandate cannot be relied upon. If one accords with Heaven, one’s mandate congeals; if one does not accord with Heaven, one’s mandate slips away and cannot be congealed.” Thus the noble person dare not forget Heaven even for an instant.

☳ below, ☳ above
*Shock*: offering.

When the thunder comes with trembling dread, laughter and speech return in cheerful tones. The thunderclap resounds for a hundred *li*, yet the sacrificial ladle is not lost.

*The Judgment* says: Thunder brings offering. When the thunder comes with trembling dread, fear brings forth blessing. Laughter and speech return in cheerful tones, for afterward there is a model to follow. The thunderclap resounds for a hundred *li*, startling the distant and alarming the near. Thus one may go forth to guard the ancestral temples and altars of soil and grain, serving as the master of sacrifice.

*The Image* says: Repeated thunder is *Shock*. The noble person, in fear and trembling, engages in self-cultivation and reflection.

**First Nine:** When the thunder comes with trembling dread, afterward laughter and speech return in cheerful tones—auspicious.
*The Image* says: When the thunder comes with trembling dread, fear brings forth blessing. Laughter and speech return in cheerful tones, for afterward there is a model to follow.

**Six in the Second:** The thunder comes perilously; a fortune in cowries is lost. Ascending to the ninefold hills, do not pursue—within seven days it will be regained.
*The Image* says: When the thunder comes perilously, it is because one rides over the firm.

**Six in the Third:** The thunder stirs restlessly; moving in shock, there is no blame.
*The Image* says: The thunder stirs restlessly, for the position is not appropriate.

**Nine in the Fourth:** The thunder sinks into the mire.
*The Image* says: The thunder sinks into the mire, for its light has not yet shone.

**Six in the Fifth:** The thunder comes and goes perilously; a great fortune is lost, and there is activity.
*The Image* says: The thunder comes and goes perilously, for one moves in danger. The matter lies in the center—great indeed is the loss.

**Top Six:** The thunder trembles ceaselessly; one’s gaze is fixed and wandering, and to advance is ominous. The shock does not strike one’s own person but one’s neighbor—there is no blame. Yet in marriage alliances, there are words of reproach.
*The Image* says: The thunder trembles ceaselessly, for the center has not been attained. Though ominous, there is no blame, for one fears the neighbor’s warning.

Thunder is the eldest son, the master of arousal. When the thunder comes and one is thus filled with dread and unease, it is because one fears that what brings forth blessing may not accord with the will of the Supreme. Therefore, one is startled into reflection, dare not rest at ease, and instead strengthens self-trust and self-cultivation. Then laughter and speech return in cheerful tones, saying: “From now on, I shall follow the seasons of Heaven and act without the slightest deviation from the Supreme’s model.”

A single thunderclap startles the distant and alarms the near. If the lord of a hundred-*li* domain truly engages in fear and trembling, self-cultivation, and reflection, then the reach of his virtue and awe will extend for a hundred *li*, and it is fitting that his sacrificial ladle not be lost. Thus, as the eldest son, how could he not be the master of the ancestral temples and altars of soil and grain? How could there be no offering?

In ancient times, when the lord departed, the heir would guard the altars of soil and grain and serve as the master of sacrifice for the ancestral temples. Hence it is said that he may serve as the master of sacrifice.

The first line is the master of the completed *Shock*, so its words match the hexagram’s words, and its *Image* also matches the *Great Image*.

Six in the Second rides over the firm, directly facing the coming thunder. Its peril and unease are extreme, and thus it suffers great loss of what it has long treasured. If it ascends to the ninefold hills, it becomes as the empty sky. No longer does it chase after the realm of appearances or the domain of thought. Thus, after seven days, the shock subsides, and it regains itself.

For Six in the Third, the lower shock is ending while the upper shock is beginning—its image is that of restless stirring. *Su* means revival. Six in the Third inwardly revives its resolve, reflecting and examining its conduct, knowing that its inner actions are of utmost importance. It guards against the slightest misstep.

Nine in the Fourth dwells in the time of doubled thunder, its heart filled with nothing but shock and dread. Yet the essence of shock lies in retreat and reflection. If one remains mired in fear without knowing to retreat and reflect, how can one ever expand and illuminate?

Six in the Fifth experiences shock upon shock—not merely the coming of thunder. Six in the Second says “the thunder comes perilously,” for it rides over the firm and is thus in danger. Now Six in the Fifth says “the thunder comes and goes perilously,” for it moves in danger both coming and going. Yet Six in the Fifth, though it hears nothing, instead loses what it possesses in great measure. How could this be in a time without shock? Six in the Second, facing peril, lost what it treasured, thus losing what it fundamentally possessed—only afterward did it regain itself. This was the power of a single shock. Though Six in the Fifth moves in peril coming and going, it instead loses what it possesses in great measure because what it fundamentally possesses cannot be gained or lost by shock. This is what shock cannot shake. Ah, how perfect!

Yet if one does not move in the midst of peril coming and going, how can one test the depth of one’s learning and confirm that one cannot be moved to such an extent?

The Top Six dwells at the peak of shock, neighboring the Fifth. The Fifth is neither gained nor lost by shock, for it is beyond shock. Yet the Top Six is far from shock. The shock does not strike its own person but instead trembles ceaselessly, inwardly searching its heart and finding it empty, outwardly observing its conduct and finding nothing within—why then does it vainly pursue externals? Its ominousness is clear. Fortunately, it is close to Six in the Fifth and can trust in its neighbor’s great loss and activity. Thus, fearing the neighbor’s warning and hoping to share in its merit, it may yet avoid blame and escape misfortune.

If Six in the Third, in marriage alliances, reproaches it, saying: “You abandon your trembling, forget your shocked conduct, forsake your emptiness, and neglect your utmost importance—no longer will I join you in affairs,” it need not be concerned. One may know the thoroughness of the learning of the Way.

Those below do not arouse without shock; those above do not cease without shock. Thus, in the aftermath of a single shock, each may lose something, each may arouse something, each may gain something, each may cease something—this is not comparable to other hexagrams.

Ah! When thunder crashes like swift lightning, all under Heaven are unsettled. The rebellious are executed, the unfilial are punished, and how could the lord who cultivates the Dao, the eldest son, fail to bring forth blessing?

The noble person, observing *Zhen* (Shock), may reflect with vigilance. The *Great Image* states: "Thunder crashes—*Zhen*. The noble person, in fear and trepidation, cultivates introspection. There is reason for this."

**Appendix**
Master Wu says: "Qian’s yang is the lord; Kun’s earth is the state or domain. Dividing Qian’s single yang to preside over Kun makes the lord of a hundred-li domain."

Master Yu says: "‘Distant’ refers to the outer body of the hexagram; ‘near’ refers to its inner body. With shock both within and without, there is the image of alarm near and far."

Zheng Kangcheng says: "In the ruler’s sacrifices, only the spoon, sacrificial animal, body parts, and black millet are offered. When the sacrificial ox is raised upon the platter, the ruler’s minister who wields the spoon carries it forth. The fragrant wine and well-prepared millet—these are the refined offerings."

Xiong Guo says: "*Zhen* is the foot. The foot mounts the first nine, hence it is called ‘Nine Ridges.’ The upper *su* speaks of the fading of the lower shock’s sound; the lower *su* speaks of the rebirth of the upper shock’s sound. ‘Shock comes and goes perilously’—the initial shock is the going, while the fourth’s harmonious thunder is the coming."

Wu Youqing says: "*Su-su* is death and rebirth."

---
*Gen* below, *Gen* above
*Gen*: "Restraining the back, one does not grasp the whole body. Walking in the courtyard, one does not see its people—no blame."

The *Xiang* says: "*Gen* means stopping. Stop when it is time to stop, move when it is time to move—neither action nor stillness loses its proper moment, and thus the Dao shines brightly. 'Restraining the stop' means stopping where it should be stopped. Above and below oppose each other, with no mutual response; this is why 'one does not grasp the whole body.' Walking in the courtyard and not seeing its people—this incurs no blame."

The *Xiang* says: "Two mountains together—this is *Gen*. The noble person reflects and does not overstep his position."

**First Six:** "Restraining the toes—no blame. Favorable for eternal steadfastness."
The *Xiang* says: "Restraining the toes—this is not losing correctness."

**Six in the Second:** "Restraining the calves, unable to lift what follows—his heart is not at ease."
The *Xiang* says: "Unable to lift what follows—this is not yielding to retreat."

**Nine in the Third:** "Restraining the waist, splitting the spine—danger scorches the heart."
The *Xiang* says: "Restraining the waist—danger scorches the heart."

**Six in the Fourth:** "Restraining the body—no blame."
The *Xiang* says: "Restraining the body—stopping at the self."

**Six in the Fifth:** "Restraining the jaws, words in proper order—regret vanishes."
The *Xiang* says: "Restraining the jaws—this is central correctness."

**Top Nine:** "Reverent restraint—auspicious."
The *Xiang* says: "The auspiciousness of reverent restraint—this is ending with thickness."

*Gen* is the mountain. The two hexagrams *Zhen* and *Gen*—these are the great matters of the sage’s Dao of inquiry and learning. For all who engage in learning: as learning and inquiry grow daily more vast, what one hears and sees grows daily broader; as what one hears and sees grows broader, principles accumulate daily; as principles accumulate, cherishing them grows daily deeper. Day by day, accumulation deepens, and month by month, obstructions harden. Even the heroic and outstanding cannot free themselves—this is precisely what is called the deep pit of profit and desire, the snare that kills. Even if a sage dwelled among them, there would be nothing he could do. Yet they do not know that learning values knowing when to stop—stopping must have its place. Thus *Gen* takes the back as the place to stop, defining 'not grasping the body' and 'not seeing people' as the back. Stopping of itself where there is no place to stop—this is the perfect stop, the stop that knows where to stop. If even a thread of impurity remains, it is not the stop of the back. Thus from the toes upward, the five lines are all stops, but not of the back—they are not true stops.

*Gen* of the toes is the learning of taking even a single step. *Gen* of the calves is the learning of wishing to stop but resenting the inability to stop—this is what one may hope for. *Gen* of the waist is restraining at the point of movement, forcibly demanding stillness—this is Gaozi’s learning of forcibly controlling the mind, with whom one can never speak. *Gen* of the body is disciplining the self. *Gen* of the jaws is being cautious in speech. These are all *Gen*, but they are not the difficult *Gen* to speak of. Without *Gen*, one cannot speak of learning, and thus the text concludes with "reverent restraint." Yet the true learning of the world is not merely stopping. Stopping leads to stability, stability to stillness, stillness to peace—one can then reflect like a mirror, resonate like a struck bell, achieve balance, and govern the world while transcending it.

However, if one is not roused by thunder to the point of losing life and fate, losing countless treasures, or losing great affairs, it is not easy to cleanse and suddenly stop. Fang Shihua says: "The First Six, at the place of the toes, is able to restrain and stop—its freedom from blame is evident. For good fortune, misfortune, regret, and remorse all arise from moving the toes and then stopping; not moving would incur blame. Yet what is valued is 'favorable for eternal steadfastness.' How can one remain forever unmoving? Thus it says: 'no blame, favorable for eternal steadfastness.' If one can be eternally steadfast, then even movement incurs no blame."

The Master says: "Stopping is difficult for people. But if one can restrain and stop, one naturally does not lose correctness. Perhaps through stopping one may even attain the place to stop—who can say? The world has never seen one who loves movement and does not stop who can stop where he should stop. This is why the images of the lines mutually elucidate one another."

The Six in the Second is at the place of the calves. The calves do not move on their own—this images the second line’s stopping of the calves, which cannot but follow the feet in movement, imaging the second line’s failure to find its place to stop. The calves, by nature, do not wish to move. Yet when they follow the feet in movement and have no means to lift them, this is why the Six in the Second is always ill at ease and resents its inability to subdue its own heart. Thus it says: "not yielding to retreat." Yet the aspiration of the Six in the Second is truly pitiable.

**Appendix**
Yang Jian says: "One who stops well stops while walking the good path. One who knows where to stop but does not walk does not truly know where to stop. One who knows walking but does not know stopping does not truly know walking. Knowing that neither walking nor stopping is wrong, yet unable to unify the two—if both are timely, it is still not the bright Dao. Only when the four seasons alternate in their courses, when the sun and moon take turns shining, is there brightness—only then is the Dao of the *Yi* attained.

A person’s spirit is entirely in the face, not in the back; entirely in the front, not in the rear. Thus the sage teaches 'restraining the back,' so that what the face turns toward, what the ears, eyes, nose, mouth, hands, and feet do, all follow the back. Then, though the applications intersect and the myriad affairs swarm, all is ultimately still. Seeing, hearing, speaking, moving, and the twists and turns of thought are like the transformations of heaven and earth. Only this is *Gen*, only this is stopping. If one restrains the face, though one stops, it is still movement. Knowing it moves and forcibly stopping it—it will never stop. Only by restraining the back does the face become like the back, the front like the rear, movement like stillness. Serene and without self, 'one does not grasp the body.' Though walking in the courtyard and interacting with people, one truly does not see them. Stopping where it should be stopped—this is stopping without a place, stopping without stopping. It is not stopping because one desires to stop, nor forcibly stopping what is not by nature still."

Fundamentally, there is stopping; fundamentally, there is a place. Now, to speak of "stopping where it should be stopped" is to stop at the fundamental place. Without a place, there is no stopping. Stopping without stopping—this is true stopping. This is what is meant by "stopping where it should be stopped." If there is a self, then there is a place.

Xiong Guo says: According to ancient script, the character for "back" (背) is written as "north" (北), or sometimes as "stop" (止). Master Zui states that following the hexagram’s text, "back" is correct. Wang Ji says: Two thunder, two wind, two fire, two water, two marsh—all imply the meaning of going and coming. Only two mountains stand in mutual opposition, not going or coming to each other; thus, this is stopping. The office of the heart is thought; thought that does not transgress its position is called correct thought. Like water in a mirror, it responds to things yet remains constantly still. Like the sun and moon, their steadfast brightness transforms and acts, illuminating the myriad things yet never moving. When the function of the thinking heart clings to non-thought, it becomes sunk in emptiness. When it clings to thought, it becomes ensnared by things. Non-thought without non-understanding—this is the lost learning of a thousand sages.

Former scholars spoke of "not transgressing one’s position" as in "one who is accustomed to wealth and honor acts within wealth and honor, one accustomed to poverty and lowliness acts within poverty and lowliness," focusing solely on responding to the near at hand, yet failing to clarify the fundamental purport of thought. However, "thought that does not transgress its position" is precisely the meaning of "not desiring what is external." Heart and the near at hand are not divided into two separate matters. *Gen* means stopping. "Stopping at the back" is stopping where it should be stopped. The functions of the ears, eyes, mouth, nose, and the four limbs all manifest in the face. Only the back does not move; thus, it is taken as the image. Though the back does not move, the five viscera all press against it; the nourishment of the nine orifices and the hundred bones is conveyed through the back. Thus, it is the function of non-function.

*Yiyin*, Lower Canon, Scroll Two, ends here.

#5 "center" [央] as "end" [夬]; #6 "follow its constancy" [道其常] as "encounter its changes" [遭其变]; #7 "all" [咸] as "complete" [成]; #8 "woman" [女] as "you" [汝]; #9 "lead" [牵] as "bind" [系]; #10 this sentence as "thus alone contains the beauty of one’s own chapter, serving the firm center of the nine twos"; #11 "cannot" [不能] as "can" [能不]; #12 the Jigu Pavilion edition omits one "chapter" [章] character here; #13 "beneficial to have somewhere to go, following Heaven’s mandate" [利有攸往，顺天命也] originally "beneficial to have somewhere, following the great mandate" [利有攸者，顺大命也], now corrected according to the standard *Zhouyi*; #14 after "with" [以], the Jigu Pavilion edition omits "as" [为], now supplemented; #15 after "one line ascends" [一爻升], likely missing "then" [即]; #16 originally "great" [大], the Jigu Pavilion edition has "wood" [木], now corrected accordingly; *Yiyin*, Lower Canon, Scroll Three
*Gen* below, *Xun* above

**Gradual Progress (Jian)**
*Marriage of a woman is auspicious; perseverance in rectitude is favorable.*

**The Judgment (Tuan):**
Gradual progress means the woman’s marriage is auspicious. Advancing to attain position brings merit in one’s undertakings. Progress through rectitude can rectify the state. Her position is firm, with the yang attaining the center. Stillness combined with compliance ensures movement is not exhausted.

**The Image (Xiang):**
Beneath the mountain grows a tree—this is *Gradual Progress*. The noble person abides in virtuous power and refines customs.

**Line Statements:**
**Initial Six:**
The wild goose gradually advances to the shore.
A youth is in peril; there is talk, but no blame.
**The Image:**
The peril of the youth is blameless in principle.

**Six in the Second Place:**
The wild goose gradually advances to the sandbar.
Feasting with joy—auspicious.
**The Image:**
Feasting with joy is not mere idle satiety.

**Nine in the Third Place:**
The wild goose gradually advances to the highland.
The husband goes forth and does not return; the wife conceives but does not bear—ominous.
Favorable for warding off raiders.
**The Image:**
The husband goes forth and does not return—he parts from the flock.
The wife conceives but does not bear—she loses the Way.
Favorable for warding off raiders—compliance ensures mutual protection.

**Six in the Fourth Place:**
The wild goose gradually advances to the tree.
Perhaps it finds a perch—no blame.
**The Image:**
Perhaps it finds a perch—compliance and submission prevail.

**Nine in the Fifth Place:**
The wild goose gradually advances to the hill.
The wife does not conceive for three years, yet none can overcome her—auspicious.
**The Image:**
None can overcome her—auspicious, for she attains her desire.

**Top Nine:**
The wild goose gradually advances to the highland.
Its feathers may be used for ceremonial adornment—auspicious.
**The Image:**
Its feathers may be used for ceremonial adornment—auspicious, for they cannot be disordered.

**Wind over Mountain: Gradual Progress**
Fang Shihua says: *Influence (Xian)* and *Gradual Progress* both concern the union of man and woman. Yet *Influence* means mutual resonance—a matter of great significance in learning and inquiry. Thus, though it takes the marriage of a woman as its theme, it does not speak solely of marriage. *Gradual Progress*, however, addresses it directly.

The initial stage of a woman’s marriage is like the first line. The second and fifth lines represent the proper course of a woman’s marriage. The third and fourth lines represent its expedient adaptations. The top line embodies the constancy of a woman’s chastity.

When a woman is about to marry, she is like a wild goose leaving the water—hence the reference to the shore. Marriage is not yet complete, so she is called a "youth." A young woman, newly betrothed, must leave her parents and brothers, feeling uneasy and anxious—hence the terms "peril" and "talk." This is the natural state of a woman; how could it be blameworthy in principle?

The sandbar is a stone in the water. To advance there and feast with ease, self-reliant and unperturbed, is like the steadfastness of Meng Guang. Such a woman does not merely seek satiety—hence the auspiciousness.

The fifth line’s advance to the hill is not unstable, but she refuses to settle rashly. Though she does not conceive for three years, she is not barren—she refuses to bear children through a hasty match. Yet none can overcome her, for she either chooses a single partner without remarrying or rejects many suitors before finally fulfilling her wish—like Liang Hong. This is the proper course of a woman’s marriage, embodied in the second and fifth lines.

The third line has no proper correlate. With whom can it pair? The wild goose advancing to the highland is a sign of instability. Separated from its kind, it rises alone among two yin lines. The husband’s failure to return is not by choice; forced to accept the fourth line, he dares not openly raise a child. The wife’s failure to bear is ominous. Yet a lone woman without support cannot form a union. Who, then, wards off raiders?

The fourth line is also uneasy. If the wild goose advances to a tree, is that a proper perch? Its compliance and submission arise from necessity, having no proper correlate or support. A woman without support faces grave peril. If she finds a perch, she may avoid blame. This describes the third and fourth lines forming a union through mutual proximity—the expedient adaptation of a woman’s marriage.

Alas! If a wild goose loses its mate, it will never pair again. Flying alone, it cries out sharply—so unyielding that its feathers may be used for ceremonial adornment. Like the third line, it has no proper correlate and is unstable, hence the shared image of the highland.

**Appendix**
Wang Fusi says: Advancing to the highland and aligning with the fourth line means it cannot return. The husband does not return—does he delight in depravity? If he takes a wife, she cannot uphold chastity. Conceiving by another, she cannot bear—this is the path of misfortune. The third line, originally of the *Gen* trigram, abandons its flock and aligns with the fourth, never returning. This leads the wife to conceive but not bear—a path of misfortune. Yet when different natures unite in harmony, compliance ensures mutual protection, and none can intervene—hence the favor in warding off raiders.

Xiong Guo says: The fourth line, of the *Xun* trigram, is the remnant of wood—hence the wild goose advancing to a tree may find a perch. Li Dingzuo notes: The fourth line occupies a yin position, fitting the image of a perch.

Master Yu says: The fifth and second lines correspond—this is the proper union of husband and wife, hence auspicious. The third and fourth lines are adjacent—this is an improper match, hence ominous.

**Marrying Maiden (Guimei)**
*Advancing brings misfortune; nothing is favorable.*

**The Judgment (Tuan):**
*Marrying Maiden* embodies the great principle of Heaven and Earth. If Heaven and Earth do not unite, the myriad things cannot flourish. *Marrying Maiden* marks the beginning and end of human life. Joy in movement—this is the marriage of a maiden. Advancing brings misfortune, for positions are not proper. Nothing is favorable, for the yielding rides upon the firm.

**The Image (Xiang):**
Thunder over the marsh—this is *Marrying Maiden*. The noble person, understanding decay, ensures lasting fulfillment.

**Line Statements:**
**Initial Nine:**
The marrying maiden serves as a concubine.
Though lame, she can walk—advancing is auspicious.
**The Image:**
The marrying maiden serves as a concubine—this is the norm.
Though lame, she can walk—auspicious in mutual support.

**Nine in the Second Place:**
Though blind in one eye, she can see—favorable for the chastity of a reclusive person.
**The Image:**
Favorable for the chastity of a reclusive person—she has not altered her constancy.

**Six in the Third Place:**
The marrying maiden waits, then returns as a concubine.
**The Image:**
The marrying maiden waits—her position is not proper.

**Nine in the Fourth Place:**
The marrying maiden delays her wedding; her return awaits the proper time.

**The Image:**
The delayed wedding’s intent—acting with proper timing.

**Six in the Fifth Place:**
When Emperor Yi marries off his younger sister, the lord’s sleeves are not as fine as her concubine’s sleeves.
The moon nears fullness—auspicious.
**The Image:**
When Emperor Yi marries off his younger sister, the concubine’s sleeves surpass the lord’s.
Her position is central—she proceeds with nobility.

**Top Six:**
The woman holds an empty basket, yet no fruit;
The man slaughters a sheep, yet no blood.
No advantage in any direction.
**The Image:**
The Top Six’s empty basket—she bears a hollow vessel.

**Thunder over Marsh: Marrying Maiden**
This is the gradual progression of a woman into marriage.
Though wed, she does not immediately assume the central position of the inner palace as empress.
From consort to empress, she advances by degrees—hence the term *gradual*.
Now, in *Marrying Maiden*, all who are given in marriage are women.
Yet only the woman who marries as the primary wife is auspicious, while the marrying maiden’s advance is ominous—why?
Because the woman who marries as the primary wife assumes the proper position, that of the *Lady Sovereign*.
Thus, it is said: *favorable for constancy*—*constancy* meaning *proper position*.
By advancing gradually to approach the ruler’s rightful place, her progress yields merit—hence: *advancing brings merit*.
By gradually assuming the proper position within, she may rectify the virtue of all women in the state—hence: *she may rectify the state*.
Moreover, the ruler’s position is firm and centered—this is the Nine in the Fifth Place.
The position of the lord’s wife is both *still* and *yielding*, her movement inexhaustible.
Such is the advantage of propriety—this is why the woman who marries as the primary wife is auspicious.
Now, in *Marrying Maiden*, the Top Six’s lines depict a young girl given in marriage to another—mere concubinage, how can it compare?
Furthermore, she moves only when pleased by another’s will.
If she acts on her own, her advance is surely ominous—what advantage could there be?

The passage speaks of the maiden bearing a basket with nothing inside, and the gentleman presenting a sheep with no blood—this signifies that when the woman’s family gives her in marriage bearing a basket, it should contain something, yet if she is not a concubine or secondary wife, then she bears a basket with nothing inside. When the man’s family takes a bride and offers a sheep to present its blood, if concubines and secondary wives are not included, then though a sheep is slaughtered, there is no blood to present.

Moreover, the Top Six, being yielding, rides atop the Fifth’s firmness, hence there is no advantage in any direction, for her position is improper. To rise above the position of the lady of the house is called "advancing brings misfortune." Thus, this line is fittingly assigned to her.

Of the remaining five lines, excluding the Nine in the Fourth Place, which occupies the upper position without correspondence—representing the elder sister awaiting her time and not yet married—while the Fifth occupies the central position, a place of honor and nobility. This is the younger sister of King Di Yi, married to the eldest son. The Second is her proper counterpart—is she not the favored concubine? Yet she is not as fine as the concubine, for the Fifth is yin while the Second is yang. "One-eyed yet able to see" means she follows the lady and dare not look directly. She does not presume to take the evening watch, guarding her solitude in seclusion—hence it is said: "advantageous for the secluded one to maintain integrity." How is this different from a maiden in her chamber? Thus, it is said: "she has not altered the norm."

The First and Second share the same body, following the Nine in the Second—this is constancy. At home, she follows her

The *Thunder-Fire Feng* Hexagram: Fang Shihua’s Commentary

Feng means greatness. When the grand yang illumines the heavens, all beings stir into motion—thus its greatness is evident. Yet if dusk approaches, all motion ceases. How then can its greatness be perceived? This greatness—who makes it so? The king makes it so. The king, turning toward brightness to govern, esteems greatness; hence it is said, "the king attains it." *Jia* means greatness. The king values greatness, yet in this hexagram, the radiance of *Li* lies below while the movement of *Zhen* thunders above. *Li* below is not merely past its zenith—it nears dusk. *Zhen* above not only obscures the sun at noon—it brings dark clouds and gloomy rain. One fears the lack of brightness—how can there be greatness? Yet the sun at noon will reverse its course, and once reversed, it must return to midday. Thunder’s movement brings only darkness, but when it passes, brightness must return. Though *Li*’s radiance now lies below, the sun will naturally reach its zenith again. Though *Zhen*’s thunder now rolls above, the sun will naturally illuminate all under heaven once more. Why fear the sun’s failure to reach midday or the world’s lack of illumination? Thus it is said: "Do not worry. It is fitting for the sun to be at midday, fitting to illuminate all under heaven." At midday, there is brightness; with brightness, there is movement; with movement, there is greatness. This is the king’s work. Who but the Sixth Five can fulfill this? Hence it is said: *lai zhang* ("bringing splendor"). *Lai zhang* means the Sixth Two’s midday splendor is drawn forth. The auspiciousness of celebration and praise is the prosperity of *feng*. The Sixth Two is the master of *Li*, the sun at its zenith, yet with thunder above, it bears the image of *feng*’s obscuring reeds and the Dipper seen at noon. Though one may doubt the sun’s presence when traveling, what harm befalls it? Its brilliance remains, and its sincerity never fails to shine. Who causes the obscuring reeds? The Nine Four does. Four resides in the *Zhen* trigram, the thunder’s first stir. Thus it is said: "Its position is not proper, for it obscures its place." The Dipper seen at noon—dim and unclear—is injured; is this not *ming yi* ("darkening of the light")? Yet when clouds disperse and veils lift, the sun at noon reappears, and one again encounters the lord of brightness. Though dim and unclear, how can this not be auspicious? The auspiciousness of Four is like that of Five and Two. Since it is midday, the celebration of greatness is shared by all. Thus it is said: "Auspicious, for it moves forward."

The Initial Nine and Six Two share the same luminous body. When the Initial meets the Second, does it not meet its proper lord? Yet because the Second is obscured by the reeds of *feng*, it is told: "Even if ten days pass, after rain there will surely be the celebration of midday. Going forth, you will naturally find your match—why fear misfortune?" The *Xiang* commentary further judges this as certain, for the principle of "beyond ten days" does not apply. If it exceeds ten days, it becomes calamity—no need to worry.

The Nine Three, positioned beneath the thunder’s movement, brings not only dark clouds and rain but torrential downpours. When the roads are awash with foam, even at midday, can one venture out? Of the three lines in *Li*, only the Second is at midday; the Initial and Third are like left and right arms. Yet the Third cannot move—it bears the image of a broken right arm. "No misfortune" means the passing of dark rain is not the fault of Nine Three. Truly, without brightness, there is no movement; to move without brightness is not greatness. Thus greatness for all under heaven is what the king esteems.

Those who "enlarge their houses" act otherwise. They do not consider the sun at midday illuminating all under heaven, ensuring no one beneath it is left unenlightened. Instead, they diminish above to enrich below, enlarging their own houses, as if to say: "I stand high above—who can do anything to me?" Yet what use is this to anyone? For when a household has no one, when doors are shut and no one is seen, when ice melts and tiles crumble, leaving no trace—how dire is the misfortune! Thus the *Xiang* mocks them: "When they enlarged their houses, they thought themselves soaring at heaven’s edge, as if roaming the skies—yet they did not realize this was precisely how they buried their own homes. For when a house has no household, when doors have no one to hide, this is called self-enlargement and self-burial—what use is a grand house then? This is no fault of others but of self-illumination that does not consider illuminating all under heaven. Those who illuminate themselves cannot illuminate others and instead fall into darkness. Yet those who illuminate all under heaven, though thunder and rain fill the skies, remain as if the sun is ever at its zenith. Thus the king should be at midday, illuminating all under heaven. To illuminate all under heaven is to bring greatness to all under heaven. Bringing greatness to all under heaven, even at its utmost, is not about seeking grandeur or delighting in achievement—celebration and praise naturally return to one. How can this compare to those who enlarge their houses and self-obscure, becoming isolated and ultimately burying themselves?"

**Appendix**
*Fa qi hu, ban qi wu ren* ("shutting the door, planking over the absence of people"): *Fa* means to eliminate; *tai* means to discard. *Wei* means a small view; *tai mu* means to discard the eyes. Thus it speaks of first looking narrowly, then even with eyes wide open, seeing no one.

---
**Hexagram 56: *Lǚ* (Traveling)**
*Lǚ*: Small prosperity. Perseverance in travel brings good fortune.

**The *Tuan* Commentary**
*Lǚ*: Small prosperity. The yielding attains the center externally and follows the firm. It halts yet clings to brightness—thus there is small prosperity. Perseverance in travel brings good fortune. The timeliness of travel is greatly significant.

**The *Xiang* Commentary**
Fire beneath the mountain: *Lǚ*. The noble one uses clarity and caution in applying punishments, not prolonging imprisonment.

**The Lines**
**Initial Six**: In travel, petty and mean—this brings misfortune upon oneself.
**The *Xiang* Commentary**: Petty and mean in travel—misfortune arises from a constrained will.

**Six Two**: In travel, one lodges at an inn, holding one’s resources, and obtains a steadfast servant.
**The *Xiang* Commentary**: Obtaining a steadfast servant—ultimately, there is no blame.

**Nine Three**: In travel, one’s inn burns. One loses one’s servant. Perseverance brings danger.

**The *Xiang* Commentary**
The inn burns in travel—this is indeed grievous. To travel and yet yield to inferiors—by principle, one loses all.

**Nine Four**: In travel, one finds shelter, gains one’s resource-axe, yet the heart remains indecisive.
**The *Xiang* Commentary**: Traveling in shelter—one has not attained position. Gaining one’s resource-axe—the heart is not at ease.

**Six Five**: Shooting a pheasant, one arrow is lost; in the end, praise and mandate follow.
**The *Xiang* Commentary**: In the end, praise and mandate—this reaches the highest.

**Top Nine**: The bird’s nest burns; the traveler first laughs, then wails and weeps. An ox is lost at the frontier—misfortune.
**The *Xiang* Commentary**: To travel while in the highest position—by principle, it burns. An ox lost at the frontier—none in the end hear of it.

**Fire over Mountain: *Lǚ***
Fang Shihua says: Fire rising above the mountain—travelers set forth. Lodging in the wild, eating and resting—such is the traveler’s nature, hence it is called *Lǚ*. How does travel bring prosperity and good fortune? With *li*’s yielding in the outer position attaining the center, and submitting to *gèn*’s stillness within—the master’s firmness—thus the inner trigram’s stillness can cling to the outer trigram’s brightness. The master thereby achieves merit for the traveler, and the traveler naturally attains prosperity and good fortune, all through the constancy of travel.

The inner three lines represent the travels of merchants, scholars, and commoners—ordinary travel. Thus their gains and losses concern only the inn and servants. The outer three lines represent the travels of lords, ministers, and grandees—here, travel is momentous. Some rise through travel, others fall, hence it is said: *The timeliness of travel is greatly significant.*

**Initial Six**, yin at the lowest, bears the image of petty travel, knowing only what travel should be. Petty and ignorant—this is why misfortune befalls. Had it been more generous, even the servant would have received a share of grace. Yet yin’s softness risks pettiness, while yang’s firmness risks excess. **Nine Three**, yang in a yang position, is excessive. Dry and near *li*, it bears the image of a burning inn; below, it loses the first, bearing the image of a lost servant. For Initial Six, of the same body below, is the servant’s image. Six Two, yielding and centered, draws near the first and gains it; Nine Three, overly firm, distances itself from the first and loses it. Thus the *Xiang* says: *The inn burns in travel—this is indeed grievous.* How could it also involve losing a servant? For in travel, to yield to inferiors thus—if one did not lose a servant, it would defy principle. Hence it is said: *by principle, one loses all.* Pettiness is difficult, but enduring excess—how can one bear it? Excess only deepens pettiness.

**Six Two**, centered, bears the image of lodging at an inn; yielding and correct, it bears the image of holding resources; gaining the first, it bears the image of obtaining a steadfast servant—this is the most fortunate of ordinary travels. **Nine Four**, ascending to Five, bears the image of traveling in shelter. Those above should occupy their position and fulfill their duties. Yet if today they lodge here, tomorrow there, drifting from place to place, even if they gain their resource-axe, they remain rootless wanderers—how can the heart be at ease? At its extreme, **Top Nine** has *li*’s fire above, bearing the image of burning. *Li*’s eye sheds tears, bearing the image of wailing and weeping. *Li* nurtures the cow; the top yang loses it, bearing the image of an ox lost. Yet below, the inn burns; above, the nest burns—because the traveler below stops at the inn, while the traveler above, by necessity, loses their nest entirely. Were not Xia Taikang and Sui Yang Guang such cases? At first, they laughed in self-satisfaction; in the end, though they wailed, what good did it do? To seize great things and lose them all—had they even heard of their own misfortune, how could it be anything but dire?

Thus it is said: "With the traveler above, its principle is burning." This means that one who dwells above others yet remains a traveler on the road—if their nest is not burned, it is still inevitably doomed. Only the fifth line achieves success in this position. *Li* is the pheasant; the fifth line’s traveler shoots the pheasant. Initially, it was the image of Qian’s three yangs, like three arrows striking true. But when yang suddenly shifts to yin, one arrow is lost. In travel, this becomes the image of initial ventures failing or first undertakings going awry. Yet, abandoning Qian’s firm strength to attain the gentle brilliance of *wen*, one ultimately gains literary renown and a lofty mandate through the act of shooting the pheasant—hence it is said to "reach above." Were not Xia Shaokang, Qi Xiaobai, and Jin Chong’er such cases?

The noble person observes travel and knows that those below cannot avoid going forth, while those above cannot lightly depart. Those who cannot avoid going forth do so either to seek fame and achievement or to amass wealth and profit.
