They treat servants as their own children and serve their masters as elder brothers; they do not covet money, nor do they lightly express joy or anger—thus they remain free of trouble forever. But how can those above travel on the road? If travel is unavoidable for those above, it is not for the sake of reputation or mandate; to "reach above" is decisively impossible.

**Appendix**
Wang Ji said: "Fire upon the mountain shines but does not illuminate—this is the image of brightness restrained and caution. Fire moves and does not stay—this is the image of impermanence. Travel is always adverse, but nothing is worse than being imprisoned. Prisons are established out of necessity; how can one remain trapped and long detained? Be as illuminating as fire, as steadfast as a mountain. All who sojourn abroad are travelers. The Son of Heaven has the Son of Heaven’s travels—such as when the Heavenly King resided in Zheng. The feudal lords have the feudal lords’ travels—such as when the Duke was assigned to Qianzhou. The grand officers have the grand officers’ travels—such as Chen Wenzi’s sojourn in another state. The sages and worthies have the sages’ and worthies’ travels—such as Confucius’s circuitous journeys and Mencius’s diplomatic missions.

Liu Yongxiang said: "King Wen traveled amid hardship, yet his intimacy was profound and his thoughts abundant—hence it is said, 'The traveler is petty and brings disaster upon himself.' It is also said: 'When traveling and lodging, though one gains their resource-axe, my heart is not at ease—how grievous! Traveler, who but King Wen could bear this?' The Master traveled east, west, south, and north—thus it is said: 'To travel with those below is to lose; to travel with those above is to burn.' There is no permissible course, whether above or below."

**Xun (Wind)**
*Xun*: Small success. Beneficial to have somewhere to go; beneficial to see the great person.

**The Judgment** says: "Repeated *xun* to issue commands. The firm *xun* in the center and correct, and their will is carried out. The yielding all submit to the firm—thus there is small success. Beneficial to have somewhere to go; beneficial to see the great person."

**The Image** says: "Following wind is *xun*. The noble person issues commands and carries out affairs."

- **Initial Six**: Advancing and retreating—beneficial for the warrior’s steadfastness.
  **The Image** says: "Advancing and retreating—this is a wavering will. Beneficial for the warrior’s steadfastness—this is a disciplined will."

- **Nine in the Second**: *Xun* beneath the forest. Employing scribes and shamans, profuse and auspicious—no blame.
  **The Image** says: "The auspiciousness of profusion—this is attaining the center."

- **Nine in the Third**: Frequently *xun*—regret.
  **The Image** says: "The regret of frequent *xun*—this is a will exhausted."

- **Six in the Fourth**: Regret vanishes. In the hunt, three kinds are obtained.
  **The Image** says: "In the hunt, three kinds are obtained—this is meritorious."

- **Nine in the Fifth**: Steadfast and auspicious, regret vanishes. Nothing is initially beneficial. Without beginning, there is an end. Three days before *geng*, three days after *geng*—auspicious.
  **The Image** says: "The auspiciousness of Nine in the Fifth—this is the position of correctness and the center."

- **Top Nine**: *Xun* beneath the bed. Losing one’s resource-axe—misfortune.
  **The Image** says: "*Xun* beneath the bed—this is exhaustion above. Losing one’s resource-axe—this is truly misfortune."

**Xun is wind.**
Fang Shihua said: "Wind is Heaven’s decree; commands are the ruler’s wind. When the great lord issues a mandate, thrice commanding and fivefold reiterating, he desires *xun* to penetrate—hence repeated *xun* to issue it. The nine in the second, firm yet *xun* in the center and correctness of the fifth, thus the will of issuing commands is carried out, and the yielding all submit to the firm. Therefore, there is small success, beneficial to have somewhere to go, and beneficial to see the great person. The benefit of having somewhere to go and the benefit of seeing—what else could this be but the small success of the lesser?"

Ni’s *xun* says: "The lord issues commands to proclaim to the four directions." Huan’s *xun* says: "Huan sweats its great proclamation." Thus, nothing is as yielding yet able to *xun* and penetrate others as wind; nothing is as firm yet nothing can resist it as wind. Nothing dares not submit—only the nine in the fifth is sufficient to embody this, hence it is called the great person.

Master Yu said: "*Jia* is the first of the ten stems, the beginning of affairs. When disorder is restored to order, it is as if returning to the start—hence in *Gu*, *jia* is said: 'In the end, there is a beginning.' *Geng* is the turning point of the ten stems, the proper moment for change in affairs. If the beginning was not good, it is changed and afterward becomes good—hence in *Xun*, *geng* is said: 'Without beginning, there is an end.' Three days before *geng*, three days after *geng*—repeatedly issuing commands—precisely means being cautious to the end. Thus, the end is auspicious. Therefore, only the fifth is steadfast and auspicious, with regret and nothing initially beneficial."

The initial six, at the beginning of *xun*, in terms of constant correspondence should respond to the fourth, but in terms of timing should submit to the fifth. Advancing and retreating without resolution—this is doubt. Not knowing that the fourth has decisively submitted to the fifth and eliminated the regret of indecision, it becomes the small success of the lesser and the meritorious minister. How martial! Moreover, the fourth submits to the fifth, and the three lines below all submit to the fifth—is this not the hunt obtaining three kinds? The fourth hunts and obtains three kinds—this is the warrior. Thus, it is beneficial for the initial line’s steadfastness as a warrior. Yet in the hexagram, only two yielding lines both submit to the firm—the fourth is their forerunner."

The *xun* wood becomes the piglet in the second line, situated below—this depicts the image of submission. Employing scribes and shamans in profuse confusion—is this not what is meant by "firm yet yielding, centered and upright, with aspirations fulfilled"? Thus, it is called auspicious and blameless.

As for the nine in the third place, it inquires doubly into submission, seemingly desiring to yield to the fifth but with aspirations truly fixed upon the nine above in its exhausted adversity. The nine above, dwelling at the apex of double submission, also yields to what lies below. One who reaches the utmost height must return to what is below. To avoid losing one’s axe and resources is impossible. The fourth’s obtaining of three kinds was for the sake of securing the initial line’s benefit as a martial figure, yet now it forfeits its axe and resources, unaware of whom it should submit to. Though it yields beneath the forest, what use is it? Thus, it is said, "steadfastness brings misfortune." This means that in a time of submission, submission itself is indeed steadfastness, yet without knowing whom to submit to, even steadfastness leads to misfortune. Hence, it is also said, "Is this upright? I see only misfortune." This should be read as two separate phrases.

Xiong Guo remarked: "Zhai Yun’s 'three kinds' refer to the three lower lines." Yang Tingshu said: *"The Shuo Gua* states that Xun (☴) embodies advance and retreat, hence its ultimate form is a restless hexagram, thus martial." **Dui (☱) below, Dui (☱) above: Dui.** Smooth progress, beneficial, steadfast.

**The *Tuan* says:** Dui means joy. Firmness within and softness without—joy brings benefit through steadfastness. Thus, it accords with Heaven and responds to humanity. Joy leads the people, and they forget their toil; joy faces adversity, and they forget death. The greatness of joy spurs the people to effort.

**The *Xiang* says:** Twin lakes form Dui. The noble person engages in discourse and study with friends.

**First Nine:** Harmonious joy—auspicious.
**The *Xiang* says:** The auspiciousness of harmonious joy lies in unhesitating action.

**Nine in the Second:** Trusting joy—auspicious, regret vanishes.
**The *Xiang* says:** The auspiciousness of trusting joy lies in the sincerity of one’s intent.

**Six in the Third:** Seeking joy—misfortune.
**The *Xiang* says:** The misfortune of seeking joy stems from an improper position.

**Nine in the Fourth:** Deliberating joy—restless, yet illness yields to joy.
**The *Xiang* says:** The joy of Nine in the Fourth brings celebration.

**Nine in the Fifth:** Trust in stripping—danger.
**The *Xiang* says:** Trust in stripping arises from a proper position.

**Top Six:** Drawing out joy.
**The *Xiang* says:** Top Six drawing out joy lacks brilliance.

Dui is the marsh. Fang Shihua said: "Twin marshes form Dui. Dui means joy. Two marshes adjoining, two joys trusting—this is the Way of teacher and friend." The hexagram extols this Way to the point of moving Heaven and humanity, spurring the people to effort. Yet how is this achieved? Through friendship alone. Pervading the Five Bonds, all actions are deliberated, rectifying ruler and minister, so that all above and below become teacher and friend. Though this hexagram Dui derives its image from a single yin line, yin softness is not upright—how could it bring smooth progress? Thus, the hexagram speaks of "beneficial steadfastness," and the *Zhuan* states: "Firmness within and softness without—joy brings benefit through steadfastness." Only firmness within ensures steadfastness. Only firmness within with softness without explains why the four yang lines excel. Without firmness, there is no steadfastness; without steadfastness, misfortune follows—this is why the two yin lines falter. Observing the six lines, the meaning of friendship becomes clear.

The first line embodies joy and is called "harmonious," while the fourth also embodies joy yet is termed "deliberating"—deliberation precludes harmony. At the outset of friendship, before faults emerge, all is pure harmony, hence it is struck as "harmonious." Is this not the time when Master Liu of the Willow first emerged into the world? The weight of righteousness and the joy of reunion reside in the First Nine—stepping beyond the door, a single day’s fellowship. The fourth, however, suffers from the excesses of harmony, finding peace only through restraint. It does not delight in "harmonious joy" but rejoices in "deliberating joy"—does this not diverge from Bo Yi and Shu Qi’s remorse at blocking the horse? If one merely harmonizes light and blends dust, why seek friends at all? For this reason, both benefit. Not only can Liu Xia Hui remain unyielding before the Three Dukes, but even the sons of Mount Shouyang ultimately set aside old grievances—such is the mutual illumination of the first and fourth lines.

The second and fifth lines also mutually illuminate. Nine in the Second, firm and centered, directly trusts its intent, finding joy in sincerity. If joy arises from trust, how could it be false? Regret stems from joy, yet the second has no joy—how could regret arise? Thus, it aligns with the fifth. The fifth, receiving this, shares its firmness and centeredness, its trust and sincerity. Though the second’s direct pursuit of its intent seems to strip the fifth, the fifth trusts in this stripping. To be stripped yet accept it seems perilous and unbearable—yet its position is not properly aligned. Without this, how could one befriend a minister? Is this not like King Gaozong’s timely rain or Fu Yue’s salt and plum?

The third and top lines also mutually illuminate, yet their people are unworthy. The third seeks to draw near, using joy as its lure. The top follows, using joy to amplify its light. This is the exchange of marketplace dealings, the demeanor of flattery. Rare in ancient and modern times, yet still called "my teacher, my friend"—is this not lamentable? This hexagram exhaustively depicts the conditions of friendship, yet within it lies the great person. The great person seeks only to rectify themselves, not to rectify others, yet all friends are encompassed in their all-embracing care. Those who "deliberate joy" cannot avoid restraint, while those who "harmonize joy" verge on irreverence. Only Nine in the Fifth’s trust in stripping, unperturbed by danger, approaches this ideal.

**Appendix:**
Hu Yizhi said: "It is not called 'joy' (*shuo*) but 'Dui' because the sages’ influence on the world transcends words and speech, hence they remove speech and leave Dui."

Xiong Guo said: "The fourth and third lines, though in different positions, remain sharply divided."

**Kan (☵) below, Xun (☴) above: Huan.**
Smooth progress. The king approaches the ancestral temple. Beneficial to ford great rivers, beneficial to be steadfast.

**The *Tuan* says:** Huan—smooth progress. The firm comes and is not exhausted; the yielding attains its position externally and aligns above. "The king approaches the ancestral temple"—the king resides at the center. "Beneficial to ford great rivers"—riding wood brings success.

**The *Xiang* says:** Wind moves over water—Huan. The former kings, offering to the Supreme Emperor, established temples.

**First Six:** Rescuing with a strong horse—auspicious.
**The *Xiang* says:** The auspiciousness of First Six lies in compliance.

**Nine in the Second:** Huan flees to its support—regret vanishes.
**The *Xiang* says:** "Huan flees to its support"—desires are fulfilled.

**Six in the Third:** Huan disperses the self—no regret.
**The *Xiang* says:** "Huan disperses the self"—the intent lies outward.

**Six in the Fourth:** Huan disperses the multitude—supremely auspicious. Dispersing forms a mound—beyond the thoughts of the common.
**The *Xiang* says:** "Huan disperses the multitude—supremely auspicious"—its brilliance is vast.

**Nine in the Fifth:** Huan sweats its great proclamation; Huan disperses the king’s dwelling—no blame.

**The *Xiang* says:** "The king’s dwelling is without blame"—it is the correct position.

**Top Nine:** Huan disperses its blood, removing and distancing itself—no blame.
**The *Xiang* says:** "Huan disperses its blood"—it distances itself from harm.

**Wind over Water: Huan**
The inner trigram is Kun, and one yang line of Qian comes to reside within it, thus generating water and forming Kan. Water arises without end, hence it is said: "The firm comes and does not exhaust." The outer trigram is Qian, and one yin line of Kun attains its position externally, thus forming wind and generating Xun, aligning above with the fifth line. Hence it is said: "The yielding attains its position externally and aligns above." Qian and Kun intermingle, and wind and water arise; wind and water interact, and the great proclamation is made. Thus, "Huan sweats its great proclamation"—who but the ruler could do this? Yet Nine in the Fifth, residing at the center, remains unmoved like the wind, gathering spiritual essence to borrow the ancestral temple—how would it dare to proclaim? Yet the great proclamation is made. Thus, it is Six in the Fourth that truly effects "Huan sweats its great proclamation." The ruler issues commands; the wind’s voice is established by him, yet he allows a minister to act in his stead—how could there be no blame? However, this minister is one like Yu, whose teachings resounded to the four seas. With a minister like Yu, one may humbly face south, doing nothing yet governing—would this bring blame to Shun? Hence it is said: "The king borrows the ancestral temple." The king is indeed at the center. To say "the king is at the center" is like the North Star dwelling in its place. Commands issue forth in all directions, and the people all comply. Thus, crossing the great river and building boats and oars is due to the fourth line. The people are like water; wind moves over water, and boats sail with the current—what disadvantage could there be? Therefore, though Six in the Fourth disperses, the king truly resides within it. The king does not await dispersion. Hence it is said: "Huan, the king’s dwelling is without blame." If the minister disperses while the king resides, then the one who disperses, though a minister, is in truth the king—what blame could there be? Thus, Six in the Fourth is the master of dispersion, the one who effects it, yet only the fifth line can bring it to fruition. How would the top line dare to claim this? Hence, the fifth line is without blame, for it entrusts responsibility and fittingly borrows the temple to offer sacrifices. The top line must distance itself to avoid blame, for one who receives offerings for great merit fears harm may befall them. Hence it is said: "Huan, its blood disperses, distancing itself within."

The three yin lines all receive dispersion. The third line resides above water—this is precisely what is meant by "the face receives the harmonious wind"—how could it not disperse itself? Only Six in the Fourth, as the master of dispersion, is supremely auspicious. The great cry disperses the multitude—this is the dispersion of private concerns. Thus, water flows downward while wind disperses upward, forming a mound against the current. Hence it is said: "Huan forms a mound," for water levels and does not flow. When the dispersing wind flows, it moves with the current and forms patterns. Hence it is said: "Huan disperses the multitude." This is the great achievement, naturally so—all beyond the thoughts of the common. How could it not be supremely auspicious? How could its brilliance not be vast?

The First and Second lines employ strength, rushing about with purpose—how could there be no smooth progress or advantage? The initial line of Kan, in relation to horses, has thin hooves, not comparable to a strong horse. Thus, the yielding first line resides below. Therefore, to employ rescue, one must have a strong horse to be auspicious. Hence it is said: "Rescuing with a strong horse—auspicious." Otherwise, it would not comply with receiving dispersion. Nine in the Second, a single yang residing at the center, is like riding wood—this is the wooden support. Since the first line employs a strong horse, the second desires to fulfill its wish—how could it not rush to its support and ride upon the wood? Otherwise, regret would surely follow.

Above is Xun, below is Kan—wind above, water below. Wind and water interact, dispersing sweat and issuing the great proclamation. This hexagram’s image is like Yao obtaining Shun, and Shun obtaining Yu—no other can match it.

Fang Shihua said: "Kan, in relation to wood, is hard and multivalent, hence the image of rushing to the support."

**Water over Marsh: Jie**
**Jie:** Smooth progress. Bitter restraint is inadmissible—persist in correctness.
**The *Tuan* says:** Jie brings smooth progress. The firm and yielding are divided, and the firm attains the center. Bitter restraint is inadmissible—persisting in correctness leads to exhaustion. Joy moves through danger; attaining one’s position through restraint brings central correctness and smooth progress. Heaven and Earth are restrained, and the four seasons are completed. Restraint through institutions does not waste resources nor harm the people.
**The *Xiang* says:** Water over the marsh—Jie. The noble one establishes measures and deliberates virtue and conduct.

**First Nine:** Not going beyond the courtyard—no blame.
**The *Xiang* says:** "Not going beyond the courtyard"—knowing what is open and blocked.

**Nine in the Second:** Not going beyond the gate—misfortune.
**The *Xiang* says:** "Not going beyond the gate—misfortune"—losing the extremity of the time.

**Six in the Third:** If one does not restrain, there will be sighing—no blame.
**The *Xiang* says:** "Sighing over lack of restraint"—who else to blame?

**Six in the Fourth:** Peaceful restraint—smooth progress.
**The *Xiang* says:** "Smooth progress through peaceful restraint"—following the way above.

**Six in the Fifth:** Sweet restraint—auspicious; advancing brings esteem.
**The *Xiang* says:** "The auspiciousness of sweet restraint"—residing in the central position.

**Top Six:** Bitter restraint—persistence brings misfortune, but regret vanishes.
**The *Xiang* says:** "Bitter restraint persisting in misfortune"—its way is exhausted.

**Water over Marsh: Jie**
Kan water flows, and Dui marsh receives it. Kan water flows to bring smooth progress, hence Nine in the Fifth, though in Kan, still brings smooth progress. Dui marsh receives it to bring restraint, hence Nine in the Second, though in the marsh, presides over restraint. Yet Dui has an opening above—if the opening is not blocked, though restrained, it is as if unrestrained. Six in the Third is yielding and cannot restrain, thus bringing sighs of misfortune. Nine in the Second is firm but not in its proper position, and fears the reproach of overstepping. Hence the sage speaks twice of its misfortune: its correctness lies in not going beyond the gate—it could go out but refuses to do so.

Here the crux lies in reaching the extreme of lost timing—one could observe the moment and act, yet fails to move.

Now, the trigrams unite as one body, sharing the same marsh in mutual reception. How can one sit idly without thought of blocking or restraining? Thus, the Way of presiding over restraint truly resides in Nine in the Second. Between the second and third lines, one firm and one yielding, Nine in the Second alone is firm and centered—hence the fullness of its responsibility.

Above, Kan water surges and overflows, no place for restraint. To insist on restraint at such a time is truly bitter, and cannot be called correct. Upper Six, yielding and miserly, bitterly ignorant of the Way of proper restraint, thus bears the warning of misfortune in correctness and the sorrow of exhausted Dao.

Had Nine in the Fifth not been centered and correct in its smoothness, had it not practiced sweet restraint with due measure, allowing the flow to proceed, then the sweetness of Dui marsh would have turned into a bitter sea, its sweet waters transformed into bitterness—misfortune would have been unavoidable. Thus, the Way of smooth restraint lies entirely in Nine in the Fifth. Between the fifth and upper lines, one firm and one yielding, Nine in the Fifth alone is firm and centered—hence the sole attribution of auspicious sweet restraint. Thus it is said: "Restraint brings smoothness."

Bitter restraint cannot be correct, for it originally sought to achieve smoothness through restraint. Now, by bitterly insisting on restraint, it leads only to exhaustion and blocked passage—this is not its proper principle.

Yet the upper trigram Kan water remains smooth, and smoothness naturally entails restraint. The lower trigram Dui marsh presides over restraint, and restraint naturally ensures flow. Thus, Nine in the Fourth in the upper trigram, following the upper Dao to find peace, speaks of "peaceful restraint bringing smoothness." Smoothness here means the sweet restraint of centered correctness that enables flow.

In the lower trigram, Initial Nine, though not venturing beyond the gate and courtyard, may sit peacefully without blame. Without blame means knowing that there are those who manage flow and blockage, thus avoiding fault. Flow has five, blockage has two—Nine in the Second restrains them. Nine in the Fifth sweetens them, while Initial Nine and Nine in the Fourth merely enjoy the results—is this not fitting?

**Appendix**
Liu Yongxiang says: Restraint, though correct in principle, if bitter, becomes misfortune even in correctness. Once restraint is taken as correctness, how can one regret it? Thus it is said: "Regret vanishes"—this is reproach, not approval.

Alas! Those who take frugality and austerity as great virtues, who pride themselves on hardship and thrift, would they ever admit their bitter restraint as wrong and regret it? Among ten thousand, scarcely one can regret their own bitter restraint—calling it "regret vanishes" is fitting.

In this hexagram, firm and yielding are divided, with the firm attaining the center: one yielding line from Kun is placed at the top of the lower trigram to form Dui marsh, and one firm line from Qian is placed in the center of the upper trigram to form Kan water.

**Wind over Marsh: Zhong Fu**
"Pig and fish: auspicious. Beneficial to ford great rivers. Beneficial in correctness."
*The Image* says: Zhong Fu: yielding within while the firm attains the center. Joyful and yielding, sincerity transforms the state. "Pig and fish: auspicious"—sincerity extends even to pigs and fish. "Beneficial to ford great rivers"—riding an empty wooden boat. Zhong Fu with beneficial correctness responds to Heaven.

*The Image* says: Wind over marsh: Zhong Fu. The noble one discusses lawsuits and delays executions.

**Line Statements**
**Initial Nine:** "Trust in anticipation: auspicious. With other matters, no peace."
*The Image* says: Initial Nine’s trust in anticipation is auspicious—its will has not changed.

**Nine in the Second:** "A crane calls in the shade, its young responds. I have fine wine, I share it with you without end."
*The Image* says: "Its young responds"—this is the heart’s desire.

**Six in the Third:** "Encountering an enemy, now drumming, now ceasing; now weeping, now singing."
*The Image* says: "Now drumming, now ceasing"—its position is not proper.

**Six in the Fourth:** "The moon nearly full, the horse’s mate lost—no blame."
*The Image* says: "The horse’s mate lost"—cutting ties to ascend.

**Nine in the Fifth:** "Sincerity in bonds—no blame."
*The Image* says: "Sincerity in bonds"—its position is correct and proper.

**Upper Nine:** "Cock’s crow reaching heaven—correctness brings misfortune."
*The Image* says: "Cock’s crow reaching heaven"—how can this last?

**Wind over Marsh: Zhong Fu**
Zhang Zai says: Zhong Fu: above, Xun wind acts; below, Dui joy receives it—there must be transformation through resonance. Sincerity is like a brooding hen, embodying the principle of inevitable birth.

He also says: The child is transformed by sincerity. Only through Zhong Fu can one transform the child; only through Zhong Fu can one respond to the mother. Thus it is said: "Zhong Fu with beneficial correctness responds to Heaven." To respond to Heaven is correctness—this refers specifically to Zhong Fu’s ability to respond. If it does not respond in reality but merely bears an empty name, it becomes Upper Nine’s cock’s crow. Though it reaches heaven, can heaven transform through sincerity?

Thus, the line statement for Nine in the Second says: "A crane calls in the shade, its young responds. I have fine wine; I share it with you without end." Upper and lower forget themselves in this "you and I," the fine wine ungrudgingly given—rereading this line, how is it different from a shared song? It perfectly embodies the *Three Hundred Poems*’ expression of the sage’s sentiment.

This may show Nine in the Fifth’s "sincerity in bonds," but it is fundamentally because Nine in the Second truly responds to it. Both Nine in the Second and Nine in the Fifth are firm and centered. Firmness in the center is substance; substance enables sincere transformation. Yielding within is emptiness; emptiness makes sincere transformation difficult—yet who could have known otherwise?

Who could have known that in this hexagram Zhong Fu, with yielding within and firmness centered, joyful and yielding, it naturally embodies sincerity and transforms the state? Had it not sincerely responded to Nine in the Fifth, even Upper Nine’s firmness would not escape the flaw of fine sound without substance—why insist on yielding? If it can sincerely respond to Nine in the Fifth, even Six in the Fourth’s yielding can nearly reach fullness and achieve great things—why limit it to firmness?

Thus it is said: In Zhong Fu, even the pig and fish are auspicious; it is beneficial to ford the great river. In this hexagram, the third and fourth lines within are both yin, empty and insubstantial—these are the pig and fish, the hollow boat. Now observe Six in the Fourth and Six in the Third: of the same kind, they were once paired as mates, as old rivals. Yet Three, embodying joy without rectitude, loses balance in sorrow and delight; Four thus rejects its impropriety, severs its kindred bond, forsakes its mate and rival, and ascends directly to bear Five—thereby attaining blamelessness. How then does Four’s sincerity transform Five? Can one say that Zhong Fu’s sincerity cannot extend even to pigs and fish?

Moreover, the two yin lines within form the image of central emptiness; by this inner void, riding the compliant qi of Xun, one crosses the hollow boat to ford the great river—how greatly does this benefit Five? Can one say that Zhong Fu cannot bring auspice even to pigs and fish, or that it is not fitting to do so?

Thus, speaking of Zhong Fu’s substance: its sincerity transforms Two, as the crane’s call summons its young in harmony. Speaking of Zhong Fu’s utmost: its sincerity transforms Four, as near-perfection dissolves all rivalry. Only when sincerity transforms the state and even pigs and fish are trusted can one verify that nothing lies beyond Zhong Fu’s transforming power. What then remains of distinctions between firm and yielding, empty and full? How could it be confined to the firmness of Initial Nine?

For Initial Nine, at the hexagram’s beginning, alone finds joy and contentment in self-preservation, reasoning that if it cannot attain the sincerity of Five and recklessly alters its will to follow another, it would be less auspicious than preserving itself. Does it not stir in response to Two, burning incense in anticipation? And by this, Five reaches the fullness of transforming sincerity.

Pigs and fish belong to the yin class. In *Gou* (Hexagram 44), "the fish is enveloped"; in *Bo* (Hexagram 23), "Six in the Fifth place strings the fish together." *Gou*, *Yingshi* (Hexagram 43), *Daxu*’s Six in the Fifth, and *Jishi* (Hexagram 26) all exemplify this. The crane and the heavens both refer to Five, while its young refers solely to Two. *Xun* (the trigram) represents the chicken, called "the sound of wings." Wang’s edition annotates: "In yin, it is the second yin." Ma Fengyang states: "*Zhong Fu* (Hexagram 61) resembles the image of a bird covering its eggs. The two yin lines form the shape of a chicken’s feet."

**Appendix**
Wang Ji says: "Wind stirring the marsh embodies the image of *Zhong Fu*. The noble person, embodying Heaven and Earth’s virtue of cherishing life, deliberates on punishments and delays executions—this is the meaning of *Zhong Fu*. When a case is about to be concluded, it is deliberated; when a sentence is about to be carried out, it is delayed, thus fulfilling the human heart. To deliberate on a case is to seek a path from judgment to life; to delay is to seek life within death. Shun’s compassion in punishment, Yu’s tears for the guilty, Tang’s prayers at the net, and Wen’s burial of bones—if these did not arise from the sincerity of the heart, who would be moved by them? The Way of utmost sincerity can tread upon water and fire, melt metal and stone, reach Heaven and Earth, and move humankind—how much more the spirits and gods? The king listens to the Three Dukes, listens to the Minister of Justice, listens to the deliberation of cases—this is the deliberation of punishment. After ten days, the case is reviewed; after twenty days, it is reviewed again; after three months, it is submitted—this is the delay of execution. The deliberation of punishment corresponds to the *Dui* (trigram) image; the delay of execution, to the *Xun* (trigram) image."

**Gen below, Zhen above: Xiao Guo (Hexagram 62)**
*Xiao Guo* (Small Exceeding): Smooth progress, beneficial for constancy. Suitable for small matters, not for great ones. The sound of a flying bird leaves its echo—it is not fitting to ascend, fitting to descend; greatly auspicious.

**The Judgment says:**
*Xiao Guo* means that small excesses lead to smooth progress. Exceeding in a way that benefits constancy aligns with the movement of the times. The yielding attains the center, hence small matters are auspicious. The firm loses its position and is not central, hence great matters are not suitable. There is the image of a flying bird: the sound of a flying bird leaves its echo—it is not fitting to ascend, fitting to descend; greatly auspicious. Ascending is contrary, descending is compliant.

**The Image says:**
Thunder above the mountain—*Xiao Guo*. The noble person, in conduct, exceeds in reverence; in mourning, exceeds in grief; in use, exceeds in frugality.

**Line Statements:**
**Initial Six:** The flying bird brings misfortune.
**The Image says:** The flying bird brings misfortune—nothing can be done about it.

**Six in the Second Place:** Exceeding one’s grandfather, encountering one’s grandmother; not reaching one’s lord, encountering one’s minister—no blame.
**The Image says:** Not reaching one’s lord, the minister must not exceed.

**Nine in the Third Place:** If one does not exceed in guarding against it, following may lead to harm—misfortune.
**The Image says:** Following may lead to harm—how could misfortune be avoided?

**Nine in the Fourth Place:** No blame. Do not exceed, but encounter it. Going forward is dangerous—be ever vigilant. Do not persist in constancy.
**The Image says:** Do not exceed, but encounter it—the position is not appropriate. Going forward is dangerous—be ever vigilant, for in the end it cannot last.

**Six in the Fifth Place:** Dense clouds, no rain, from my western suburbs. The lord shoots and takes that which is in the cave.
**The Image says:** Dense clouds, no rain—this is excessive ascent.

**Top Six:** Not encountering, but exceeding it; the flying bird is ensnared—misfortune. This is called disaster and calamity.
**The Image says:** Not encountering, but exceeding it—this is excessive arrogance.

**Commentary on Xiao Guo:**
Four yin lines attain their positions, two yang lines lose theirs—this is *Xiao Guo*. The small, having gained their time and position, exceed. How could this not lead to smooth progress? Yet exceeding may bring smooth progress, but lacking constancy is not permissible. Exceeding in a way that benefits constancy aligns with the movement of the times, for the time is ripe for action. The yielding also attains the center, hence small matters are auspicious, and thus it is said to be suitable only for small matters. However, occupying a position and attaining the center, whether as lord or minister, the affairs are great, not small. Only a firm and strong noble person can manage them. Now, the firm has lost its position and is not central. Occupying the upper position without centrality, losing position and authority—though firm and strong, what can be done? Hence great matters are not suitable, and thus it pertains to the small. If small matters can be accomplished, one acts in accordance with the times; if great matters cannot be accomplished, one defers to the noble person and hopes for their assistance. If one can act with sincerity, knowing one’s place and the times, then even if the position of the small exceeds, in reality it leads to smooth progress. Thus it is said that the small exceed and attain smooth progress because of their constancy. Yet the yielding and weak cannot know their place or the times. They refuse to encounter the yang. Now, the two yin lines occupy the center and attain their positions, assuming the roles of lord and minister. Yet those they align with are the two flying birds of the initial and top lines—even if one or two yang lines remain, they naturally retreat in haste. Therefore, the sage, observing the image of flying birds in this hexagram, uses it as a warning: the flying bird leaves its echo—it is not fitting to ascend, fitting to descend; greatly auspicious. When a bird flies upward, it is contrary; when it flies downward, it is compliant. Now, to claim that descending is fitting and ascending is not—though it may seem self-admonition, it is in truth admonition for the two yang lines. How could a firm and strong noble person wait for the yielding and weak to admonish them to leave before bowing their heads and departing? I fear the flying bird will be ensnared, and though it may wish to leave, it will be unable.

**Initial Six**, dwelling below, delights in advancement and reckless ascent—truly a flying bird. Thus the sage strikes it, saying: This is a flying bird. Though it is at the very beginning and below, the omen of misfortune is already formed. How much more when it clings to the four yin lines or climbs to **Top Six**? It is indeed a creature of misfortune—truly nothing can be done about it. For the two yang lines, is it not better to avoid it early? Yet **Nine in the Fourth Place** responds to it and instead attains no blame, for it knows its strength is insufficient to exceed and ultimately meets it well. However, where there is coming, there must be going; where there is going, there must be danger—can one not be vigilant? How could the world tolerate matters of constant danger and perpetual vigilance?

Thus it is said: where there is danger in going, one must be vigilant—yet this cannot endure forever. From this it follows: how much better to refrain from action altogether. To refrain is to preserve one’s primordial danger; preserving primordial danger is to maintain constancy and firmness.

As for **Nine in the Third Place**, though it knows it cannot surpass and thus strives to guard against it, **Top Six** will take this as intentional provocation. Then, failing to measure its own strength, it may seek to destroy it—like Chen Fan or Bao Wu. I know not how much greater the misfortune will be.

For in the time of *Small Exceeding*, the first and top lines stand in comparison, wielding power and securing position. The firm and strong nobleman has but one move to consider: to refrain. There is no other good strategy. This is what is meant by "it is fitting to be below and not fitting to be above."

**Six in the Fifth Place**, a yin cloud risen high, does not meet with yang. A lone yin cannot complete the image—it becomes "dense clouds with no rain." Thus it is said: "Dense clouds with no rain, for it has risen above." The qi of **Six in the Fifth Place** has already ascended; it is utterly incapable of descending as rain to nourish the people. Though it dwells above the people, in truth it is no different from those below—whether as minister or as matriarch, how can it accomplish anything?

One master said: Does this not warn the three men who, unable to serve as ancestors, instead honored their matriarchs; unable to serve as rulers, instead honored their ministers? **Six in the Second Place**, yielding and soft, cautiously preserves itself from blame, yet in truth it cannot surpass its ancestor, nor even reach its ruler. Yet it deludes itself into believing it has surpassed its ancestor and reached its ruler—is this not folly?

The line does not speak of "surpassing its ruler" but of "reaching its ruler," for the sage’s single word weighs heavily for rulers of all ages. Thus it is said: "It does not reach its ruler, for a minister must not surpass." Therefore the Master, in the *Image*, specially elucidates this principle to warn all generations.

Yet **Six in the Second Place**, like a creature in its burrow, is a small thing unable to take flight. The master, then, knows only to hunt the small creatures in their burrows, unable to mount the dragon and ride the heavens to behold the greatness of humanity. How then can they join together to accomplish anything? For yin does not meet yang—how can the two be completed? Yin seeking yin again—how can it obtain the timely rain of Fu Yue?

**Top Six** has reached the extreme of *Small Exceeding* and is cut off from **Three**; not only does it refuse to meet, but it exceeds it by far. If **Three** does not depart, the flying bird above will soon leave it. As a creature of misfortune, it is no longer like the first—excess brings disaster, excess brings warning. I fear the solitary yang will ultimately find no escape.

Alas! The first and top are creatures of misfortune; the second and fifth are wooden idols. Though there be a Master, what can he do for Ding and Ai? Not serving is the best strategy; tending fields and managing granaries is the middle strategy; serving as Minister of Justice in Lu is the worst. Resigning after three months saves half.

Su Zizhan said: *Small Exceeding* bears the image of a bird. Four yin lines occupy the positions of action, forming its wings. Two yang lines are imprisoned within—its belly and back. The wings wish to fly toward the belly and back, yet cannot restrain the wings; they wish to restrain the belly and back, yet cannot act. Thus, the control of the flying bird lies in the wings.

Chao Yidao said: In times when there was the overreaching Mo Ao, there was also Zheng Kaofu, who corrected him by "walking along the wall." In times when there was Zai Yu, who observed short mourning, there was Gao Chai, who corrected him with "weeping blood." In times when there was Guan Zhong, who took three wives and returned with blemish, there was Yanzi, who corrected him by "rewarding furs." Though not following the middle path, they were sufficient to rectify the times and sharpen customs.

Below *Li*, above *Kan*: *Already Across*. *Already Across*: small prosperity, profit in constancy; at first auspicious, in the end disorder.

The *Tuan* says: *Already Across*—prosperity, for the small prospers. Profit in constancy, for the firm and yielding are correct and their positions proper. At first auspicious, for the yielding attains the center. In the end, cessation brings disorder—its Way is exhausted.

The *Xiang* says: Water above fire—*Already Across*. The noble person, mindful of calamity, takes precautions in advance.

**First Nine**: Dragging its wheels, wetting its tail—no blame.
The *Xiang* says: Dragging its wheels—rightly no blame.

**Six Two**: The wife loses her hairpin; do not pursue it—it will be recovered in seven days.
The *Xiang* says: Recovered in seven days, by the central Way.

**Nine Three**: Gaozong campaigns against Guifang; after three years, he conquers it—petty men must not be employed.
The *Xiang* says: After three years, he conquers it—exhaustion.

**Six Four**: The ragged one has tattered clothes; all day on guard.
The *Xiang* says: All day on guard—there is cause for doubt.

**Nine Five**: The eastern neighbor slaughters an ox, yet does not receive as much blessing as the western neighbor’s simple offering.
The *Xiang* says: The eastern neighbor slaughters an ox, yet does not match the western neighbor’s timely offering. Truly receiving blessing—great fortune arrives.

**Top Six**: Wetting its head—danger.
The *Xiang* says: Wetting its head—danger; how can this last long?

In the hexagram *Already Across*, each instance speaks of "small prosperity," never "prosperity in smallness." Now, *Already Across* speaks of "small prosperity," for prosperity after crossing must be small, not great. If great, it ends in disorder, worse than the initial auspiciousness of the small. Why? The small is *Li*—*Li* is emptiness. The great is *Kan*—*Kan* is cessation.

Now observe: the first line, positioned below the clarity of *Li*, marks the beginning of *Already Across*. Though already crossed, it remains as if uncrossed, with the peril of *Kan* ahead. Wetting the horse’s tail, it drags its wheels, not daring to hastily ford—such is its heart. Could this bring blame? Is there such a principle? Thus, it is rightly without blame. This is the first principle of *Already Across*.

The second line, with luminous virtue at its center, is free from obstruction. Not only is this self already across, but this heart also crosses with it—body and heart both crossed, truly *Already Across*. As a pure yin, a wife’s image, it was once unobstructed, losing its hairpin. After seven days, the central Way returns. Having once been obstructed, it grasps the cause of losing the hairpin, no longer chasing after scenes of success or splendor, thus preserving *Already Across* forever. Thus, it is said: "At first auspicious, the yielding attains the center." Is not *Already Across*’s small prosperity due to this? How could it resemble the third line’s fondness for grandeur and love of achievement?

Now observe: even the worthy Gaozong could not avoid employing petty men to raise troops and attack. How much less others? Even with a great state’s might, campaigning against the petty Guifang still required three years to conquer. Though conquered, its exhaustion was extreme. Had it not been for Gaozong’s worthiness, how could he have avoided the rise of extravagant ambitions?

The rise of extravagant desires breeds indolent complacency, and *Already Across* can no longer be preserved—its ultimate disorder is assured. Thus, at the ninth pentagram, seasonal sacrifices are conducted, with grand offerings of livestock to beseech blessings from the divine spirits. Yet how could they know that those who make timely, modest offerings—rather than lavish displays—truly receive such blessings?

For once the self has attained *Already Across*, stilling the heart becomes easy. The sixth yang line, positioned above the perilous *Kan* (Water), indulges in dissipation and drenches itself—precisely the figure of one who brings final disorder. How could such a state endure?

Some may ask: If this is so, then why is one who remains vigilant all day also counted among those who bring final disorder? The answer: The noble person’s constant vigilance arises from fear that even one person might fail to receive the warmth of their beneficence. Yet if one’s vigilance extends only to the initial garment—merely clothing the destitute—then it amounts to nothing more than charity toward the poor. To obsess over a single garment, to doubt and fear its loss day and night, is laughable. To preserve what need not be preserved, or to fail to preserve what should be preserved—both lead to the same loss of what truly matters. Thus it is said: "To halt in the end is to invite disorder." The act of halting derives from the image of encountering *Kan* and stopping.

Fang Shihua observes: *Li* (Fire) means "adherence." It does not resemble *Li* as armor, spears, or weapons; thus, the campaign against the third line is called "governing darkness," and the target is termed *Guifang*. "Vigilance all day" arises from riding the culmination of *Li*. *Li* is the female ox. *Li* is bound, and *Kan* is its neighbor—hence the fifth line. It is said: *Kan* neighbors the west, while *Li* neighbors the east. The second line is called "west," neighboring the second through timely use.

Hui Gong, frugal and without demands, forever ensures success—thus it is called "auspicious." Is this not the "initial auspiciousness"? Yet by the sixth line, *Kan*’s waters have overflowed, and having enjoyed *Already Across*, one knows only feasting and revelry to the end. The noble person drinks in adversity, drinks when awaiting opportunity—to demonstrate composure. But who has heard of drinking after *Already Across*? To indulge in revelry after success is to court sorrow in the end—how could one not drown like a tile soaked in wine?

**Appendix**
Yang Jian says: *Ji* (既) means "exhaustion." *Already Across* means nothing is left unaccomplished. The phrase "favorable to constancy" refers to the first, third, and fifth lines—all odd, firm, and unadulterated—while the second, fourth, and top lines are even, yielding, and equally pure. The fifth yang line is properly positioned above the sixth, and the second yin line is properly positioned below; all firm and yielding lines occupy their rightful places. If this is not constancy, what is? *Nuò* (糯), as Zixia writes, is the same as *nuò* (懦, "weak"). The *Nei Ze* speaks of the rites for a youth: "Clothing should not be of silk or *nuò*-fabric." The *Shuowen* states: *Nuò* means "short garment." Tattered clothing, like decayed greens, is called *rú* (茹).

**Below Kan, Above Li: Not Yet Across**
*Not Yet Across*: Prospering, the young fox nearly crosses but soaks its tail—no advantage.
**The Judgment** says: *Not Yet Across* prospers, for the yielding attains the center. The young fox nearly crosses, yet remains within the center. Soaking its tail brings no advantage, for it cannot continue to the end. Though the lines are not in their proper positions, the firm and yielding respond to one another.
**The Image** says: Fire above water—*Not Yet Across*. The noble person carefully distinguishes things and assigns them their proper places.

**First Six**: Soaking its tail—regret.
**The Image** says: Soaking its tail—it knows no bounds.

**Nine in the Second**: Dragging its wheels—constancy brings good fortune.
**The Image** says: The second yang’s constancy brings good fortune, for it moves rightly from the center.

**Six in the Third**: *Not Yet Across*—advancing brings misfortune, but crossing the great river is favorable.
**The Image** says: Advancing in *Not Yet Across* brings misfortune, for the position is improper.

**Nine in the Fourth**: Constancy brings good fortune, regrets vanish. With thunderous force, it campaigns against *Guifang*; after three years, it is rewarded by a great state.
**The Image** says: Constancy brings good fortune, regrets vanish—its will is fulfilled.

**Six in the Fifth**: Constancy brings good fortune, no regrets. The noble person’s radiance—having sincerity, good fortune.
**The Image** says: The noble person’s radiance—its brilliance is auspicious.

**Top Nine**: Sincerity in drinking wine—no blame. Soaking its head—sincerity loses its rightness.
**The Image** says: Drinking wine and soaking the head—it knows no moderation.

**Fire and Water: Not Yet Across**
*Not Yet Across* means not yet having crossed. To hastily attempt crossing without having done so is the folly of the young, who know not the peril’s extremity. This is the meaning of "the young fox nearly crosses but soaks its tail"—no advantage, for it cannot continue and thus ends in failure. Like Fu Jian’s million-strong army encamped at the Huai River, it was truly so. For when the self still lies in peril, unable to emerge, yet rashly soaks its tail to cross—what is this if not ignorance of the peril’s extremity?

The second yang line understands this and alone drags its wheels without crossing. Thus, the line alone speaks of constancy bringing good fortune, and **The Image** reiterates its right conduct—no empty words. The third yin line, though above *Kan*, has not yet left *Kan*; to advance would bring misfortune, and it cannot simply proceed. Yet it ascends to the fourth yang line, which occupies the third position of *Li*. For what it rides upon—now the fifth yin line has sincerity toward the fourth, and the fourth, with thunderous force, campaigns. Though it does not follow the fourth’s trust in the fifth to advance, the fifth gains the fourth’s aid to cross *Kan*, meeting the moment and opportunity. Though the third does not advance, it naturally finds advantage in crossing the great river. Why rush? For where there is a ruler, there must be ministers; where there are ministers, affairs must be accomplished. *Not Yet Across* will ultimately become *Already Across*, and naturally, crossing the great river will be favorable. This is why *Not Yet Across* prospers.

Thus it is said: *Not Yet Across* prospers. In the time of *Not Yet Across*, there must naturally be one who can cross. Where there is one who can cross, there must naturally be ministers who can cross. Where there are ministers who can cross, there must naturally come a day when crossing is achieved. Once crossing is achieved, it prospers. Thus it is said: *Not Yet Across* prospers—the yielding attains the center. Yielding yet attaining the center, inwardly radiant with culture, it can thus secure the perilous crossing.

Thus, in the fifth line it is said: "Steadfast and auspicious, free from great regret." And further: "The radiance of the noble one, with sincerity, is auspicious." This speaks of the fifth line’s steadfast auspiciousness without regret, truly because its cultured brilliance resides within, possessing the radiance of the noble one. The reason for this radiance of the noble one lies in its ability to hold sincerity toward the nine in the fourth place, its surplus light extending to the fourth—hence its auspiciousness. Therefore it is said: "The radiance of the noble one, its splendor is auspicious." Where there is radiance, there must be splendor; without splendor, its radiance would not be seen.

Thus, in the time of *Not Yet Across*, though there be such a luminous ruler, one cannot but lack splendor. One cannot but prosper. Observing this, I see that in the sage’s engagement with the world, there is not a single day that is not a time of *Already Across*, nor a single day without the heart of *Not Yet Across*. There is not a single moment that is not a day of *Not Yet Across*, nor a single day without the thought of crossing. Anxiety may arrive while joy still lingers, or joy may just arrive when anxiety arises again. With joy and sorrow following one another, this is why the sage exerts effort without realizing old age is approaching.

Therefore, *Already Across* and *Not Yet Across* unite as one hexagram, and the Dao of the *Changes* reaches its end. The *Creative*’s ceaseless self-strengthening, diligent all day and vigilant by evening, does so for a reason. As for those who drink wine until their heads are soaked, taking *Already Across* as an excuse for slackening, unaware that *Not Yet Across* follows behind—who are they? They are the ones the sage calls "those who cannot endure," "those who do not continue to the end." The ruler loses the state, the minister loses his house, the commoner loses his life, and the scholar loses his heart. From ancient times to the present, who has escaped this? Alas.

Master Fang Shihua said: The fox embodies *Kan* (☵, the Abysmal), and *Kan* remains *Not Yet Across*. Only upon reaching *Li* (☲, the Clinging) is it *Across*. Thus the *Commentary* speaks of *Not Yet Across* as offering prosperity. "The yielding attains the center" refers to the outer *Li*’s six in the fifth place. "The young fox nearly crosses" speaks of not yet emerging, pointing to the inner *Kan*’s initial six. Now, the campaign against Guifang: in *Already Across*, one should refrain from action and guard against three years of exhaustion; in *Not Yet Across*, one should act with awe and hope for three years of reward. *Already Across* warns against stirring up trouble; *Not Yet Across* delights in achievement.

The fifth of *Li* governs the fourth, and "holding to constancy brings good fortune and dispels regret"—the fifth itself should thus "hold to constancy, good fortune, and no regret." The top nine relies on the six in the fifth’s trust to offer sacrifice, and the great enterprise of *Already Across* is feasting and revelry. Yet before long, to soak one’s head in wine is to equal the top six of *Already Across*, and the momentum is bound to fail. Such is the brilliance of trust in the enterprise of *Already Across*—yet how swiftly it reverses! To cross only to lose again—were not the Tang Emperor Ming and others such cases?

Minister Liu Yongxiang said: Heaven-one generates water, Earth-two generates fire. Water and fire are the great functions of Heaven and Earth. *Qian* (☰, the Creative) and *Kun* (☷, the Receptive) thus conclude with *Kan* and *Li*. When *Kan*-water resides below and *Li*-fire above, though they possess the functions of Heaven and Earth, in truth they do not serve one another. *Xian* (☶, Influence) and *Heng* (☳, Duration) thus conclude with *Li* and *Kan*. When water ascends and fire descends, *Kan* and *Li* interact, the heart is at peace, the body is healthy, the state is harmonious—what affair would not be *Already Across*? This is the culmination of Heaven and Earth. How could there be a principle of initial good fortune ending in disorder? Is it not fitting that the Dao of the *Changes* concludes thus?

**Appendix**
Wu Youqing said: In *Not Yet Across*, none of the lines occupy their proper positions. Yet the *Image Commentary* singles out the six in the third place because yin-softness dwells at the extreme of danger.

Wang Fusi said: Though the nine in the second place is not in its proper position, it moves with correctness from the center. The six in the fifth is yielding, compliant, and radiant, occupying the honored position. It entrusts matters to the capable without self-exertion, employing martial strength with culture and governing hardness with softness—truly the brilliance of the noble person. To entrust things to the capable without doubt is to make others exert their utmost, and success is thus achieved. Hence it is said: "With trust, good fortune."

Yang Tingshu said: The six in the fifth encounters the era of *Not Yet Across* yet responds with yin-softness. Yet by holding to constancy it attains good fortune, by sincerity it attains further good fortune, and by brilliance it attains yet further good fortune—why? The six in the fifth embodies *Li*. *Li* in Heaven is the sun; on Earth, it is fire. The sun in summer grows ever hotter, and fire at night burns ever brighter. The six in the fifth’s radiance is so abundant that it can empty its center to illuminate all, while the hundred officials stand firm in sincerity and trust. How could worthy talent fail to sweep away great peril and transform an era of primal difficulty into a time when *Not Yet Across* becomes *Already Across*? Possessing the fullness of threefold good fortune without a trace of regret or blame—what doubt could remain?

*This concludes the third scroll of the Lower Canon of the* Yi Yin.
